
Contact Quarterly's Contact Improvisation Sourcebook


PREFACE
'7he whole point of Newsletter is staying in contact
... if a ball can be got rolling and letters from various
sources added in, we may get a synergistic handle on
issues. Addressing issues from Newsletter to Newsletter
will contribute dimensions in Newsletter. I feel pithy pros
and cons promote our growth and make our newsletter a
better reflection of our multiple stands and moves."
Steve Paxton, CQ Newsletter, Vol 2 #1, Fall 1976
It is Contact Improvisation's 25th anniversary this year, an occasion to celebrate and
reflect on Contact Improvisation as a dance form and its growth from a week of performances at
the John Weber Gallery in NYC in June of 1972 initiated by Steve Paxton with a group of young
dancers, to a movement form practiced by thousands of people all over the world. It has been a
remarkable evolution, much of it chronicled in the pages of Contact Quarterly.
In 1975, a few years into the work, we began hearing of physical injuries resulting from
people doing Contact from having seen it in performance, without an introduction to the subtle ·
sensing work that underlies the more vigorous physical activity. How to keep the work safe? we
wondered. Steve, and the small group continuing to practice and show the work with him, con-
sidered trademarking the name and certifying or authorizing teachers. Preliminary papers were
drawn up but were never signed. The prospect of policing Contact was not appealing. More
interesting was the idea of creating a vehicle for communication, a newsletter, in which to report
activity and current thinking within the work, in this way keeping the work open, and inviting
ourselves and others further into the dialogue.
This seems to have been a crucial decision, one leading to the considerable expansion of
the work and its enrichment by many individuals over the years.
For the First European Contact Teachers Conference, in Holland in 1985, Margot Rijven,
Karen Nelson, and I manufactured a quick, xeroxed compendium of writings on Contact Improv-
isation from all the issues of Contact Quarterly dance journal that we could get our hands on in
Amsterdam, including several of the early issues. It was a handy, fascinating volume which we
reprinted twice for the Contact Teachers Conferences at the Breitenbush Hot Springs Contact Jams
in Oregon in 1986 and 1987. We sold the leftover copies through CQ and that was the end of it.
In honor of Contact's silver jubilee, and because many of our early issues are now out of
print, CQ has produced this new and updated, limited edition CI Sourcebook. In it you'll find
the majority of writings on Contact Improvisation that have appeared in CQ from its inception
as Contact Newsletter in 1975 through Volume 17, 1992. CQ issues from 1993 to the present,
containing increasing amounts of Contact dialogue, are available through CQ as back issues.
The making of the CQ/CI Sourcebook was a big job, bigger than any of us expected.
Each page has been carefully prepared by Harriett Jastremsky and Mette Gustavsen, photocopied
directly from the original magazine pages with minor alterations, adding volume and year
citations at the bottom of each page to orient you. They handled all aspects of the increasingly
complex production with grace, curiosity, creativity and good humor.
Some explanation about the recurring section headings in the book: "Definitions" were a
regular feature in the first years of CQ. We included a different definition of Contact in every issue
along with a photo, in this way hoping to record the variety of styles used for presenting/describ-
ing this new movement form as it developed; many are included here. As CQ grew, several
"departments" were created in the magazine to support the increasing exchange amongst Contact
practitioners. "Round Up: CI in perspective" ran from 1976 to 1981 and included a variety of
reviews and views of the work. In 1984 we instituted "Still Moving: Contact shoptalk and
dialogue," to encourage in-depth discussions of the working process that went beyond the newsy
CQ!CI Sourcebook ix
reports in the Contact Newsletter section of the magazine. This column has continued to grow
in substance and in size over the years.
The CQ/C/ Sourcebook contains lively and personal evidence of the growth of Contact
Improvisation as a duet movement form, the community that grew up around it, and the concur-
rent evolution of the magazine. Evidence of people taking initiative to make events happen and
attempting, in many forms, to articulate their findings. Reading through these years of CQ writ-
ings is like going on an archeological dig, into the heart and mind of the work as it lives in the
people who do it. It is satisfying to have these pages together in a single volume. The spirit of
discovery and exchange is strong; the reflection, dimensional.
We hope you find the Sourcebook a useful and enjoyable addition to your library. Your
responses, as well as your own Contact writings, are always welcome at CQ's mailbox. Please
don't hesitate to be in touch.
Nancy Stark Smith
Coeditor, CQ

Interview with Steve Paxton
by June Johnston
June Johnston: Steve, could you tell me what hap-
pened in the instant of innovation when you sudden-
ly discovered you had something?
Steve Paxton: (laughs) No, I couldn't. That in-
stant began for me probably in 1961 and is still
continuing. It's simply part of the process of ex-
ploration that dance work has been for me. There
was not an instant. There were many, many years
of classes, compiling of material from different
movement forms. There was a time when several
of us in v ermont who were working on this form
decided to name it. I was actively looking for a
name for an '.it' that we· obviously were all involved
with.
June: Could you say what that 'it' was?
Steve: Well, Contact Improvisation was chosen
as a name because it expresses it well. It's a
strange name, I think, but it it hasn't seemed to
have hampered it at all because it is accurate.
It is definitely a physical art and you deal with
the other person physically but there's also all
the other aspects of person that are contacting
each other.
Volume 4 1979 CQICI Sourcebook 1
A SHORT HISTORY
During a Grand Union residency at
Oberlin College in January 1972, Steve
Paxton made a work for 11 men in which
they threw, caught, flung, collided and fell
among one another continuously for l 0
minutes. Then they stood for a few min-
utes. The dance was called 'Magnesium'.
That spring, while teaching at Benning-
ton College, Steve met with two students-
Leon Felder and Nita Little-who had
been stimulated by a similar curiosity with
the physical forces that effect motion, ex-
perimenting with lifting, falling and ex-
changing weight with mutual support. In
June 1972, with funding from Change,
Inc., Steve gathered about 15 "of the best
athletes that I had run into in the course
of a year or two of teaching," both men
and women, to explore the principles and
potential of communication first evident
in 'Magnesium'. The first week was spent
in rehearsal. The 2nd week was a public
showing of the working process, 5 hours
daily, at the Weber Gallery in NYC. This
event was called Contact Improvisation.
Among the participants were Laura Chap-
men, Steve Christiansen (video), Barbara
Dilley, Leon Felder, Mary Fulkerson,
Danny Lepkoff, Nita Little, Alice Luster-
man, Curt Siddall, Nancy Stark Smith,
Nancy Topf and David Woodberry.
Inspired by this event, the work contin-
ued. A tour in early 1973 brought Steve
Paxton, Curt Siddall, Nancy Stark Smith,
Nita Little and Karen Radler to the West
Coast, performing and leading workshops
in Contact Improvisation under the name,
'You Come, We'll Show You What We
Do'. The ambience of the work in per-
formance continued to be informal: no
music, special lighting or costume, audi-
ences in the round, duets and trios inter-
spersed with solo dancing. In June 1973,
Steve brought a group to Europe for the
first time to perform Contact at L' Attico
Gallery in Rome.
By 1974, Nita, Curt and Nancy were in
the San Francisco Bay Area teaching the
form to others, creating more dance-mates
and developing a context for the work's
expansion. Joined by Steve Paxton, in
1975 they formed ReUnion, the first
formal Contact Improvisation company,
meeting once a year to tour the West
Coast with performances and classes. The
continuing dialogue concerning Contact's
development soon included hundreds of
other individuals and groups. The Contact
Newsletter (now Contact Quarterly) was
initiated by ReUnion in 1975, edited and
produced by Nancy Stark Smith, to foster
communication among the geographically
dispersed teachers, performers and practi-
tioners of Contact. Inspired by the work,
the work continues. ~
Lisa Nelson & Nancy Stark Smith

NOTES AFTER TEACHING SECOND CLASS IN MISSOULA MONT ANA
After teaching I walked home and sat and talked
with Janet - I realized today that I am not
interested in the form of Contact Improvisa-
tion but in the spirit the form contains - this
spirit is a real thing and with out the spirit
Contact turns into something like Judo - the
spirit is a living work process that is not nec-
essarily confined to Contact Improvisation but
is a way of playing of attaining the freedom
to play with yourself and others with a tree
or a pole. I want to drum up this spirit. In
teaching Contact I feel two things:
1. That I know and understand the form
but in order to convey the spirit through
the form I must find a something to
teach which is not outside to myself but
is me
2. I've come here to Missoula and I feel
that the form "Contact" is pretty much
com man knowledge or thought to be by
.._,,
alot of people and there are lots of ex-
pectations to what it is - "Get out the
mats - do a round robin"
So I think in order to teach the spirit of con-
tact I cannot teach Contact.
I want to create a situation in which free play
- FREE PLAY - can happen -
Where a certain concentration is going - with
a certain practice - and nothing happens until something is happening
I want to create a situation where
1 nothing happens (an exhausted state)
AND
2 (when you dont know whats gonna happen is happening)
SO TEACH A CLASS IN WHICH YOU REACH A STATE WHERE NOTHING IS HAPPENING BUT POS-
SIBILITIES ARE OPEN - THATS ALL YOU HAVE TO DO ------SURPRISES - -VOICE WORK ---

Volume 1 1975-76 CQICI Sourcebook 3
DARTINGTON MARCH:
Images. Spent time with Mary Fulkerson, teaching and taking classes. Several of our number
[contacters] have studied with Mary, and she has spent time in Contact, though she prefers
her own work as a basis from which to teach. However, the continuing juxtaposition of her
Anatomical Release work and Contact has provoked a headful of thought; her students in Eng-
land and those from Rochester, N.Y. take to Contact with remarkable ease compared to other
groups in the U.S.
This ease of assimilation is of interest. While the students are lying down, crawling, walking or
running, Mary Fulkerson speaks ritually, encouraging greater sensitivity to alignment and efficient
patterns in the body. The manner of presentation tends to by-pass the though~ of the listener.
The listener is preoccupied with moving or relaxing at the time, and the ritualization has a lulling
effect, soothing in tone and timing. The images she provides are simplified anatomical 'cartoons'
of functioning anatomy as opposed to intellectual or visual anatomy. These images describe .sen-
sations - their location in the body and possible directions in which they might move.
The students then assimilate material directly from their own sensations, culled from releasing
unnecessary tension, work, strain.
Mary Fulkerson finds a rare balance of authority and humility which gives fundamental weight
to her voice without usurping the motivation of the student and avoiding their rebellion or
fascination. Nor does her voice question the integrity of the sensations unique to each student.
Their confidence in the process gathers momentum with the growth of awareness of the functional
anatomy, and soon, non-anatomical images can be simply physicalized. Earlier in the process
however, before these channels become clear, an illogical image would more likely provoke bewild-
erment, scorn, or intellectual interest, than movement.
The parallel is with the Contact 'stand': The mind of the listener is encouraged to become a
receiving instrument. Fulkerson's images further provide guidance to sensations which improve
function. The stand in Contact isused to remove the habitual masking of the forces-that-be by
relaxing the listener into states of mind where time, gravity, mass and inertia are apparent, where
balance is seen as subtle movement, and social expectations in partnering can be dropped; it is
possible to talk directly to the body~ and for the listener to listen directly with the body. Each
person can hone and direct real physical sensations into their own most direct & efficient dance.
As Mary Fulkerson is currently presenting her work at Dartingtim College (Devon, England) it
is used as a first step providing the student with a map into private anatomical sensations and
improvements. Great care has been given to the anatomical propriety of the images, because
in this work, the idea each person's mind holds of their body is seen as crucial to the awareness
each develops.
Beyond Mary Fulkerson's work there is a question for the student at Dartington. What to do
with the vocabulary they have sensed and the improvements they find in their use of the body?
Many possibilities exist, and one path, that of Contact, seems quite compatible; moving from
the interior of one's functioning anatomy, to functioning with another. I hope in the coming
years more of us have an opportunity to sample this connection.
STEVE PAXTON
4 CQ/CI Sourcebook Volume 1 1975-76
A MATTER OF DELICACY:
In response to Daniel Lepkoff's letter last issue (Number Four/Spring 76), a question:
How removed from the defined Contact classes and concepts should one be and continue to call
the material Contact Improvisation? There are two sectors this question affects: the contactor5,
and the public; that is, students, prospective students, or views of contact - the public image as
it were, by which to some degree Contact Improvisation is defined.
Contact is not a finished thing. Changes in its methodology and its result are expected.
However, the definition it has achieved has been a consistent one, giving a useful continuity to those
new performers who wish to communicate, especially on paper, what they are doing in concerts
and classes. For the students of Contact (of whom there have been many hundreds at this writing)
it seems only fair to either follow procedures designed to aid them in dancing with community of
contactors at large, or to indicate in titles and descriptions of classes (as Mangrove has done) the
bridge between Contact Improvisation and new attitudes stemming from Contact, that are actually
quite different in practice. The delicacy comes here: How wide is the definition of Contact to be,
and is Contact well used by this extrapolation? An extreme position relative to the established core
of Contact Improvisation should probably be given another name or reserved for advanced contactors
to whom the references would be clear.
This is a century of extrapolation. It is not coincidental that industrial revolution, the
modern arts, the increased pace of communication and travel have all occurred this past 100 years.
Change of attitude, environment, ideas, reality, happen far more easily now and the problem is
this, change is cheap. Definition has become difficult to establish or to maintain.
The artist has a duty/right to manipulate the techniques which contribute to their art.
There are however, responsibilities to these techniques to keep them clear of confusion. For those
dancers already familiar with Contact techniques, it may be that outside the Contact framework more
improvisational adventure may be found. Should it, however, be called Contact? The dilution or the
refinement of that central experience depends on the consideration_ we give its presentation.
STEVE PAXTON

Nijinsky, rockets, platform shoes, Notre Dame, top-hats, the World Trade towers, every spire, every
pedestal, the pre-occupation of Western dance with leaps, the whole notion of Heaven.
Amidst mania for escape, (when did it begin?) from Earth's hold on us, Contact Improvisation springs
up and spreads love of the fall. Instead of the rebellion of blast-offs, there is a surrender of surfaces.
The lust for the "impossible," a moon-landing, a test-tube child, we replace with attention to the actual.
"Find out what the body does; not what you can make it do." The crusade to capture a There is aban-
doned, leaving us here, to fall, and roll and fly.
Christie Svane

EDITOR LETTER
Nancy Stark Smith
Welcome. Yes; you're in the right place. The Contact Newsletter has moved on to become the
Contact Quarterly - a publication put out voluntarily by Free Association four times a year (seasonally).
Free Association isn't an official name for those of us behind the Quarterly, but a term that catches the
spirit of our collaboration. Our personnel shifts with each issue depending on (1) which coast is hosting the
production, and (2) the availability and interest of local contacters for work on the production.
Our common center is a working and experiential knowledge of Contact Improvisation. So far,
everyone who has 'freely associated' on any of the Newsletter/Quarterly publications has had some exper-
ience with this new, now almost five-year-old, dance-movement-meditation-art-sport-event-piece. On that
basic ground we 'stand', and from that stand we swing out - into our overlapping worlds to collect material
and enthusiasm to channel back through the center and out as far as it travels. Arriving safely and with news.
Contact is interface. And though there are only certain faces you've seen before, there are many
more where they came from. Parallel transparencies. Two people inventing electricity within days of each
other on opposite sides of the globe. Coincidence? Yes, Co-Incidence. And in a likewise but less dramatic
manner, we find more of each other every day.
Contact Improvisation has the unique quality of being believed - whether admired, respected,
scorned, berated, inflated or ignored. Yes real time. Yes real bodies. Yes real people. This particular
credibility allows Contact Improvisation and those involved in it, to run tangent to many worlds - relating
deeply in to those worlds without either becoming distracted or dilute. In fact, the communication which we
continue to develop among ourselves and articulate in our dancing serves us well outside our immediate
numbers. It is what creates our access to the unfamiliar faces, the rapidly surfacing and the as-yet unsurfaced
numbers of people who are working with the material of their lives in just such a direct manner as we attempt
in our teaching, performing and writing of Contact Improvisation.
We 'stand' together at the ring of a watering hole; our weight brings the water up, our bodies,
the weight down.
So, that's where we come from. And here is where we are.
Volume 2 1976-77 CQ/CI Sourcebook 7
EDITOR'S REPORT I High Moon - 11th Hour Reflections
Nancy Stark Smith
The image I had for the last issue (Winter 1976) was the water hole. The
place where we collect to drink. A circle where everyone who comes fits
in. All those cows bent to the water, the lines of their backs, helped me
re-invent the wheel. At the watering hole we stood in order to bring our
weight down; our weight brought the water up. We came full circle.
This time we started again with nothing. New moon. We collected ourselves.
As the last light left, we arranged ourselves in a blind configuration; (blackout
on stage, the lights come up and the performers arc all in place). We started.
Then the crescent moon - we seemed to use the light as it grew, filling in the
implied circle. Journal entry, March 24: " ... Musing new directions, transition
at its most spread out point, as entropy of possibilities peaks." The moon, a new
image to fill, and we, in as much detail as possible, articulate the filling.
Learning the ropes means knowing how far they can take you; how much weight
they can hold. One day, the arc of the rope's swing is predictable and suddenly
short. There is another side; the side just outside your reach. But is your reach
the rope's, or, if you can find a vehicle with a larger range should you ride it. "Lim-
its are what any of us are inside of," Charles Olson said in a talk that was recorded.
In ReUnion's Contact classes we often talk during the warm up. Not necessarily to
suggest specific activities, exercises or structures, but to create an environment, to
supply images that may call out a particular awareness. We talk to the body as
it experiences sensation. We say, "You know that place when you stretch, the
place where the muscle has eased out to its limit, that place on the edge of
stress, on the edge of pain." We say, "Work there, spend some time there,
on this side of the edge." "The edge is home," Lee Breuer said during a
radio interview in Washington, D.C. in 1975. But it seems the edge is
moveable - that by sensing the limit, respecting it, you encourage it
to open. A push comes to a shove. But if you go only as far as you
are welcome, you are invited back.
What has this got to do with the Contact Quarterly. Not sure,
only that I sat down to present the image I was having for
this process. How we stand. How we stand still, and
keep moving.
Palm Sunday. Full Moon. Lunar Eclipse.
NANCY'S FIST & WRIST
LISA'S ErnOW

CONTACT IMPROVISATION - A STATEMENT OF APPRECIATION
Danny Lcpkoff
Contact Improvisation is a way for people to come together and interact on a purely physical
level. Fostering a healthy interaction is the important concern.
In coming to a dance one must be ready to engage in a constant effort to focus on the physical
sensations of weighted movement, balance and stillness. It is not the issue to identify through any
conscious thought process what the sensations are; you need not know "what physical sensations
you are experiencing," you must know how to focus with constant effort. It is. the strength and purity
of this effort which will foster a healthy interaction in dancing with another.
The simplicity of the form Contact Improvisation is a gift. Its value is that it Is A Way for you and
I to dance together. To befriend the forces of gravity and momentum is to dance with a sense of humor
and constant humility, is to discover one's being self, is to discover true communion.
The essence of this dancing is the concentration and focus one brings as a dancer, it is only through
this constant effort that our dancing will continue to be.
Four laws of ecology: "(1) everything is connected to everything else;
(2) everything must go somewhere;
(3) nature knows best; and
(4) there is no such thing as a free lunch."
Barry Commoner in The Closing Circle,
A. Knopf, New York, 1971, p. 18.

A LOOK BACK AND A LOOK FORWARD
Danny Lepkoff
The work I have done in Contact Improvisation has, needless to say, had a powerful eff~ct in my
life. In the late spring of 1972 Steve told me of a piece he wanted to do in N.Y.C., and I went down
there to rehearse and perform a dance called Contact Improvisation. The work that was done for that
performance and subsequent gatherings for intense periods of dancing was larger and affected more
levels of my being than I was aware of at the time. Most forceably I was struck by the simple yet over-
whelming experience of communion with another person achieved through the dancing, and the freedom
as well as discipline found within that communi~n. I remember feeling literally transported to another
world when dancing, a world in which I was able to become a stronger self than I lived with day to day.
I don't think, in that first performance, anyone fully realized the power of the forces brought into play
among us. Many injuries, pulled, sore, and knotted muscles were the physical result of irresponsibility
in the face of those forces. I became impressed with a sense of responsibility to myself and those I was
dancing with to create a healthy interaction.
The work done during that concert became not something I could forget. The realm of Contact
Improvisation I realized was not "dance" but living, the forces at play between two people dancing are
those forces which exist day to day, the strong self I had experienced was nothing but myself. I was left
with the ever present challenge to realize my strong self in day to day living as well as the responsibility
of achieving a healthy and true communion with those people I meet day to day. I suppose this task
is universal for all living beings, however I have received a lot of strength and focused energy specifically
from the work I have done with Contact Improvisation.
I realize now that in order to continue this work I must be involved in the process of transmission.
lt'seems that with understanding and experience of the forces at play in Contact Improvisation, receiving
becomes transmitting, student becomes teacher, dancing becomes giving. It seems very natural, and I
do not hesitate to think that any one who has had enough experience with Contact will feel this shift of
role. It is no wonder there are now many teachers of Contact Improvisation.
The dancing is just beginning to expand on a social level, into our society. What will be the direction
of this expansion? Contact was created in the context of the dance art world. This is a good place, how-
ever the traditions of possession and ownership among artists are unhealthy for Contact Improvisation.
My dream is that Contact will grow to become a social function. Periodically people living in the same
area will gather for a day or week of dancing, for the sole function of communally reinforcing and acknow-
ledging the strong self inside each person in the face of those one lives with. Walking down the street and
greeting someone, buying groceries in a store, and all the daily interactions of people will be on a truer
more realistic footing, and a general climate of health may be achieved. This may seem far fetched but
I feel the beginning is at hand and in view of this direction it is obvious that we need to:
continue dancing and deepening our experience and familiarity with
the forces at play in Contact Improvisation
keep lines of communication open, i.e. this newsletter,
and cooperatively give and share the experiences and
information gleaned from dancing and teaching.
The class I tried to start in Bennington this summer did not work out. I am trying again this fall
to get some people together to dance. I think I will be picking apples this fall. The seasons are changing
here, color on the trees and colder nights and shorter days, new feelings emerge and don't fit comfortably
yet. Hope to see some of you again, maybe hit the west coast again this year.
Hello to All

CQ/CI Sourcebook 11
Bodies in Contact , cont'd.
As a performer I have often been approached
. by members of the audience who are in wonder
about the phenomenon of Contact Improvisation.
A 170-pound man is dancing with a 120-pound
woman, rolling up and down her back and over
her shoulders, or being carried by her. Is it a skill
of gymnastic trickery? Not at all. Contact Im-
provisation is an expression of sensitivity in both
the physical and emotional realms. It demands,
above all, the ability to yield to the present
moment.
On a descriptive level, Contact Improvisation
. is a new movement form, improvisational in
nat.ure, involving two bodies in contact. Impulses,
weight and momentum are communicated through
a point of physical contact that continually rolls
across and around the bodies of the dancers.
Take two human bodies standing face to face.
Each one has a center of gravity within the body
somewhere in the vicinity of the navel, the
exact location depending on the proportions of
the individual. If they lean towards each other
and support each other simultaneously, they then
create a weight/mass system that ttas a center
of gravity in space somewhere between their
na~els. Like boleroes, fluid and eccentrically
weighted, the dancing bodies swing, bounce,
roll, swim and fly through and with the the
common center of gravity. As the participants
move, this center moves.
The original format for conducting contact
sessions is a "round robin." As the participants·
circle around the space a dynamic environment
is created; in response to this environment, the
dancers allow certain beginnings to occur. In·
tention and forcefulness have nothing to do with
all of this: The dance is ever changing, never
the same. A series of events takes place as un-
interrupted, pure transition from a beginning to
an end. Working with Contact Improvisation is
discovering how it feels to let this transition
take place organically. In each individual duet it
becomes apparent when an ending needs to
happen. The dancers are obligated by the form
to let these endings occur.
Like any discipline, Contact Improvisation
requires serious work to gain proficiency. As in
learning a new language, the movement and
concepts feel awkward at first, but with practice
one is able to find a personal range of expression.
Participation is not limited to "dancers," but
rather to anyone who wants to develop new skills
in movement.
The technique is most influenced by the disci-
plines of aikido, hatha and pranayama yoga and
gymnastics, all of which teach us tt1at energy is
conserved if the body knows how to flow along
12 CQ/CI Sourcebook
with what is happening instead of exerting energy
to determine the flow or to avert it. People, like
all creatures, are fearful of things unfamiliar.
Worki11g in this form one develops an apprecia-
tion of the unexpected within the realm of impro-
vising and, I have found, outside of it in every-
day life. In particular, one learns to feel com-
fortable in difficult or awkward body positions
and transitions. Certain physical skills are
developed and practiced: rolling, balancing,
posting, sliding, lifting, being lifted, falling,
relaxing, centering, using peripheral vision, using
dizziness, accepting the loss of horizon. These
specifics of physical discipline allow the body
to grow into this new type of expression safely
and smoothly.
For instance there is a basic concept of a
"post." The post is the means of support in a
weight/mass system at a given point in time.
The person acting as the post may change back
and forth ten times in twenty seconds or the post
may maintain that role for ten minutes. "Peripheral
vision," another basic concept, is a defocusing
of the eyes. Information from anywhere. within
the field cf view has equal importance. For most
people this useful field of view is almost 180
degrees.
An extreme example of this type of perceptlon
occurs in spinning: the eyes are open wide and
directed straight out, not attaching to objects
but rather noticing the patterns that define the
immediate environment and how it is changing.
When other people are moving in the space the
information that can be gained from this tool of
peripheral vision becomes extremely important,
with it the dancer can negotiate to safety and
flow. Another physical reality the dancer must
adjust for is "loss of horizon." In contact impro-
visation we can depend on only three things:
(1) one's self, (2) that the force of gravity works
continually in one direction, down, and (3) that
the floor supports our weight. It is a difficult task
to say goodbye to that level line that we are un-
consciously oriented to twenty-four hours of the
day, the horizon.
The hands are de-emphasized to about the
same utility as the foot; a means of support at
the end of the arm and a linear extension of the
movement in the arm. This was one of the first
lessons taught by Steve Paxton, the originator of
Contact Improvisation, back in those first work
sessions in New York: the hands never grab. This
type of action is a natural response for a beginner,
but is simply too dangerous and painful to one's
partner. Relaxed fingers and the use of every
square inch of the body as a contact surface or
"handle" is the sign of an experienced contact
improviser.
Volume 2 1976-77
Bodies in Contact , cont'd.
Another primary skill. though not necessarily
a phys!cal skill per se, is that of mental relaxation.
This involves letting go of the tendency to
c!cterrnine the movement. and the tc:iciency to
create security by keepinq on top of the events
through simultaneous intellectual explanations.
This relaxation can be achieved by focusing on
the point of contact, and on the material that
crops up. Every phrase is important, espedally
in the sense that it is the link to the next one.
All of the technical work is aimed at develop-
ing an in-depth understanding of a central concept
in Contact Improvisation: the "energy pathway."
Intellectually it is a somewhat vague concept
and is much more clearly understood when
experienced. However it may be thought of as
the path of least resistance in the moment to
moment reality of each dancer. If the dancer has
really found this "energy pathway," decisions
---
need not be made at all while partiqipating in a
duet.
Contact Improvisation can be described on
mnny levels, as it is something that takes place
on many levels. I think of language as a metaphor.
Conversation is a duet, it may be between a
number of people but the exchange of informa- ,
tion is on a one-to-one basis with a ba~k and
forth flow of ideas. Tile word vocabulary also
applies. The body is limited to a vocabulary of
expression just as in verbal communication. I
believe the sheer number of useful movement
building blocks is potentially as large as a
vocabulary such as the English language. Contact
Improvisation is a network of communication
frameworks for the dancer; the main emphasis
is on the here and now of each dance communi-
cation.

"The issue of how the scientific observer, or any other observer, separates
himself from the object being observed has been debated for 2500 years
without advancing a step, University of Georgia psychologist Ernst von
Glasersfeld told a rapt audience of several hundred scientists.
Anyone with a knowledge of aerodynamics can predict the path of a leaf
falling from a tree during a breeze. But mechanics can't explain the beha-
vior of a frog that might resist a wind strong enough to move an inanimate
object of its own weight, but will jump if a shadow falls on it.
That requires a tlieory of perception and information processing in the
frog."
Walter Barney. 'Dismantling the scientist's 'objectivity'' Sunday S.F.
Examiner & Chronicle. February 27, 1977. Reporting on the 143rd
annual convention of the American Association for the Advancement
of Science, Denver, Colorado.
SEEN FROM THE OUTSIDE
Ernst von Glasersfeld
In ordinary, every-day situations, to make contact
there have to be two. That seems a commonplace
and we simply take it for granted. Watching Con-
tact Improvisation, however, is a strange experience
at first. The making, keeping, and stretching of
contact, there, does not easily fit the accustomed
conceptual schema. It takes quite a while to discov-
er the reason: Its contacts involve not two but four.
In the world of our experience, what we call our "self"
is no more and no less than what we experience of our-
selves. In terms of our senses, our "self" is composed of
what we perceive of ourselves, visually and tactually for the
most part, plus what we internally sense about the states of
, our muscles and organs. This "self" is necessarily very different
from what we are in the experiential world of another person who,
visually and tactually, experiences us from a wholly different point of
view. In Contact Improvisation, therefore, there are two selves, each striving to maintain
contact with another person. Both are constantly the acting subject of their own actions, and at
the same time they are the object of the other's actions. And since the possibilities of making
and maintaining contact are nearly always many, the coordination of motion of the one
dancer that perceives the other and the other that perceives the one, is threatened
at every moment. There is a perpetual struggle to neutralize inherent discrepan-
cies and to preserve a reciprocally invariant relationship of contact. Seen from
the outside, this becomes a powerful and at times shattering allegory of the
precariousness of any harmony in human relations.
If this is not too idiosyncratic an interpretation, it must raise the question:
Will Contact Improvisation really remain a moment-to-moment improv-
isation or will it gradually develop regularities, fixed sequences, and
rules? Contact of two independent agents can be maintained only to
the extent to which one can foresee the motions of the other. (That
goes for ALL relationships, and that is why we always need some
rules and conventions). To IMPROVE in the art of Contact Improv-
isation (or in the art of relating to others) may mean to get better at
predicting the· other's moves AS WELL AS to get better at doing
what 'he orhe-r ex pee.rs.

You guide the student to the 'teacher in himself'
You share your knowledge of the dance by addressing yourself to that part of the student that already knows
If you hold on to your knowledge then you will feel insecure as a teacher
You must see your knowledge as limitless, this is what it is.

CONTACT IMPROVISATION / A DEFINITION
Danny Lepkoff
Contact Improvisation is a duet movement form. Two people move together, in contact,
maintaining a spontaneous physical dialogue through the kinesthetic sensual signals of shared weight
and common or counterpoised momentum. The body, in order to open to the sensations of momentum,
weight, and balance, must learn to release excess muscular tension and abandon a certain amount of
willful volition to .the natural flow of movement at hand. Skills such as rolling, falling, and being upside
down are explored:, guiding the body to an awareness of its own natural movement possibilities. Simple
and direct duet exercises allow couples to explore and focus on specific relationships which come up in
free improvisation - i.e. supporting or taking someone's body weight, giving your own weight, jumping
on someone, rolling off of someone. Once certain simple exercises and sensations are introduced the
realm of organic body movement becomes very accessible to the body, and a great deal of one's essential
energy can be expressed. It becomes important to develop a state of alertness, the senses so poised, that
one may work in an energetic state of physical disorientation with safety, trusting one's basic survival
instincts.
All the work leads to free duet dancing: together with a partner one freely explores movement
possibilities, an experiment, a time to ·play, a time to dance togetherness, a time to be and speak through
your body.
Contact Improvisation deals fundamentally with organic body movement. Organic body movement
is motion manifest in activity directly dealing with the physical forces of the environment, gravity being
the major influence, ~ovemc:nt in which the body and senses are engaged in the effort of survival.· Sports
and games develop the body's organic movement possibilities to a peak. "Contact" has been called an
art-sport. Contact Improvisation speaks for the belief that the realm of organic body movement is limit-
less and rich in the possibilities for expression of one's self, one's being.

" Language, once it comes alive, behaves
like an active, motile organism. Parts of it are
always being changed, by a ceaseless activity to
which all of us are committed; new words are
invented and inserted, old ones have their mean-
ing altered or abandoned. New ways of string-
ing words and sentences together come into
fashion and vanish again, but the underlying
structure simply grows, enriches itself, expands.
If language is at the core of our social
existence, holding us together, housing us in
meaning, it may also be safe to say that art and
music are functions of the same universal, gen-
etically determined mechanism. These are not
bad things to do together. If we are social
creatures because of this, and therefore like
ants, I for one (or should I say we for one?)
do not mind. "
Lewis Thomas
from The Lives of a Cell
EDITOR NOTE
At some point in the growth of Contact Improvisation
there was a call for definition, rules, a clear way to identify what
was {or wasn't) Contact Improvisation. ("There are no lines,
only edges." Ansel Adams) Only a few years after its inception,
Contact seemed to be spawning generations of contacters so
quickly as to be involved in some kind of 'telephone game'.
John tells Sally who shows Sue who passes it on to her brother
who tells his wife who shows their kids who bring it to school
the next day for recess. If John were walking by the schoolyard
at noon, would he recognize anything familiar going on in the
playground? For all the interest in defining Contact Improv-
isation, the answer to that quandary is in watching a contact duet
between two dancers who have just met. The definition is in
the doing.
Contact bases its language on the natural laws that govern
motion (gravity, momentum, inertia, etc.). With these laws as
our constitution, we have no need for legislation. The natural
world enforces its own laws - drop a book, consciously or not,
and it wil I fall. Clearly, Contact Improvisation is not the begin-
ning or the final stage in the opus that relates the body and its
movements to the earth and the other movers on it. The truths
we find in Contact, whether basic or elevated, are truths that
exist with or without Contact Improvisation as a means for re-
vealing them to us. Often in the enthusiasm of the work it is
possible to forget that bodies moving in contact are not new.
It is, however, the continued focus on the elements involved in
that contact, techniques for it, applications of it, that is specific
to Contact Improvisation.
Making a magazine (CQ) for the exchange of information
and viewpoint instead of making a congress, allowed the various
subtexts that existed to emerge and be shared. As a technique
for moving together, Contact resonates in the lives of the prac-
tioners. Those resonances are personal and varied, and though
they enrich the work, the basic practice seems unchanged.
After 6 years, Contact Improvisation continues, with
or without music, in twos, threes, in groups with a name or with-
out. Contacters have gathered in bodies, regional families, to
corroborate and continue. A context has developed similar to
social dancing wherein people can come together and 'jam'.
Tiirnugh Contact Quarterly a network exists, making known
places where Contact can be found. When a poker player comes
into a new town, he looks for the game.
Volume 3 1977-78
Nancy Stark Smith
Lisa Nelson

THE POLITICS OF CONTACT
Byron Brown
On Saturday afternoon August 20, 1977, some thirty participants and teachers of FOCUS 8/77 gathered
to talk about the Politics of Contact. The issue was as much one of defining the question or concern as it was coming
up with an answer. The discussion began with a minimal definition of politics: awareness of and concern for the
power relationships in a given interaction or situation, particularly those relationships which are unacknowledged.
or assumed and unquestioned. From this beginning the discussion was wide-ranging, spirited and largely unstructured.
Going back over a ta pc-recording of the seminar a month later, I have selected out and arranged (without
regard to original sequence) representative and provocative comments to share with the larger contact community.
To my knowledge this discussion was the first attempt to look at our work from this perspective and clearly it is only
one small step, but an important one. In retrospect, I saw our talk centering around two issues.· The first was seldom
defined as a focus but kept surfacing in people's comments: what kind of needs are filled by contact improvisation'?
Consuelo: I was interested in figuring out why it's come down that because of privilege like the $125
privilege and the class background privilege, why it is that this environment has developed around
contact; but I don't see that contact itself is this white cultural kind of ...
John: I'm not sure I agree with that, for one thing it's demonstrating the needs ... contact like anything
else is fulfilling certain needs.
Eleva: That's what I was going to say earlier, perhaps it's the white middle class that needs to get in touch
with their bodies.
Tom: What I see it gets down to is am I willing to go into the world receptively and attentively to what's
happening around me and to be in touch with my needs in a particular situation and other people's
needs. I really think it is something about the reason we're here is because we need to be here, else
we wouldn't be here, to learn whatever we're getting from this, and I see it very much in spiritual terms ...
this group of people is here to get what we're getting and I think a lot of people don't need it or are
doing it in another form.
Elizabeth: The same kinds of things that happen in contact are things that I think have happened to other
kinds of people in other situations, like in work situations where men do heavy physical work together
and might have to use their bodies in the same kind of cooperation and same kind of sensitivity to each
other ... but different than a dance situation ... which comes with a different kind of awareness.
Broni: Its purpose is different too.
Mark: This may relate to why this kind of event is strictly one ethnic group or one social class. I'm con-
cerned in society all over the country how it seems there's a great gap between this kind of situation
of workshops and focusing on a specific kind of dance and what I would call the masses ... their
desire to move and to socialize and be part of something which is being generally exploited. For
those people who are not coming to studio spaces and being part of something, the whole word dance
means to them Friday night and swallowing a six pack of beer and going to a bar or disco; I think
it's exploitative and I really feel there's a gap between that space and this space and it's really tough
to breach.
jerry: Contact has a lot in common with folk art and we are a folk ... we invent it as we go along, we
make it out of what we've got and the fact that what we've got is a college education, and money,
and attractive healthy bodies, and good teeth, you know, is different from having a bunch of ma-
hogany trees and a couple of rocks ... Contact improvisation has roots in dance, it was found by
dancers, and it has gone away from being a dance thing; it is a movement form. There are people
here who are not primarily dancers and the form is reaching out toward those people the way some
people seem to think we should be reaching out toward different classes of society or different
ethnic groups.
18 CQ/CI Sourcebook Volume 3 1977-78
Mark: I feel that there are some aspects of this kind of dance that are slightly different than any kind of
particular outlet that supplies the needs for any particular ethnic group. I really feel that there are
some aspects of contact that are evolutionary .. I can't think, unless somebody can fill the space, of
other kinds of dance in the past which have been this free with sharing and this much touch and con-
nections .. I think this kind of dance is more significant in specific kinds of problems, like sexual
politics, for example, men dancing with men ... and even in the sense that it doesn't rely upon other
forms like music - goes right to an essence or centering ... This form of dance crosses a few more
bridges than others have.
Consuelo: You mean in the sense that it is kind of like a future vision of how people can relate to each
other in terms of sensitivity and activity?
Jon: In terms of sexual politics we're still dealing with one on one, two people getting together, and contact
gives you metaphors for expanding that to three or four or ten or twenty or whatever and that should
be explored too. ·
Keriac: Watching a ballet that gets into these boy.girl things, I don't get much metaphor for my life from
those; whereas I really find the material in contact, the interpersenal stuff that's happening in contact,
is a really useful sophisticated metaphor for what I'm experiencing. ·
Consuelo: Right now I feel really committed to art for change. It's really not enough to do a contact
performance for me, it really isn't. I feel like I want to know more what's going to come out and
deliver a message; and I don't mean just whatever message is there but I mean pretty specific messages.
Kris: There's something about pure energy that gets exchanged between people, we become something
of everything we are around, and when I see a performance I become those performers; and it means
a lot to me and it's the strongest and most pure learning experience I have and it's devoid of content.
It's not statements.
Consuelo: Well, that sounds like a pretty powerful statement.
I see that most of these needs in relation to contact have to do with the desire to break down the uneven
power of the various roles that people assume in relating to one another, be it male to male, dancer to nondancer,
or performer to audience. Contact improvisation as a political process can thus be seen as a means for bringing
people together in a way that prevents or at least discourages the assumption of roles and inequalities of power
which roles so often establish. There seemed to be agreement that, on an immediate personal level, contact achieved
this aim if the integrity of the physical form was respected.
The second issue of the discussion seemed to center around the kind of relationship the contact community
and the contact form is establishing with the larger society. Does and will this relationship maintain the same freedom
from inequalities in power and thus allow all people access to the vital (role-less?) movement relationships which are
contact improvisation?
Karen: The way to deal with it is to work on both levels - one is on the normal ground level of literally
dealing, believing that change takes place and revolution takes place within my own body first and
then at the same time being aware of where my body is: is it in the studio, in Cat's Paw, and that
door is closed unless I have $125, and then what do I do about that; and I think there are two dif-
ferent issues and both are necessary.
Brent: But we made this experience available for ourselves ... at least I did.
Karen: Because it is within your program in life to be able to do that. We're talking about a whole part
of the world where it isn't part of the program - it's not there ... so is art going to be something
we're talking about, an eclectic thing that stays where it is, or can this particular art form go out
because it doesn't have to have all that professional dilettantism that other forms have. Can it just
be a way of reaching people on another basis?
Laurie: Not right now.
john: This is a white middle class trip and other people out of that might not necessarily want to have it.
Volume 3 1977-78 CQICI Sourcebook 19
Laurie: And they might and they can't because they can't afford to.
John: That's not true.
Laurie: If one person wants to and can't ...
John: There have been set up free situations for people to study this and it is only taken by white upper
class people. '
Ernie: Sometimes it becomes a real trip that we are laying on them because we don't have the sensitivity
to know what they need.
Mark: What is the balance between being crusaders for contact and making it an available form for those
that are interested?
John: It is a manifestation I think in many ways of class differences and therefore class problems that we
should deal with; solutions are tough.
Evelyn: Most of the arts are a social ... I mean there's a certain class of people that do them. For a long
time they were a very elitest thing.
Ernie: But is it elitist? That word really blows my mind cause it's a power term. It assumes that if we
develop a dance form, it is automatically elitist. It's a dance form! ... something that came out of
our culture. Our culture may be elitist in a lot of ways - money and social structures generally, but
that doesn't mean the dance form is elitist.
Laurie: But it reflects the society.
Karen: I am forever grateful in one way, in an aesthetic sense, and in other ways, for things that have come
out of formalized, in a sense elitist, art forms and I value them. Then I hear what we're saying on the
other side. Now is there room for both ways to be?
Keriac: If you are thinking of contact as a folk art and experiencing it, it is quite a different setup than if
you are thinking of it as a performing art or a spectator thing. Like Karen was talking about, how can
we have both of these things, that they both enrich our lives? I think that it is facetious to say that
it is a folk art so that we can all rest our minds about that, you know, that we don't have to be con-
cerned about ... that we're clean; this is a folk art, it's ok. One can use contact lots of other ways -
the relative morality, the rightness or wrongness or whether it fits into your value system should be
examined.
Ernie: If you take something like contact ... and you can make it into where you're more sensitive to
life in general, the balances and flux of life in general, then it becomes evolutionary; but if you take
it and it becomes another package, then that's what it is, another package.
Some questions arc asked. concerns expressed and a dialogue begins. I hope the excerpts above may have
provoked thoughts in you and that you will share them with others. It is part of the dance. I hope that l have not
distorted too much the meaning of what people said by taking comments out of context or order. And in reading,
please realize the text above is in some sense my interpretation, my reflection, my recreation of what took place.
You had to be there. . . ·

sponsored Focus: 8/77. He lives and teaches contact in San Francisco, California.

IN THE COURSE OF TEACHING
Alan Ptashek
Accept your limits. I ask that students allow themselves the permission to realize their limitations.
I also encourage the sudden stretch beyond limitation. To see within this language the existence of
a subtle yet expansive definition of the word 'risk'. "Isn't that very risky?" asked a student looking
up at a friend pivoting in the valley between neck and shoulder of her partner. I believe this work
and other explorations compatible with Contact always involve the element of risk, whether we are
rolling along air-born arcs or standing still.
As flow does not mean slow, receptivity needn't be measured by passivity. 'Listening' is one of my
favorite words in describing the force behind transition.
Attention needs to wander to stay interested. In the teacher, your partner, yourself. Re-circulate.
Hearing Robert Creely opinionate that the full human attention is there for forty minutes.
The concern for rhythm and balance of class time. Developing trust in the quiet of before and after-
images. The forms of students moving, trucks on the freeway, the teacher's words measured and heard
by breath, inter-relate and create a whole entity.
The class assists a teacher in locating his or her placement between the skills of observance and verbal-
ization. Besides the basic sense of too much or not enough (alternately, I've been told by students
how there is excessive technical input or a frustration due to a casual and modest directive), we learn
to use the word if it fits. Of course there is still much more to say.
In one of his first introductory classes, asking: "ls the contact point the tension between trust and
responsibility?" In agreement - yes, Danny, there seems to be many levels of lifting experience.
Dancing well with new students is the most advanced part of my learning.

REMEMBERING .......... FORGETTING
It's easy after teaching awhile to forget the beginning, to forget how to share the first steps
of an idea or experience. Remember learning how to walk? It was a logical organic progres-
sion. The body was the teacher. It knew just how to prepare for the next step-it wriggled
and squirmed to get ready to roll, rolled to get ready to creep, creeping became crawling,
crawling prepared for sitting, squatting to standing to falling to standing to stepping to fall-
ing to walking. Watch babies. They have much to teach about how to teach and learn.
In teaching/learning contact improvisation, what are the first steps? What are the basic ex-
periences needed for the next step and the next? Perhaps it would be helpful to remember,
to retrace, the first year of contact improvisation.
What is the last thing you learned?
Can you remember learning it?
How did it happen?
Teaching should reflect the learning process. And learning mirrors the breathing rhythm.
INformation EXploration Pause !Nspiration.
"Oh I get it," said the small child,
"First you think it. Then you see it.
Then you fo~get it. Then it happens."•
That's a good lesson plan. And a reminder to teachers and learners: Remember to forget it.

THE SMALL DANCE
EUZABETI-1: At the very beginning of a Contact Improvisation workshop,
teachers often talk about the 'small dance'. Why do _YOU start
with the 'small dance'?
STEVE: Well, first of all, it's a fairly easy perception: all you have to do
is stand up and then relax-you know-and at a certain point you real-
ize that you've relaxed everything that you can relax but you're still
standing and in that standing is quite a lot of minute movement: .. the
skeleton holding you upright even though you 're mentally relaxing.
Now in that very fact of you ordering yourself to relax and yet continuing
to stand-finding that limit to which you could no further relax without
falling down; you 're put in touch with a basic sustaining effort that goes
on constantly in the body, that you don't have to be aware of. It's
background movement static-you know-that you blot out with your
more interesting activities, yet it's always there sustaining you. We 're
trying to get in touch with these kinds of primal forces in the body and
make them readily apparent. Call it the 'small dance' ... It was a name
chosen largely because it's quite descriptive of the situation and because
while you 're doing the stand and feeling the 'small dance' you 're aware
that you 're not 'doing' it, so, in a way, you 're watching yourself perform;
watching your body perform its function. And your mind is not figuring
anything out and not searching for anv answers or being used as an
active instrument but is being used as a lens to focus on certain
perceptions. DC
ELIZABETH ZIMMER interviewing STEVE PAXTON on CBC Radio
bottom

THE SMALL DANCE, PHYSIOLOGY AND IMPROVISATION
by Ann Woodhull
There is beauty in standing relaxed, allowing body sways to occur and be cor-
rected, doing 'the small dance'. For me, the beauty of the small dance is not only
in feeling it, but also in its biology. "Imperfections" like swaying are necessities
of life; deviations and corrections happen on all levels from molecules to evolution.
By giving some details about the physiology of standing, I can convey these ideas
more concretely.

The stretch reflex is a dandy mechanism for keeping standing without thinking
about it, since all this action is at the spinal level, below consciousness (which
resides in the brain somewhere). But in telling about it, I have ignored important
parts of the system: the links to awareness and the controls on the reflex.
Awareness of the knee moving happens
as a result of impulses travelling on
nerve cells up to the brain. The stretch
receptor nerves extend branches up through
the spinal cord to parts of the brain,
to carry information about muscles
stretch (Fig. 5) Also, there are other
nerves coming from tendons, joints, con-
nective tissue, and skin that send mes-
sages concerning stretch, degree of
nerves carrying;.

and
inhibitory)

to brain
where up in the brain this informa-
tion can reach awareness, but it does
not have to. Whether you are aware of
it or not, the sensory information from
many sources is being integrated in the
brain to aid in balancing. An interest-
ing way to explore what types of infor-
mation are being used is to stand on
one foot and check how much your body
sways. Then close your eyes. Then try
it with your head tilted (so the grav-
ity/balance receptors in your inner ear
are affected), with eyes open and closed.
Does tensing your leg and body make it
harder or easier? (I bet no one has ex-
plored this aspect scientifically. There
are a zillion simple questions unanswered
motor
in this field.)
Control of the stretch reflex is neces-
Figure 5
muscle and
stretch receptor
sary so that we can move. For standing,
your brain "sets" the stretch reflexes in
CQICI Sourcebook 25
Volume 3 1977-78
certain muscles to hold an erect body position, and once set, the reflexes take care
of themselves. Then in order to walk or to fall, you inhibit the stretch reflex to
allow the knee to bend. Awareness and control must be interconnected, as we can
use the information about stretch or bend to inhibit the reflex at just the right
moment to start a walk.
The beauty of swaying and feedback
The stretch reflex is called a feedback system because the information about
how long the muscle is (how much it has been stretched) is "fed back" through the
spinal cord to the muscle contractors or relaxors, to stay the same length. The
alternative to feedback is brute force. A statue doesn't fall over, even in a
fierce wind, because it is exceedingly strong. We are relatively frail, yet we
can stand in the wind, using feedback to check our tilting and to govern our muscles.
As another example of the brute force method, I was talking to a ballet dancer
about the beauty of body sway and feedback. He said, "But I can stand perfectly
still," and he proceeded ta stand quit:e (not; perfectly) still. He was tensing sets
of opposing muscles, being fairly rigid, and swaying very little. I'd call that
modified by brute force. He was in fact using some feedback and some rigidity, or
you could call it a "tight" feedback system with little tolerance of deviation.
It is necessary for the body to sway if it is to use feedback. The deviations
and "failings" arouse mechanisms that take you back toward center. You can try to
get yourself exactly right, dead center, by brute force. Or you can allow deviations
to happen and be corrected. From a biological viewpoint, it's the same w~th popula-
tions as with standing: if there were one perfect person for all conditions, we
should all have the same body type, skin color, vitamin requirements, and so forth.
As it is, the variations have enabled us as a population to survive because we have
the diversity to adapt in the long term.
Using feedback takes less energy. Doing the small dance, one can relax muscles
most of the time, with just a tug here and a twitch there to keep the whole pile of
bones upright. Contrast this relaxed state to the more exact but more energy-consum-
ing task of trying to stand without swaying.
Feedback can be used to guide movements as well as in stillness. As you open
your front door, you are following a pattern of movements. That pattern is probably
generated in the basal ganglia of the brain and sent down the nerves of the spinal
cord. The nerves and reflexes of the spinal cord carry out the brain's orders, at the
same time using feedback to correct for little problems like sticky doorknobs. (See
the Merton article in the bibliography for more on this idea.)
The beauties of feedback are many: the mechanism is adaptable (e.g., to high winds
or tired muscles); deviations don't matter; and the system consumes little energy
for its task. But by definition, feedback is used for goal-oriented behavior,
whether the goal is standing still, or execution of precise movements like typing.
In Contact, we continually use feedback very sensitively, and we transcend it.
Contact is different from other forms of dance in not having long-term goals. In
other dancing, feedback is used to get the movements correct in a certain form. In
Contact we use feedback from moment to moment to compensate for unexpected weight, to
balance, to walk, etc., but not to achieve a correct external form. I think the sen-
sory information gets used in fundamentally different ways, since it's not needed
for "correctness". We use messages from the senses to catalyse change rather than
to stick to a goal.
"Even standing you execute a continual fall." -Steve Paxton

~ JOURNAL STUFF
Hooray, a few days ago I achieved a release from my bodily self-consciousness.
We were sitting in a circle watching duets. I had a typically uneasy feeling in the pit of my
gut. Should I go out and dance? Do I have to? I feared that moment when guilt outweighs
self-conscious embarrassment. What would my former lacrosse coach say, (a man I admire
and respect.) "Awww Agnew, you going in for that fruity stuff? What's wrong with you, boy."
Yikes .... I sat in the midst of a subtle form of panic waiting to dance.
A few feet away a woman got up. She was one of the better dancers and served in
some official capacity. God knew what. She started shuffling towards me. When she
reached me she turned and put her head heavily against mine. Oh shit. Who me? Putting
duty before my chicken heart I arose and we began to dance. Our movements were
synchronized. She felt vibrant, alive and at the same time soothing. I could feel tension
easing out of my back like water from a sponge as she rolled across me.
Delightful. I began to dance with a freedom and ease of movement I had never
before experienced. I felt her energy reaching into me. In sort of a psychic body massage it
began caressing my fears away. Even her most sudden movements were taken in stride,
sweet body of ours moving through gravity's pull like water through a sieve. We ended with
exuberance and a righteous finality. I rolled back to my place with a shining shit-eatin grin
spread clear across my face. Jesus, did I feel good. I felt great knowing the bars of my
bodily prison could melt and just dribble away in a few moments of dance. oO
David Agnew
DAVID AGNEW participated in a 3 week Contact workshop at Hampshire College in Amherst, Massachusetts
during January 1977.

EYE IMAGES: Let Your Eyes Go For A Ride In Your Head
By Ellen Elias
Last night in contact class, Danny Lepkoff said, "Let your eyes go for a ride
in your head." I imagined this:
as opposed to this:
A tiny woman, sitting in my eye sockets watching the scenery pass in front of her.
Other times I imagine her straining to get out, trying to look up when my head is
looking down, or simply reaching far ahead of where I am. If I close my eyes and
let her relax deep into my eye sockets, I can imagine her sinking into a soft feather
mattress, and she becomes heavy and soft herself.
I am thinking about eyes: how impor~ant they are on so many levels. Frequently
in contact improvisation we talk about peripheral vision. Often this implies some-
thing about central vision, its importance so great that we don't even need to empha-
size it. In all movement, and even more so in dance, people depend on eyes for bal-
ance, coordination, spatial information, and rhythm. In contact, vision is used
somewhat differently than in other llDVement forms in that often, the eyes are "going
for a ride in your head," and at the same time, the importance of peripheral vision
is emphasized. So I will add this to my drawing:
Now I am imagining two additional tiny women
who are very active. As the first woman sits in
my head getting a free ride, the second and third
women are stretching and looking around towards the
sides of my head, up and down and around. Without
the latter two women I am restricted to the path
of vision of the direction of my head, but with them,
I can relax my eyes while stretching my perception.
In other words, the first woman represents sensation
and the second and third women represent perception
and attention. Try this: look at something directly
in front of you. Now relax your eyes but still look at it. Now, without shifting
the eyeball, think about whatever is to your right and to your left. In my case, I
am looking at the typewriter ribbon; now I shift my attention to the bathroom door
on my right and the bookcase on my left. The original looking is as if the first
woman were reaching forward. Then it is as if she were relaxing and the other two,
becoming active, were stretching to the sides.
Another exercise for eyes is really about not-eyes. We did this during the
Vermont Contact Improvisation Conference last August: in duets, one person closes
28 CQ/CI Sourcebook Volwne 3 1977-78
her/his eyes and th~ other leads her/him wherever their joint energy takes them.
When I did this tlie ' 1 first thing I realized was that I had to trust Ann, my partner.
She ~ my eyes. I could still trust my own sensations but they were limited. I
found myself running and hoping that there would be no rocks in my path. My normal
defense system operates in part through my eyes.
Defenses, and all el!X)tions, register in the eyes. A study was done which showed
that the pupils of people dilate quickly when they see something they find attract-
ive. On the subway or in a crowded elevator people often avert their eyes in order
to avoid what can be an aggressive act: looking directly into the eyes of someone
they don't know. Dogs and cats do the same thing. (I always find it easy to out-
stare my dog. It seems as if he feels it is very aggressive when I look into his
eyes for too long a time.)
To use the word eyes is not quite accurate.
move the eyeballs in their sockets. What I have
the process of vision and part the "kinesthetic
There are seven muscles which
been discussing is actually part
consciousness" (Mabel Elsworth
Todd, The Thinking Body, 1937). Todd wrote:
We are able to say that a table is four feet away or a piano
ten feet, not, as one might suppose, because of any special
quality of vision or a specific reaction to light, but chiefly
by means of the muscular sense. The ability to differentiate
distances is built up from a multitude of impressions conveyed
from the eye muscles used in focusing the visual image upon the
retina •••• Impressions of the work done by the eye muscles as
they move are reported to the brain, where they are interpreted
as indicating a certain distance in space. This interpretation
in turn is effected through an association of ideas or memories
of various cumulative experiences, muscular and tactile, and
of visual impressions of color and size. This is due to memories
of former experiences - movements of the eye, movements of the
body in going towards objects and reaching them, together with
tactile impressions of their shapes, extent and textures derived
from handling them ••• Similar associations of ideas also aided
by the labyrinthine sensations reporting the position of our
he~ds in relation to the earth, enable us to perceive an object
in its actual position, right side up, although the retina receives
an image that is upside down as projected through the anterior
refracting surfaces and media of the eye. pp. 31-32.
Lately in my contact work I have been experimenting with relaxing my eyes,
especially when I am upside down. At first I feel disoriented. This is because
"when an attempt is made to keep the eyes directed so as to move with the head in-
stead of against it, the primary reflexes have to be interrupted, which at once
brings a sense of strain and tension" (Todd, p.103). It is this sense of strain
and tension that I am trying to release. Howeuer, without the labyrinthine balance
mechanism I would not know, after I have flown up and over someone's shoulders,
which way is up and which is down.
The eyes are part of the kinesthetic consciousness that we, as contact dancers,
hope to perceive and sense fully. Different images or fantasies about what is going
on in the eyes can help us to think differently about their activity and help to
change that activity. Some exercises:
"Start the head nodding gently like a toy (the kind of dog you see in the back
windshields of cars). While rocking the head as a delicately balanced weight, note
how the eyes m:Jve automatically against the direction of the head and how their
vision is kept focused on the horizon." (Todd, p.103).
Volume 3 1977-78 CQICI Sourcebook
"Lay a tape on· tihe ground for a guide, then with an opera glass or field
glass look down at your feet through the large end, and see how straight you can
walk. You will find it very hard to keep on the tape so long as you keep looking
at your feet through the glasses." (Todd, p.115).
Draw a squiggly line on paper. Place mirrors on the vertical sides of the
inside of a small cardboard box and place the paper inside. Looking only in the
mirrors and not directly at your hand, try to trace the line. (This exercise I
remember from a high school fair.)
Rub your hands quickly together until they are very warm. Cup the hands and
place over the eyes.
Spinning: start slowly. One foot turns out, the next parallel. Turn out,
turn parallel, repeat. Begin w~ile hands are in front of you, held together,
elbows bent. As the momentum begins to build let your arms slowly release and they
will extend to the side. Your eyes rest in your head, unfocused, although they
will try, from time to time, to fix on something. If they do you will notice you
become dizzy very quickly. Also, any sudden change, like bending your arms quick-
ly, will result in dizziness·. Come to a stop very gradually the way you began.
Focus on your hands as they come together. (It is a good idea to have a friend
stand near you in case of sudden dizziness. The friend should, if s/he sees that
you are starting to lose your center, extend her/his arm and continue walking in
a circle with you until you are alright.) With this technique of nonspotted spin-
ning I spun for 45 minutes for the first time recently. Among my other discoveries
I realized that I can let my eyes remain unfocused while I move for a long time.
Copyright October, 1977
Ellen Elias

INFORMATION SHEET
The words "Contact lmprovisation"neatly describe work of an evolving
movement form, affectionately known as Contact. Contact Improvisation is the
beauty of natural movement combined with full communication.
Rather than working towards masterpiece repertory, Contact is an on-
going investigation of immediate action, yielding to flow and the economy
of force. Some fundamental views it contains: all forms are constantly changing,
all forms participate as parts of larger forms, and nature tends to equilibrium.
Participation in Contact lmprov is a study of movement through aware-
ness and a study of awareness through movement. It provides a joyous
meeting ground for moving bodies in spontaneous, genuine, un-self -conscious
flow as parts of a larger whole: two movers, one duet.
To an unaccustomed eye this activity smacks of rough-housing, love-
making, wrestling or martial arts. ·Actually participants learn to move together
on a common path of least resistance in which impulses, weight and momen-
tum are communicated through a point of physical contact that continually
rolls around their bodies. Like bolos, fluid and · eccentrically weighted, the
moving bodies swing, bounce, roll, swim and fly around with a common center
of gravity. These skills are developed in non-competitive classes through
relaxation and patient physical discipline, in direct association with other movers.
DO
Curt Siddall
CURT SIDDALL has participated in the development of Contact lmprov through perfonnance, teaching and writing
since helping to initiate the movement almost6 years ago. In addition to being a ce>founding director of Berkeley
Moving Arts, Curt is a member of San Francisco based MANGROVE. a performance collective of 8 men and
REUNION; a touring company comprised of Contact Improvisation's early developers.
