A FEW (TIGHT) MARBLES FROM MY TEACHER'S NOTEBOOK
Human body moves, steps into the stream of information, absorbs change, and
grows to become the body of knowledge.
Information is gathered, invented, codified, organized by finders of form. In
form, at ion-level and all the way out towards all universe, units of fact are dis-
covered in their arrangement, pattern, shape. Muscles structures, skeletal mech-
anics, nerve-sensations are examples of existing phenomena which can be perceived
by mind and feeling, about which humans articulate language so that a system emerges,
a matrix of facts that can be studied, increased, modified, transmitted.
Dynamic information flow is the learning situation. It exists as soon as a
human recognizes a capacity to know about something and feels the need/desire to
exercise that capacity. All learning begins with the learner.
In Contact Improvisation what the learner learns is her/his own body, going
to the body as the source of knowledge and delivering to the body all the informa-
tion gathered. Humans who learn often begin to share their bodies with other humans;
allow their flesh, brain, and imagination to be a pathway through which information
may flow; such humans when they are in condition of being employed by learners as
resource are called teachers.
Teachers, given ego-logical structures as part of human nature, can and do
seek learners, can and do make mistakes, can be and often are astonished by what
they learn.
Contact Improvisation, the form as teacher, teaches us to follow the mutual
energy pathway and to avoid follower/leader patterns. Some human persons have had
speculations about how to teach the form; some wonder whether it can be taught.
There hovers some difficulty in semantics, having to do with the idea that Contact
Imp is a special sort of form, that it is not a system, like tennis, with rules of
play; perhaps Contact Imp is more like an attitude, and being a contactor is like
being an American, or a Buddhist, or a poet.
What about the duet, the round robin, the 58? What about the meditations, the
small dance, the leaps and falls? Aren't they knowledge? Can't they be learnt?
Can't they be taught?
Certainly they are, and can be. Just because a river is full of ever-changing
Water does not mean a human can't learn the river: the pools remain pools, the
rapids occur wherever the rocks are just so. Just so, the arm and the leg support
weight in a fairly predictable way, and the person attached can learn to use them
to accomplish acts -- sharpening the pencil, paddling the canoe, balancing on the
partner's back and falling off unharmed.
The teacher, by the permission of learners, seeks to form a shape out of units --
exercises, moods, images, ideas -- so that the learner can find a clear way to ap-
proach, to find body. Often the teacher represents the body of knowledge by putting
a shoulder to the wheeling learner, articulating events directly in flesh-language.
Often the teacher uses word-language as well to address the mind, imagination, feel-
ings; to guide attention, to help the process of self-discovery. Observation, ques-
tion/answer, repetition, analysis, experiment are some of the shapes information
flow takes -- just as water can be found in waves, ripples, eddies, raindrops, and
32 CQ/CI Sourcebook Volume 3 1977-78
cocktails and sti).Dbe water -- and these shapes can be devices to facilitate
learning.
Certainly Contact Improvisation, since it is a form, can be learnt, and any-
thing that can be learnt also can be taught. To say otherwise is to begin playing
with language -- a fascinating and valuable activity, and a device of information
itself. In discussion about the form it will be wise always to avoid confusion in
mixing levels of discourse, to avoid being unclear as to whether one is speaking of
the physical or the metaphysical counterbalance; the classroom moment or the class-
ification of moments; of momentum as applied to spinning, as applied to planetary
orbits, as applied to non-applied sub-atomic physics theory. There has always been
a care to keep language precis.e and spare in regard to Contact Improvisation, and
that care is part of learning the form.
A minimalization of effort, the efficiency of movement which is grace, is es-
sential to the form of Contact. It is that grace the teacher helps the learner
look for, stepping together into the flow, moving with it, playing in it, working
through_ it, towards the simultaneity of human body and body of knowledge, growing
to be the body of light.
Jerry Zientara

Dear Steve, Nancy, Lisa,
This evaluation form has been sitting around for awhile waiting to
be mailed, still blank in the 'open spaces', and now back at
Bennington what I appreciated most about the conference has
been thrown into sharp relief. That is the space there was for
discovery, the openness to new solutions. Here (Bennington College)
is is assumed that what I want to know is . already known, by
people who can teach it to me. But what I value most is what I find
myself. I like my teachers best who give me intimations of a vast
region to be explored, a few signposts and maybe a sketched map,
but never a guided tour. I felt so free to wander in Putney, called
back by my perception of what was happening around me, rather
than by breathing in various counts and plies - these things have
their uses but the other, less programmed exploration spreads out
into my life so much more gracefully, calmly, without residues of
tension. So thank you. And I feel that I am going to learn a lot here
this term - am very excited by the fledgling contact group, for
which there is much enthusiasm. I am still warming up

IN PRAISE OF BADDANCING
I learn from knowing I am uncomfortable, and going ahead; and the
going ahead being the letting-go-ahead. So the discomfort is to be appreciated.
It is an environment from which springs an opening. Contact embraces this
fear of itself. this seminal verge.
We are concerned with where the dancing originates. At times it
appears to me that the important thing is not the dancing in the center of the
circle. but the experience on the edge of the circle, the often disquieting
dance on the verge. This view, which might be seen as antithetical to the
work. is also necessarily part of its premise. The invitation to feel the dread of
opening is an inherent part of the total occurrence.
Dances in which both bodies ride a shared wave of truly unleashed
rise and falling are rare. Yet the dances which abound - halting, starting
repeatedly, responding to impulses a fraction of a second too slow to flow,
doubting one's perceptions - are rich with valuable information, honest
manifestation of fluctuation between positive and negative response. We
fear surrender. make our homes in the almost, and venture into readiness
only when we must. The discomfort at the verge serves by intensifying the
contrast: the darks get darker, the lights lighter, and the picture gets easier
to see.
I value this work not only for the rare glimpses of in-touch, in-time,
in-tune. but for the companionship of the ever-present indications of what is
possible. The form itself, if one exists beyond the instances of attempts to
experience it. is none other than the existence of the possibility of a dance in
which wills. instincts. and verges merge. Emergency, emerge, merge. It
amounts to having to believe in a possibility which one may never have .
experienced.
All this to say I am devoted to something which I rarely feel, the
possibility of which I never leave. We extend to ourselves an invitation which
we rarely accept. but the extension of it is what matters. Living for so long on
the verge of that acceptance, I figure it's time to call it home. o0
Christina Svane
CHRISTl:\A SVA'.\:E :s a dancer·poet·teacher·video artist living in New York City.

A DEFINITION..
Contact Improvisation is a duet art-sport currently receiving international attention by those insti-
tutions concerned with new departures in the forms of dance.
Dena Davida, Carol Harwood photo: Marc-Andre Gagne
In the years since the inception of Contact Improvisation, in 1972, it has been adopted as perform-
ance mode by some 10 dance companies across the U.S., and used as a teaching technique by those
grass roots dance organizations, institutions and individuals concerned with investigation of d'ance
improvisation ephemera before the concerns of art aesthetics arise.
It is an activity related to familiar duet forms such as the embrace, wrestling, martial arts, and the
jitterbug, encompassing the range of movement from stillness to highly athletic. The exigencies of
the form dictate a mode of movement which is relaxed, constantly aware and prepared, and on-flow-
ing. As a basic focus, the dancers remain in physical touch, mutually supportive and innovative, me-
ditating upon the physical laws relating to their masses: gravity, momentum, inertia and friction.
They do not strive to achieve results but rather, to meet the constantly changing physical reality with
appropriate placement and energy. ~
Steve Paxton
Volume 4 1978-79 CQ/CI Sourcebook '37
John Faichney
In looking forward to the European performances of
MOMENTING, it was readily apparent that the piece
could not be restaged intact each time. After toying with
some of the formal manipulations that might be imposed
upon it, I finally chose to let the piece be adapted to the
situation as I found it. In the context of formalism, the
'piece' became a piece about 'MUTATIS MUTANDIS':
about 'making the necessary changes'; e.g., on the level of
multiple performance.

JUNIOR! HOW JUMP CAN YOU FAR? 1
1 Child's cry overbedrtl in a playground.
by Jerry Zientara
By arranging and rebalancing our scrutiny and
our relaxation of focus in our daily routine we
find ways to our appropriate redemptions. The
constant prayer that leads the pilgrim, Saint
Therese's "little way", the yogi's systems of
breathing, and the mover's attention to the body
as it washes, waits for the bus, cleans out the
catbox or ties a shoelace are among ways we
focus our being towards whatever unexpectable
or hoped-for visionary experience waits grace-
fully for us.
Verbal languages and body languages both pre-
sent regions of articulation, regions of common
understanding, and regions of the unknown. By
looking at "what we know" we approach the fas-
cinating places of "the unknown". Historically,
Contact Improvisation began with everything un-
known and in the recent handful of years, a body
of knowledge, perhaps a grammar of words and
motions, has been evolving. We know we belong
to the same tribe because we understand each
other; we share a context.
flexibility active bounce round robin
circle breath play partner
attention gravity momentum pathways
floor honest dialogue wave
trust weight float stillness
skeleton mood glide interfacing
contact rise skin center
(This example is a random selection from lists of
"words related to Contact improvisation" made
during a writing seminar led by Nancy Stark Smith
in Seattle, January 1979.)
By now, hundreds and hundreds of people have
had some exposure to the form and its language,
through classes, workshops, performances, re-
views, magazines, and photographs. People from
all walks of life have attempted to describe, ana-
lyze, teach Contact in terms of their own vocab-
ularies, which range from the scientifically
spare to the wildly rhapsodic. Meeting changes
and development within the practice of the form
and colliding, assimilating, and crossing with
words from other contexts, the language of Con-
tact Improvisation attempts to stay clear, direct,
and as immediate as the movement itself.
The very obvious and the very subtle are al-
ways present in any utterance, pose, or figura-
tion. Understanding the shapes and shades of
meaning and of momentum is as easy and as dif -
ficult as understanding music or the weather.
Embodying a concept may or may not use words
before, during, and/ or after; when we seek
discourse, the universe of words enters the pic-
ture. While a woman rolls in a wavelike motion
across the floor, another woman waves to her,
waving her hand. - (Homonym phenomenon.)
40 CQ/CI Sourcebook
Volume4 1978-79
During "the stand", a condition of vertical
rest, in which the skeleton is balanced so the
muscles don't have to work to hold the body up,
small adjustments and rebalancings were noted
to go on involuntarily. When the accumulating
jiggles and twitches were seen to be a continuity,
there had to be something to call it, and it is
"the small dance". This happened in the old days.
Also in the old days, so many experimental move-
ments were attempted that they were. given num-
bers. These days the only one that is called by
its number is "the 58". "58" is a vault pivoting
off the partner's thighs, around hip-to-hip, rol-
ling over an arc across the partner's back, fi-
nally rolling off into the air or someplace else.
Influx and innovation occur in oi.tr lingo through
borrowings like aikido rolls, t' ai chi stepping;
forward rolls; from physics and metaphysics we
adapt and adopt referent points like counterbal-
ance, chakra, yoga asanas, field .energies, ma.g-
netism. Innovative word-invention can produce
terms immediately understandable, for example,
Simone Forti's use of "artsport" soon swept the
Contact village and one feels again how perfect
a word it is coming upon the ungainly "sportart"
in a newspaper clipping. Other terms like "blotto
dance" never quite caught on. "Finger ouija"
may or may not be current.
Obviously Contact's language functions among
practitioners-only groups, and in mixed groups
which include non-contactors. In the former case,
assumptions are frequently taken for granted,
based on common experience. Just as you can
tell at once if your partner is really in kinetic
dialogue with you, when verbal conversation flows
the words are subsidiary to the moment, suppor--
tive of the meaning. In the case of the latter,
often contactors find ourselves making wild anal-
ogies with rock-climbing or talus-running (which
we may not have done a whole lot of ourselves),
trying to clarify concepts without having to de-
monstrate at cocktails just what a 58 is. When
we are faced with a reporter, our mother, a
dance teacher, a baseball fan, or a child who
sees us doing it in the park, what words do we
call up? At the best of these moments the prin-
ciples that live within our process emerge mo-
mentarily, and some of the phrases stay around.
Repeating definitions, describing the same mo-
ment several times, feeding our words and ges-
tures into the common experience keeps refining
and redefining and refinding the same· old gravity
and change.

CENTER OF GRAVITY
by Ann Woodhull
"Center of gravity" seems to have a lot of meanings for dancers. If you look the term up in a
physics book, you find about half a page; they seem to think that it's so simple as not to be worth discussing
a lot. But the concept is pretty deep when you think about it and use it to look at movement. Here I will
be talking about center of gravity in the same physical sense that a physicist would use the term. I will leave
aside meanings such as "center from which you move" or "spiritual center," and try to show the richness of
the simply physical interpretation. However, I am emphatically not saying that dance is "nothing but
physics" - more about that later. First I will explain and define the center of gravity for a rigid body, and
then talk about the more interesting cases when the body changes shape and the center of gravity changes.
C.Onsider the shapes in the first picture as made of flat cardboard, and imagine that you want to
balance each one on its edge on a pencil. Where would you put the pencil for each one so it would not
turn or fall?
Then suppose they were turned around, as below. Now where does the supporting point need to be for each?
OK, what does this balance stuff have to do with the center of gravity? I assert that there is a
unique point in or near each of these shapes, such that whenever this point is above the point of s11r--ort,
the shape wil I be balanced. This unique point is called the Center of Gravity. Another way to defin.
center of gravity is the point around which the body is perfectly balanced: if you stuck a support through
the center of gravity, you could turn the body any way, and it would still balance, not tending to tip in any
direction.
Volume 4 1978-79 CQ/CI Sourcebook 43
Now you really need to cut out a shape of cardboard and try it, to see if this concept works. Cut
out an odd shape like one above, but bigger. Put it flat up against a vertical surface like a wall so it can't
flop over, and balance the edge on a pencil. When you find one way it balances, hold it still and draw a line
straight up from the point of contact. Now balance it on a different edge,
and draw another line straight up from this other point of contact. The
two lines will meet, and this point of meeting should be the center of
gravity. Is it? Well, if it really is, then any time this point is above the
point of support, the shape should balance. TRY IT in other positions.
Does it balance? Try putting the center of gravity above the point of
contact but to the left of it. Now the shape falls to the left, doesn't it?
The diagram below shows the forces. The two forces (weight and support).
aiming past one another, create torque
that turns the body. Whenever the
center of gravity is not over the point
of contact, the body tends to turn and fall on the side where the center
of gravity is. It's as if all the mass were concentrated in the center of
gravity. We use this turning and falling a lot. When you are standing and
you allow your center of gravity to wander until it's not over your feet
any more, then you start to fall in that direction; you can allow the fall
to continue, or use the momentum to go into a walk.
To go back to the first shapes, here they are again with the centers
of gravity I found for each. Notice that in one case the center of gravity
is outside the figure! This is hard to conceive of, except if you try to balance the shape many ways and see
where the lines converge. Another way of getting an image of a center that is outside a body is to think of
a donut. For a donut, the center of gravity has to be inside the hole, no way around it.
The formal physical definition of center of gravity gives another perspective. Think of the body as
a set of segments, each of which has its own mass and position. The center of gravity is the point at which
the sum of all those masses times their (directional) distances is zero - the point at which all those masses
on all their lever arms cancel each other out. For a simple example, think of a seesaw. If one person on
the seesaw is a lot heavier, the lighter person can still make a balance by sitting way out on the end, to get
leverage. To be exact, each person on the seesaw has a turning force (torque) equal to her/his mass times
her/his distance from the balance point. So this combination in the picture would be balanced (assuming,
oh physics!, a weightless seesaw board).
44 CQ/CI Sourcebook Volume4 1978-79
Using the definition, it is possible to calculate the center of gravity for real people in different
positions. I analyzed these two pictures and marked each person's center of gravity with an X. The
tracing is my working drawing for the calculations of center of gravity.**
In the first photograph, notice how Nita's body balances around her left leg. Her right leg is
sticking out, balanced by her head and torso leaning the other way. The head and torso are not leaning
far, but they are much heavier than the leg. Her center of gravity is close to the "normal" position for a
standing person (inside the pelvis, a little below the belly button), and it is directly above her foot, so she's
stable. In that same picture, it's not immediately clear whether Steve is leaning on Nita or not. But see,
his center of gravity is not directly above his supporting foot, so he must be leaning on her, or be falling.
Comparing Steve's posture to normal standing, his left leg is tilted up and sideways relative to the straight
line of his torso, and his left arm is also up and to the side. Hence, his center of gravity is not so near his
belly button, but is displaced up and to the left. From the cases I've looked at, the center of gravity can
move around 6 or 8 inches from its standard position in the body - not a huge distance, but enough to
make a difference. Because the head and torso are so heavy (being about 55% of the total weight of most
people), the center of gravity tends to stay around the belly.
L
Volume 4 1978-79 CQICI Sourcebook 45
0
0
"§.
Llsa Nelson, Danny Lepkoff
The photo with Lisa Nelson nicely illustrates some points: her center of gravity is outside her body,
but it is still above the area of support, making a stable position. Note how her different body segments
extend to the left and right of the support, but they balance out. A lot of parts, particularly her legs, are
hanging off to the left of the center of gravity, but they are cancelled out by the weight of her torso and
thighs to the right.
We change center of gravity when we change shape, and often compensate so automatically that we
aren't even aware of it. For example, if I start out standing and bend forward, my center shifts forward and
I'd fall right over if I didn't make immediate subtle corrections. I allow my legs to tilt back, so my hips
are shifted back and my new center is above my feet. If you don't believe it, try this trick: stand with
your back against a wall, then try to lean forward without falling over. You can't because your automatic
corrections are prevented. However, sometimes our "instinctive" changes of center of gravity do not com-
pensate correctly. I used to do this one: when up in a "flying angel," and feeling insecure, I would try
46 CQ/CI Sourcebook Volurne4 1978-79
to save myself by putting my arms up above my head and bending my knees. Then I'd fall on my head
fast, because both these moves shifted my center towards my head.
The height of the center of gravity affects stability. The further your center is above your base of
support, the less stable you are. Lying flat on the floor on your stomach, you are extremely stable, having
a low center and huge base of support. Now if you arch your arms and legs up off the floor, you are less
stable and find it easier to get mobilized. Why? Your base is smaller and your center of gravity further off
the floor, both factors making you less stable.
In Contact Improvisation, we purposely change center of gravity in order to move. When you are
rolling on another person, you change your body shape, the center of gravity and point of support change,
and you move - not because you have pushed or pulled yourself {and if you do it slowly, not by imparting
momentum to your body), but simply by shifting center of gravity and allowing gravity to do the rest.
When your center is no longer above the support, your whole body starts to move. This way of initiating
movement looks and feels very different from grabbing and pulling. I think a lot of the visual and tactile
thrill of Contact comes from support and movement that is non-grabby, non-invasive. Shifts in the center
of gravity of the person supported and shifts in support by the bearer lead naturally to mutual, shared motion.
In many situations, the idea of center of gravity alone is not enough for analyzing the mechanics.
When there is more than one point of contact, the center of gravity no longer has to be over a point of
contact. One easy example is a person standing on two feet equally; then the center is directly over a spot
between the two feet. Not bad. But if the person is leaning hard against something, the situation isn't
even symmetrical. You have to consider not only the two forces we've thought about so far (gravity and
the resistance of the floor), but all the other forces, acting at angles to one another, and how they tend to
push and pull and turn the body.
When a body is moving, the physics gets complicated fast. As long as a person is static or moving
so slowly that momentum is small, the center of gravity tells a lot. However, in analyzing real movement
physically, you have to consider momentum, acceleration, angular momentum from whole-body turning
and from turning at joints, and so on. For vivid examples of bodies in motion, look at pictures of Contact
Volume 4 1978-79 CQICI Sourcebook Lf7
Improvisation. Your eye immediately tells you whether a person was probably in motion, or whether
s/he could be stable in a particular pose. Your well-educated intuition fills in the blanks of where the
body must be moving to, what momentum it must have had to get to such a position.
One application of the center of gravity concept to a fast-moving body is quite useful, though -
that's when the body is in air. During a free jump, there are no outside forces on your body once you
leave the ground. As you go up, travel through space, and come down, your center of gravity describes
a parabola, no matter what your arms, legs and head are doing. At the top of that arc is where you have
minimum velocity (none up or down for an instant, but some in a forward direction maybe). Minimum
velocity means minimum momentum (momentum= mass x velocity), so that's the time it is easiest for
someone else to receive your body with least impact. If the other person then allows you to nearly follow
the curve you were on, the motions look smooth and little force is required, little impact is experienced.
This physics is all very interesting, but does it do anything for our dancing? Rarely, I'd say -
mostly in learning and practicing skills. For me, the physicist's view of center of gravity is quite intellectual;
I don't sense directly where my center of gravity is. I sense some results of where it is, as the pressures on
my feet as my weight shifts around, and other sensations. ---
What happens in dance does follow the laws of physics, but to view Contact Improvisation as
simply physics - or worse, to experience it as simply physics - is not my aim. Despite this long article,
I am wary of thinking and talking too reductionistically. I certainly don't want to contribute to making
Contact an abstract form that is only about physics and not about humans. Contact is about communication
and sharing, and one element we share is playing with the consequences of physical laws.
**FOOTNOTE
I used a standard but tedious way of finding the body's center of gravity from the maso; of each segment
and its location (see Wells and Luttgens, Kinesiology, 1976, pp 270-275).
1) I located the center for each joint (dots in my tracing).
2) I joined the dots and used "well-known proportions" (other people's data) to find the center of mass
of each segment; for example, the center of mass of the thigh is supposed to be 33% of the distance
from the hip to the knee. (X's in tracing.)
3) Taking an arbitrary point as reference, I found the distance of each segment's center of mass from that
point, measured as 'x' and 'y' coordinates.
4) Not having the actual masses of the body segments of each person involved, I assumed values from the
literature for the percent of body weight in each segment. Although total weight varies a lot, people
don't differ too much in how the weight is distributed, so this is reasonably OK.
5) I multiplied the mass of each segment by its 'x'-coordinate and added these products. Then I multiplied
each mass by its 'y'-coordinate and added these products. Thus I had the vector (directional} sum of all
the masses times their distances. Dividing each sum by the total mass, I got the 'x' and 'y' coordinates
of the center of gravity. C'3..
ANN WOODHULL teaches biology at Hampshire College in Amherst, Massachusetts and has been doing Contact for a year
and a half. This article is part of her ongoing integration of different aspects of her life: teacher, dancer, scientist, feminist,
parent person.

For a long time it appearedthat Contact Improvisation ore concerned with sensat n than achievement and . There is the sense in racticing Contact tha · 'right'. Anything go s. Yet there is a li ollection of expe ences one coul is the poi of Con eels backed up against the running

an unplanned and spontaneous way, where you're free to invent and they're
free to invent and you're e other-that's a very pleasant social form." However, it was by first establish-
ing priorities that Contact was able to provide its particular kind of pleasure.
Contact can be seen as a means for communicating through physical contact. Dialogue proceeds, using the exchange of
weight between partners to tune them to the physical forces that effect their motion and to tune them to each other. At the
outset of Contact Improvisation, a choice was made to minimize the social content of the contact and to work, initially, with
the physical elements involved in the exchange. Stated or by example implied, this choice along with others brought depth
to the practice of Contact by limiting its scope. By narrowing the focus of the activity, subtle information was made visible:
the experience of gravity; the "small dance" of the skeleton balancing erect; the flow of weight through the body as it rolls
across the floor; the exchange of weight between partners during lifting, throwing and catching; the sensation of sharing a
dynamic center of balance with another mover.
It has been 8 years since the decision to work with these specific physical elements. In this time, practice has
revealed many principles that, serving as anchors, allowed the scope of partner exchange to widen. The practice of CI
continues to suggest ways in which its principles and skills can be applied. Following these suggestions leads to fas-
cinating work in dance, theatre, therapy and education. As the concern with physical contact and weight ex-
change becomes less of a focus in their work, many Contactors wonder whether they are still doing "Con-
tact Improvisation". Others continue their studies regardless, not caring one way or the other what it is
called.
Contact, as an evolving system, tends to attract the forces that work to open what is fixed, change
what is static. Ever since its formation as the Contact Newsletter in 1975, Contact Quarterly has
reflected this eagerness to find relevance in a broad range of sources. In considering the direc-
tion of our interests, as editors we ask ourselves, 'What does shooting guns have to do with
Contact Improvisation?' In shooting, one tests the accuracy with which a message
of energy (power) can be delivered to a distant point (moving or still). As a
'vehicle for moving ideas', Contact Quarterly shares that aim.
Nancy Stark Smith
Volume 5 1979-80 CQ/CI Sourcebook
"There is a bit of insanity in dancing that
does everybody a great deal of good."
Edward Denby
Letters
Editor
Note
When I first started falling by choice, I noticed a blind spot.
Somewhere after the beginning and before the end of the fall, there was
darkness. And then the floor. Luckily, there were mats at first. Soon
I learned that the end of the fall was the beginning of another move,
usually a roll. That gave me somewhere to go. So I rolled. At the end
of that roll was another roll, and at the end of that, another. Then, I
noticed another blind spot. Somewhere after the beginning and before
the end of the roll, there was darkness. In that darkness, however, I
noticed a body moving, a body that knew just where to go.
The blind spot in the fall is the moment when the fall and the
faller lose synch. The faller falls asleep and her body falls without guid-
ance to the floor. Truly lost in space. Ever fall asleep at the wheel?
You 're so tired your eyelids stick together each time you blink ·until
they pop wide open as you realize you've just been driving 60 mph on
the highway with your eyes closed, for how long? The same sensation
occurs while falling. You wake up shortly before reaching the floor,
just in time to hastily steer yourself to safety. Adrenalin makes your
hair stand up.
The more I fell, the more familiar the sensation of dropping
through space became, the less disoriented I was during the fall. Staying
awake from the first moment of balance-loss, I found that falling was
in itself a dynamic balance. One in which the forces at play-gravity,
momentum and mass-were all operating in their natural order and if
my mind was with me I could gently guide that fall toward a smooth
landing. Confidence came with experience and soon enjoyment took
the place of fear and disorientation. In the course of a Contact Improv-
isation duet, I found myself taking every possible option to fall. I
would slip out unexpectedly from under my partner just to take the
ride: head first, feet first, horizontally, diagonally; falling became
irresistible.
It has become clear; there is no where we can stay put. Even
standing, we execute a continuous fall. The expression, 'fall from grace,'
becomes an impossible statement when falling is itself experienced as
a state of grace. This is not to say that disorientation, confusion and
dis-ease have no place in the geometry of balance. They, in fact, stim-
ulate the balancing mechanism. Stimulate us to ask questions: what is
dis-ease, what does healthy mean, how do we recognize and maintain
it? How can we stay awake during the fall; this is the healing issue.
Nancy Stark Smith

CONTACT & HEALING
Just saw the Winter '79 issue of
CQ and it looks really good! I see
that the next issue is on healing ...
a very important topic on many
levels ... If anything comes out of
the discussion it will be an awareness
of the connectedness of all things,
and an affirmation of Contact as a
connector.
If I may ramble on a bit longer
and share some insights and a view-
point ... It seems the pop'lllar notion
of 'health' today is a state of 'not
being sick'. Healthy, however, means
'to be whole'' and is linked to the
words "holy" and "beauty" (the
Greek word for health is "koilo"
meaning beautiful). An unhealthy
person is damaged, not whole. A
holy person is a whole being. So,
the meaning of health expands be-
yond the physical aspect. Being
healthy involves the dynamic integra-
tion of body, mind and soul in a pro-
cess of creative self-expression ... we
are healthy to the degree that we are
manifesting our possibilities.
Our culture seems to be overly ra-
tional and intellectual-separating
mind and body. I know, for example,
that most architects live in their
heads and experience with their eyes.
They're very tight and far from spon-
tant0us (if I may generalize that
grossly). Contact Improvisation en-
courages integration within the self.
50 CQICI Sourcebook
Volume 5 1979-80
It asks for looseness, openness and re-
sponsiveness ... as well as .a sensitivity
to one's own needs and those of
others ... making one like moldable
clay-impressionable, sensitive, tan-
gible ... pulled, thrown, melted, so-
lidified, broken and pulled together-
so much physical activity that one
cannot help but change on the emo-
tional, mental, and spiritual levels as
well. With this awareness we can en-
courage a healthy cross-linking of all
the levels ... toward a wholeness of
b.eing ... The environment surround-
ing us can be a 'stage for life' ... sup-
porting 'wholeness'. A healthy en-
vironment nurtures healthy people;
healthy people build healthy environ-
men ts.
With this spirit,
Namaste!
Ross Chapin
Mpls, Minnesota
Dear Contact Quarterly,
Coming from a background of
psychology and movement therapy,
I am intrigued by the similarities be-
tween what I have experienced in
Contact Improvisation and my exper-
ience in psychological therapies. The
following is a brief synopsis of the
psychological theories I have looked
at in this way.
Gestalt Therapy-focuses on "being
in the now." By paying attention to
our inner needs in each moment, we
relinquish the avoidances in our lives
and commit ourselves to confronting,
and living in the present.
Jungian Approach to Therapy-
based on Carl Jung's belief in the ex-
istence of the feminine and masculine
in each of us regardless of our gender.
The essence of the work in Jungian
therapy is to balance out, and come
to a resolution of the imbalanced or
conflicted opposites within us.
Various theories of Group Dyna-
mics-predictable behavior patterns
inherent in the development of a
group which evolve out of the unfold-
ing of contact and intimacy amongst
the members of a group.
Theme of rejection-a common de-
nominator to most therapies. Based
on denial of a human being's primary
need for love and acceptance, usually
going back to one's early relation-
ship with parents. The building of
trusting and honest relationships is
a key factor in any mode of therapy
to help counteract feelings of un-
worthiness, and the fear of rejection.
Some thoughts: I am wondering
if more attention should be paid to
Volume 5 1979-80
Contact Improvisation as a healing
art by bringing psychological con-
sciousness to Contact classes, jams,
and workshops? And if so, how do
we do this? (Also, has it been, or is
it presently being done by anyone?)
Will doing this change Contact Im-
provisation from an educational to a
psychological process, and if so, do
we want to do this?
So, to sum up, can (and how can)
an exploration of psychological
theories and techniques shed some
light on to the psychological frame-
work within Contact Improvisation
in order to open up greater potenti-
ality for the teaching and dancing
experience of Contact Improvisation?
Would love to have an exchange
of ideas on this by contacters who
have experience and expertise within
the field of psychology.
Toward growth and wholeness,
Deborah Goldberg
Greenfield, Mass.

Dear C.Q.:
I enjoyed Beth Goren's article,
"The Anatomy of Man and Woman"
in the last issue of C. Q. [Vol. IV
No. 314]. It provided me with a new
way of perceiving the relationship of
femur to pelvis and doubtless will be
of direct use in my continuing quest
for better alignment. I would, how-
ever, like greater clarification of the
following statements: "When the
legs and feet take a parallel stand,
the neck and head of the femur direct
energy upwards into the socket in a
back to front orientation. This is the
sense that I find to be most lacking
in Contacters and what.we most need
to develop." There's not enough in-
formation here. I don't understand
clearly how this orientation applies
to actual movement, especially Con-

Peter Ryan
Vancouver, B.C.
Dear Peter: We were able to contact
Beth by phone and interpret a brief
answer to your question. She reiter-
ated her observation of a basic lack of
buttock-sacrum-coccyx support in
people doing Contact Improvisation.
She speculated that images designed
to facilitate a release of tension or
'give way' in the lower back (e.g.
'dropping the tail') could cause a men-
tal and physical lack of support; that
'release' is an important first step in
changing habits; and that once 're-
leased' one must create a new sense
of support. As Contact Improvisation
makes special demands on the body
to be a supporting structure, a proper
image for support is essential. In a
parallel stance, you can experience a
sensation of support by placing the
thumbs in the back of the greater tro-
chanter with the fingers wrapped a-
round the front of the pelvis and pres-
sing the thumbs upward, inward and
forward. This action will direct the
support from the leg up into the thigh
socket and give the sacrum a lift. Not
only will you sense the weight flowing
down through the bones but will also
sense a counterforce travelling up the
leg and into the pelvis. Once you've
identified this sensation, you can work
with it as you move, supporting your
own weight and who's-have-you. For
more information we suggest you
write to Beth directly at 339 E 6th St.
Apt B, NYC 10003. [Eds.}
CQ/CI Sourcebook 51
A DEFINITION Round Up
CONTACT IMPROVISATION is an evolving system of movement based on the communication between
two moving bodies and their combined relationship to the physical laws that govern their motion-gravity,
momentum, inertia. The body, in order to open to these sensations, must learn to release excess muscular
tension and abandon a certain quality of willfulness to experience the natural flow of movement. Practice
includes rolling, falling, being upside down, supporting and giving weight to a partner. Alertness is developed
in order to work in an energetic state of physical disorientation, trusting in one's basic survival instincts. It
is a free play with balance, self-correcting the wrong moves and reinforcing the right ones, bringing forth a
physical/emotional truth about a shared moment of movement that leaves the participants informed, centered,
and enlivened.
52 CQ;CI Saurcebook Volume 5 1979-80
Round Up
In Contact Improvisation we fall alone and together. By blending the weight of two bodies through a shifting
point of physical contact a shared center of gravity is established. Around tbis center partners ride weight along com-
mon paths of momentum. Releasing, folding and activating weigbt flow through the joints, opens and softens partici-
pants to each other and the floor. fo this softened and receptive state we ,1re able to adapt quickly and quietly to tbe
most charged of falling encounters.
Stephen Petronio
Volume 5 1979-80 CQICI Sourcebook
Round Up_
-Contact Improvisation in perspective-
A DEFINITION
CONTACT IMPROVISATION is a
movement form, improvisational by
nature, involving two bodies in contact.
Impulses, weight and momentum are
communicated through a point of
physical contact that continually rolls
across and around the bodies of the
dancers ... Like bolos, fluid and eccen-
trically weighted, the dancing bodies
swing, bounce, roll, and fly through
and with a common center.
Curt Siddall [1975]

The Educational Value of Contact Improvisation
for the
College Student
by Daniel Lepkoff
This statement was submitted to
tbe Educational Policies Committee
of Hampshire College, Amherst, Mass.
on November 29, 1979.
Contact improvisation is a move-
ment technique. Though much young-
er than ballet or modern dance, it
proves to be as rich and dee~. It ei_n-
bodies a progression of physical skills
which can be used to train the begin-
ner as well as challenge the virtuoso.
Students of contact improvisation
acquire access to a large realm of
movement very quickly and begm to
make significant changes in their
movement patterns, strength, and sen-
sitivity within three or four classes.
This makes the technique particularly
desirable for the theater student and
non-dancer.
The skills acquired in contact im-
provisation are by no means limited
to the form itself. A deep understand-
ing of weight, movement flow, and use
of strength greatly aid anybody who
desires to study ballet, modern dance,
or other physical disciplines. The ex-
tensive exposure to the physical and
mental states involved in improvising
and discovering movement can help
choreographers in their attempts to
gather material for their set work.
Contact improvisation directly
strengthens one's presence. Just as
one can become aware of the eyes
seeing, the ears hearing, and the skin
feeling, one can become aware of the
mind sensing. Through practice of
this dance form one becomes able to
experience and observe the state of
one's nervous system particularly
when in an intimate physical relation-
ship with another. This spawns an
element of control over one's percep-
tual reality. This control and ability
to focus is training in developing pow-
er. Personal power and strength of
presence is essential in any performing
situation as well as in daily life.
Contact improvisation nurtures a
basic physical trust between two
people and among a group. The form
demands a care and consideration of
one's partner and towards this end
seeks to develop sensitivity and con-
fidence. This trust and the freedom
which ensues kindles a rich and fertile
environment for working creatively
with another person. Knowledge and
experience of this environment aids
in any situation where people are
working together, actors on stage
doing a scene, or dancers performing
a piece.
The working relationships which
develop in this form cross the bound-
aries created by sexual roles; men
dance with men, women with women,
and men with women alike. I do not
know of anv artistic discipline in
which non-~exual, deep, and honest
physical contact is explored with_ to-
tal equality relative to sex. In this
form women lift men as often as men
lift women. Women learn to exercise
a kind of strength which often has
never been encouraged, likewise men
learn to allow themselves a soft, re-
sponsive, and non-competitive posture.
It is a revelation to both partners that
a one hundred and eighty pound man
can be easily & completely supported
by a one hundred pound woman.
Again, the trust derived from freeing
the traditional sexual roles creates a
more comfortable and fluid atmos-
phere among any group of artists
seeking to work together.
Contact improvisation seems to be
of particular value for the college age
person. College students find them-
selves in an intense period of transi-
tion. They are coming to grips with
the image and reality of themselves
as adults. Thev are intently exploring
the psychological politics of sexual
relationships. This form offers them
a grounded, simple, and clear oppor-
tunity to interact deeply and honestly
with each other. It seems to serve as
a reference point or forum for expe-
riencing oneself in relationship with
others in an unthreatening environ-
ment and without the need to formu-
late consciouslv or to talk about what
is going on. The strongest evidence
for the emotional supportive value of
this work is the very real attraction
students have for this form. They
want to experiment and expand. This
form gives them the opportunity to
go ahead.
In and of itself contact improvisa-
tion has become a significant move-
ment within the dance world, recog-
nized bv audiences and critics as a
fascinating and evocative performance
experience. Many up and coming
dancers and actors are using the skills
they've acquired through practice of
this form to advance themselves in
their artistic work. Considering its
value as a supportive discipline for
the actor studying theater or the danc-
er studying traditional techniques, as
well as its presence as a new and viable
dance form, I feel no hesitation in
recommending to any college or uni-
versity that contact improvisation be
a permanent part of the curriculum of
any program in dance or theater. ~

AIKIDO- INFORMATION IN DEED
by Steve Paxton
reprinted from Contact Newsletter, Vol I No 5, Summer 1976
] ack Wada (Sensei) Aikido instruc-
tor at [the University of California at
Santa Cruz] gave a demonstration
during the Contact Improvisation
workshop 19 April [1976]. Aikido
is a ] apanese Martial Art developed
early this century by M. Uyeshiba,
then a master of the extant martial
arts. Aikido has valuable information
for those who have eyes to see. Wada-
sensei did much to make the forces
visible; spirals and curves to meet the
pro-forma aggression of his 3 ukis, the
energy of easy blending, guiding, lead-
ing and directing attack energy to its
own neutered completion. Most val-
uable to me was Wada-sensei's unvary-
ing unromanticized unstressed con-
centration. Seeing him react was in-
formation in deed.
The martial arts are the arts of
war. They have each been forged in
the heat of life or death, and have
matured through a lineage of survivors.
Aikido is of particular interest due to
breadth of vision of the situation of
war. It does not stress the vs. but the
non-polar 'we'. The intense realiza-
tion which death brings to activity
has graced the martial arts including
Aikido with efficiency. This elegance
of means is based on profound (be-
cause essential) information of body
mechanics, training procedures, and
physical possibilities. Here is infor·
mation embodied in a seamless flow.
The concentration which cannot
bend. The accommodating winning
maneuver.
Contact Improvisation is not a
martial art. It is perhaps an art sport
if it is defined by the context in which
it currently operates. The means of
teaching are not those of life or death
confrontation but life to life confron-
tation. The teaching philosophy is
that of mildness, supposing that learn-
ing can be joyous, that the danger of
finding is a pleasure in depth. Con•
tact as a means has produced a com-
munity of particular information, an
'it' against which other things can be
assessed, by which 'it' 'itself' can be
gauged in living manifestation. It is
time to question: What is the result?
Is the fire hot enough to forge the
solid concentration? ls mildness
means enough to calm the seeker and
show the teacher and the student a
calm balanced center, to obliterate a
hierarchical I (invitation to war, Atlas
vs. Heracles) and to expose the We of
us and the forces? Or is it a cheap
thrill, placator, builder of influence
for those with the sweets, waste of
time spent in orgy of sensation? Com-
passionate teachers? Balanced teach-
ers? Concentrating teachers? What
will it be? The current generation is
the test animal. Contact will always
be reflected in our karma. Contact
is serene. We think we know: the
forces in play. 'It' /us are on the line.
Things are always life or death. ~

Volume 5 1979-80

THE BUSINESS OF CONTACT TOURING
Ellen: We're discussing the business
of Contact-How to be a business
manager or how not to be. What are
the primary ingredients in putting on
a performance? We came up with: a
mailing list, space (size and location),
scheduling, organizing. Are you doing
it for a friend or, as Dena called it,
are you taking a cut? What kind of
expectations did you have as the or-
ganizer? Were they shared by the
performer?
Peter: I think that as a promoter you
have to maintain your integrity. The
fact is that we are part of the Contact
community that stretches from coast
to coast. There are times when peo-
ple are bringing in someone they know
only from reputation. There is a dan-
ger that you may get in over your
head. You have to be able to say,
"no" even though you want the work-
shop to happen, "I'm sorry, I can't
guarantee this amount of money or
this number of students." You must
be clear as to what you can generate
and let the artist make the decision.
Dena: Concerning integrity, there
have been inklings and rumblings of
competition for markets, like "don't
touch Oregon, it's mine." The Bay
Area Contact Network in San Fran-
cisco, initiated bv Alan Ptashek and
others [see CQ Vol IV No 3/4] is a
model we can think about in the
future to help us cooperate instead
of compete. Isn't that the philosophy
of our dance form?
Ellen: In terms of competition among
producers, the four of us mentioned
that we hadn't been "doing it" lately,
so someone else is obviously "doing
it" instead.
Dena: The first year that I sponsored
people, I did it "as a gift", to help my-
self learn and to support contact in my
community. I've grown to understand
that it is important to take a percent-
age so that you don't feel that your
energy is exploited.
Roger: I've always thought that man-
agement is just as much of an art as
the dancing.
Ellen: On the East Coast Tour in
1978, it was suggested that the man-
agers and the performers all take an
equal cut. In this instance we were
seen as equal partners.
Roger: In actual fact, this arrange··
ment never happened.
Dena: In Montr(al, we've never of-
fered a guaranteed fee to the perform-
er because we are not producers and
have no money to back the perform-
ance. On the other hand, I've worked
for the Muse"e des beaux-arts de Mon-
tr~al which can produce the perform-
ance and take the risk of losing money.
Ellen: What we're talking about is
two styles, one where you are the
manager and have all the responsibi-
lity and another where you broker
the services of an institution. Clearly
if you can broker these services it
takes the risk, time, and frustration
out of your hands to some degree. If
you're going on your own, the failure
or success is all yours. The expecta-
tion of a good performance can be
crushed totally when no one shows
up and there is no money to pay any-
body, including yourself.
58 CQ/CI Saurcelx:Jok
a conversation between Peter
Ryan, Dena Davida, Ellen
Nayduch and Roger Neece
at Current Exchange 1979
Peter: Dena's letter in the Contact
Quarterly last year requested that
people not take it for granted that if
they gCJ on tour they're going to be
able to get sponsors in each city.
Again, if you are planning a tour,
please be sensitive and respect the
people who live there; they rely on
their community for their livelihood.
So, if you come in as a high profile
performer you are going to milk the
market. If you are on the road a lot
or want to be on the road a lot, con-
sider the fact that we are a limited
market. You are placing a great bur-
den upon your promotor to go be-
yond his/her neighborhood and dance
community to generate that extra
revenue for you.
Roger: The easiest way to organize
something is to affiliate yourself with
a local dance studio that does modern
or experimental work. You can use
their mailing list, studio, facilities,
etc. to piggyback with your own per-
sonal resources instead of taking on
all the organizing yourself. A finan-
cial guideline: if you co-sponsor with
someone (and they provide the post-
age and mailing, box office and pub-
licity) the standard split is 50150.
One situation or avenue that has been
very successful in New York City is
affiliation with galleries. The Paula
Cooper Gallery has hosted three sets
of performances, and the Holly Solo-
mon Gallery sponsored one. In some
cases they guarantee the performers
a small fee, but usually they furnish
only the space. Donations are re-
quested.
Peter: Vancouver is not a visual arts
center, and galleries have not been a
good alternative here. ._,.
Volume 5 1979-80
Dena: In Montr~al, we've worked
in three settings: Studio Catpoto,
universities, local studios. Luckily
we have always had our"own work
space and, given enough time, we can
free it up for a week. We usually get
it for a bargain price since we are the
major renters, say 100 dollars for the
week (and free classes for our land-
lords). Concordia University recently
hired Andrew Harwood to teach, and
Universit€ de Qufbec hired me-and
paid us on guest teacher salaries. Oc-
casionally Studio Nouvelle Aire has
co-sponsored someone with us.
Peter: If you can do it, get an insti-
tution to back you. Look for the
museums, the community colleges,
libraries, churches, public buildings
with downtown plazas, art councils.
Roger: It is also important to know
your local area and what can and
can't be done there. It's one thing
to organize a workshop for ten peo-
ple with a teacher who is your friend;
it's another thing to plan a series of
workshops, two or three performances
and guarantee a fee of $500. You
have to know beforehand what you're
going to be able to handle.
Dena: Getting very practical, I think
about all the expenses. There's the
airfare, fee for the space, poster de-
sign and printing, office supplies, post-
age, telephone, housing and food (in
Montreal we usually bed and board
the artist with us). Sometimes there
are additional costs such as video,
lighting equipment, tech personnel.
Ellen: Listening to Dena's list of ex-
penses and how they are or are not
paid for, I would describe this process
as "grass roots". If one looks at the
actual costs to produce a concert, it
can be quite expensive. It seems that
in the Contact community we are re-
lying much more on volunteer human
resources, than financial resources.
Dena: I find we're designing a new
business context. I've also been in-
volved with the National Endowment
for the Arts touring program, and I
see that contacters are working on a
totally different basis (day to day sur-
vival, maybe?)
Peter: In the winter of 1978, I spon-
sored Beth Goren and Danny Lepkoff.
The workshops were each a week long
and the fee was $ 50. There were 10
to 15 people per workshop so there
was a healthy amount of money. The
costs were not that great because air-
fare was not included.
Ellen: So out of this gross income of
$500 how much money did you make
and how much time did you put in?
Peter: I put in a lot of time, I did a
lot of groundwork and I haven't even
thought about the percentage. I had
a separate bank account for all money
I made as a sponsor and did not break
it down for each individual project,
but I probably made about$ 75 for
each, about 20%.
Ellen: One question that comes up in
terms of percentages is the amount
you take as the manager. I've also
taken from 10 to 20 percent, depend-
ing on my experience at the time.
Dena: The workshop classes have been
the real money-making part of each
residency. The performances have
brought in a minor amount in every
case.
Ellen: Production costs are usually
high compared to performance revenue.
Whereas in a workshop, the overhead
is low, the fees are high for the parti-
cipants, and you're paying only one
or two teachers.
Dena: The cost is infinitely greater
for five people. It's much easier to
do a successful duet tour.
Roger: One of the biggest problems
has been finding a suitable space
(large enough and with resilient floors),
and then having to pay $ 5 or $10 an
hour for many hours of classes.
Peter: Sometimes it's difficult to
negotiate a reasonable fee for a week
of classes. In Vancouver, I've rented
the Western Front and made arrange-
ments to cover the cost of the space.
I included that in the fee for the work-
shops. We don't work on a percentage
basis either.
Dena: In Montreal, we've always
offered a flat rate for space, not by
the hour or by a percentage.
Ellen: Another thing you might con-
sider, especially if you are in a learn-
ing phase, is bartering. Maybe you
want to take the workshops for free
in exchange for your services. Be clear
from the beginning with yourself and
the performer that that's all you want
out of it. I think you'll find the
amount of time it takes to produce a

CQ/CI Sourcebook 61
Beyond the
Rambling Rolling Rut
by Kris Wheeler
The following is a collection of
scores designed to encourage range
and skill with creative process while
involved in contact improvisation.
Creative process is the essence of im-
provisational artistry. It taps the in-
tuition and is therefore able to em-
brace great quantities of information,
and to perceive simultanegusly pat-
terns and relationships of many
dimensions-rhythm, poetry, design,
imagery ...
Dancers, especially those with no
other movement training than contact
improvisation, seem to limit their
movement material and restrict their
contact dance to a narrow range of
possibilities. Rather than shut out
the reception of info~ation we can
allow our intuition to absorb and play
with choices of a wide variety of di-
mensions.
When first becoming aware of and
expanding the range of our moment
to moment choices, we can find our-
selves intellectually deciding the ·
future. This interferes with the intui-
tive choice process. Let it be a phase
to pass through and emerge with more
awareness and greater participation
with the totality of the moment.
The Content of the Dance, the
Emerging Elements
Two people beginning in physical
contact, dance. (Later they can be-
gin out of contact.) When either one
notices an emerging idea, it is an-
nounced and both explore it as the
source of their dance. It needn't take
longer than one minute to announce
an idea and it can be anything from
the specific to the abstract, (e.g. slow,
in and out of contact, balancing, bub-
bling, I support as you fly, "Rock a
bye Baby," spinning ... ). The dance
should be at least a five minute ex-
ploration of the idea. If the other
dancer doesn't understand what was
said, or doesn't see how it fits, that
person says, "I don't get it" and the
dance continues until another idea
is announced. This is to keep from
trying to recreate what is already in
the past.
and the circle can announce what is
perceived as the movement idea to be
used as a source. Each time a new
dancer joins a new idea is announced.
(It is interesting to hear the circle's
observation of your movement.) With
time, more depth and subtlety will
develop in observation and vocabu•
lary-at about this stage dancers are
quick to perceive the potential of
their movement material and the an-
nouncement will be superfluous.
Stillness: to Clear the Space and
Frame the Dance, or Beginning and
Ending
First work with short solos-1 to 3
seconds in length! Clearly establish
both the beginning and the ending.
Ask for feedback from observers
about their perceptions of exactly
when ~d with which part of the body
the dance began and ended.
Two people work together, starting
out of contact, finding their own indi-
vidual beginning, spending some time
in physical contact and then finding
their own individual ending with what-
ever seems appropriate. It is impor-
tant to recognize and stay committed
to the ending moment. Eventually
the dancers become more perceptive
of each other and their interface so
that the dance seems to determine
the beginning and ending.
The Line
The part of the dance between the
beginning and the ending can be
thought of as the line. The line repre-
sents the flow of the dance; anything
from the sometimes local develop- •
ment of an idea to a series of chaotic
and random events. Some improvisa-
tional lines work and some don't (and
some work for some people and not
for others). The point is to be aware
of the line, or the flow of the dance,
in order to more fully participate in
it.
In a round robin, both the dancers
In groups of 3, two dance and one
watches. A short dance at first, 2 to
S minutes with clear beginning and
ending. Eventually work toward much
longer dances that hold together as
a whole. After each dance spend some
time with feedback and discussion.
How did it hang together? Did it end
too soon, chopping off the line? Did
it ramble and not utilize the opportu-
nities? ...
Uniqueness of each Dancer
After dancers have grown more
sensitive to their partner, their sen-
sitivity can be interwoven with their
own unique creative process: Two
dancers start individually-simulta-
neous solos, time to gain a sense of
their own dance; at some point they
come into contact and work with
maintaining their own individual line
as well as creating the duet. Eventu-
ally they leave physical contact to
find an individual ending. ~

DIFFERENT DIRECTIONS IN CONTACT
by Keriac
Until I wrote this article, I hadn't
realized how many different directions
I've gone with Contact in the past
year. All of these experiences are
united by one personal goal: I'd like
to contribute to the development of
Contact as a folk art, a meaningful art
activity which ordinary people do for
pure enjoyment in the normal course
of their lives. I wantto be a dance
teacher/performer within a community
of people who may have chosen not to
dance full-time, but still retain a sense
of the dancer in themselves. I've been
reaching out to people who might not
otherwise think of getting into Con-
tact dancing, even if they should have
heard of it. I've been contacting:
with Seniors ...
I did a Contact workshop with
SAGE, a senior citizens group in
Oakland, California. When I arrived
I was pleasantly surprised to find all
the senior citizens and social workers
lying on the floor of a plushly car-
peted living room, each with their
head on someone else's belly, laughing
hysterically. Naturally, I immediately
joined the fun.
As I led the group through contact
exercises, I noticed a nervousness in
myself. I feared being out of touch
with the stamina and flexibility of
older bodies and the degree of risk
appropriate to older people. The pos-
sibility of injuries floated up into my
mind. However, this group responded
to the same exercises that I use in my
regular classes with only slight modi-
fication, and I soon felt at ease. I
stayed primarily with exercises that
did not take the participant very far
off center, but by the end of the ses-
sion, we had even gotten into weight
taking, and the only person who had
dropped out was an 83 year old wo-
man who had recently broken her
ankle.
As the two hour session was com-
ing to an end, I found myself doing
active-passive duets (I was the active
one) with one person after another.
Everyone around me began claiming,
"I'm next!" "I'm next!" It was fun.
Every single person danced a duet
with me, even the woman with the
broken ankle. These older men and
women let me balance them in the
air on my hip or back without clutch-
ing or showing any signs of fear. They
melted into the floor and took my
weight with just the right amount of
active-passive involvement. I was a-
mazed-we were really doing contact!
At our final hug, everyone was in-
credibly high, including myself-des-
pite my sweaty exhaustion. As I left,
I overheard them saying that they
wished the experience had been video-
taped so that I could be shown at an
upcoming Senior's conference in the
Midwest. Gray power is powerful!
with the Blind ...
I also taught a series of classes at
the Lighthouse for the Blind this last
year. There were six students, some
were senior citizens. Within this par-
ticular group of blind people, there
were multiple handicaps. Some were
mentally retarded or psychologically
disoriented, some had excessively
tense, rigid bodies, one was obese, one
had a fractured collar bone from a re-
cent fall, others had arthritis, diabetes
and other conditions which made them
short of breath or easily dizzied.
I altered my class goals, deciding to
use contact as a movement therapy
tool rather than as an end in itself.
We spent about an hour on warm-up
and relaxation, then spent the last
hour on contact exercises. Since I
couldn't just say "Do it like this" and
then visually demonstrate the activity,
I was forced to describe many sensa-
tions that I experience in Contact with
precise language. This was a major
challenge for me and I learned a lot
about Contact in the process.
Using Contact experiences to im-
prove the student's real-life function-
ing increased my awareness of how
really beneficial and healing Contact
is. I feel incredibly enriched by the
luxury of my present Contactor's life-
style which includes so much touching,
trusting, and ease of movement.
Since classes at the Lighthouse have
come to an end, one of the visually dis-
abled students (without other impair-
ments) is now attending my regular
class where he is much in demand as
a Contact partner among the other
students. ~
Volume 5 1979-80
CQICI Sourcebook 63
with Therapists ...
Teaching workshops for therapists
with my sister, Ilene Dillon,-who is a
therapist with a private practice in
Marin County, has also increased my
vocabulary and awareness of Contact
as a therapeutic tool. Ilene immedi-
ately recognizes principles of assertion
at work in the dynamics of Contact
interplay. Her theories on manipula-
tion are amply illustrated in what
makes a Contact duet work or not
work. It seems that the same laws
which are operating on a physical
level in Contact activities are also op-
erating on a psychological level in re-
lationships. Vocabulary used to de-
scribe emotional realities has a very
strong impact when one senses that
it is also true on a literal level when
one dances Contact, and vice versa.
Bruce Dodson, a member of the
"Issues in Radical Therapy" news-
paper collective, and I co-led a Con-
tact session at the Radical Psychiatry
Summer Institute at Fort Mason this
year. Here the language and emphasis
differed slightly from Ilene's, but it
seemed that Contact also fit in well
with the Radical Psychiatry point of
view. I found it easy to communicate
the notion of giving body surface to
case a falling person's descent (rather
than grab them to try to keep them
from harm) to people who were al-
ready familiar with the concepts of
the "rescuer-victim-persecutor" tri-
angle (when a person tries to 'help'
someone by 'taking care' of them,
they create a victim who will later
resent"-persecute] them for it.) In
other words, the person with the prob-
lem should have control of solving it.
My communal family and I attend-
ed the Summer Institute together. We
have started using Radical Psychiatry
techniques in our emotional sharing
meetings and our personal lives. It
has helped us achieve more intimacy
as a group, and a better clarity in re-
solving conflicts. It feels good to sense
the harmony between the principles
used in my family and emotional life
and in my dancing.
with Communes ...
The Communal Grapevine, (a net-
work of communes in the S.F. East
Bay) invited me to lead a Contact ses-
sion at their monthly potluck gather-
ing. Over 40 communal houses belong
to the network.
It seemed like all 40 of the house-
holds were in attendance. The very
large living room of the host commune
was filled to the brim with people
64 CQ/CI Sourcebook
ready to experience their first Contact
session, and beyond their heads I could
see many others pouring in the front
door. This was a greatly diverse group,
ranging from early teens to seniors,
and encompassing a wide variety of
lifestyles. For some, it was clear that
Contact teetered on the edge of what
was sane and/or respectable behavior.
But an "I'll try anything once" atti-
tude prevailed.
About a quarter of the group was
still dancing two hours later after
hungrier companions drifted off to
sample the temptations of the pot
luck. By then we were involved in
pretty athletic movement and the de-
crease in density gave us room to move
safely. At the end, the diners and dan-
cers re-grouped and discussed values of
interdependent, cooperative, trusting,
and responsible interaction held in
common by Contact Improvisation
and communal living.
Several communal houses invited
me to come to their regular house
meetings to lead their family in an
evening of Contact sharing. I accepted.
For some houses, this meant the first
breaking of an unspoken taboo against
physical contact among the house
members, and caused a mixture of an-
ticipatory excitement and apprehen-
sion. So far I have not heard of any
resulting orgies fulfilling anybody's
worst (or best?) fears after indulging
in the communal contact sessions I
have led.
Some of the communards in the
East Bay wanted more Contact than
a one-shot family meeting. At the
time, no one was teaching Contact in
the East Bay, so I started a class which
was held at Berkeley Moving Arts and
then later at the Dwight House com-
mune. It was attended primarily by
members of three communes although
publicity eventually opened it up to
others as well.
(Historical note: I began teaching
Contact more than two years ago in
much the same series of events. First,
just a weekly sharing session with my
communal family in our living room.
Then my family began bringing their
friends, the friends brought their
friends, and soon I was putting up
posters and teaching four classes a
week in the Walkabout studio in San
Francisco.)
in Community performances ...
This summer, a small loose group
of contactors calling ourselves "Public
Works" met informally on Sundays
in Golden Gate Park and performed
outdoors for the crowds lined up wait-
ing to get into the King Tut exhibition.
I initiated this project, modeling the
structure on a street performance
group of which I was a member five
years ago. "Public Works" differed
from the earlier group in that we used
props rather than white face to pro-
voke curiosity and we did Contact
Improvisation rather than theater.
Wearing construction worker hats
and bright orange T-shirts, and sur-
rounded by our props, which included
road crew pylons and flags, plus two
sawhorses stenciled with the "Public
Works" logo over the bright orange
stripes, we evoked the image of work-
ers doing street repair. The dancing
started with high energy jumps and
lifts, and the contact duet movement
was amplified with hand-held road
crew flags. This was the most effec-
tive way we found to attract attention
and gather an audience. "Public
Works" participants agreed it was an
interesting experiment. We plan to
revise and recycle it after the rainy
season is over.
This fall, I've continued my explor-
ation of Contact as a performance
form with the advanced students in
my regular classes. ] oe Dowling,
Robin Snyder and I recently gave a
Contact demonstration and perform-
ance at the Noe Valley Public Library.
Conditions were difficult: a cold,
linoleum covered concrete floor in a
small room with low ceilings. A par-
ty with a live band was going on in
the room above us, and our space
was the only access route to the build-
ing bathrooms. However, we had a
capacity crowd of enthusiastic people
who joined us in an hour of Contact
experiences, watched our Contact per-
formance, and then eagerly partici-
pated in the question and answer
period. The physical limitations •
had not prevented us from turning
people onto Contact.
This fall, Charles Campbell and I
also did a Contact performance and
demo at the Jewish Community Cen-
ter, which included a lively question
and answer period. People in the
Bay Area are beginning to notice
the term "Contact Improvisation"
and are eager to find out what it's
all about. 'Bo

CONTACT IMPROVISATION is about acquainting,
adapting, balancing, centen'ng, connecting, curving,
dancing, draping, embracing, exchanging, falling,
folding, gliding, hurdling, joining, launching, lever-
aging, lifting, listening, merging, orbiting, oozing,
pivoting, rising, risking, rocking, rolling, shifting,
slipping, spinning, spiraling, supporting, tossing,
twnbling, undefining, venturing, waiting, watching
and yielding-not necessarily in that order.
-Robin Feld
Volume 6 1980-81
CQ/CI Sourcebook 65
Steve Paxton and Daniel Lepkoff
CONTACT IMPROVISATION is an
activity related to familiar duet forms
such as the embrace, wrestling, martial
arts, and the jitterbug, encompassing
the range of movement from stillness
to highly athletic. The exigencies of
the form dictate a mode of movement
which is relaxed, constantly aware
and prepared, and on-flowing. As a
basic focus, the dancers remain in
66 CQ/CI Sourcebook
photo: Stephen Petegorsky
physical touch, mutually supportive
and innovative, meditating upon the
physical laws relating to their masses:
gravity, momentum, inertia and fric-
tion. They do not strive to achieve
results, but rather, to meet the con-
stantly changing physical reality
with appropriate placement and
energy.
Volume 6 1980-81
Q &A
by Steve Paxton
"What is Contact Improvisation?"
That the question is asked reveals
either that the term is investigated by
people with no opportunity to see
performances or jams by contactors;
no recourse to social dancing, a friend-
ly wrestling match; or, as in my case,
too much exposure to the above.
When, in 1972, a group of dancers
began work on Contact Improvisation,
it was a study of the way communica-
tion was possible through touch. My
own background was mostly the Mod-
ern Dance, which may be seen as a
study by individuals in physical isola-
tion, usually within a group. In classes
of this sort one maintains a sphere of
isolation from other students to avoid
belaboring them with battements.
Crossing the floor, it is customary to
move serially, enabling the instructor
to see each student as they pass.
From this background the commu-
nication possible through touching
was, and still is, a profound experience.
Likewise the movement which resulted
from contact improvising-a non-ration-
alized, intuitive movement leading to
unforeseen phrasing, positions and
gambits-was a relief from years of
willful technical applications.
Although improvisation has long
been a sophisticated approach to
music and, more recently, to drama
in the U.S., in my background it didn't
exist. Although there were many ref-
erences to improvisation as an impor-
.tant factor in the development of mod-
ern dance (Isadora Duncan and Rudolf
Laban,) by the time I came to study,
Modern Dance seemed in a period of
codification. From the many 'main-
line' modern dancers with whom I
studied, I can recall only classes full
of rote exercises.
This state of affairs is so contrary to
the spirit of Duncan or the ideas of
Laban that one might suppose Modern
Dance had been born and died during
the first half of the 20th Century. Yet
the term 'Modern Dance' holds promise
of renewal: it seems less useful to limit ~
Volume 6 1980-81
CQ!CI Sourcebook 67
LETTER FROM STEVE PAXTON
TO THE CRITICS in England
Totnes, Devon March '80
the phrase to a particular period than
to presume that the name, phoenix-like,
can rise from the ashes and become
again applicable. In this interpretation
it becomes an umbrella for avant-garde
efforts of any sort, which is more or
less the situation I encountered.
From this perspective, basing move-
ment upon intimate communication
(rather than aesthetic dictates) was
appealing. It spoke to my body, which
had been in isolatiori for many years.
Having found the phenomena, the
next step was to select ingredients in
preparation for the extremes of move-
ment the communication could pro-
duce. Aikido, gymnastic and medita-
tion techniques were included, and
material gleaned from the contact im-
provisation itself.
It was here, I think, in the effort to
create a technical approach to insure
greater safety, that misunderstanding
of the form was born. Many folk who
are drawn to Contact Improvisation to
explore their physicality, will begin the
practice willfully, trying to 'under-
stand' and move intellectually (as is
their habit) instead of exploring wil-
lingly. For some of these students,
the techniques suggested by the teach-
er become doctrine. They 'know'
what Contact Improvisation is because
they remember the words and perform
the techniques. I believe there is no
form of dance for which this approach
will liberate the dancer. Certainly not
Contact Improvisation which is not a
form of set movement phrases but an
approach to movement in which the
student moves from the motivations
of reflex and intuition. There is a con-
tradiction in a technical approach to
improvisation. While one is aiming for
a freedom from rationalized movement,
one is still studying movement from the
example and words of a teacher.
Ideally, each student would move at
their own rate, developing physical
skills appropriate to their own improvi-
sational explorations. However, in the
case of willful technique, a teacher
might retard the evolution of the class
