Nancy Stark Smith have also taught
weekend workshops. Additionally,
Ms. Smith gave a lecture demonstra-
tion with Laurie Booth at Dance Um-
brella's Conference on Dance Artists
in Education (Nov 30-Dec 2, 1979).
There have been no performances
given by American practitioners who
have been developing the form for the
past eight years. In short, experience
of Contact Improvisation in England
is incomplete.
I hope the questions of definition
and rigor of the form can be addressed
next season. Freelance Dance may be
invited to this country for extended
workshops and performances. This
is a company of men and women in-
strumental in developing Contact Im-
provisation and adept in its practice.
Meanwhile I recommend the Con-
tact Quarterly as a source of informa-
tion about the farm.
CRITIC'S RESPONSE:
LETTER FROM ANN NUGENT of
Stage and Television Today TO
STEVE PAXTON
March '80
Dear Steve Paxton,
Thank you for your circular letter,
which I was very glad to receive. I am
sorry if I was one of the offenders in
my definition of your work, and I
would indeed welcome the chance to
discuss it with you.
It was certainly my impression that
what you were doing at Riverside in
particular was 'Con tact Improvisation',
especially since there was an article on
the notice board there which seemed
relevant to what we had seen and
which went into clear detail about
your method. But in any event I
found myself unable to read fully into
what you and others were doing, and
I kept on wishing that the Dance Um-
brella had allowed time for sessions of
informal discussion so that we could
all share in closer understanding of
some of the work. Obviously if one
sees a two-hour performance of work
that has taken say a decade to evolve
the results may not be fully evaluated,
and it helps to have a working know-
ledge of some of the guidelines that
have been worked out . ..
I hope this is of some use to you.
I'd be happy to answer any further
questions.
Volume 6 1980-81
so that fundamentals of moving are the
focus, giving everybody an extended
opportunity to sink past the conscious
mind into more appropriate states. The
student is unlikely to remain 'willful'-
extracting learning on only one level
of mind-if the material is a 20 minute
concentration on rolling the body
around the floor. In the search for
further thoughts, the student's mind
becomes witness to the body and the
sensations of movement.
There are other reasons for retarding
the explorations of a class, and other
methods. Especially when taking the
first several classes with a group, I try
to gauge their perceptions and respon-
ses to simple situations. I watch for
fainters and those who look nauseous,
both common enough for people who
maintain rigidity in their bodies or
attitudes toward them. I have found
students who can't remember ever
doing a forward roll, or who become
disoriented if they roll over more than
'h roll at a time. As a teacher, these
students are especially dear to me;
their bravery in beginning to move is
more beautiful to me than most of
the throwabout expert maneuvers
expectable from 'advanced' students
of the form.
On the other hand, there are peo-
ple who will throw themselves with
astonishing abandon through the air,
onto the resources of a relative strang-
er. In abstract, the equations in
classes are fascinating. When some-
body who is flabby and insecure con-
nects with one of the devil-may-care
types, the chemistry is ... sensational.
However, the experience of these
students may not be pleasant, and
really, it should be if the learning be
of such stuff as to provoke further
exploration.
Teaching has taught me that hu-
mans operate under a wider variety
of senses and perceptions than I had
imagined, that each student has tuned
their senses differently in relation to
their experiences. One of the first
jobs is to tune the senses for the
dance at hand. I have seen willful
students straining their muscles in an
attempt to improve their dancing, but
I have yet to observe them strain their
senses. Nor would I consider this de-
sirable. The senses are tuned the way
they are for reasons of survival. New
perceptions should not be forced, but
instead, suggested or invited.
I tend to conduct classes with cau-
tion therefore, especially at first.
Stefan Szczelkun remarks that in
the videotape Chute a certain rough
and tumble attitude is seen in the
first group to work with Contact Im-
provisation. It's true that a great
many things were tried and that no
injuries resulted from the experiments.
But this was an invited group, whose
physical states and abilities were
known. And there were mats to
soften the errors. Backing up the ef-
fort was another important element;
we were doing nothing else than this
dancing for two weeks.
To recapitulate: first there was a
communicative movement form, then
there were technical developments to
implement extremes of the form.
During this period Contact Improvisa-
tion was known physically and in
common by those whose dancing de-
veloped it. We were contact improvi-
sation.
That initial work came to presenta-
tion, and those first dancers began to
teach, and to move around the coun-
try.
Christina Svane remarked that it
was like a poker player moving into
a new town ... to continue their game,
the first step is to teach it. Contact
is not a form of solitaire ...
The essence of the form is easy to
understand. It has been dont::. by wo-
men in their fifties, and one who was
8 months pregnant. It has been done
by men who were tender and other-
wise.
Technical development has been
an attempt to formulate and collate
habits of movement to further the
dance within the stream of time and
forces converging in this work. ~

Is Contact a Small Dance?
Mark Pritchard addressed the con-
tact community with observations,
comments and criticisms in 'New Age
Shuffleboard'. His concerns seem to
stem from a sense of passivity, self-
righ teousness and faddism, coupled
with a loss of creative integrity in the
contact community. Having built my
life, if not my lifestyle, on contact
improvisation for the last five' years,
I am interested in his perspective as
a relative newcomer. Is it indication
of a path of evolution the form has
taken? Coincidentally, the latest
Contact Quarterly has reprinted an
article Steve Paxton wrote four years
ago. In it he speaks of the mild life-
to-life confrontation of contact set
against the life-or-death urgency of
aikido: " ... is it [contact] a cheap
thrill, placator, builder of influence
for those with the sweets, waste of
time spent in orgy of sensation?"
Mark concludes in his article that
"contact risks becoming dull, at least,
and self-righteous and pretentious at
worst." Are these similar concerns?
Is it a lack of urgency which has
brought on passivity and a death of
creative outreach?
I have recently returned from
Minneapolis and the largest gathering
of contactors ever held. The Ameri-
can Dance Guild Conference was not
only a meeting ground for east/west/
central/north & south, for veteran and
neophyte, but also for contactor and
noncontact improvisor. It was a fo-
rum for witnessing, among other
things, various choices about perform-
ance: practical and philosophical. It
was a brief intersection of many diver-
gent paths; an opportunity to turn
360 degrees and gain some external
referents for the paths we are on to-
gether and alone.
So where are we? Is contact a fad-
another part of the everything natural
trend these days? Perhaps more im-
portant, is it a dead end for creativity
particularly in a performance vein?
Or as Mark asks, does it degenerate
into some other discipline, or no dis-
cipline at all?
From my recent experiences at
home and abroad I feel there is much
life in the contact community but
there are more questions than there
are answers. We seem to be at the
by Byron Brown
"They wrestle, they shove, they kick. But the rule always is, 'Bluff when you
can and don't get hurt!" [from Ranger Rick's Nature Magazine, September 1977]
" ... Where do behavioral and relational contact lead us in the
future? How do they relate to dance and choreography?"
72 CQ;CI Sourcebook
Volume 6 1980-81
u
c
cL
:J
c
"Chivalry" in rehearsal with Jiangrove members Charles Campbell, Byron
Brown and Ernie Adams
Volume 6 1980-81
end of one cycle of seven or eight
years of growth, and entering anoth-
er. The contact form has spanned
this continent many times and made
a name for itself in the dance world.
It boasts literally dozens of teachers
and performers and thousands of
practitioners. What I find particular-
ly lacking are conceptual frameworks
for what we are doing as artists and
a corresponding lack of ability to
have meaningful creative dialogue as
perf~rming artists. In this paper I
would like to share my perception of
contact improvisation as it relates to
two different performance modes:
the behavioral and the relational. In
so doing I hope I will speak to ;\lark's
concerns by stimulating continued
dynamism in this "artsport".
Contact improvisation is based on
certain principles and assumptions.
Mark Pritchard focuses on the sup-
posed contact hang-up with the term
"natural". I would like to substitute
the word "functional": contact de-
rives from functional movement. In
other words we learn to use our
bodies in necessary and efficient ways
in order to relate to our environment
and a moving partner. It is functional
to relax while falling because it takes
less energy and it causes less energy [sic]
upon impact. It is also functional to
catch oneself with the arms because
they can break a fall and not jar the
system if the wrists, elbows and shoul-
ders give with the weight. For our
purposes in dancing it is also function-
al to roll upon meeting the floor,
transferring the direction of the mo-
mentum from vertical to horizontal
in order to keep the energy moving.
To me the word "functional" helps
bypass the confusion over a nebulous
word like "natural". Being functional
as a principle of moving is integral to
contact for safety, speed and ease. In
the world of nature it is often said
that form follows function. In contact
the "natural" elegance of the pure
form could be seen in the same way:
one watches the everchanging forms
of the human body as it falls, supports,
jumps, catches, lifts and follows an-
other body. This quality of function-
al grace is shared with most sports,
and it is herein lies the connection be-
tween contact and nature. -+
CQ/CI Sourcebook 73
However, the questions of aesthe-
tics and creativity still remain. Sports
do not focus on either because the
functional goal of achieving or win-
ning remains paramount. Contact
only moves into the realm of art when
the efficiency and subtlety of physi-
cal interaction become tools for some
other process. And if it is to be a
shared art form, contact must serve
a performance process. The Dance
Guild Conference confirmed most
clearly the fact that contact improvi-
sation can emerge in several different
performance forms. I do not believe
there is a single contact aesthetic, or
even that an aesthetic is a necessary
part of doing contact.
A second principle which I find
confused in Mark's experience of the
contact community, and which is a
key to the creative leap in contact, is
personal responsibility. That is learn-
ing to take responsibility for oneself,
to take care of oneself, to be able to
respond. It is this goal on a physical
level which leads so logically into ho-
listic health and self-healing. Becom-
ing responsive means becoming aware
of one's own body, what it can do,
what it needs and how to take care of
it. Doctor/drug oriented medicine
does not generally support self-aware-
ness, self-control or personal respon-
sibility. In terms of dancing itself,
a sense of personal responsibility
means learning efficient movement
for safety and ease; and it also means
learning that one is an active partici-
pant in every movement interaction.
One cannot be passive and at the same
time able to respond without actively
choosing that particular response. Part
of the ongoing activity is to monitor
the movement input in order to guar-
antee personal safety. As the skill and
range of responsiveness expands this
part will become less and less of the
whole. Safety becomes understood
and personal responsiveness becomes
the basis for creative play. With "func-
tional" physical interaction basically
"informing" the dancing, the creative
interplay arises from mutual awareness
of weight, rhythm, momentum, en-
vironment, attitude and feeling. It is
at this level of awareness, and not the
level of functional form, that aesthetics
emerge. Sharing a physical vocabulary
74 CQ/CI Sourcebook
of contact improvisation does not
guarantee a shared aesthetic, especial-
ly performance attitude. This was
clearly demonstrated for the audience
in a duet performance which I did
with Nancy Stark Smith at the Con-
ference. Contact gave us a way to
bring together two different histories
and styles of performance-a creative
interplay. Let's now look at these
two aesthetics. I remind you that
these are my observations and may
not relate to how others define these
approaches. Especially those dancing
them.
* *
Because contact improvisation is a
process of unmediated (by thought or
socialization) human action-reaction,
it is a perfect vehicle for the observa-
tion of human and personal behavior.
This fact underlies much of Steve
Paxton's interest in contact as a per-
formance medium as he explained to
us in Minneapolis. Watching the flow
of physical attention through the re-
sponsive bodies, one sees unedited
minds in motion, unpremeditated and
unmediated by social or theatrical
forms. The dance provides us with
immediate access to the flow, each
moment significant and, in theory,
none more so than any other. With
the focus on the dance, the audience
energy serves to heighten that focus,
not draw it out. The result, as evi-
denced in the performances of Free
Lance [Danny Lepkoff, Lisa Nelson,
Steve Paxton, Nancy Stark Smith,
Christina Svane] , is not virtuoso per-
formances but dance stripped of its
artiface, the artless art. The form
points to its own meaning; we watch
the body-mind operating largely free
of conceptual discrimination.
Contact can always inform the be-
havior of those who practice it. Yet,
when performed as a "behavioral"
process it will always run the risk of
appearing dull, self-righteous and pre-
tentious to certain audiences. This is
largely a matter of signification. We
all exist and operate in worlds of pri-
ority and meaning; a multi-faceted en-
vironment takes on significance
through socialization, ambition and
habit. Contact improvisation steps
away from these into a world of pre-
cognitive, nonconceptual physical
activity: the small dance. This is
the ongoing process of awareness and
responsiveness which requires no
thought. (It puts functioning and
creativity beyond our faculty of rea-
son.) To watch the small dance is
to confront one's needs for signifi-
cance and meaning as habitually un-
derstood. If performed with integrity
it is a constant revelation of the
watcher's process of signification.
At the same time the dancing it-
self tends toward an evenness of ten-
sion and dynamic. As the signifi-
cance of action is from moment to
moment, at a distance the activity
has little meaning or shape. Are we
left with a small dance one must sit
next to in order to see? A small dance
with no meaning in the larger picture
of emotions, politics, morality and
progress? A small dance only bound
to touch a small number of people?
The paradox is there. In each of us
is the small dance with no "mean-
ing", the small dance which belies
the importance we place on mental
and social forms in our lives, the
small dance which is closest to life
and death and hardest to feel.
It is also of interest to view what I
will call the behavioral contact per-
formance from the dancer's side.
Mark's concerns are again relevant
here. For him a danger related to
this approach is the dictum to "go
with the flow of natural events, rather
than attempting to shape those events
on one's own. This viewpoint is de-
structive because it precludes creativi-
ty." What is the creative process for
the behavioral performer? Without
the dancer's traditional involvement
in dramatic, rhythmic, spatial or dy-
namic tension, wherein lies the crea-
tive act? Is contact a minimal art? A
nonexistent art? I would propose that
the creative process lies between the
behavioral process (the contact dance)
and the observant mind, both of
which lie within the dancer.
The fihe line for the dancer/artist
lies between purely experiential danc-
ing and mindful editorial directing,
between self-indulgent movement
(i.e. no care for the audience) and
performed improvisation. The disci-
pline is walking the line, and the aes-
thetic lies in that distance, that ten-
sion, that space created for witnessing
the unmediated behavioral process.
This creative tension within the
dancer is perhaps what distinguishes
behavioral contact from other disci-
plines: aikido, massage, therapy, im-
provisational dance. The loss of this
tension explains why contact so often
loses its integrity especially in per-
formance. A performer will often
gravitate towards a form which is
more actively creative on a physical
level (i.e. theatre, dance improvisa-
tion, acrobatics, slapstick comedy)
or less demanding on an attitudinal
level. When the integrity holds, be-
Volume 6 198(}.81
havioral contact is a joy to watch.
Much as one marvels at and is en-
thralled by wild animal behavior or
primitive human ceremonies, here is
added the fact that the performers
are so like us but stripped of ego and
convention as they move.
* *
Mangrove began its improvisation-
al performance process essentially in
the behavioral mode, but intentional-
ly as a group process open to sound,
solo and improvisational impulse.
Gradually the creative focus became
the transformational process within
and between us. Contact created a-
wareness, physical vocabulary and
energy exchange for our process. Yet,
perhaps more fundamentally, it estab-
lished an unmediated flow into which
we could submerge and out of which
we could emerge. Our intention was
not simply to observe the behavior
but to play with the forms that ap-
peared through the dancing: push
them, alter them, shape them, echo
them. In other words developing
awareness over time as well as in the
immediate moment and encouraging
recognition of energy forms in the
movement flow other than just kin-
esthetic. These include leader-follow-
er, mirroring, rhythmic play, gesture,
focus of attention, attitude and even
conceptual meaning. Extensions of
this have led us into vocalizing, use of
character and musical instruments.
The resulting performance form has
created a very different pattern of
meaning from the behavioral mode.
Is it contact? Are we involved in
a new discipline with a different con-
tact aesthetic? Or have we simply put
new movement skills at the service of
an old form? I was very interested to
watch a group from Washington, D.C.
performing at the Guild Conference.
Free Association did a 45 minute set
which was essentially a free-flow of
movement/theatre improvisation. I
recognized many elements of process
work; I could empathize with all the
different choices which confronted the
dancers. This company I believe is six
years old (Mangrove is five) and they
do not use contact as such. Is there
some fundamental difference in the
nature of our works and is it related
to contact? Mangrove is certainly not
closer to Free Lance than to Free As-
sociation.
Earlier I spoke of two aspects of
contact improvisation: its functional
nature and its relationship to personal
responsibility. A third aspect which
for me triangles out from the other
Volume 6 1980-81
two is the realm of communication.
This is necessary for and a result of
each of the other two; it is the basis
of relationship. Awareness of the
small dance implies the opening of
freer communication within the body.
Weight exchange implies communica-
tion between bodies dancing. Sharing
awareness of the <lance's flow in terms
of rhythm, space, dynamic and atti-
tude implies subtle communication on
a more expanded scale. By appreci-
ating this range of activity from inter-
nal to external, awareness and commu-
nication may be viewed as different
facets of energy flow.
We relate to an arbitrary boundary
of the skin surface for interacting with
others especially in contact because
we are generally aware of feeling be-
yond it. However, we see beyond it,
hear beyond it, sense air currents,
temperature, balance and tension be-
yond it. With the eyes closed and put-
ting our body image out of mind, we
build a very different picture of our
presence: it may extend from the
street noise outside to the throbbing
in the head; from the feet on the
floor to the sense of others watching.
Just as the small dance of a stand is a
dynamic balance of continual internal
movement so a dance space can be ex-
perienced as a system of constant en-
ergy flow in dynamic balance. Ener-
gy moves when a body is lifted, when
eyes shift focus, when breathing fills
one's awareness, when an onlooker
gasps.
As dancer and performer I want to
sense the energy flows, give them sub-
stance through my dancing, play with
them and transform them. I want to
communicate with an audience the re-
lational rather than isolated or fixed
nature of the world, through the inter-
play of many levels of energy, includ-
ing theirs as audience wa.ching. In
Mangrove this energy flow abstraction
is realized through the many facets of
us as human beings. We move through
space, we touch and interact, we cre-
ate vocal sound, we verbalize images,
commands, rhetoric and dialogue, we
indicate emotional energy, we creai:e
group rituals, we play and laugh at our-
selves, we take on roles and we play
music. Sometimes we are vessels for
the spirit, sometimes we are craftsmen
of the theatrical space, sometimes we
are cut off frcm others and ourselves.
For all of this contact is a bridge, a
path, a door. Its functionality helps
minimize our show, our front, our
cleverness. The sense of personal re-
sponsibility fostered has been at the
heart of our collective process and
our aesthetic mix. Communication
fuels our personal/physical relation-
ships and those with our audiences.
It is this sense of relationship (de-
scribed by one critic: "no matter how
close there was always space in be-
tween, no matter how far there was
always connection") which I feel dis-
tinguishes us from Free Association
and Free Lance; and for me it is an
aesthetic derived from contact im-
provisation. Our relational process
runs the gamut from being undisting-
uished servants of the space's energy
to insensitive manipulators of the
same. Certainly the discipline is much
harder to pin down in relational con-
tact. Mangrove deals with traditional
creative tensions: space, rhythm, fo-
cus, emotion and meaning. But as the
form is one of relational process, I see
the primary tension being between en-
ergy and form: can the everchanging
form reflect the energy in the space?
Is it contact improvisation and does
it matter? Is the relational form a
distinct discipline or an amalgam of
other disciplines? Where do behavior-
al and relational contact lead us in
the future? How do they relate to
dance and choreography? What about
Catpoto, Contactworks, Men Work-
ing, Mirage and Faircatch? Are there
other performance processes and con-
tact-derived aesthetics? As contact
improvisation is the folk-art-sport of
an ever diversifying community, we
can expect the nature of our public
dancing to reflect the state of our art.
The questions remain. I leave you
with one final query:
Is contact a small dance? ll

Editor's Note
JUDSON/CONT ACT RELAY
A Legacy of Ambiguity
Seven days after the Judson Dance Theater Recon-
structions at St. Mark's Church and the event seems a thing
of the past. More so, even, than the original events done in
the '60s. It was a little bit like seeing an old family photo
of an event that is indelibly etched in your memory, but
when viewed directly seems very far away. The event is
more clearly gone, but even so, the memory remains current.
Maybe that's because a memory, as it's carried through time,
can change, and adapt to the new time. Though memories
tend to slip away when they're no longer relevant, fixing
them extinguishes their fire much sooner.
I'd never been to Judson, and I was glad to see the 17
reconstructed works, but I was not nearly as filled with the
spirit of Judson in the '60s as I had anticipated. I feel the
spirit of those times best in the sense of humor, support,
and challenge that I've witnessed between artists whose
friendships were forged in that period-the permission to
undermine one another, in fact the hope that if one got too
comfortable, someone would pull the rug out from under
you and set you spinning again.
And so it is ten years since the first public perform-
ances of Contact Improvisation. People are talking now of
producing historical Contact Improvisation events, writing
books about Contact, and this issue of Contact Quarterly is
Cl's 10th anniversary issue.
At least one baton that seems to have been passed
from the Judson world of the '60s to Contact in the '70s
is a resistance to being solidified-the sense that to stay in
one place too long is to freeze there. But whether you stay
or go further, a "place" has inevitably been cleared by your
passing, and that point of reference exists. Every day more
trails are blazed to, from, through and around that place,
with other landmarks forming along the way; and so it be-
comes increasingly difficult and in fact pointless to fix its
outer limits.
There does seem, however, to be a center-a down-
town, as it were-to this sprawling Contact metropolis.
But that center isn't exactly on the map. It's more implied
than stated, suggested by the overlapping spheres of experi-
ence that the work produces. The activity of Contact itself
is remarkably straightforward and recognizable, but the ex-
perience of it is relative. An atmosphere of curiosity has
made the work a currency of exchange rather than a place
to go-awkward for those who had expected to someday
arrive somewhere. The way is the going itself; we remem-
ber what we are left with, but not always where we left
from.
One night at the Vermont Movement Workshop in
Putney a few summers ago, about 50 people gathered for
a discussion about Contact. No particular subject was
slated. So, we started from scratch and asked, What is this
thing called Contact Improvisation that people are so hot
to do?, What is it? Some people said it was about commu-
nication, a code of sorts; others said it was about sharing
weight, getting a rigorous workout, acquiring new skills,
and others said it was about developing sensitivity. And
the list went on. After the discussion, on our way back to
our rooms, a few of us were walking past a cabin where
some of the students were living and I heard through an
open window as we walked past, "They know what it is,
but they just won't tell. That Steve Paxton has passed on
a legacy of ambiguity that keeps everyone from saying
what it is, but they know." I laughed when I heard her,
but I've thought about what she said a lot since then. And
I think she's right. Not only did Steve offer a dance, but a
built-in way of keeping it alive. Thanks, Steve, for that one.
Nancy Stark Smith
April 25, 1982
IMPORTANT CQ MESSAGES
CONTACT QUARTERLY has always oper-
ated 'in the black'. Subscriptions, single issues,
back issues, distribution through individuals
and a few bookstores, and advertising (mini-
mal at the moment) support CQ's printing,
production supplies and distribution. This
does not however include payment of any
fees for editing, contributors, design, type-
setting, paste-up, clerical or bookkeeping.
Production for each issue takes between 4 to
6 weeks. Mailing for each issue takes about
a full week. The business of processing mail,
sending issues and information, keeping re-
cords go on all year. We have slowly come
to grips with the fact that this labor of love
is no simple pastime. As freelance artists,
all the people who have worked on CQ have
had to juggle schedules between performing,
teaching and touring. Last year we received
our first grants and were able to begin pay-
ing a business developer part-time, and what
amounts to a snack fund for the editors dur-
ing production. In March 1982, NEA award-
ed us an extra $1500. making a total of
$4000. from them for our 82-83 fiscal year.

Contact Improvisation views Round Up
CONT ACT IMPROVISATION may be described as spon-
taneous mutual investigation of the energy and inertia paths
created when two people engage actively-dancing freely, with
their sensitivity to guide and safeguard them. Parts of the body
may come to support weight which are unused to this, and sev-
eral weeks of conditioning are essential to strengthen and com-
municate to the muscles what new stresses they may expect.
We are working on the premise that the way to communi-
cate with the body is gentle direct active exercise daily, for a
suitable period prior to energetic workout. Certain part's of
the body require special attention in communication, limber-
ing, and strengthening: the neck, shoulders, thigh, pelvis, knee,
ankle. Disorientation can be avoided by careful training of
the peripheral vision, easy tumbling, and investigation of man-
euvers which tend to confuse.
Steve Paxton, 1972
Carol Harwood, Gurney Bolster

CQ/CI Sourcebook
SIX YEARS AGO
reprinted from Contact Newsletter No. 2
[C.Q. Vol I No 2] Stinson Beach, California,
1975.
I began a fall series of classes ("Contact Improvisa-
tion-The Art of Controlled Abandon") September 1st
[1975] at Cat's Paw Palace in Berkeley, meeting twice a
week for 21h hours in the evenings. Only now are things
really starting to click, ideas and relationships popping
into perspective. Contact Improvisation is an awareness,
internal, and of the immediate environment/situation,
but that's only half the story-rapport and communication
follow. Hence this letter and the newsletter it is contained
in. But there's more to it than just communication, with
only that it's boring. It's the giving behind it that catches
people's interest, the generosity that allows them to give
too. That's the form "give weight", "take weight";
sharing is it. We have been throwing back and forth in
the "core group" talk of people who wish to teach contact
before we feel they are ready, and what to do about the
situation. Obviously communication is the first answer
and this newsletter the first solution. But in the future,
to take a serious responsibility for the Form (Contact),
to see that it is passed along to others well, we need to be
more generous of ourselves, in terms of time and involve-
ment so that our students can·be fully immersed in contact
for a good solid period of time and realize the responsibil-
ity before being carried away with the desire to teach.
Possessiveness with the form only causes people to go off
and do it themselves or "teach" their friends.
I also feel the need for developing some kind of
cross-pollination between the major groups in this area
(Bay Area). Some students just can't get enough, so if
one person isn't teaching or not frequently enough we
can refer over to another class. We need to provide enough
"Contact" situations for people who are really hungry, to
be immersed in it for a good amount of time. I have final-
ly accepted the idea that the only way to be accountable
for the form is not to be possessive of it and try to control
it but to be generous and open, with the form, showing a
sense of unity between us core people, rather than having/
showing. A lot of this has come out of long discussions
with Nita [Little], and Nancy [Stark Smith] in the last
month. Also I have talked it over extensively with Mar-
garet Fish~r, and tonight I had a long talk with Byron
Brown who I invited over for dinner.
. Concerning my class it has been going well. I've
been learning at least as much as my students, and that's
a lot. The beginners were really beginners, and the form
has a lot of material to work with. The class over the last
two months has been 95% women and not as consistent
attendancewise as I would like. I feel that familiarity and
friendship/committment amongst the group is a very im-
portant factor. I guess that's the way it is here in the city.
The biggest challenge has been working with people who
aren't very aware to begin with of how they feel or how
a duet feels to them, the challenge is to get them to feel
something and to talk about it. For the most part sessions
are energizing and time goes very fast for all involved.
I look forward to more sharing and work and accept·
ance among the core contactors.
Sincerely Love,
Curt (Siddall)

Values
KERIAC: Someone at the seminar on politics in FOCUS
'78 in Berkeley, my first Contact Intensive, postulated
that perhaps Contact is what we white liberals need to
make ourselves whole: it balances us. Other cultures have
not deprived themselves of touch and the rest that Contact
provides, so they don't need it as much; they need other
things.
MARK: In what ways does Contact fit in with tbe values
and needs of the white counterculture?
K: Contact is concerned with intuition, aesthetics, physi·
cality, "being present in the flow." That's definitely
"counter" to the mainstream culture's emphasis on
rationality, intellect, status, and achievement.
I'm a third generation Contactor: I learned about
Contact not from the originators, but from their students.
I keep thinking of questions I'd like to ask the originators,
because I'd like to know if some of the political implica-
tions that I see in Contact are just my own projections, or
were the values of my teachers, or actually came from the
originators themselves and were consciously intended to
be a part of the work. For instance: why was this form
named Contact Improvisation and not Steve Paxton Tech·
nique? In the entire modern dance world, every new
approach except Contact is based on a cult of personality:
Graham technique, Cunningham technique, Hawkins
technique, etc. The Contact originators probably pro-
ceeded into this name very naturally, without even think·
ing about it. But to me, that name is revolutionary-it
reflects a whole new value system in dance.
M: What was this value system?
K: A notion of equality among people working together,
without stars. I understand that Paxton was a teacher at
Bennington at the time he began exploring Contact, and
that his co-workers were mostly students. And yet, if two
people try to do Contact while maintaining the typical
student-teacher relationship, I think those two wouldn't
end up doing much Contact at all. Both the name and the
form itself assume that Contact is a collective creation that
is bigger than individuals. There is a letting go of ego, and
a letting go of traditional role expectations, that is unpre-
cedented in dance.
Effort-Shape work is another approach to dance that is
not called by the originator's name. But Effort-Shape is
based on an amazingly thorough and rigorous analysis of
Volume 7 1981-82
doesn't take over the duet or solve what is basically your
partner's problem. Giving body surface to ease your part-
ner's fall, rather than grabbing your partner to "save him"
is a prime example. In a Contact duet, each partner takes
assertive actions, but each does so in a way that leaves
choices open to the partner. When you jump on a contact
dancer, she may roll out from under you; she may choose
not to take your weight in the way you predicted, and
that's part of the dance. Contact makes an important poli-
tical statement about relationships-about what is my re-
sponsibility, my territory, my decision making power; and
what is yours.

San Francisco, Summer 1979
movement by Rudolph Laban. Laban's contribution was
essentially intellectual, and the dancers who made use of
his theories contributed the physical application that is
now referred to as "Effort-Shape". Contact is essentially
a physical manifestation. Have you ever struggled to de-
fine Contact? Every time someone asks me "What is
Contact Improvisation?" my mind goes blank, even though
I have carefully read and studied many written definitions
of CI trying to prepare for this inevitable occasion. I in-
variably end up demonstrating physically. I think the best
definition of Contact is "that which happens when you do
Contact." We can extrapolate theories from Contact, but
what we have been given is a physical legacy.
l remember that Laban's theories had a similar impact
on my dance work as Contact has had. It was a joyful
release from the mind-set: "the correct way to study dance
is to memorize and duplicate the movements of a famous
dancer." This method does give you the advantage of
learning someone else's lifetime of bodily insights, but
whose body? Laban's work outlined the basic principles
of dance design much as the Bauhaus has outlined the
principles of visual and three-dimensional design for visual
artists. It gives the dancer a choice of the whole universe
of dance material, not just the iota that one famous person
has dedicated his or her life to. That way, each person can
choose the iota that best suits his or her own temperament,
which is likely to produce a much more deeply felt com-
mitment to their work. The result of freeing dancers from
the master-apprentice model will be a lot more diversitv
and creativity in dance. Contact also frees dance from 'the
master-apprentice model. A maxim I picked up from my
Contact teachers: "The dance (Contact) is the teacher."
M: In what other specific ways does Contact reflect a new
value system?
K: Well, take the notion of taking responsibility for your-
self, which is a big lesson to be learned in Contact. In
Transactional Analysis there is a concept called "the rescue
triangle", containing the roles of the victim, the prosecutor
and the rescuer. According to this schema, when a person
tries to "help" someone else by "taking care of her'', they
create a victim who will later resent and persecute them
for it. The way to avoid this dynamic is by not doing
more than 50 percent of the work. To do more is "taking
over" someone else's problem, and laying a subtle power
tnp. In a Contact duet, you only do 50 percent of the
work: you 're simply responsible for yourself. You are
empathetic with your partner, in some situations you even
extend help to your partner, but you do so in a way that

Folk Art & State Art
K: I have a pet theory I'd like to throw out, based on
Curt Sach's book World History of the Dance: Art can
be divided into "Folk Art" and "State Art". When ballet
began, it went like this: all through Europe there was folk
dancing; each village had its own dance. Dance had a real
function in the ordinary lives of ordinary people; it was
for weddings, funerals, religious festivals. It was for mark-
ing passages in individual lives and for coalescing a com-
munity spirit. It was inherently participatory. Political
power in Europe was scattered in fiefdoms among many
nobles. Then one of those nobles became more powerful
than his neighbors, took over the lands around him and
became king of that land. The King brought the disenfran-
chised nobles to his court, and absorbed their power. At
court, the nobles didn't have anything useful to do; society
life took over-dances, parties, social events. For enter-
tainment, peasants were brought in from the country to
demonstrate their dances. The nobles watched, the peas-
ants danced. The nobles applauded the highest jumps, the
fanciest turns, the flashiest costumes. Technique and
showmanship became the content of the dance; Dance
became a performance art.
When the dancing was over, the peasants were given
a good meal and sent back to resume their work on the
land. One or two peasants, the ones who had been the
most amusing to the audience, were asked to remain at
court. These few spent their days refining their technique,
giving performances and teaching dance (it became
fashionable for nobles to learn the peasant dances.) The
art of the King's dancers gave the King power over others,
in the same way that the King's stable of fine horses added
to his status and power over others. As long as their ballet
continued to awe the audience, the dancers did not work
at crafts or till the soil; they ate at the King's table. They
were professional dancers.
I have told this story as though "State Art" only arose
in Europe, but I believe it has happened all over the world.
"State Art" is characterized by an emphasis on technique
which is mastered by a dedicated few, who then are
watched by an audience. It is inherently elitist-a few
talented people are given the resources necessary to sup-
port their artistic growth on a full-time basis; their achieve-
ments are then used to enhance and increase the status
and power of the aristocratic patron who has the wealth
to pay the bill. It is characterized by competition between
artists and between patrons, by separation of performer
from audience, by its emphasis on style rather than spirit.
"Folk Art" is characterized by its accessibility to all
people-the steps are simple, the spirit in which you do it
is more important than virtuosity. It is non-professional-
you kick your heels in the village festival, and then go
CQICI Sourcebook 81
back to your survival support~ng work t?e next day. It is
participatory-it's your weddmg, your life happenmg
through the dance. You do not dance because you have
to, you do not dance for someone else's pleasure, accord-
ing to someone else's standards. You dance by your own
choice, for your own satisfaction and because it co~nects
you with others. There is no applause, the reward ts the
dancing itself.
Contact as Folk Art
K: I see Contact as a folk art, with folk art values inher-
ent in the work. Its basis is kinesthetic, rather than visual.
The dance unfolds from giving primary attention to the
bodily sensations of the dancer rather th:in from atte~ding
to how it will look to the vi~wer. I~ ts a cooperative,
connecting-to-others form. While dancmg Cont~c~, y_ou
exist in the present, from moment to moment-it ts liter-
ally your life in the act of living. The context chosen for
Contact activities at its onset reflected folk art values:
no stage lights, no costumes, no proscenium stage, "per-
formers" would grunt, groan, talk casually to each other,
talk to the audience, quit dancing abruptly. Conta.ct
"performances" frequently seen:ed like. a commumty
gathering in witness of an event m the lives of the perform-
ers rather than an entertainment for the audience. The
weh-established phenomenon of the Contact Jam is unique
among modern dance activities: the jam is n~t a class to
learn better technique, not a performance to impress . .
others, it is dancing for its own sake, and a way to socialize
with your community.
M: That may be the way Contact was performed and
practiced at the beg~nning, bu_t th~t doesn't seem too
descriptive of what is happening right now. [Note date
of interview-Eds.]
K: True. Contact performance groups are showing a trend
toward incorporating "State Art" values into the work.
Take Mangrove as an example. They s~ill perform. most of
the time with simple lights, no proscemum stage, mformal
atmosphere. These are all anti-elitist elements. H~wever,
Mangrove has steadily increased the!r use of the~tncal ele-
ments blended into the contact, which makes this essen-
tially participatory form more "performance. orie~ted".
Now they are blending choreographed material wit~ con-
tact as well, and also including modern dance techmque
and "theater courses as part of their workshop classes.
M: What political implications does this have?
K: Bringing folk art values int~ a perform~nce situation
and making it work is a revolut10nary act, m my book. It
not only re-defines what is possible to be performed, but
also fundamentally alters our perceptions of interpersonal
and power relationships. Are we (as audie~ce member~)
stand-ins for our aristocratic predecessors, mtent on bemg
entertained; are we stand-ins for our peasant predecessors,
witnessing a community ritual; o~ is this new perfo~mance
mode actually building a new societal structure which does
not presume class division?

Traditions
M: There are now individuals and groups doing Contact
who not only perform for money but who are supported
by grants.
K: Right. They're doing a form which is anathema to
"State Art" values, yet they're plugging in to state sup-
port. Revolutions are not necessarily what will earn a
living. My intuition is that Mangrove and _other grou~s
like them will have to make some concess10ns to tradi-
tional values in order to survive professionally. If you
try to make your living as a performing group, there is
pressure to fit into the traditional _mold. The pressure.
does not only come from the outside, but al.so f!o~ with-
in. As performers, we naturally w~nt to ~amtam ht~h
standards and communicate effectively wtth our audience.
Our internalized sense of what is "good quality perform-
ance" is largely based on the previous model availabl~ to
us-"State Art". This model has also shaped the audi-
ence's expectations to such an extent th~t the public may
not be able to perceive your work at all if you do not
conform to tl}e assumptions and values expected.
M: It seems as if there is a set of considerations, in tradi-
tional art, that people think validate their dance, such as
working with a teacher, preferably a well-~nown one, or
an established choreographer, or an established ~ompany.
Also: working on specifically choreographed pieces.
Doing performances that resemble traditional ones. Going
on tour. Doing publicity. Applying for grants.
K: All those things validate your dance. But what di:> they
validate? Your technical competence, not your creative
skill. My technique was always lousy, and I w~s more in-
terested in creative work. I wanted to create first, then
develop my technique to get under it. But th_e opposite is
the expected way. First you learn how to pomt your toe,
Volume 7 1981-82
and after years and years of practice, when you are really
good at pointing your toe, you might be given the respon-
sibility of determining which direction you point your toe.
That really limits your creativity, because after all that
time of learning someone else's choreography, you develop
habits and blind spots of what your own capabilities are.
This split between technique and creativity is vety unfor-
tunate; and one of the marvelous things about Contact is
that the technique and creativity happen simultaneously.
M: Do you agree that Contact is esthetically revolutionary,
that it was a conscious attempt to subvert traditional
dance esthetics?
K: That's definitely what it seems like to me. But I wasn't
at Judson Church in New York in the sixties, when Steve
Paxton and others were turning a lot of performance as-
sumptions upside down, so I don't know for sure if it was
conscious or not.
The abstraction which became prevalent in modern
dance in the 60's and which still dominates a lot of today's
dance was a reaction against the intense emotionalism of
Martha Graham-type dance. Choreographers went to the
opposite pole, using depersonalized bodies moving through
space, dealing with visual design without concern for psy-
chological ramifications.
M: So Contact is a reaction against abstraction?
K: Yes. It's a form where dancers have become human be-
ings again. And these "human" dancers are, at least on a
physical level, connecting and' relating to each other. Con-
tact dancers wear their own faces, not the neutral facial
expression or "mask" of the modern dancer.
M: So Contact bas neither the abstraction and alienation,
nor the romantic theatricality.
K: Right. The difference between Graham "human-ness"
and Contact "human-ness" is largely one of scale. Graham
Volume 7 1981-82
goes deeper into herself, delving into her inner psyche, and
then she projects further out to the audience, the way an
actress would, to the back of the opera house. Contact
dancers project on a person-to-person scale. Your facial
expression isn't intended to go any further than you and
your partner, and the concerns expressed are usually direct-
ly related to the physical interaction happening in that
moment.
M: It's more intimate in the sense that people are touching,
and also it's simply on a more intimate scale.
K: Real events happening between real people in real time.
That's why the audience sits so close, because otherwise
you miss half the action.
M: Isadora Duncan swept away so much of the rigidified
tradition in dance in her time, and ''Modern Dance" was
born. Now ''Modern Dance" seems traditional. The con-
temporary parallel to Duncan's impact is the Judson Church
group. Contact is just one of the offshoots of the Judson
group. In so many ways-in Deborah Hay's work, Simone
Forti, Anna Halprin, as well as Steve Paxton-they're still
making an incredible revolution. Those people and their
students will continue to revolutionize dance.
K: Contact can also rigidify, given time. We need to keep
alive a dialogue on these concerns, and foster the conscious-
ness raising of those of us who have been given this gift.
Attitudes
K: I was really hesitant about returning to teaching.
The traditional teacher-student model is inherently
corruptive of what I consider to be healthy power
relationships. The fact that one of my early exper-
iences of learning contact (the Focus Intensive in 1978)
was in an atmosphere of community was really important
to me. Focus was taught by a diverse group of people,
none of whom claimed to have all the answers. The teach-
ers talked openly of their own weaknesses and inadequa-
cies; they encouraged the students to teach one another;
they asked for criticism and input from the students; and
they scheduled community feedback sessions into the
workshop structure. There was a two-way dialogue be-
tween the two groups of people who were experiencing
different but interdependent parts of the same experience.
Students had power and were expected to take that power.
To me, it was set up with the same values as a contact duet.
The Focus model of teacher-student relationships made me
feel that I could be a teacher, and still be "politically
correct".
M: It seems as if Contact is a form where that community
can happen readily because of its anti-elitist aspects. On
the other band, the form doesn't necessitate a communi-
tarian atmosphere, it can be elitist as any other form. Like
you said, the inventors of contact could have just as easily
infused the form with traditional values. Fortunately they
didn't, so now Contact does contain certain progressive
values, such as sexual equality, which are now inherent in
it. Yet it still retains elitism in some areas: bead trips
(whether warranted or not), classes where the instructor
and student roles are cleariy defined and separate, and in
some per[ ormances. ~
CQ/CI Sourcebook
K: Contact began with the progressive, anti-elitist values
of its inventors. But third and fourth generation contacters,
who may have little or no contact with the originators, now
outnumber the originators as teachers of the form. It's
quite possible to teach someone "Contact" in the physical
dimension only, without any conscious reference to values,
or even in relation to values that conflict with the form.
To me, it's important for teachers of contact to cultivate
in themselves and in their students a heightened awareness
of the political and spiritual richness of this form of dance.
We can support all forms of contact activities, not just
those which seem to be "making it to the big time." The
variety of ways in which Contact can become manifest is
endless. The Bay Area Contact Network is a prime force
which makes visible the variety of ways in which contact
activities happen in our area. Before the establishment of
this organization, Mangrove's vigorous and stimulating
growth as a performing company was so much more atten-
tion-getting than the other teachers, groups, and loose
gatherings in our community, that we were in danger of
being obliterated by their strength. Contact in the Bay
Area was becoming synonomous with Mangrove. Hope-
fully, by networking, contacters can "count~rb~lance"
each 0th.er so that each part of our community 1s recog-
nized for its unique contribution, and our strengths, al-
though not always equal, will still support each ot~er.
I think we in the Contact community have a unique
opportunity to foster the revolutionary model th~t was
generated by the Contact originators. We_ c_an validate the
activities that do not conform to the trad1t10nal expecta-
tions, because our own experience in Cont~ct has given us
intimations of another type of "good quality performance".
Instead of looking for the harmony of visual design caused
by the parallel placement of arms and legs, we can perceive
the harmony of flow that results from the dancer's aban-
donment to the energy pathways of the movement. We
can appreciate the skills of support rather_ than the skills
of comparative athleticism. We can pe~ce1ve performers .
as real people in real time, making ch01ces that have physi-
cal results neither good nor bad, rather than applaudmg
"stars" whose image would be ruined by a "mistake". ~

Contact Journal
26th July, 1981
Precipitation! out of breath! late! . ... To watch! A
forest of bodies is standing up, quiet, motionless. Some
others evolve between themselves. I am watching. Feel
like being there. To calm; To not be there and to be there
through the others. Which sensation? What is your colour?
Are you here? presence-dilation-absence-concentra-
tion-contraction-aperture-releasing-the forest vibrates
with thousand little lifes! And everything becomes ani-
mated again: mobility! (motion) Me: little tree trunk
left on the bank, I decide to put me in the fiux (fiow).
Sometimes I meet a smile, eyes to answer mine. Why to
be so afraid of perceiving in only one glimpse! Later,
bodies won't be able to cheat anymore: We'll pave to give
all our weight, to receive all the weight of Other. Feeling
the body spreading, spilling on the ground. To Forget
Bones-ground also is able to not be fiat anymore-diverse
bodies, diverse persons, diverse sensations-acceptance
and sharing. Refusal and rejection-insecurity and frustra-
tion. Question!? Trust and pleasure-Answers?!
Beyond the "look", there's someone living, trying
let's make the experiment of it. He came to me, behind
me: so soft, moving is a real authenticity. ls the body
able to lie? Not be afraid of void anymore. Dancing!
And Nancy who says things we do! Happy moments
when we don't do what Nancy says. When we discover
that little treasure in ourselves. She gives us the way . ..
How excited it is to discover that we too . .. I use Nancy's
experiment, bring it closer with mine: Newness. To
progress towards oneself-life-
Nancy is very beautiful. I thought she was someone
else because I misunderstood what was written under a
picture of someone. A long braid Yau-chu-chu style (who
I loved so much) undulating like her spine, prolonging it,
its double (duplicate?) . ... The vibrating and propelling
tail of the little squirrel-Tsshi! Tssschi! Her voice is deep,
a little bit detached it seems to me: I don't know yet. A
beautiful face, something indian, a gingerbread complex-
ion and jade eyes, glittering like a crystal. And because I
like I identify myself .. . She is not very tall neither,
slightly more than me, she is not very big neither, slightly
less than me and then that magnificent energy! Do we
look alike all of us?
Catherine Meyer
CQ/C1 Sourcebook
"CHUTE" is a videotape edited in Aug-
ust 7979 at the Western Front in Van-
couver, B.C. during Current Exchange-
a gathering of people to exchange ex-
periences related to the practice of Con-
tact Improvisation. Ten minutes of vi-
deo material was chosen from about 15
hours of dancing taped by Steve Chris-
tiansen during the first Contact lmprov~
isation concert organized by Steve Pax-
ton in N. Y.C. at the john Weber Gal-
lery in June, 1972.
This material had sat for 7 years be-
fore an attempt was made to organize it.
The result is a document of a point of
view about movement that evolved dur-
ing those years of practice and observa-
tion. In 1979, the 1972 dancing looked
wild and woolly, raw energy meeting it-
self head-on, with automatic reflexes
getting a thorough workout.
It seemed apt to tune our search
during logging to images of falling. Con-
sidering the perception that even a walk :
is a series of falls, we found that our def-
inition was so inclusive that logging was
unwieldy, and classification became in-
evitable: sloughed falls, horizontal falls,
a_ccidents, falling up, falling down, con-
trolled falls, freefalls, 'splats', et al.
After Steve finalized the script and·
audio-recorded it, he, Christiansen and
I edited the video to complement the
audio which appears here.

Mary Fulkerson, Mark Petersen
Volume 7 1981-82
by Steve Paxton
I am examining the passage from up to down.
I look at the range of the passage, from the small
dance of standing, to the linked falls of walking, to swoops
and dives from high points on other bodies.
With experience, the act of falling has come to
include an ability to adapt my body to the variables of
distance, position, and direction and to intuit which part
of my body will take the first moment of impact.
The first part of my body that touches the ground
I can use as a lever. By extending to that part, I can unify
limbs and torso to prepare a sequence through which to
smoothly transmit the energy of the fall, and my weight,
into the floor. Within the brief freedom of the fall, my
body can convert a sudden accident into a controlled
descent.
The result of so many changes in spatial and kines-
thetic orientation in a short time has caused me to perceive
space as spherical. The sphere is an accumulated image
gathered from several senses-vision being one. As if quick-
ly looking in all directions gives me an image of what it
might be like to have a visual surface all over my body,
instead of skin.
The skin is the best source for the image, because it
works in all directions at once. If we could turn the skin
off, we would appreciate it much more. But the skin works
most of the time on automatic pilot. The conscious mind
is alerted if unusual stimulation appears on the surface of
the body, but, I don't notice the touch of my clothes or
my weight in a chair most of the time.
In Contact Improvisation, however, I find I am hang-
ing by my skin. And relying on its information to protect
me, to warn me, to feed back to me the data to which I am
responding.
We are looking at material from 1972, training for
the first performance of Contact Improvisation, at the
John Weber Gallery. It was a hot New York City day, but
everyone seemed game.
What can be learned by catching a hurtling body?
The importance of timing. That there is such a thing as a
correct preparation. That understanding the technique
with the mind is different than understanding with the
body. That momentum can be extended and used once
the initial impact is absorbed or deflected. Or that the
seemingly impossible juxtaposition of a small woman
catching a large man has possibilities-most of them lead-
ing to the floor. But that is reasonable. Trying to stop
or hold too much weight is dangerous. The idea is to dis-
cover what is simplest to do.
In a state of trust of the body and the earth, we
believed we could learn to handle the forces involved in
physical interactions between two people who permit
each other the freedom to improvise.
Volume 7 1981-82
The first instant of any fall is crucial. At that point
a roll may be prepared. If the preparation isn't made at
the first moment, falling takes place without guidance
from the faller.
What can a body do to be safe? Commitment to
something which isn't happening is a barrier between me
and my body's circumstance.
Memory of past judgements tells me that prejudging
is not secure. And memory cannot function consciously
because at these speeds I can't think my way to safety.
If thinking is too slow, is an open state of mind use-
ful? Seems to be.
I remember deciding to learn falling skills, which
have become muscle memories. I figured my chances of
survival were greater with these skills than without-an
assessment of things to come. In this way, prejudgement
and memory do play a part in what is happening now.
Although adults and children fall at the same veloc-
ity, adults are more liable to injure themselves because
their mass is greater in proportion to structural strength.
It is possible to develop responses to deal with this safely.
Slides, rolls or full surface landings, which spread the im-
pact of landing over as much of the muscle area as possible,
are useful in Contact Improvisation as they are in the
martial arts of Japan.
I am concerned with the possibilities of guiding the
momentum of the fall away from collision with the floor
or partner. A body could endure for decades. I don't
want to injure joints. On the other hand, shouldn't focus
on doom. Can't exclude fear.
Efficient falls tend to turn into slides or rolls, trans-
forming vertical momentum into horizontal travel.
I may find a last minute leverage from my partner
to enable me to land upon my feet. My fall might be
guided so it spirals around the body of my partner. Thus,
a fall starting on the downward path can be guided into a
circle orbiting my partner, and finally, its momentum used
to aid in regaining a high perch.
Contractive energy or tensions overpower the sensing
of subtle movement, and so gravity is masked.
Relaxed is almost right; but, flaccid is wrong.
In exploring the small dance of skeletal alignment
while standing, I am sensing subtle falls of parts of the
skeleton.
One thing is clear. I have little memory, muscular
or mental, of what I've danced. The specific movements
my body executes when I improvise do not register con-
sciously, and I can't reconstitute them. I feel transparent
in the action. Causing it only a little, and holding no
residuals. ~
CQ/CI Sourcebook
by Randy Warshaw
As the sixties gave birth to new ideas, the techniques
of the seventies evolved to provide tools for applying and
teaching those discoveries. When Steve Paxton invited that
first group of people to perform contact improvisation in
1972, he had no technique for showing them what they
would be doing, or how they would do it safely; but he did
have a group of gifted athletes, people with strong physical
backgrounds, that could do potentially dangerous move-
ment with minimal risk of injury. Starting with that solid
physical base, they developed a movement form that was
at once both spectacular and imaginative.
As contact began to be taught, physical surity could
not be assumed. Over the last decade, a comprehensive
technique has evolved whereby students can very gently be
introduced to the range of physical states encountered in
the dancing. Moments from the dancing can be extracted
and introduced to students in a controlled and structured
setting. Because of this, it has been possible to teach
88 CQ/CI Sourcebook
people of greatly varying physical abilities complex physi-
cal maneuvers. The capacity to teach skills and develop
physical range and confidence has made contact improvisa-
tion highly accessible and attractive. It's provided the nec-
essary and important function of laying a solid foundation
for full and open improvising.
In contact improvisation, the senses need to be edu-
cated. Disorientation must become an acceptable sensation.
A released body, supported in length, can be developed.
Trust, patiently nurtured through confidence and familiar-
ity, prepares the way for diversity. Through trust, awk-
ward, uncomfortable moments of vulnerability and power-
ful moments of virtuosity become equally rich.
When I began teaching contact, I wanted to pass on
to others the simplicity and accessibility of the form. As
teaching material, I chose those skills which I had been
taught and felt confident and competent in. I found it
possible to teach others to do them also. I got a lot of sat-
isfaction in seeing my students develop abilities to soften,
to roll, to give and take weight in diverse situations. But
after several years of teaching this way, I began to feel
limited. I felt that in teaching skills I was operating within
a very finite field of experience. Though I was approaching
what I considered to be the heart of the dancing, I wasn't
quite there. Who was it that said there were 87 million
permutations in contact? Well, I felt up against those per-
mutations. It might take a very long time to find and
teach all those permutations, but all I would be teaching
was permutations. It wasn't quite the dancing. "'
VoZ.ume7 1981-82
It became burdensome to stay at least one step ahead
of my students, each week coming up with new things to
do. They were all learning lots of physical possibilities,
types of physical moments, but.week to week I felt weight-
ed by the task of finding new combinations for them to
experience, weighted by the dependency that kind of
teaching fostered .
. Developing skills is a necessary part of teaching and
le~rnmg contact. But there's a danger in making these
skills overly precious. It's important to remember that
skills are meant to introduce types of experiences that may
be encountered in the dancing. What is found in that ex-
perience is the important thing. It's important that stu-
dents notice their growing acceptance of disorientation,
their a~areness of the body's ability to organize and take
care of Itself, ~he trust and confidence they gain in them-
selves and their partners to stay with a moment, and meet
the needs of each changing moment. Too often, though,
stud~nts focus exclusively on mastery of maneuvers, chal-
lengmg themselves with more and more complex maneu-
vers as ~heir confidence increases. Mastery of maneuvers
can be important in gaining confidence, but I think it's
more important to take that confidence into the dancing
than to take the maneuvers. It's the orientation towards
a higher and higher refinement that runs the risk of dis-
tracting students from the opportunities for a more per-
s?nal exchange with their partner. The subtleties of any
given moment can so easily be buried beneath the execu-
tion of that next move.
When people like Steve Paxton, Lisa Nelson, Daniel
Lepk?ff, and Nancy Stark Smith were performing and
teachmg regularly, there seemed to consistently be new,
good dancers emerging through contact. A couple of years
ago a lot of g~od teaching and performing of contact drop-
ped off (here m North America). Perhaps this was due to
the fact that many of the early pioneers of contact had
fo_und a fullness and maturity in their dancing that they
wished to apply to other areas of interest. It seems natural
to_ me t~at eight or so years of attention to any one thing
will begm to call forth derivatives. Maybe, as contact had
established itself fairly solidly at that point, a new freedom
existed wherein they could let go of its nurturing. Also,
as contact was just beginning to catch on in Europe, there
was the need for teaching and performing there.
Volume 7 1981-82
In the absence of these dancers, I thought two things
happened. First, many people who had been doing contact
for awhile mistook these people's change for a change in
contact improvisation. A feeling began circulating that it
was time to "do other things with contact." I remember
the phrase "Contact is pead" jokingly tossed about, cou-
pled with references to the "Contact Obituary."
The second, and more important, is that contact con-
tinued to be taught, mostly to newcomers, but with the
absence of high caliber dancing to be seen. It's occurred to
~e th~t though the teaching at that time was sophisticated,
It was imbalanced, and the balancing element has never
been introduced. I know that, for myself, once I had ac-
quired a certain degree of physical competence through
classes, my real development in contact came through
watching and dancing with those high caliber dancers.
Through them I learned to improvise. Improvising was
something I never really learned to do through classes.
. This poin~s to a whole area that has been neglected
m the the teachmg of contact. Though certain aspects of
the work can be easily articulated, the relationship of a
dancer to a physical moment is harder to formulate. I can
explain my weight settling into a fold of my partner's
body, but I cannot explain the sensitivity or tenderness I
may feel. whic? make~ that moment alive for me. The pres-
ent challenge 1s to articulate that personal involvement
which makes for a dynamic relationship with a physical
event. Any moment can be more than just following the
contact point. The person in the dancer can be engaged;
persona! richness. can inhabit the dancing. This challenge
1s especially pertment today. Up until now, we have relied
on the accessibility of inspiring improvisors to watch and
have taken a sense of freshness and originality from them
back to our dancing. We have been able to dance with true
artists. They have guided or thrown us into the unpredict-
able. Today's need is to talk about how one keeps the
dancing fresh and original; how one perceives one's relation-
ship to the changing physical material. Can we understand
and share what "improvising" means? Can we teach it?
This winter, I began teaching an advanced contact
class with the hope that this was possible. My notes for
the first class read, "Focusing on improvisation-moving
from the general, perhaps we will make our way towards
CQICI Sourcebook
specifics. By making certain types of physical sensations
familiar to us through skills and exercises, we've learned to
be comfortable with, or at least accepting of, being disori-
ented. At a certain point, known physical pathways were
realized to be limited and repetitive, and the quest for new
pathways had become overbearingly goal-oriented, dry and
lacking in improvisational unpredictability and richness.
I'm interested in the range of states, qualities, and types of
moments that occur and are possible in a dance with a par-
ticular partner. How can our imagery and qualities slide
through spaces and settle into nooks and crannies in the
ways we have become familiar with on a physical level, and
bow can this more active state direct our dancing or parti-
cipate in our dancing?"
One idea I had was that it takes an internally active
state to look for and become aware of the range of qualities
possible in a dance. It's the activity of identifying, person-
ally, with moment to moment occurrences in the dancing;
allowing oneself to make a personal connection with a
physical event, so that moments in the dancing are occu-
pied, filled, lived-but not latched onto and played out-
just stayed with for their duration in the same way that a
physical pathway is followed for the length of its phrase.
The first difficulty I had in getting to this material
was that pursuing and moving thr_ough known movement
pathways had become so automatic there was little room
left for anything unknown to occur. Mentally, students
seemed so bent on finding things to do that they had diffi-
culty allowing their minds to receive impressions. Moments
in the dancing had no time or space to linger with the im-
agination. The dancing may have been cunning, but it was
not imaginative.
One of the first things we began to do was to individ-
ually inhibit a way of dancing that in class was referred to
as "blabbing your body." In this type of dancing, there is
always a space of time between seeing a thing to do, decid-
ing to do it, and doing it. We began to work with the idea
of eliminating that next, known movement pathways as an
option; we could do anything but that thing we already
knew we would do. As long as there was time in which we
could decide to do something, there was also time to decide
not to do that something.
We decided that as long as we had time, why not take
it. An attitude st;emed to exist that in that time between
one event and another, nothing was happening. The possi-
bility of lingering was "re"-introduced. Most contactors
are familiar with the "finger dance," where two people,
facing each other, extend a single finger each out into the
space between them; letting their fingers meet, they feed a
little pressure into that area of contact. They notice the
small, shared movement at their fingertips and as they allow
themselves to be willing and open, they follow and stay
with that small movement as it wanders through the space
between their bodies. So long as both are willing to stay
with whatever that wandering is, they will find natural
pauses occurring, moments bursting with possibility, as
they stay with their commitment to a common decision
that is larger than either one of them.
These moments seem among the most exciting to me
because they are completely unknown. We might stay here
forever. We could end this dance here. Anything might
happen so long as both partners are willing.
Moments of lingering had all but disappeared from
the dances l watched that first class. By re-introducing it
as a possibility, and inhibiting old, automatic responses,
space was created in the dancing for fresh things to happen_
We began to hone in on these moments, looking first at our
90 CQ/CI Smtrcebook
physical activity. In those moments we noticed types of
possibilities that we realized were there for every moment.
They were ongoing possibilities. In that space, we realized
a lot was already happening. By looking at what was im-
mediately available, we could always find something that
had already begun.
As an example, we noticed the sensation of pressure
where our bodies were in contact, and realized if we needed
something to do we could work with that presstire. Some
of the more interesting dances I'd seen or had in awhile
occurred while working with this idea. Anytime a person
felt they needed to do something, they could change the
degree or quality of pressure. All our technical skills were
still there, although we weren't "playing them." Instead,
we were just playing, and those skills were there to support
our play. -
Another thing we worked with was the possibility of
being involved in any given moment solely for the sake of
our partner. Just because a space opened for me did not
necessarily mean one also opened for my partner. He or
she may be very involved in something, and by looking and
seeing what I could do for them, I might support their fol-
lowing their involvement further than they might other-
wise.
Perhaps the main territory I've become interested in
exploring as teaching material is the imagination. I'd like
to be able to talk about the kinds of things I do that let me
be involved in different moments of the dancing. I'd like
to be able to talk about those moment to moment images
that flash through my mind-personal identifications that
correlate with physical moments in the dancing=--and talk
about how my imagination operates when I improvise. For
me, improvising is the imagination spontaneously relating
with form. When I am improvising, I feel that I am being
brought into sharper focus and find that more of myself is
engaged and expressing that engagement than in any other
activiry.
And it's for this reason I love contact improvisation.
It offers me the possibility of feeling alive in a way that is
both amplified and focused. It's that sense of being alive
that I saw and felt in dancing with those high caliber danc-
ers. A lot of other dancing seemed focused and amplified,
but it did not necessarily show the artistry or richness of
the dancers or the dancing. I think artistry and richness
spring from the imagination, a person's response to or
empathy for a physical event. I'm interested in how
these images and emotions that occur as personal identi-
fications within the dancing, cycle the person back into
the movement. ~
