Editor Note
DEALING WITH THE HEAT
This is about friction, about two things rubbing up
against each other, and the value of that heat. I'm the kind
of person who tends to harmonize opposing forces. When
faced with a conflict, I'm always eager to jump to a perspec-
tive that encompasses both sides, one in which they are no
longer at odds-a view that renders each side valid and useful
and yet, as a result, often impersonal, removed from the
initial charge that formed it. But I'm beginning to see what
I've been missing.
There's something to be said for two things rubbing each
other the wrong way. The friction between them wears
each side down to its undercoat, its framework, thus testing
its strength and revealing its ground. True, the way the
friction is used and why are important factors. Sometimes
when sharpening an edge, it is possible to cut off completely
what you're just trying to hone down. But that's what
practice is for. In Push Hands, a Tai Chi C::huan sparring
form, for instance, one is looking through the touch, arm to
arm, for the resistance to the push. The partner tries to
'empry' wherever the other pushes, to offer no resistance,
thus hoping to draw the pusher off balance. The pusher
perseveres with a delicate but relentless touch, to search
through the other's inner space for their resistance, trying
to force the question. Having finally backed the partner into
a corner, so to speak, inside their own body, the pusher has
only to push lightly (no more than 8 oz. of pressure) to send
the partner flying backward against the wall. They resume
starting positions eagerly-facing each other, body centered
in a low, grounded stance, forearm to forearm-ready for
another go at it. Like hide and seek, it's only really fun to
play if someone's out to get you. And the more heated and
skillful their search, the better the practice.
Volume 9 1983-84
The Studies Project discussion between Bill T. ] ones and
Steve Paxton, printed in this issue of CQ. is a juicy and per-
sonal play, full of edges, pushes, falls and fakes. Several
times during the talk, I felt myself wincing at the action as
one might while watching a boxing match when the swing
connects. I noticed myself subtly backing off from their
verbal sparring when it seemed to be getting the most heated.
But luckily, I couldn't change the channel. What I saw light
up in the heat of that friction was each man as an individual;
his unique perspective came into focus as it was forced to
narrow from a more general field of vision to a distinct point
of view, teased and poked and pushed into the light. And
though I squirmed in the heat of that confrontation, I was
at the same time struck by the commitment behind the
stand.
I've learned a lot from doing Contact Improvisation about
coordinating with the forces-that-be: Accepting graviry, fall-
ing, following momentum, blending with a partner's move-
ments-i.e., 'going with the flow.' But lately, I've been
feeling feisry. I'm a little too comfortable now with the
dizzy of flipping horizons, torquing pathways and unexpect-
ed chutes, and more and more I find myself playing ag!linst
the forces-making myself heavy instead of light when a lift
starts, adding a splash to the easy pouring of weight, insisting
instead of yielding, adding fierce to gentle, no to yes. It's a
start. I've been in the harmony business a long time now.
It might take a while to really get the hang of holding my
ground instead of so gladly letting it slip out from under me.
As much as I love running around, I think I'm going to try
running into things more often, or at least up against them.
Nancy Stark Smith
CQ!CI Sourcebook 91
MAKING CONTACT
I promised my waiter at this diner that I wouldn't be
writing about the restaurant, but I was wrong. There's
something in the way the waitress calls out, "One tossed,
one string bean, one turnip, two setups," that gets me.
She isn't just saying it, she's transmitting it, imprinting it ·
definitely and temporarily on the minds of the men behind
the counter who are already in motion sliding the three
bowls and two setups down the formica in a flash. I look
at her eyes as she telegraphs these orders. They are weary
but come instantly to life and focus once she begins the
order. Her intention, voice and message are joined in a
single certain direction. They aim to get across. And re-
markably, in this fast-paced short-order full-dinner diner,
the meals get served up right.
Obscurely, but surely, this rubs up against the issue at
hand. Contact. Intention. Message. Moving. The Quart-
erly. As a vehicle for moving ideas, what is Contact
Quarterly moving, and to/for whom? Not long ago, it was
common for people to say that they didn't read CQ be-
cause they didn't do Contact. Now, after years of extend-
ing the scope of our 'what', 'to whom', and 'how', we seem
to have nearly thrown the baby out with the bath water ...
Having breakfast the other day with a long-time practi-
tioner, teacher and performer of Contact Improvisation, it
occurs to me that he probably hasn't seen the new issue of
CQ and I eagerly reach down for the one in my bag. But
he's squirming uncomfortably and I hesitate, and look up.
"You know I really don't read the Quarterly anymore,"
he says, "Maybe I should tell you why." "Please," I say,
slipping my hand out of my bag and onto my lap. In
short, he says that as the mouthpiece for Contact Improvi-
sation, CQ isn't getting to the substance of what people
92 CQ/CI Sourcelx>ok
Editor
Note
are doing, but only printing superficial, undeveloped re-
ports. Sympathetic and amused to see the magazine in this
strangely inverted position, I agree with him (almost) com-
pletely, and ask him why he hasn't written about the great
workshop he's just spent the last hour telling me about.
It is true that people are busy doing, perhaps too busy
to be telling of it. And perhaps it's also true that their
tales, were they to be told, might no longer fit comforta-
bly-in form or content-into the format of the CQ News-
letter, now slated for Contact exchanges. More import-
antly, is there really a call for this kind of exchange?
Judging not only from my recent breakfast talk, but from
the number of charged conversations that found their way
into and around the busy schedule at St. Mark's Church
during the two week Contact At 10th and 2nd festival
in NY last June, it is clear that the appetite is there to talk
and hear about how people are working in and from Con-
tact. The real hands-on stuff. As is said, It furthers one
to have somewhere to go.' So, as of this issue, we've
opened a new column, Still Moving. to make room for
that rich flow of ideas to run through.
But let it be said that Contact Quarterly is not the final
word on nor the official mouthpiece for Contact Improv-
isation. If anything, we're simply a mouth, changing shape
to fit the words that you put in it. So go ahead, put words
in our mouth. But, like the waitress in the diner, it's not
the words themselves that will give the message life; only
the desire to communicate can finally bring them across.
Nancy Stark Smith

STILL
by Steve Paxton
Cynthia Hedstrom is leaving
Danspace (at St. Mark's Church, NYC).
She has directed its use for three years
and created a center in which the dance
culture of Lower Manhattan moved
and thrived. The space is one of the
desirable venues for dances born in
loft studios. The proportions are larger
than the usual studio, but intimate
enough to convey the sort of non-the-
atrical detail frequently laced through
new dance work. The floor is hard and
fast. The audience sits on carpeted
steps under a balcony on three sides of
the room. The upper area serves for
lights and video/photo documentation.
High above the floor, the ceiling vault
is a great curve which caps the air
lightly and elegantly.
94 CQ/CI Sourcebook Susan Milani and Alan Gardner
photos by Steve Paxton
Volume 9 1983-84
it a conference-call, a sounding board.
We 're calling it Still Moving. Please
participate by sending your writings
as well as your responses to what you
read here, to us at CQ, P.O. Box 603,
Nortbampton, MA 01061.
MOVING
Carol Mullins does the lighting and
Dan Froot coordinates other elements:
sound, slides, films and sets.
This team handled the many details,
changes, complications of CONTACT
AT 10th & 2nd with care and efficien-
cy. It is something of a test to produce
new works and improvisations because
much remains unknown, untested or
mutable right down to the wire. A
tight, light, responsive system is inval-
uable for breaking new ground. How-
ever fine and airy the space itself may
be, it is this team which made the
house comfortable for the several
dozen choreographers and performers
who gathered there for 7 productions
June 10, 11, 12 & 16, 17, 18, 19, 1983.
The performers and choreographers
had a great deal to pull together them-
selves. They arrived from England,
California, Canada, the US, and from
around Manhattan, with their concepts,
improvisations and dancing bodies.
They arranged themselves into the
general format of Contact Improvisa-
tion or Choreographed/semi-set works
with minimal ado. As (traffic) director
of these events, I am eternally grateful
for their grace under fire.
•
Contact Improvisation has been a
performance form since its inception.
Audiences and critics have challenged
the appropriateness of this decision,
and I think it true that the audience-
performer relationship may serve the
performer here as much or more than
the audience, certainly for those ob-
servers who require more explicit sig-
nalllng of intent in their audit.
Their expectation is a rather fruit-
less approach to Contact Improvisation.
It is, after all, improvisational. Intent
and manifestation amount to the same
thing. Further, any contact duet is a
manifestation of a fraction of the pos-
sibilities of the form, so, audiences
seeing a performance for the first time
must be content with the specifics of
that dance, if not the general idea.
Much that is excellent in Contact
Volume 9 1983-84
Improvisation is the result of omis-
sions-avoiding injury, for instance,
yet allowing moments of instability
while playing with powerful physical
forces. Omit jarring the body, mis-
communication, physical confusion,
willful accomplishment. These omis-
sions are about survival.
Survival may be taken for granted
by audiences. The performer of CI is
not so phlegmatic. The notion that CI
performance is 'ungenerous', as audi-
ences have observed, results from CI
performers paying close attention to
life and limb. If the audience feels
neglected due to lack of eye contact,
for instance, they might consider that
in CI training peripheral, non-focussed
vision is employed. This is very awk-
ward socially (try it at a party) but
creates a state of mind in which vision
is not paramount, for once, in the sen-
sory spectrum of the performer. Mass
is therefore more clearly sensed, as is
positioning and change.
Audiences of televised sports are
accustomed to seeing athletes prepar-
ing for their tasks, and watching them
work (play). These performers are
primed to make unusual efforts and
we watch the results of their play
knowing what they are attempting to
do, and, with some, we understand
how they hope to do it-an empathy
born of knowing the rules of the situa-
tion. I believe knowing the premises
of CI creates a similar empathy, but
the spectator must become used to the
idea that a Form which is proposing to
deal with improvisation formally,
rather than being goal-oriented or
aesthetically based, requires some other
sort of empathy-perhaps that of the
naturalist on a ramble. It is the mi.tu-
ralist who organizes the view into
forms. The river will not speak of
gravity n·or surface tension nor subsur-
face currents. These are attributes for
the naturalist to infer. The birds signal
their intent to the other birds. The
naturalist is eavesdropping. Birds just
bird. The naturalist must bird along.
In short, theatrical forms rely
mostly on contrast and conflict. CI
depends on communication and co-
operation.
Sports rely on conflicting goals and
'plays'. CI has no point-system and
the maneuvers are relatively unpre-
dictable.
Fred Holland & Ishmael Houston-Jones, June 1983
Dance has visual aesthetics, Cl has
accepted a much more 'general look'.
It just happens. Its subsurface currents
must be inferred.
I suppose we are asking audiences
to look in unusual ways, but what of
that? Every medium requires a shift
of focus, every culture. Even every
day. I would like an audience of
Sherlock Holmeses who would discern
the interpersonal currents, understand
the dialogue, and maintain a stance of
no blame, even in the face of the guilty
performer who could not fulfill his
own idea1s in performance.
I gather from what I have written
that I do not see the audience-perform-
er relationship as one where the per-
former has a duty to the audience. I
see it as a relationship where they have
a relationship with each other, and if
the audience may have expectations of
the performer, well, so may the per-
former have expectations of the audi-
ence. You no doubt get the drift. "It
matters what you give an audience to
do" (Christie Svane). "Art criticism is
for artists as ornithology is for the
birds" (Barnett Newman).
CQICI Sourcebook
•
Two works from CONT ACT AT
10th & 2nd, one of the semi-set variety,
the second improvisational, remain es-
pecially vivid in my memory. Fred
Holland and Ishmael Houston-} ones
performed a gritty duet which segued
seamlessly from contact to conflict to
contest; through emotional areas into
clear physical spaces; to sense-altering
moments when the two bodies seemed
to drag or claw through space; to
events of high play, and casual conver-
sation.
Nothing settled as the dance pro-
gressed. It was one fevered long trans-
ition. It spoke of men, Blacks; they
were at war at play at art at odds at
one.
The space was used darkly. A
sound score of soulful mechanical
grating and chugging added emphatic
menace to some moments. The same
sound, more frequent toward the end
of the work, became a wash of rather
loud pulsations, but were merely back-
ground noise when I perceived two
men working in a factory. The throb
of sound was hypnotic, and I had a
feeling of falling back and forth
through time, from the present mo-
ment of contact to several apparent
references in their/our past: scrapings
from old newsreels, late night TV wars,
items in smoky bars.
On the Saturday night of the second
weekend, 5 folk who teach or perform
contact in Lower Manhattan did an
amazing thing which I have not heard
brought up for discussion, though if
there is orthodoxy in improvisation,
this performance was a challenge-
gauntlet down from the git-go.
Looking the length of the space, I
saw the lights come up on an Indian-
file of performers facing right. The
leader whirled and slapped the second
across the face, which turned the sec-
ond around to face the third; 2 slapped
3, 3 slapped 4, 4 slapped 5, who spun
completely around and returned the
compliment back through the line.
The lights then faded.
The rest of the evening proceeded
in a more normal vein. The lights re-
surfaced, dancing began, street clothes
were shed and contact appeared. The
dancing was a clear sample of regional
style. I realized that much of contact
performance around the country looks
rather more like itself than did this
version. This had its own nervous
humor, a sense of high energy even in
repose, and subsurface irony. The en-
semble feeling was strong, but it is an
ensemble of anarchists and in group-
ings the performers seemed indepen-
dent, as though they might leave, solo
or stay according to thoughts passing
quickly behind quiet faces.
Danny Lepkoff, Robin Feld, Diane
Madden, Stephen Petronio, and Randy
Warshaw made clear, eccentric, some-
times brilliant dances and showed me
that psychology, humor and intelli-
gence need not be stressed to be visible
and catalytic. The ability of the per-
formers to occasionally enliven the
whole space was partly due to the fact
of training in solo work (all are experi-
enced soloists, three dance with Trisha
Brown) so that their solo shapes and
plots looked secure and centered, not
like an interval between duets.
Further, they made use of the room,
making exits and traverses of the
space, rather than hanging around the
edge as is customary in Cl. They did
do that also, but created images of the
lounging or waiting. One was to do
with the back wall which has a kind
of illusion built into the architectural
detailing, partial columns and horizon-
tal ledges onto which they climbed to
stand and wait.
Carol Mullins was lighting that wall
in pale rosy washes, and the standing
figures looked like substitutes for the
sunbathing nude women in Maxfield
Parrish's pictures. But lounging on the
veranda is over, the terrace is now oc-
cupied territory. Such images arise,
have probably nothing to do with the
work except that one way of looking
at this sort of theater is to divine the
images. When one is engaged, but im-
ages and directions are not poked at
one, the daydream-divination prospect
can produce private subtexts of some
power.
The trick here is assigning responsi-
bility. We are not, of course, discus-
sing referential images in the usual way
("a witty reappraisal of sentimental
painting appeared on the upper reaches
of the back wall"). Rather, engage-
ment of the audience, which the per-
formers accomplished; and reverie in
the midst of their action, which I ac-
complished. I suspect something the-
atrical was begun in the first striking
image of this work. Slapping is on the
one hand aggressive, but on the other
hand quickening-like the slap admin-
istered to a victim of shock, or the
Zen honk on the head. Wake up. The
slap form was done as a ceremony, and
broke the old CI mind-set of good will
and 'total trust' (with irritation and
frustration suppressed in some other
mental compartment) and provoked
fresh appraisal about what they were
up to.
Nina Martin and Paul Langland
were absent from the group this night,
but will join them when next they
surface, in the name of Channel Z, on
January 28th & 29th, 1984 in NYC.~

CONTACT AS THERAPY
by Irina Harris and Rita Ossi
In July of 1980, Rita Ossi partici-
pated in a contact workshop with me
in Venice, Italy, for rehabilitation ther-
apists and others interested in Contact
as a therapeutic matrix, and thus began
a fruitful collaboration which contin-
ues to enrich our respective fields of
interest.
Rita was then doing psychotherapy
for a public health service, helping
long-term patients become autonomous
and reintegrated into the society. What
most preoccupied her about these pa-
tients was the sense of expropriation
that their bodies had suffered, the lack
of care for themselves physically, and
their fear or inability to adequately re-
late to others. She found that only by
direct means and experience-body
work-was it possible to improve their
self-concept.
As Rita continued to study Contact
with me in Rome, she found the con-
cepts of Contact Improvisation star-
tlingly powerful (even if some she had
previously encountered in Reich,
Lowen, Perls, and Feldenkrais): estab-
lish contact with yourself and others,
turn yourself upside down, feel weight
Volume 9 1983-84
and energy, perceive and occupy space,
give and receive weight (physically and
psychologically!), understand where
the fears knot up inside. Each of us
from her own starting point was con-
vinced of the therapeutic value of
contact.
So last summer in Treviso, we held
a workshop together. Most partici-
pants said that their primary reason for
wanting to experiment with Contact
was to widen their movement experi-
ence. Little by little as we worked, we
noted that everyone found the work
psychically therapeutic also.
The workshop begins. After a brief
introduction, I start some Contact ex-
ercises: with the floor, with various
body parts, with space, weight, with
another's body (with and not against).
It is for them both an exploration and
a discovery, a putting into focus, a
union and a clash with their own sen-
sations and postural habits.
We go on to balances, somersaults
and other upside down movements,
feeling weight and energy, trying active
and passive roles. There are moments
when people seem confused because
they are feeling new sensations, coor-
dinations never before explored, rela-
tionships up to this point barely recog-
nized; and there are other magic mo-
ments in which the movement flows
CQ!CI Sourcebook %b
as dance and everyone perceives the
fusion of mind and body.
When someone in the group encoun-
ters an apparently insurmountable dif-
ficulty, we stop. I try diversi:: physical
approaches. Rita mentions the sym-
bolic significance the movement might
express, the psychological valence of
that individual or group difficulty.
She tries not to be categorical but pro-
poses various hypotheses, interpreta-
tions which each can then investigate
for himself.
She gives some examples: not being
able to give one's own weight or take
another's may mean lack of confidence,
fearfulness, mistrustfulness of others,
rigidity in relationships; holding the
head and neck stiff perhaps means
wanting to control everything ration-
ally; holding the legs, especially in
couple work, could mean fear of sex-
ual invasion, etc ....
Memories of old traumas arise,
consciousness of habits (postural and
psychic) taken on at particular times
in one's life, fears that have never been
overcome.
When, on the last day, we put to-
gether sound and movement-roaring-
there is a moment of panic. Broken,
held sounds come out and they sudden-
ly realize that these sounds open a new
realm of the body. Eagerly and at
length they work on the impasse.
At the end of every day, Rita and I
discussed the work accomplished, in
what manner each participant had
shown us something of himself. We
examined our impressions of their per-
sonalities, their problems. The follow-
ing day I proposed exercises relating to
what had emerged, integrating this
work into the general development of
the workshop. And so the workshop
was guided by psychological as well as
physical considerations. The therapeu-
tic effect was confirmed by the parti-
cipants themselves in our final discus-
sion when they talked of their sense of
well being and physical pleasure, and
also of awareness, each at his own level,
of "dark areas", of the lack of accept-
ance of parts of their bodies, and of the
possibility of accepting those parts and
better integrating them.
Rita and I had decided not to com-
ment specifically but generally, to al-
low the experience itself to bring even-
tual insights.
Rita has been able to verify, even in
other situations, that the discovery of
certain personality disturbances, if it
happens in the body, with its own reac-
tions and sensations, is more quickly
recognized and can provoke more radi-
cal changes than an analytic psycho-
therapy based exclusively on words
which often mask, color, and dilute
significance and change.
96c CQ/CI Sourcebook
Certainly Contact is a dance form,
full of life and expression, but we
maintain that it can be used also as an
efficient psychotherapy, or better, as
"body therapy". Both in Italy and
America where present physical fads
tend towards negation of the body
rather than self-discovery, we would
like to see Contact better known and
used. f!
I would be very interested to know
if and what anyone else is doing with
the therapeutic aspects of Contact
work. Irina Harris, 516 Nordhoff Dr.,
Leonia, NJ 07605.
IRINA HARRIS divides her time
between New York and Rome. A
registered movement therapist, she
teaches contact improvisation as well
as performing and choreographing.
She is coordinator of the performance
group of the School for Creative
Movement.
RITA OSSI is a psychotherapist living
near Treviso, Italy. After many years
in public health she is now in private
practice using body work for therapy
as well as more traditional methods.
RANDOM NOTES
from the Breitenbush jam
by Terrie Yaffe
Time's up. Now words must reach the paper. Highlights. It's history-no fancy
anybody's moving around like they owned the world. We owned the clock on the
wall; we owned the gold in our teeth. The sun in the morning, the hot steam in the
air, the hot sweat on our backs-we owned it. We owned the day before and the day
after. We owned the color green and the color orange and all the blues and the color
black. If my language offends you, forgive me. This is an ex-marxist speaking in
tongues. Anybody home?
It's good to get turned around, to get turned upside down because maybe for a little
while you don't remember where you are ie. disorientation.
I never counted who cares how many so many dances all different dances. I remem-
ber my black eye but not how I got it only that it felt (how should I say it)-earned.
Not a day went by that I didn't refer to a poem I'd been working on. No matter
how many times I tried to rewrite it, the beginning few lines always came up the
same: Dance/ a million laughs for El Salvador/ for god's sake/ Someone dance. I'd
started it many weeks before when someone I know was dying fast :ind was waiting
on what? I a miracle. And really I had never thought much about miracles since,
well, hebrew school in N.J. The light burning on Hannukkah was a miracle when
one day's worth of oil in a burning lamp lasted an entire 8 days because someone
with a fast horse and a few extra days to spare took a chance suspended his imagina-
tion and against all odds traveled to retrieve more oil.
Before the jam, the poem had seemed vague and simple and incomplete. Five days
later, without changing even one word it seemed to take root as a poem for the first
time. Before the jam, my words seemed to belittle the very serious political and
social aspects of the war in El Salvador but as I began to let go of the poem much in
the same way as I began to let go in my dancing the actual meaning of the poetry
became more clear.
Dance/ a million laughs for El Salvador/ for god's sake/ Someone dance
The current trend bespeaks the mood/ Bread and more bread/ and more bread
Noone is alarmed at nature/ Midwives slap each grave image
Sweat and muscle culminate beyond what waits
A broomhandle blooms/ and winks
TERRIE YAFFE teaches improvisa-
tional theatre, writes, and dances in
Seattle, Washington.

Editor Note
STILL IN THE MIDDLE
L ast summer in Boulder, Larry
Fagin told me about an exer-
cise he gave his poetics students
as a lesson in improvisation: Go to a
movie in the middle. Sit down and
watch. As soon as you've figured out
what's going on, get up and leave.
For me too, improvisation is a prac-
tice in disorientation-training the
reflexes to read confusion as a challenge
not a threat; that a moment cut loose
from its moorings has the advantage of
being able to move in any direction,
and further, that a temporary absence
of reference points can clear the way
for impulses from unexpected quarters.
In the past few years, disorientation
has reemerged as a central theme for me
in teaching & practicing Contact Im-
provisation and in performing improvi-
sation. What began as a physical survi-
val skill for Contact, has become for
me a keystone-not only for the survi-
val of the bodies in contact, but for the
vitality & heart of the improvisation.
One of the earliest exercises in
physical disorientation for Contact
Improvisation, and still at the heart of
the work for me, is Steve Paxton's
'small dance of standing'. Relaxing
erect, the intelligence of the body is
revealed as it fires the appropriate
muscles just enough to keep the body
mass hovering within range of its verti-
cal supports. The micromovements
that occur to keep me balanced are so
tiny and yet so magnified, and arise
from such a deep feeling of stillness
and space, that I get giddy, tickled by
the impossible. magnitude of such subtle
sensations. The disorientation in the
Volume 10 1985
stand comes from the feeling that in-
side the apparent solidity and stillness
of standing, there is nothing but move-
ment and space! Each time, I come to
feel as precarious standing on the floor
on two legs as I might on one leg on a
trampoline.
Without the willingness to risk one's
point of view, to be temporarily at a
loss, improvising can become a sleepy
exercise in restating what's already
perfectly clear. When nothing is really
at stake, one's survival is assured, but
some deeper satisfaction, both as a
watcher and as a performer, is lacking.
But it's tricky keeping the cutting
edge sharp-the body/mind adapts
incredibly fast to unfamiliar circum-
stances. What is attempted and survived
with hesitation the first time, is ap-
proached with more courage the next,
until soon it is done automatically.
After awhile the real challenge changes
from 'how can I survive this disorienta-
tion' to 'where do I find the next one?'.
It's easy to come into the middle of
a movie if you know when it's scheduled
to end. There's an added mystery to
the middle when the duration isn't
known. I love the performances of
improvisation that end themselves.
When a simple gesture, delivered with
every intention of being followed by
another, leaves you unexpectedly
finished. Realizing you're home, dizzy,
delighted, you say goodnight.
photo: Bill Arnold
But most of the time it's the per-
formers that choose when to end. And
they have their preferences-sorne tend
to end on an upswing, others at a climax,
or just after, while some prefer to fade
away over the horizon of some long
plateau. I used to have a bad habit of
ending things too early-just when the
substance of the thing was about to
come through. Once a form was recog-
nized, I thought it was complete. In
my dances, I was constantly changing
the subject, interrupting with a new
thought, a new dynamic, a shift in
focus. From years of watching and
dancing with other improvisors, I slowly
learned how and why to stay with
things a little longer-that a form has
substance as well as shape, and to get
to the substance, you have to penetrate
the form. Recognizing the form gives
you somewhere to start, but it's the
substance that gives you depth.
I'm still learning that lesson. Both
Contact Improvisation and Contact
Quarterly have now passed their ten
year mark. Regularly, I sense the end
approaching, of my engagement and
curiosity with these forms. I feel too
well-oriented and begin to think I've
figured it our. But through the work
itself and my partners in it, I've learned
a little patience, at least enough to keep
finding out that what appears to be the
end, isn't. As it turns out, I'm still in
the middle.
Nancy Stark Smith
CQ/CI Sourcebook
Editor Note
NOW AND THEN
Practicing improvisation in the '80s is not the same as
it was in the '70s. But then, it isn't altogether different.
In the '70s, when Contact Improvisation began and was
developed, the time was ripe for risk and discovery. Th~
'60s had shaken so many basic assumptions, that by the
'70s, it was almost impossible not to approach things with
a fresh look, ready for news. The times not only supported
but demanded an ability to improvise. Contact Improvisa-
tion offered a movement practice that developed physical
and mental flexibility and strength within a state of con·
stant flux. Its invitation to extend one's limits-physically,
conceptually, aesthetically, psychologically-was welcome
and well taken.
The enlivened state of being that resulted from this
movement practice created a strong motivation and refer-
ence point for its practitioners. Just knowing that such
freedom was possible generated tremendous willingness to
engage in the process. The work was fed by its practition-
ers' recognition of and faith in that possibility. And it was
largely because of that nourishment that it continued and
grew.
In 1977, Christina Svane wrote "In Praise of Bad Danc-
ing" for the Contact Newsletter. In it she said, "I value
this work not only for the rare glimpses of in-touch, in-
time, in-tune, but for the companionship of the ever-present
indications of what is possible. The form itself, it one exists
beyond the instances of attempts to experience it, is none
other than the existence of the possibility of a dance in
which wills, instincts and verges merge. Emergency, emerge,
merge. It amounts to having to believe in a possibility
which one may never have experienced. All this is to say I
am devoted to something which I rarely feel, the possibility
of which I never leave. We extend to ourselves an invitation
which we rarely accept, but the extension of it is what
matters. Living for so long on the verge of that acceptance,
I figure it's time to call it home."
Contact Improvisation has always been compelling work
for me. I've invested a lot in it, and I've gotten a lot in re-
turn. It is a generous form in that way. Smitten as I was
by the enlivened state it had the capacity to induce, for a
long time it seemed to me that the form existed totally in-
dependent of our insatiable pursuit of it. It gave but didn't
need.
I've since come to realize that the freedom improvisation
offers doesn't come without bidding. Its pleasure is a disci-
pline. In the practice of Contact Improvisation, one is con-
stantly faced with one's tendency to make the same choices
over and over again. Within the form, this tendency is
countered by working in close contact with a partner who
Andrew Harwood and Nancy Stark Smith
is in a position to remind you that there are options you
hadn't considered. Appreciating that invitation is one of
the basic challenges improvising offers.
I've also come to realize that it's possible to practice the
physical techniques of Contact Improvisation and never
make the choice to improvise. ls it because one hasn't been
shown the possibility, doesn't see it, or simply doesn't
choose it?
Though they greatly encouraged it, it was not only the
climate of the '70s, nor the improvisational forms it gave
rise to that enabled us to appreciate the value of staying
open to unexpected possibilities. The potential for open-
ness is human, and can be chosen and actively cultivated
any time, by any individual, through any form. It is the
desire for it that makes it possible. Like the fairy godmother
said to the destitute woman in a theatre improvisation I
once saw, "I can make your wish come true. But you have
to make the wish."
Nancy Stark Smith

STILL MOVING
RESPONSE TO "CONT ACT AS
THERAPY"
Byron Brown and Carol Swann photo: Bill Arnold
Dear CQ,
After I read the article by
Irina Harris and Rita Ossi, "Contact
as Therapy," in the last CQ [CQ IX 3,
Fall 1984], I felt encouraged to write
to you.
This year I'm finishing my studies
in psychology in Heidelberg and for
2'h years I've been doing contact im-
provisation and dance therapy. My in-
terest is to integrate contact dance into
my studies. Experiences you can have
in this kind of dance, like flow of ener-
gy, weight exchange, the falling, trust-
ing, flying, ... will extend both your
physical range of possibilities and your
personal growth as well.
At the moment I'm working on the
conceptualization of boundaries/limi-
tations in body therapy with drug
addicts.
First I'd like to introduce a little
bit of the psychological thinking, which
is very much based on Gestalt therapy,
before I come to my questions about
how contact dance might support a
psychotherapeutic process.
· From the psychological point of
view, ego-boundaries are seen as con-
stitutive [sic] for the ego, as a place
where the identity of one's self mani-
fests. Boundaries are the points of
contact. It is like a membrane which
allows a certain interaction between
an organism and the environment, a
point of contact where energy passes
through constantly.
Exploring your boundaries, your
points of contact, you experience
yourself, your ego, in relationship to
the other. The ego is existent through
the contact point and defines the
boundary between the personal and
the unpersonal space. Contact at the
boundary creates a sense of indepen-
dence and isolation, and at the same
time it is contact and a possibility to
share. This dualistic character of the
contact at the boundary is necessary
for the development and stabilization
of what is called identity.
Because of its importance for per-
sonal growth, you have to care about
the very complex concept of bounda-
ries in the psychological field. Some-
times you have to support people to
build up (ego-) boundaries, sometimes
you have to support them to become
less rigid and more flexible at their
100 CQ/CI Sourcebook
Volume 10 1985
boundaries or to rediscover the func-
tions of contact, so that they can de-
velop more ego-strength, autonomy
and responsibility, etc.
At this point, I'd like to find out
how contact dance might support this
psychotherapeutic work. In contact
improvisation, the point of contact is
a basic principle, first of all on a physi-
cal level, and for me, it always has to
do with exploring and experiencing
boundanes and limitations. The body
1s the pnmarv boundary between inner
world and o~ter world ·and a manifes-
tation, a personal system of what I am.
I'd like to look at contact dance for
a while in these terms and I'd appreci-
ate very much, if anyone would like
to respond to my following questions
or share their experience in another
way with me. I hope my German-
English is understandable.
Thanks a lot to Bob Rease and
Robin Feld, who encouraged me during
the August Summer Intensive in Hei-
delberg to come out with my interest.
e What kind of boundaries do you ex-
perience doing contact dance?
How do they manifest?
• What kind of boundary-experiences
do you have in relationship/inter-
action (in a duo, trio, ... group)?
-physical level
-emotional level
@ How do you experience contact at
the boundary in terms of:
-in between space (Zwischenraume)
-surface-contact (Oberflachen-
kontakt)
-full weight exchange (Gewicht-
saustausch)
What kinds of experiences do you
have dealing with boundaries in
terms of:
-respecting boundaries/limitations
-breaking through boundaries/ "
-protecting with boundaries/ "
-going beyond boundaries/ "
What kind of support ... and ...
What kind of demands ... do vou
need (from the teacher, from the
group or environment), to have
boundary-experiences? ... to be
able to extend boundaries?
• Other associations, thoughts, fanta-
sies on this theme .....
Best wishes,
Gila Albers
Kuhlhof 24
4450 Lingen/ Ems 1
W-Germany

RESPONSE TO "DEALING
WITH THE HEAT"
Dear Nancy,
I appreciated your Editor Note last
issue, "Dealing with the Heat," {CQ
IX 3, Fall 1984], because I too, have
begun to see value in the friction of
opposition. I have felt my tendency to
harmon_ize conflict in so many aspects
of my life, from social interaction to
simply how I reside in my own tissue.
And lately, I have been exploring in
both my personal life and my dancing
the parts of myself that I had dismissed
by omitting 'no's, by resolving differ-
ences rather than feeling the heat of
the friction. I perceive that there is so
much ~o be gained in this exploration
and thmk that it applies to much of
what I have experienced with Contact
Improvisation.
To engage in conflict one must be
willing to draw upon the passionate
charge that emanates from one's
"guts'', tapping the heart of the mat-
ter. I am only beginning to appreciate
the value of this juice. I have avoided
it out of fear of sounding unreasonable
or being too ego bound. Now I see it
as a beautiful force that can orient me
toward knowing-viscerally-my sense
of purpose.
I see the basic aesthetic of Contact
as one of merging with natural forces,
going with the flow, and experiencing
union with both our partner and a
process of engaging the physical laws
of nature. Asserting control, saying
n_o to this_ union, or establishing a dis-
tinct self m the dance is generally not
done. As we continue to explore the
mysterious beauties of the original
aesthetic, I'm curious about how Con-
tact will change if we bring more of
ourselves to the dance, if our relation-
ships include difference and separation
as well as union, personal passion as
well as the impersonal play of weight
and balance.
I see the orientation in Contact Im-
provisation toward harmony and pro-
cess as an important voice on a leading
edge of our culture, evidence of some-
thing more spontaneous and essential
in dance and relationships than the
superficial and goal oriented nature of
much of our world. And I see the in-
corporation of conflict and passions
as an important passage if Contact is
going to continue to be a valuable so-
cial force.
In order to discuss this point and to
offer a perspective on Contact Improv-
isation in relation to our culture I'd
like to share some of the work o'f
Charley Johnston, M.D. He is a psychi-
atrist with a recently completed book,
The Creative Imperative, about a model
of all creative processes, from artistic
endeavors to the process of developing
a relationship, to developmental shifts
over a lifetime, to cultural change and
the creation of civilization . . . He sees
similar structural shifts through all of
~hes_e processes as they grow. Although
1t will be unfortunately oversimplified,
I'll describe the gist of this model in
order to offer a point of view on Con-
tact Improvisation's relationship to our
current culture and on Contact's own
development.
. The basic pattern Dr.Johnston ~es
m all creative process, all of life, is from
formlessness/essence/mystery into
form/manifestation/materialization and
then into an integration process that
eventually embraces both the struc-
ture and the essence. In other words,
~rom oneness into twoness, from unity
~nto separatwn, from what is archetyp-
ically fei:nale mto archetypically male,
from universal and spiritual into mater-
ial and intellectual; and then into inte-
~ratio~. He _divides these three stages
mto five which I'll call: 1) Pre-stage,
2) Early Stage, 3) Middle Stage, 4) Late
Stage, 5) Integration.
I'll describe each stage with exam-
pl~s o~ developments in an art project,
a ltfenme, and culture. It is important
to note that each shift into the next
stage reflects a transformation into an
entirely different paradigm or system
of values. And each transformation is
a quantum leap into a larger capacity
for experiencing aliveness, for being
able to handle and even enjoy existing
amongst unknowns.
Pre-stage
This is a gestalting time, a time of
unity, before one is particularly aware
of a cr.eative direction beginning to dif-
feren t1ate from this womb of possibili-
ty. In a lifetime, it is prenatal experi-
ence and early infancy, when the child
bonds with parent. Culturally, it is the
time when life is ordered by connection
with nature-a powerful and mysteri-
ous ~orce. One lived within the unity
of tnbe which was inseparable from
nature and her cycles.
CQ/CI Sourcebook 101
Early Stage .
The early stage is the time df inspir-
ation, the spontaneous possibility of
essence taking form, the first notion of
direction in creating a work of art. It
is childhood, a time of fantasy. It is
the time when cultures moved from
tribal nature to the sophistication we
associate with the early Greeks, the
Aztecs, and Ancient Zimbawe. Basi-
cally, life is still experienced as a mys-
terious force of which we are in awe
and by which we are totally influenced.
Many of the special beauties of Con-
tact Improvisation are reflections of
this stage. We dance with spontaneity
and participate in an often magical
process that is larger than ourselves
individually. Culturally, this is a spir-
itual time. Zen emerged from a culture
at this stage. Not until the next stage
do we become more personal, more
passionate.
Middle Stage
As the first shapings of inspiration
take enough form, they begin to chal-
lenge their source, their essence, for
attention and belief. Here, distinction
Integration
The next stage, integration, is re-
flected by the leading edge of our cul-
ture. The essence of this stage is most
apparent to me as I think of those peo-
ple who, as they age, become sagelike.
They come to gradually encompass all
of the stages-they appreciate the mys-
tery of life as well as the passions while
maintaining facility with the intellec-
tual and material aspects. With a work
of art, this is when the work is com-
plete and the artist becomes separate
from it. Even though you may have
created it, it will be quite a while be-
fore it is understood. Now it becomes
a distinct entity with the capacity to
teach even you who created it. I see
Contact Improvisation as an aspect of
Western Culture's movement into inte-
gration. I think it emerged as a coun-
terswing from the materialistic direc-
tion of our culture. It has served me
well in this regard. It has given me a
taste of community while all I had
known was transience. It has taught
me about shedding of ego to enjoy the
dance beyond my control; there truly
is something to be trusted as alive with-
out willful effort to make it happen.
These are rather foreign notions to the
goal oriented lifestyle of America in
the twentieth century. But what about
the twenty-first century?
Ultimately, integration is more than
a counterswing. Contact rather success-
fully synthesized the mysterious, the
more unformed and core aspects of the
early stage with the articulate, discern-
ing, and mechanical aspects of the late
stage. But in this time of overstated
glory for the intellect, I see much more
value in engaging the heat of difference
than for ideological articulation. In the
same way that revitalizing the teachings
of ancient prophets has been so mean-
ingful to expand our capacity for living
sanely in these seemingly insane times,
I think we have much to gain in re-
claiming the personal juice that is a
powerful part of the uniqueness of each
of us. I am excited to experience the
potential embodiment of the charge of
passions in myself and in connection
with Contact Improvisation as it grows
with me and with all of us. What will
these changes be?
Thanks Nancy, for all of your work
with the Quarterly and sharing your
growing edge.
Sincerely,
Kris Wheeler
Seattle, WA
Sept. 21, 1984
drawing by Christina Svane
becomes very important. But not for
empirical insight-that comes in the
late stage. Instead, attention goes to
distinguishing right from wrong, good
from bad. As a work of art moves in-
to more formedness, the original
source acts as a sort of quality control
continually questioning the integrity
of the emerging form. At the same
time, the urge to form must continual-
ly forge ahead or it will be swallowed
by doubt. In a lifetime, it is the time
of adolescence. In the polarization of
forces arises a personal and even moral
charge, a strength to know your posi-
tion and stand up for it. (Sound famil-
iar?) In Western Culture, the middle
stage began at the birth of monotheism
and continued through the middle
ages. A result of that time was the cre-
ation of polarized social roles, e.g.:
aristocracy and peasantry, male and
female differentiation, and Christians
and heretics.
If we create a context in our Con-
tact Improvisation gatherings that en-
courages people to engage in polarized
relationships, then I'd say that the de-
velopment of Contact as a whole is
moving from the early stage into the
middle stage. In my recent past, I have
felt that emotional tension was a prob-
lem in a Contact class or Jam. It is
useful to me to see the value of this
stage rather than worry about conflicts
shattering the unified foundation we
have built. Actually, participation in
this realm can create a capacity to
meet one another in more ways.
Late Stage
In creating a work of art, the late
stage is the time when attention goes
to detailing the form and polishing the
surface, now that the original source
has taken satisfactory shape. In a life-
time, it is early adulthood when one is
more oriented toward establishing
oneself materialistically in culture.
With the age of reason and into the
industrial revolution, Western Culture
shifted into the late stage. The forces
of nature no longer appeared as a
threat as we separated ourselves and
focused on our capacity to control our
environment. We have developed elab-
orate structures both materially and
intellectually.
We live in a culture that is in the
midst of this stage. Aspects of Con·
tact certainly reflect it: we have a
highly articulate language for addres-
sing the skills of Contact-a surprising-
ly mechanical language of weights and
levers, when actually the essence of
Contact is union with natural forces
and diminishing our effort to control.

STILL MOVING
In celebration of Contact Quarterly 's
10th Anniversary Year, we are reprint-
ing the 'still moving' writings on Con-
tact Improvisation that appeared in
issues of CQ now out of print. "Solo
Dancing," was written by Steve Paxton,
7 July 1973, Rome, Italy, following a
series of Contact performances at
L 'Attica Gallery in Rome in June '73,
one year after the first performances
of Contact at the Weber Gallery in
NYC. Both of these early Contact
events were videotaped and are avail-
able, in edited form, through Videoda
(see ad on opposite page). In addition
to reprinting older writings in Still
Moving, we look forward to printing
your current thoughts. This time no
material was sent in for the column.
Are you Still Moving? [Eds.]
~"~
SOLO DANCING
O/TVTl.i.tt;, Ao- .""'.-0'1\-W..
--~ ~Cfv
'"'r;J/v-<, --Nl.,>.(;;-1",
,Y~ ;}Vwyl..:.,,-vlVr~
by Steve Paxton
reprinted from Contact Quarterly,
Volume II no. 3, Spring 1977.
Solo dancing does not exist:
the dancer dances with the floor: add
another dancer and you have a quartet:
each dancer with the other and each
with the floor.
In the United States recently a form
of dancing has arisen which treats each
dancer as a surface to be played upon
as is the floor. Named CONT ACT
IMPROVISATION, it resembles wrest-
ling, the jitterbug, fucking, tumbling
and juggling; yet none of these quite
describes the other; and none quite de-
scribes this dancing. The physical part
is best pictured in terms of spectrums
of potentials: two people freely im-
provising movement using the floor and
each other as surface rely on gravity as
a constant; inertia into momentum as
a variable; adamant (the floor) to yield-
ing (skin-muscle-bone-total mass) as
surface features; and the variables of
Program from the 1973 Contact performances at L' Attico Gallery
in Rome, Italy.
give and take, ruled not by set roles for
each dancer but by the understanding
that each may change from give to take
at any time.
This brings us to the non-physical
part of this dancing; the state of being
or mind permitting mutual freedom,
with mutual reliance. The mind is kept
empty of preconceptions and memories;
it is in the present moments only, med-
itating on the potentials and on the
easiest paths in the energy construct
which are available to both dancers: it
is a state of abandon; trust in the self
and in each other must be total. The
ability to help each other and one's self
must be ever ready: through constant
movement one pursues mutual ease in
constant mutual change. The dancer's
weight is only his tq give; not to possess.
The final factor here considered is
time. The division of time into minutes
and seconds perhaps clouds the view of
time-as-flow: but in this dancing where
units of time are defined by the veloci-
ties of independent and semi-indepen•
dent masses involved in movement of
and upon the two bodies, time moves
as quickly as the dancers move: given
a state of constant falling, the glands
react as in those falls wherein time ap-
parently stands still, and the dancers
shift from our pedestrian concepts into
a time sense allowing some controls in
the endless chain of accidents they have
provoked: that is the improvisation in
which they make contact. 1:!
Volume 10 1985
CQICI Sourcebook 103
Sallie Lyons & Allan Risdill of Toronto Independent Dance Enterprise
One day we were exchanging weight by standing shoulder to shoulder, hip to hip,
see-sawing back & forth. A practice in giving and taking weight using any &/or all
parts. I started with Diane (everyone paired up) and got involved in a familiar com-
fortable exchange. We switched partners & I started with Lori. We immediately got
into a gripping, grabbing, climbing exchange. We stopped & illustrated both partner-
ings to the others. I myself was very unwilling to dictate how the weight should be
exchanged, feeling that either method was valid and not wanting to assume or impose
a "contact" attitude. But what surfaced was fact. The duet with Lori was manipula-
tive (about power & control= outside forces trying to dictate or control action).
Hardly what could honestly be called an exchange. Safety was another major factor.
The forcing of actions & weight. The forcing of the situation undermined safety.
Injury not only seemed possible, but imminent.
I feel in the last few years I've rebelled against what I call a "contact" body atti-
tude. It seems that in the study of C. /mprov or in the development of the form
people have embraced a certain "body attitude" and approach every movement situa-
tion with it. I started yearning for scrappers. Rule-fess instincts governing a situation.
But in the actuality-in the exchange-if that's what it's to be-reasons for rules or
methods became very clear. I've always thought it a drawback to have to understand
& experience everything from zero, but questioning & testing sends you back to the
root, to the reason, and that's so valuable.
Meg Cottam. Shaftsbury, Vermont.

Volume 10 1985
BACK IN TIME
Seven years ago, while doing a
Contact Improvisation duet, I was
stopped short by a movement I made
that wasn't mine. "Hey Lisa," I called
to her from across the studio, "I just
did your movement." "Let's see," she
said, as I rewound my sensations and '
tried to do it again. Recognizing her
idiosyncratic timing, she laughed and
suggested I keep "doing her." My part-
ner was willing, so we continued as I
tried to put myself in Lisa's skin, in her
nervous system, and dance. Instead of
my usual moderated flow, I jerked and
flinched, turning abruptly, plummeting
halfway to the floor and stopping, pull-
ing my arms back like a chicken and
making tiny darting birdlike movements
at unexpected moments. "Whoa," I
said, wanting to stop, "this is too weird."
What made me stop was the eery sensa-
tion of being in someone else's body
and in their time. My choices, reac-
tions, directions and timings were total-
ly foreign to me, but at the same time
very familiar. I learned them from
dancing with Lisa.
There are many ways to learn some-
one's dance. In this case, I learned it
through the skin. Dancing in physical
contact with a partner is a lot like put-
ting your ear to a wall and having a
conversation with someone on the other
side of it. Like the sounds in the next
room, your movements and your part-
ner's are amplified by the contact, and
very little goes unregistered, even if
unconsciously. In Contact, skin to skin,
weight to weight, you're moved by your
partner's movements and in this way
able to feel their reflexes, the rush and
pause of their time, first hand. In Alex-
ander Neascu's article in this issue, he
talks of a new kind of time called 'in-
ternal time' recently identified by post-
modern physicists. I think it might be
this kind of time-one that moves ac-
cording to its own intrinsic logic and
phrasing, not according to the standard
clock-time divisions-that is communi-
cated during Contact Improvisation.
In "doing Lisa" I was further struck
by the fact that although these were all
movements I could physically do, they
were not ones I would ordinarily choose.
I had been under the assumption that
in improvising I was calling on any and
every movement I could possibly make,
but now I realized I was in fact doing a
very limited and consistent range of
movements, textures and timings. That
I was, in fact, always "doing myself."
I found this a rather humbling and re-
vealing discovery.
Volume 11 1986
Looking back from here, the oddest
part to me is how disappointed I was
to realize I was only "doing myself"
when I danced-as if I should have been
able to do more.
What more was there to do, one
might ask. Well, Contact Improvisation
for one thing. Obviously one can only
do Contact, or anything else for that
matter, in one's own way, but I was so
busy looking at Contact Improvisation,
I didn't notice myself doing it. I was
too busy subjecting myself to "the
forces"-of gravity, momentum, iner-
tia, etc.-to notice what kind of subject
I was. And I was taken aback to even
consider it.
Within the study of Contact, the
experience of "flow" was soon recog-
nized and highlighted in our dancing.
It became one of my favorite practices
and I proceeded to "do flow" for many
years-challenging it, testing it: Could
we flow through this pass? Could we
squeak through that one, and keep
going?
I used to have a partner that I called
the Flow Machine. His considerable
mass streamed through the channels of
movement, sometimes like a rushing
brook, sometimes like molten lava, but
always ongoing. I would get euphoric
and giddy as the end of the phrase never
arrived-always there was a new curve,
and we'd bank off the edge of that one,
going on and on. There is great pleasure
in this. And it is in large measure
through establishing and maintaining
Volume 11
1986
photo: Bill Arnold
a mutual flow that the trust is formed
between partners which supports fur-
ther risk-taking, as from this baseline
one can quickly sense changes in the
flow as they occur-changes in direc-
tion, modulations in timing-and be
ready to adapt to them.
The kinetic pleasures and soothing
rhythms of time-in-flow can be hypnot-
ic and often go unchallenged in Contact.
Why rock the boat now that we've
finally gotten it steadied? As I've said,
I've always loved "doing flow" and still
do, but the time came when I began to
wonder what else was possible. What
other kinds of time could be played
out in the dancing, and where might
that take us?
What I'm finding is that it is there,
in the departures from the established
time frame, that new aspects of the
dancers' personalities emerge and new
risks are taken. Taking the lid off the
flow container, time can move in new
ways, reflecting not only the dancers'
mutuality but their singularity, in
time, and in space.
Challenging any established order is
a risk, and can come with a fair amount
of disorientation and awkwardness.
But Contact trains us so well in spatial
disorientation-with all its whirling
about, off-center and falling~surely
our practice can welcome a little
confusion in time as well.
Nancy Stark Smith
CQ/CI Sourcebook 105
ALL IN GOOD TIME
For the last month I've been going down to
Wesleyan University on Fridays to teach two l '12 hour
classes in Contact Improvisation. Though I've been liking
the students a great deal, I've been grumbling a lot during
the week about the classes being too short. But now the
grumbling is bothering me more than the shortness of the
classes, so it's time to look into the whole ordeal.
The whole ordeal is about time, not enough of it, or
more specifically, not enough of a certain kind of it.
When I teach beginning Contact Improvisation, there
are a few principles I come back to over and over-some
for safety reasons, others for flavor. Some have to do with
expanding one's orientation to space-experiencing space
as spherical, dealing with disorientation, being able to fall,
roll, and ride momentum, alone and in physical contact
with another mover. I suppose this could be called spatial
conditioning.
Then there's time conditioning, which I'm just beginning
to take particular notice of. A lot of the meditations on
physical sensation (e.g. Paxton's "small dance") that have
always been a part of Contact training are actually as much
about inducing an experience of expanded time as they are
about the physical sensations they point up.
This sense of expanded time is a subjective state, one
where time feels mutable and there are slippages-where a
45 minute dance can feel like 5 minutes, while a 2 second
fall can feel luxuriously long. It is a relaxed state, one
where time seems to have been released from its inexorable
unit-to-unit march and allowed to roam in an open field
with wide horizons and no fences, a time whose divisions
and textures vary according to the moods and perceptions
of the people moving through it.
As with spatial training, this new temporal experience
may be puzzling at first. All too often you lose your place
in the dancing, but you find out through experience that
if you just hang on a few seconds longer (those seconds feel
like hours!) your place will come floating up under you like
106 CQICI Sourcebook
a pair of lost footprints. You come to trust and in fact look
forward to those gaps because in them you 're as vulnerable
and available as you can be, and from there come new per-
ceptions and some of the more surprising and refreshing
movement material.
But how do we get there from here? Especially when
we 're coming in to the studio from Chemistry or Shakes-
peare or Ballet or a 65 mile drive.
The transition from clock time to internal time can
begin during the personal warmup-a period of time often
given at the beginning of class to relax and "arrive," to get
ready physically and mentally to dance with others. It
deepens during the periods of extended duet improvising.
Absorption in the moment expands the feeling of time and
you come out of it enlivened no matter where you've been.
It's this state of absorption and enlivening that I aim for in
my own dancing and teaching.
I spent the first few weeks at Wesleyan searching in vain
for a quick passage, a trap door into this state. But how
could I hope to transmit the spaciousness, mutability, and
mystery of time when I myself was rushing to get in as much
dancing, skills, improvising, sensitizing, etc. as possible in
the 'short' time available?
Exhausted from my struggle with time, I gave up and
found that simply by relaxing I created more time. It felt
like practicing Aikido, where one redirects an opposing
force by first blending with it, not resisting it. Meanwhile,
of course, as I was internally wrestling with all this, the
students were rolling, giggling, getting dizzy, getting strong,
confused, inspired, thinking it was silly, feeling it was
profound and in general doing Contact Improvisation. As
has often been said, the dancing teaches itself, if the teacher
can only get out of the way long enough to let everyone
dance ....
P.S. After all this, I still think an hour and a half is too
short for a Contact class!
Nancy Stark Smith

These notes were taken in February

Steve Paxton

[Warmup, people sitting]
Relax deep into the cone of
the eye socket. Imagine a line that
runs between the ears. That's where
the skull rests. Make the motion, very
small, for 'Yes." This rocks the skull
on the top vertebra, the atlas. You
have to intuit the bones. Like a donut.
The sensation around it defines it. Do
the motion for "No." Between these
two motions you can determine the
length of the vertebra .
. . . . Ballooning of the lungs.
Breathe from the bottom of the lung
up to the clavicle. Can you expand the
ribs out & up & back easily? Defining
the diaphragm in terms of sensation.
Bottom of the lung. Two domes of
muscle. So with each breath you 're
massaging the intestine .... What the
diaphragm is doing is a signal to the
rest of the body. Sky above, earth be-
low ...
CQ/CI Sourcebook 107
The head in this work is a limb. It
has mass. Mass may be the single most
important sensation. The feeling of
gravity. Continuing to perceive mass
and gravity as you move. Tension in
the muscle masks the sensation of
gravity ...
[people squatting] You've been
swimming in gravity since the day you
were born. Every cell knows where
down is. Easily forgotten. Your mass
and the earth's mass calling to each
other ...
[standing] ... Upward force of the
bones. Shoulder blades fall down the
back, relaxing the intestines into the
bowl of the pelvis . . . In the direction
the arms are hanging, without changing
that direction, do the smallest stretch
you can still feel. Can it be smaller.
Can you do less. The initiation of
stretch, along the length of bones, in
the direction the force is already going.
The small dance-you're relaxing and
it's holding you up. The muscles keep-
ing the weight throughout the skeleton.
Shifting weight from leg to leg, inter-
face, taking weight, compression.
Stretching along the line of compres-
sion. Center of the small dance.
[people begin to move down to the
floor] Freeze. What stopped, in terms
of mass? ... Bridge the joints with
stretch, so you can transfer the weight
easily. Lubricate the motion, flow ...
Flow doesn't mean slow. Silent on the
floor. Don't leave sound tracks. [roll-
ing] So you're not depending on your
eye for the horizon. The room goes by
your eye.
[in partners] Put the tops of your
heads together. Just stand for a mo-
ment. The idea of a small dance, move-
ment in and around the skeleton, the
rush and pause of the muscles ... very
small ... try to match 50/50 the other
person's small dance without manipu-
lating yours .... then as in the way
you let the small dance move into walk-
ing, let the contact point roll around
the head. Let the body adjust to the
movement and pressure of that point.
What is important is to share that point
of balance ... rely on balance and the
fact that your muscle system will work
on automatic pilot ..... try working
in slow motion for awhile and see what
evolves.
Basically what you're doing now is
a model for the process that can happen
over the entire body. So, next time an
arm comes around, transfer the process
to that point, you can take it into the
limbs. Slow it down or let it go a little
faster. Return it to the head every once
in awhile ....
108 CQIG Sourcebook
Let the movement take you places
you don't plan to go ... nothing is
wrong ... no position is wrong in this
work ... once you are obeying the
laws of gravity, it's really hard to make
judgements about them. Keep periph-
eral vision open so you know what's
going on around you.
[during dancing] Are you comfort-
able? ..... strange? ...... are you
ugly? ........ etc. Is your partner
warm .... noble ..... frightened? ...
[half hour after pairing, about
11 am] Let the movement relax onto
the floor .... comfortable .... feel
afterimages of the movement you've
been doing ... rest .... how deeply
can you relax ... it seems to just go
on and on doesn't it ... you relax at
first, then you sense a little something.
... shoulder, scalp, or your body shifts
positions which allows a whole new set
of muscles to relax ... this body ....
such an ancient form ... we admire
things like the pyramids, they're so old,
but they're fading fast ... this move-
ment, this sensation ... always new
but so ancient ..... the body is a part
of this earth, a moving, dancing part,
and it knows how to deal with these
forces, you don't have to direct, ....
knows how to play .... knows how to
align ... it was made by these forces,
molded here .... breathing this air,
relaxing with this inertia .... Body ...
.... Body ...... This being wants
information .... that you have .....
[Steve is sitting on the side, the class is
lying on backs relaxing in various posi-
tions] .... This being ... is studying ..
... this body .... the sensations that
are constantly coming to these senses
... This being wants to play ... .
amongst those sensations .... Show
this being gravity, inertia and momen-
tum ... show this being the skeleton ..
.. so that they can move with ease ...
show this being the diaphragm, the
psoas, let this being breathe pure air ..
.. let this being feel the circulatory
system ... hear the whine of the nerves
. .. and the thump of the heart .....

CONT ACT AT VARI CK STREET by Ann Cooper Albright
Contact Improvisation has a
way of physically talking about people
and their times. Although there are
specific movement skills in Contact,
there are no set rules, and the creation
of a dance is determined entirely by
the bodies involved. Now it may well
be my own peculiar tendency to see
the world in a push or a pull, and a
friendship in a lift, but watching Con-
tact Improvisation makes me think
about more than just the dancing at
hand.
The group of people-Robin Feld,
Paul Langland, Daniel Lepkoff, Diane
Madden, Nina Martin, Jennifer Miller,
Susan Seizer, Randy Warshaw, and
Nancy Stark Smith-who presented a
Contact performance for Movement
Research at Ethnic Folk Arts Center,
December 7 & 8, 1985 are all capable
and experienced Contact dancers. Yet
their interests, expectations and danc-
ing are quite different. Contact Im-
provisation is 13 years old this year,
and the two longest duets in the first
half of the concert reflect what I see
as two diverse, yet not irreconcilable,
tendencies in the form today.
Sunday evening's concert begins
with the group fooling around the
periphery; walking, strutting or jogging
in threes .nd fours. A push-pull or
bumpinf. can create a brief interaction.
Someone squirms along by themselves
and Nina Martin makes an occasional
remark. In the midst of all this perco-
lation, Nancy Stark Smith quietly rolls
into the central performing area. A
kind of declarative hush comes out of
her strong yet peaceful movement.
Easing down to the floor as if she could
almost sink below it, she releases her
weight completely, without the dead-
ening thud that intermittently scores
the dancing of others. She is as loose
and slippery as her long, dark braid,
which now and again mirrors her
meanderings with a playfulness of its
own.
Randy Warshaw joins her and soon
they seem comfortably connected,
rolling back and forth across the floor
at a pace that comes less from one
body on top or underneath pushing
another, than from the roving point
where the weight of the two bodies
meet. There is little eye contact, they
hardly even face each other. In this
dancing they don't need their eyes.
They're using another kind of sensing,
an awareness of pressure. The blending
of their weight gives them the cue. It's
not their faces, words or hands but
their backs, the nudge of a shoulder,
or a turn of a knee that whispers the
directions. Even when a spiraling mo-
mentum separates them, there is a
kinesthetic sympathy that co-ordinates
Randy's loop through space with
Nancy's spin and arched back suspen-
sion so that somehow he is underneath
to support her when she finally begins
to fall. This is the kind of crystalline
moment that Contact at its best is
known for. A physical communication
that is so immediate and clear, it
doesn't need added words or gestures.
Nina Martin and Danny Lepkoff
danced the next prolonged duet in a
style that markedly contrasted Nancy's
and Randy's. To begin with, they
faced each other a lot-they had to.
For without the eye contact and the
facial signals, their playful barrages
could have been seriously dangerous.
Hands that pulled the partner towards
110 CQ,iO Sourcebook
Volume 11 1986
Daniel Lepkoff and Jul yen Hamilton, A Cappella Motion, 1985
themselves in an affectionate manner,
might switch tone and jerk the other
person away. The two bodies would
connect in a hip-check only to propel
off into separate directions. A lift
could lead to a sustained support, or
the lifter might just as easily drop his
burden and walk away.
These two dancers seemed to be
working from a set of character traits
that allowed them to spar lightly with
one another. Although moments and
movements suggested a certain aggres-
siveness, their flippant attitude and
laughter made it clear they were at
play. Their spirits were high and the
quick steps and fast, unexpected
changes of direction reminded me of
certain sports like basketball where
there is a constant shifting of direction
to outguess your partner-opponent.
Even though they rarely sustained
a physical connectedness, there was a
great deal of contact energy generated
between them. A line monitored the
space between them, a tension came
out of the constant questioning and
maneuvering in their steps. Finally
they did meet one time in a thrilling
lift that had so much momentum, that
it orbited Nina, who was spiraling on
top of Danny's shoulder, and Danny,
who was revolving around his own axis,
around and around the space. A burst
of cheer and claps greeted them as they
landed.
Good Contact Improvisation can
have both the exciting, funny, fierce,
mimicking, popping energy, as well as
the deep awareness of a physical com-
munication. The latter leads to the
former with skill and patience. But
what I see more and more frequently
is an immediate jump to the bouncy,
witty stuff of dancing. This is an easy
way to entertain ... for a while. It
tends, however, to run dry pretty
quick! y. I think this is because it is
not based on the deeper physical
Volume 11 1986
source of the dancing, the real connec-
tion of bodily weight. Sure I like the
pizazz, but I also like the heightened
awareness that makes vision, words
and gesture less important than physical
sensitivity. u

Over the years since Contact Improvisation's
inception in 1972, many Contact gatherings have occurred
all over the U.S., Canada, and more recently, in Europe.
The gatherings have been various-in duration, style, and
focus-including intensive workshops of several weeks dur-
ation, live-in, live-out, symposia, jams, classes, and
conferences, etc.
By the late seventies, one of the more regular topics for
discussion in these gatherings was Teaching. Though there
were still relatively few people actively teaching Contact,
the interest in teaching was growing, and so the discussions
were lively and well attended. At Current Exchange, a
month-long gathering in August of 1979 at the Western
Front Lodge in Vancouver, B.C., regular Contact classes
were offered to the public, taught by a rotating staff of
teachers from Current Exchange. This format gave us the
opportunity to directly experience one another's teaching,
in addition to the by now expected, but none the less
interesting, discussion.
Over the past few years, not only has the number of
Contact teachers grown, but there is considerably more
experience behind those numbers, and with it the desire
for a more extensive forum for exchange. The forums
have evolved quite organically from group discussions to,
for instance, adding a day to the 1985 Breitenbush jam
for a teacher's conference, then another two days at the
end of the 1986 Breitenbush jam. (And three days next
year?) The structure and function of these conferences
develops further each time, as we find out more about
WHY we are gathered, and WHAT we'd like to do together.
New forms evolve to fill those needs.
The last few years have seen a great surge in Contact
activity in Europe. (Witness the swelling of the 'Overseas'
section of the 'Contacts' list since, say, 1983.) Though
many American and Canadian Contact teachers visit Europe
to teach and perform, there are now just as many Europeans
(who in most cases have learned Contact from North Amer-
icans, either here or there) who are developing the work
and their practice though their own teaching on their home
turf and in guest workshops elsewhere in Europe.
Last Fall, before I went to teach in Amsterdam, I thought
it might be a good time to have a European teachers
gathering. Margot Rijven offered the administrative, organ-
izational, and hostessing support, and Aat Hougee and
Jaap Flier of the Theaterschool offered their space so we
were all set. Margot sent out a flyer to all the Europeans
on the 'Contacts' list, plus some other names she had. It
was very short notice but by the time the conference be-
gan, 25 people were there. Many who couldn't make it
telephoned or sent letters.
Several people took notes throughout the conference.
The coordinators of the 1e European Contact Newsletter
(Cathy Caraker, Margot Rijven, Nien Mari Chatz) gathered
and edited them for the Newsletter. The following report
on the conference is excerpted from that Newsletter, some
of it directly from Liz Irvine's notes, the rest from the
coordinators' compilation.
Nancy Stark Smith
THE REPORT

1e EUROPEAN CONTACT TEACHERS CONFERENCE
November 28 ·December 1, 1985
Theaterschool, Opleiding Moderne Dans
Amsterdam, Holland
PARTICIPANTS: Cathie Caraker, Walter Weiler, Celine
Theoret, Jaap Klevering, Jacqueline de Vries, Suzanne
Cotto, Jean Bloch-Laroque, Gonne Kooy, Moniek v.d. Laak,

Kammerer, Lulla Card, Didier Silhol, Regina Lamersdorf
