Meeting on THURSDAY, new faces, some familiar,
expectancy, excitement, why were we together? To meet
and dance, to exchange material, to discuss and share.
People from different countries-France, Holland, Britain,
Switzerland, Finland, Hungary, Germany, United States,
and Canada. English establishes itself as the first language
of the group with a continuing translation to French ...
already the spoken language suggests its power in group
interchange ... and as teachers, language as communicator
is a concern ... the word vocabulary translated from one
European language to another gives different meaning/
image/association ... and someone mentions the impor-
tance of non-verbal teaching/learning/communication. -+
Volume 11 1986
Later, people offer their personal histories and some
insight into their ways of working and the situations in
their location. A recurring theme is personal explorations
in movement that give rise to the question, Is this still
Contact?, e.g., exploration of emotions in the dancing;
inter-connection with other disciplines such as yoga, tai
chi and Aikido. Recurring also a sense of isolation.
After words we jam. Non-verbal communication. The
energy is high. Much to explore. A common language
exists.
FRIDAY begins class with Nancy. Some late arrivals.
Guided partner dance warm up. Back to back. Each
ground. Connecting through point of contact. Movement
beginning in pelvis gradually including whole back surface,
legs ... gravity, momentum come into play, the whole
room is moving ....
Discussions around the most important aspects of
Contact and problem areas in teaching occurred in smaller
groups of 5 to 6 people. Later, reporting back to the
large group, some of the issues brought up:
Importance of observation for learner and for teacher;
the consideration that watching may be the beginning of
"listening. "
The usefulness of demonstration in situations where
people are unfamiliar with the work.
That teaching is a discovery; that people discover for
themselves; that change is constant.
To seek in teaching a process where the joy of move-
ment may be found.
Care to not manipulate whilst directing experiences.
Questioning how to teach in institutions where the
Contact class is compulsory for the student.
How to set goals in a given situation.
Volume II 1986
How to relate wi.th students' physical pain and injuries
and mental problems.
How to work with groups of differing abilities.
Talking about movement seems a more complicated way
of communication than demonstrating the problem.
How to be( come) ready in body and mind to lead a class.
Awareness about judging ''good-bad."
Knowledge and acceptance of limits and habits, and how
to work with them.
State of mind and how that changes.
Integration of solo experience into contact?
Working with imagery; shifting from inside-focussed
activity to outside-focussed activity. How to teach that?
Is there a way of experiencing weight (the first times) and
not get stuck in heaviness?
Is "weight" a state of mind?
How to create an atmosphere in a class.
Teacher is reminder for physical memory.
Can we be specific and still be improvising?
Joanna Bartholomew, Jean Bloch-Laroque
and Suzanne Cotto in performance in Paris.
How can we share our "knowledge," experiences?
Am I still dancing while teaching?
Not necessarily emphasize difference between "begin-
ners" and "advanced, ".but creating structures that have
exchange happening.
At the beginning: important not to shock sensations of
body, but to build trust and confidence. Using awareness
of body-architecture.
How (when) to introduce subtle work like small-dance
or stillness-work?
Improvisation: the most obscure but most interesting
question in teaching.

Shifting activities while teaching: supervising, observing,
dancing, sensing, talking ... ·
Preparation: Nancy-"! make some notes,. makes me freer
to go in and to abandon whatever I planned. Can be a kind
of anchoring . .. Detours are often the most interesting . .. "
Teaching, what? Our personal concerns?
Moving with a partner: what's the range of communication?
(Contact and sexuality, Contact and emotions . .. )
Where is the heart of Con tact Improvisation? Where
are the borders from which it spreads and exchanges?
Stressing the exploration of the form in teaching.
Working in an isolated situation (or location) can
become a strength: challenging the development of
personal ways to dance/teach.
To give and take weight, fun, security, moving effortlessly,
confidence, unknown, open attitude, curiosity, the search,
extension, space (inner and outer), falling, relating with
ground, molecular dancing, elasticity of centre, transport,
travelling, inter-dependence, support, energy, rhythm,
balancing, momentum.
The afternoon is spent in trio feedback structure (2
dancers, 1 watcher, 20 minutes dancing/watching, 10
minutes giving and receiving feedback/discussion, then
switching roles, so all get to dance and to watch).
TEACHING EXCHANGE
SATURDAY begins with spontaneous warming up and
dancing.
The teaching exchange started with a discussion of
areas where people wanted to work-personal experiments,
ideas, difficulties, etc. We soon became involved in the
subject of being in the air. .
The initial question was how to use the force which
takes you away from center (centrifugal force) to meet
your partner again, and transfer that force into weight
exchange?
Here is an excerpt from the ensuing discussion:
Nancy: Maybe there's another way to approach from the
air that asks the other person to join your force-how do
you join it, how do you read it so that you can work with
it? To me it has to do with reading the movement before
it gets to you so you can join with it. So coming through
the air from different places, poses different problems to
solve in terms of forces, momentum.
Suzanne: How to stay flexible and open, to be able to
read that movement when it comes with so mu-ch forc;e?
Nancy: How to keep dancing in the air? People sometimes
throw themselves in the air, and then lose their sensitivity
while their partner just works with them. They are trying
to hold themselves together in the air and that makes it
heavy. They are not really there, they just want to get
llld CQ/C/ Sourcebook
down.
Karen: The place where you are being supported is a little
bit small, it's not like the floor ...
Mark: What about big men, I'm not used to relaxing in
that position.
Nancy: A lot of it has to do with if you're willing and
ready to fall frum any height, because if you 're completely
dependent on your partner or passive, you're in a funny
position anyway.
Karen: It takes time when you're up there to get used to
that, too, and to try to fall, in different directions.
Nancy: Also, when you're lifting someone who is big, to
feel that it's okay to let them go, that you can start and
follow through somehow without feeling that you have to
hold them up there-otherwise you don't even stlut, cause
you think it's too much.
Moniek: One step before this ... what kind of easy exer-
cises can stimulate this process-like rolling-for people
who don't have much experience?
Nancy: Falling is good, because falling is moving through
the air with gravity, and if people get comfortable with
falling, on the other side of the fall is something that comes
up. Falling gives you a force in an upward direction. It also
makes you unafraid to go down. The down goes up and the
up goes down, so if you can get the down part ...
Jaap: I'm interested in the problems of big men dancing
with small women. When I'm going up I often feel the
support collapses.
Elinor: It's usually the men that don't want to give their
weight to the women. They think they are too heavy.
Nancy: Sometimes I find with heavier people who are
afraid to give their weight, that they don't give their center.
The center is off to the side so I can't get under it to sup-
port it and then they think, "Yeah, see? Well they can't
support me" and so ... they just keep the problem going.
There are some men who are really quite large, and they
give their center and the weight stays alive and even if it's
a lot of weight you really can work with it a little bit. And
they feel secure and you feel secure, which leads to less
tension and thus more 'levity.' There's an exercise I
do sometimes, it's a climbing exercise and if it goes slow
enough you can feel and find your skeletal support.
There's no momentum involved and it's not particularly
light, it's quite heavy. But in the heaviness you find where
the weight can be supported and how it feels. Then later
when there's a little momentum it's lighter and you know
where the places are, where you can go to support the
weight (or be supported well).
Mark: Even if I go up, it's already become a habit that I
don't stay very long, so I never hang out there.
Jaap: I'm finding out that it's not the point to stay very
long, it's not the point that you're going up, the point is
that you're moving and you happen to be up.
Mark: But I'm talking about a technical level, as far as
knowing what that is, to be suspended in the air. I know
much less about it than you guys.
Nancy: Maybe something to do is just hang out up there
for one extra second. Again, it's like working with any --'*
Volume 11 1986
limit-because it's a rhythm, up and down, up and down ..
so you're just stretching or,opening the limit a little bit
instead of 'how can I really stay so long' but just two
more seconds and see what happens.
Jaap: But there's one point-like when I'm dancing with
Jaana for example, she's small, and when she's on me I
have a lot of places for support, and when I'm on her she
is really so small!!
Nancy: People who are good supports really know how to
use the movement continually for support as well as
surfaces. There's really very little that the person up there
has to do because there's so much riding the movement
that's happening-with the little shifts to get around the
shoulders, etc. Sometimes I really feel juggled by people.
It's not 'cause they're strong but it's all the little stuff
that they're doing. I want to learn to do that more. (To
Karen) You work with a man about your size so you 're
quite equal in your weight support, which is a great oppor-
tunity, because sometimes women don't lift small women
either for very long, because they're not used to having
someone up there very long.
In the afternoon we had the actual teaching exchange.
The mats were out and first Karen had us practicing Aikido
breakfalls. Then Nancy led us in some light disorientation
practice and we went on to jumping on people who were
posts, approaching from the air and reaching your partner
while still in that light state.
During this session one question came up which seems
essential to Contact teaching: When working on skills, does
teaching specific movements or exercises have the same
result as teaching a principle and improvising within that
principle? How can you teach people to improvise if
not by letting them find their own forms and ways to do
things?
Later on, Mark led a class which encompassed focussed
duets as well as a dynamic group dance with brief and
changing encounters. Focus: dancing with different people
while still maintaining awareness of your original partner's
center, then coming back to dancing with the original part-
ner with an awareness of the group.
Suzanne led an experiment which was about accumulat-
ing energy and force during a common stillness in the
dance, while still in physical contact ... until the accumu-
lated force releases into movement through space.
Later, watching "Magnesium" and "Chute" videos
evokes a sense of history.
Post-dinner networking discussion brings us to the
publication of a European Newsletter and the setting up
of a European Jam.
DISCUSSION ABOUT NETWORKING
What is the reason for networking?
Needs, desires could be divided in:
• To keep informed about the places where Contact is
being developed: classes, workshops, performances,
addresses, dates.
• To know possibilities to meet and to dance with other
Contact dancers. Includes: knowing places and dates
where and when Contact is going on; organizing Jams
in Europe. Mark Tompkins reported about the experiences
with the first European Jam in summer 1982 in the country-
side of Southern France, organized by L'Atelier Contact.
Important points were the necessity of good space and
sleeping accomodations; costs (admission fee to cover space
and organization, then there's room and board, and travel
cost at the individual's expense!). Suzanne, Jean and
Joanna offered to set up a Jam near Paris around the 1st
of May.
• To exchange about teaching and research in Contact.
Possibilities: To invite another teacher to your studio.
To continue with this Conference once a year. Important
considerations: Who will organize the next one? The
necessity of good housing. Costs as mentioned above plus
possibly the fee for inviting a special teacher (the presence,
guidance and knowledge of Nancy was very inspiring and
supporting). The length of this conference was generally
considered too short.
To deal with these aspects brought up the idea of starting
a Newsletter. We should be clear about its aims: If it
should be to inform each other in a central way; or if it
should be a way of sharing experiences and exchanging
ways of working. We ~ecided to choose for the way to
inform each other, and to leave the sharing of the work
to the Contact Quarterly. Organizational agreements:
• Type your own letter (one page) with information
about your teaching and up-coming events in your area.
• Each time there should be a coordinator.
• Two issues a year to coordinate with CQ deadline so it
can be a part of the magazine.
• Send money to the next coordinator if you'd like to
receive a copy (or else it cannot be made and sent).
We finished the conference realizing the NETWORK
IS A FACT! -tt
[More news from this Newsletter and deadline information for the
next one can be found in the CQ Newsletter in this issue./
The thing about falling down, if you do a fall and it gets a certain kind
of laugh, a laugh that almost precedes rather than follows the fall, well,
it's like falling on cushions of air ... When they don't laugh, it's like a
misspent effort, and boy, then you feel the bones hit the floor.
---Bill Irwin, clown

RECOGNIZING DANCE
I am in a form. A form of discipline. Focused outwards and inwards. Making connections, which
make me to dance. I'm a mover alright. Eventually I'm moving the walls and the space between the walls.
Me, as a centering organ, as a source of strength. (One powerful enough command and I would fly up,
straight to the ceiling.)
What I really do is hard to explain. I'm the centre of the space around me alright, and also of everything
else, moving in that space. And it sometimes feels like it's me who makes it to move.
I know of course, it's me who's moving, but something inside my skull stays steady, in one place, never
shoving out of place ... however much I'm going upside down, through whatever angles. In fact, the more
angles I'm making, the more steady th is one part of me becomes. And the more steady this observing part
of me is, the better I can make the world around me move.
It's an interesting discovery. My body no longer distracted, because my organs no longer protest against
every leap I make. There is an adjustment which makes it possible for my mind to follow the body, and for
my body to follow the mind.
Moving around I'm getting closer. Moving around I'm opening up. Getting aware of other people in the
same space. Getting aware of other bodies (like mine, but different) moving around (but telling a different
tale). Getting aware I'm actually moving in space which is shared by all the other bodies in the very same
time. And by sharing this space, I'm sharing a conception. I'm sharing a movement, with every echo of it
rolling between the walls of the space, wherein my body is responding, like a boat on the water.
Effecting and being effected: my mind, with images. Mildly changing, disturbing changing, or however.
My body, in the space. And some of the bodies around me, dancing. With the same mild flow or strangeness
or confusion. However these waves in this stretch of time flow. And however much a mind is willing to
allow changes to happen.
My steady me is observing this. This part of me is the only part which doesn't let go. It's the part which
must lay hidden in layers there are in my brains. It's a part which comes out on those moments/in those
movements, when everything else is swimming in the stream. Comprehension. Understanding. Not the
detail. Not the current. Not the face that expresses and reveals, but a sort of silent memo. A sentence like:
"I was still, no movement at all." Which is nothing more than recognizing a situation and accepting the
absolute truth of it. Which means that on that moment, there is a balance of forces, focused on the moving
body. And the observation of such is the key of control.
Then my steady part falls back inside its hidingplace and there are only all of my senses left, sprawling in
the dance. Dancing with other bodies, all in the same room. Space, time separating. An improvisation
about survival.
Going around somebody's back, falling down, head first towards the floor, rolling, jumping up. Waiting,
out of balance. Being out of balance all the time. That's the essence of this dance form.
Trying to cope with that which moves around you. Trying to grasp a shift of weight. Feeling the floor
move away and around. Focusing eyes, sensing ears, balancing feet, flexing spine.
Finding a way to the end of that rope. Landing on hands and feet. Feeling the ground getting to rest,
while sitting. Waiting for echos to finally fade. Walls becoming walls and bodies becoming personal objects.
A sense of distance restored once more ...
Jaap Klevering
112 CQ/CI Sourcebook Volume 11 1986
TAKING NO FOR AN ANSWER
Today is my birthday and I'm sitting in my car,
parked, with the engine running, outside our building,
watching the shadow of the building rise on a high pile of
snow as the sun goes down behind the building. Maybe
sitting here facing this blank page is the closest I'll be able
to get in writing to the feeling of improvising. Because
how can you describe something that isn't there yet? I
want to be able to write from inside the movements of an
improvisation and tell from there how things look, how
they feel, how they're going. But I keep finding myself
back here walking the line, on the page, trying to make
pens pirouette, words walk and ideas bounce and split
open, like dancing can do. I'm impressed and informed by
all the efforts evident in this issue of people putting a
finger on what isn't there. It's like talking about a hole.
In this issue, we're trying to say what shapes the hole
from within.
In the early '70s, when I was first getting immersed in
the world of practicing and performing dance improvisa-
tion, I spent a lot of time watching the Grand Union•
perform. For hours, I would see material surface on
shaky ground, get nourished, worked, referred to, develop-
ed, and I'd see it begin to strengthen, come into its own,
and become the ground for what would happen next. I
saw material be given life or death (which in itself could
become the next material), and I learned that there was,
in practice, no inherent hierarchy of material. Every move
had equal potential to unify, clarify, destroy, or transform
what was going on. It was not just the material itself but
how and when it was delivered that gave it depth and
power.
Where you are when you don't know where you are is
one of the most precious spots offered by improvisation.
It is a place from which more directions are possible than
anywhere else. I call this place the Gap. The more I im-
• A Dance Theater improvisation group including Yvonne Rainer,
David Gordon, Steve Paxton, Trisha Brown, Barbara (Lloyd)
Dilley, Nancy (Green) Lewis, and Douglas Dunn.
Volume 12 1987
Volume 12
1987
provise, the more I'm convinced that it is through the
medium of these gaps-this momentary suspension of
reference point-that comes the unexpected and much
sought after 'original' material. It's 'original' because its
origin is in the current moment and because it comes from
outside our usual frame of reference.
When Katie Duck teaches improvisation, as she has
many times as a guest teacher at the Theaterschool in
Amsterdam, she often tells people not to use their first
impulse, but instead to wait for maybe the second or third
before taking action. This ability to wait and not follow
the impulse is a lot like learning how to break a habit.
Holding the moment open a few seconds longer widens
the gap where the old behavior/idea/movement/thought/
feeling would have gone. And maybe you have nothing
to put in its stead. So you put in nothing.
On January 28th, it was a year since I stopped smoking
(and, coincidentally, a year since the space shuttle Chal-
lenger exploded). I never stopped before and the change
feels enormous. Every time I want a cigarette and don't
have one I'm creating a gap. Moments that once were
easily and automatically filled, have become uneasily and
consciously unfilled. By leaving them unfilled, I'm not
only breaking a 'momentum of being', a pattern of behav-
ior, but I'm bringing attention and charge to a moment
that would have passed without remark. One of the things
that improvising and stopping smoking have in common is
that they both require an appetite for learning. Both have
the capacity to take you to new ground. Both are about
unconscious pulls and conscious choices, about reckoning
with habitual perspectives and behaviors.
Being in a gap is iike being in a fall before you touch
bottom. You're suspended-in time as well as space-and
you don't really know how long it'll take to get 'back'.
And then, when you do finally get back, no one even
noticed you were gone.
Nancy Stark Smith
CQICI Sourcebook 113
THE AWARENESS OF CHANGE
by Chris Mathias
The state or space created in
the workshop setting decreases
the stimuli of the outside world
so that the internal impulses of
our dancing can be felt, seen and
acted upon. Once the outer
arrangement has less of an influ-
ence, then the inner space be-
comes fuller, more alive and its
range of possibilities become
wider. The awareness of change
in the inner space becomes vivid
so that when the outer space of
our world increases its influence,
we do not lose the ground we
have gained from the inner world.
These statements were gleaned
from a month's worth of writings,
thoughts and discussions I participated
in at Naropa Institute Summer 1986.
However vague and general, they seem
to apply to the state of mind we reach
when awareness is brought to our
dancing. During the course of the four
weeks of Contact Improvisation classes
taught by Nancy Stark Smith, we talk-
ed a lot about the "Ready State,"
never actually defining it, but neverthe-
less knowing when it had occurred.
There never seemed to be a standard
way of preparing the body to dance,
but one ingredient was always required.
Awareness of body sensation, move-
ment and body/mind states. Once in
the "Ready State," every part of our
being participates actively. When not
ready, some part of us hangs back,
watches, refusing to participate, creat-
ing a rift within our own dancing and
between us and our partner.
114 CQ/CI Sourcebook
It seems that awareness implies
action, and if this is true, then in the
"Ready State" of Contact Improvisa-
tion we must actively know our sensa-
tions, movement and mind states. To
know actively, or to bring awareness to
dancing allows expression; the exchange
of one body of knowledge with anoth·
er. In my ignorance, I seemed to think
that the "Ready State" was something
to be achieved. A way of doing this
was to identify the stages one might go
through to reach the "Ready Stire."
They came as follows:
1. Getting into the body-requires
a shifting of mind into bodily terms,
i.e., sensation, imagery and movement
quality.
2. Body time-now in the body,
the action is of deepening quality.
3. Body Sensitivity---,body sensation
and states of mind become finely
tuned. The sensitivity to and aware-
ness of body sensation and body minds
is increased.
4. Body Expression and Exchange-
moving out of inner sensation and into
exchange with a partner. The body be-
comes the mode from which I relate.
Although these stages offered some
insight into the process, they also told
me something about my ambition to
achieve something that I thought was
necessary. The "Ready State" requires
only to be where you are, not where
you think you should be. This slight
shift means bringing an awareness to
the changes happening in our dancing;
changing and guiding the changes as
well, so that the dancing becomes an
exchange between the inner and outer
impulses. This awareness is full bod-
ied, the mind responds to the body
and the body responds to the mind. It
is a way of being with change. Being
comfortable with frustration, ambigu-
ity and basically not knowing what
comes next. A way of being which
begins without expectations of what
will come, freeing you to be with
whatever is occurring.
In looking back at the time spent
at Naropa, I see that we seemed to
create a space for ourselves that aimed
for freedom from distraction. We
cleared and prepared ourselves to
dance in the most honest way we knew
how. The discipline of Contact Im-
provisation requires this clarity of
body, mind and spirit, so that the
person can deepen within the form
and of equal importance, enjoy their
dancing.{::{
TEACH I NG SCORE 1
PUPPET-PUPPETEER: Jonathan Smith
I started the class off with unstructured solo warm up, asking for people
to soften, and to move without working at it, and then continue in this mode
into a slow duet warm up dance with the same qualities. The material that I
presented was what I call the puppet-puppeteer motif. I developed this style
of movement out of an image from a Robin Feld class working with manipu-
lations. She was dressed in black and was moving a colorfully dressed person.
I asked people to start off with a light touch, suggesting working the pup-
pet by the upper body from behind, using the puppet's upper arms, or by
pulling and leading from in front. The puppeteer does all the initiation; the
puppet can follow through but not initiate. For example, the puppeteer can
move the puppet's arm up and the puppet's arm can then slowly float down
like a feather. Then the quality can get heavier, the puppeteer can bring the
puppet into his or her center, taking more weight, nurturing and caring for
the puppet; finally bringing the puppet to the floor, rocking it and putting it
to sleep. I used this motif in a section of a dance that I created and performed
in New York, June 1985, called "The River of Cause (because because because
because because)."

NOTES ON THE 1986
BREIT~NBUSH CONTACT JAM
AND TEACHER'S CONFERENCE
by Kurt Koegel
The jam/conference was a dialogue
between doing and contemplation,
playing freely and then defining what
seemed to be happening. It was a spa-
cious time where together we sculpted
our shifting understandings of the
work.
One of the joys of this process of in-
quiry was its immediacy-the being
able to move from a stimulating discus-
sion straight into dancing-or coming
upon a discovery dancing ... and
having the context, right there, to im-
mediately share it with someone, or
the group ... so to articulate the
elusive find .. .
In this setting the work was given
range, depth, and focus-we experi-
enced it as a vehicle of personal expres-
sion and development and as an influ-
ence in the structure of today's society.
And the conference was about
COMMUNITY.
Other than finding out about the broad
and concentrated research that is hap-
pening, I recall the connectedness and
power I experienced'there. I've been
in many group situations and worked
on many projects, but I've never felt
such a strong, nurturing, and inspiring
sense of community.
There were no leaders or followers-
rather we were all leaders and follow-
ers, speakers and listeners, guides 'and
journeyers. There were plenty of op-
portunities for both taking in new in-
formation, ideas and processes-and
presenting ideas or formats or situa-
tions. It happened in structured ways-
such as the classes where four different
people would take a segment and pre-
sent an approach to teaching-and it
happened in informal meetings, dia-
logues, and dances.
This in itself was a melding of idea and
practice. One evening Steve Paxton got
into a 'where-does-contact-improvisa-
tion-fit-in to-the-history-of-dance' talk,
and described that one of the roots of
contact was its aim to break with the
conventional representations of social
structure; to deal with relationships
between people on a more anarchistic/
democratic level.
And true to its form, this open struc-
ture allowed a lot of energy to be ex-
changed. Not only did we discover our
similarities, but also our differences.
There were questions of style & process
& orientation that were not always
easy for people to negotiate-yet a will-
ingness to encompass it all and work it
through pervaded. And again, the flu-
ency .between these states was really
excmng, and I find, now gives me a
new perspective when working with
peers. Something about keeping the
space open enough to allow for the
free flow of input from all.
And we looked at the question of
PERFORMANCE ... is performance a
relevant part of contact improvisation?
. .. is it appropriate to put the work
out for audiences to see? One answer
was Steve Paxton's comment, "Yes.
For me, performing is the reward you
get for being allowed to handle the
idea."
Performance is putting the work into a
domain where other people can respond
to it, where the ideas & forms will be
examined and thereby shaped.
In the performance setting, the audi-
ence is bringing a mindset-this helps
bring the evocative power of allegory
and metaphor to the activity of the
dance.
And one of the values of performance
in improvisation to me is simply the
looking for ways to shape the work-
( or have the Work shape itself) into a
self-complete form, being able to tran-
scend the realm of research and move
into the realm of .... at least some-
thing which is enjoyable for people to
watch, participate in through watching
and reacting, through the natural use
of metaphor-putting their life into
action-out there, and providing for
the audience, just through the sheer
pleasure of the movement, the oppor-
tunity for them to relate to ....
WEIGHT
rising and falling
floating
diving
up
rushing
bouncing
hang-gliding
jagged free jazzing
So in the dancing (as well as in consid-
ering the direction the work as a whole
is t~king) it's all about "being able to
be m the spaces of uncertainty & am-
biguity with confidence ... "
We were considering the question,
"What is contact improvisation?," and
Nancy's comment was, "a group like
this preserves the recipe."
The work is, and will become, what
we bring to it.

TRIO CONTACT POINT: Sharon Tomsky
A trio score. Three people in contact. One in the middle-touching both
of the outside people. The 2 outside people are not touching each other-
but they are dancing together. (Looking at each other is useful.) The person
in the middle is the "point of contact" between them. The communicator
the sender of messages; transferring information back and forth-simultane'-
ously receivin.g & sen~ing information, about the contact-weight, pressure, etc.
The place in the middle seems to be a real different place for people to
dance from. Goin~ into a place ~f no-mi~d perhaps. Or no ego. Learning
ab~ut wha~ the pornt of c~ntact 1s .. outs~de people learning about communi-
catin.g. Bein~ clear. R~lating. Stay1.ng with the other person. Listening.
Sensing. Paying attention to the point of contact. (I learned this exercise
from john LeFan.)

FREELANCING WITH CONTACT
by Paul Langland
This piece was written in 1979. We
discovered it in our files and found the
issues in it still very much alive. We
talked with Paul about it and be agreed.
He also noted that shortly after this
period, performances of improvisation
in New York City seemed to disappear
for several years. [Eds.]
I suspect there are many
people presently involved in moving
art forms who, except for exposure to
contact improvisation, would not have
entered the field. My experience is a
case in point. As an art major in col-
lege, I enjoyed creating large sculptural
environments for the body. The
weight and awkwardness of the mater-
ials, plywood, two-by-fours, and other
construction hardware, turned building
a piece into a physical work-out.
Sculpting was an arranging dance,
using the whole body.
During this time (1973), Curt Sid-
dall, Nita Little, Steve Paxton, Nancy
Stark Smith, and someone whom I re-
member well except for her name
[Karen Radler] came to perform and
teach contact on the west coast for
the first time. I danced some and
watched a lot. The idea of people
presenting natural movement as a
performance was a pleasant jolt to me.
The democratic give and take between
partners was appealing. Improvising
dance, creating while performing was,
for me, a revelation. And my person-
ality was more comfortable with the
social circumstances of dancing than
the solitude of sculpture.
My first dancing was suffused with
comparisons between sculpture and
contact. It was as if the four-by-eight
plywood sheets I had been building
with developed power steering, and
could even lift me off the ground.
photo© 1978 Leslie Cerier
Volume 12 1987
The three-dimensional experience of
sculpture became four-dimensional,
the fourth dimension being the time
of the dance. I was no longer the
manipulator. For every impulse of
mine, there was another independent
nervous system deciding what was hap-
pening as well. I saw that contact is
very purely about process. In terms
of sculpture, that meant showing how
fascinating it is to make a piece, rather
than showing a finished piece.
Through performing, I like letting
others in on the creating side of the
work, the side most interesting to me.
Since that time, I've performed in
many different situations; contact,
other improvised structures, and set
pieces. In watching dance or perform-
ing it, I'm drawn to dancing which
doesn't impose a style on the people
who dance. This may partly be a
hangover from art training which en-
courages students to find an individual
vision from the start of training. I'm
glad this idea has a lot of credence in
the dance world now.
Improvisation is one way of work-
ing individualistically either in a group
or solo. A problem is that improvisa-
tion and performance are sometimes
friends. We all know of our on nights
and our off nights. Those who perform
improvisation have decided that the
pluses outweigh the minuses. The
pluses are that the dancers are present-
ing a raw slice of life, uncut, uncooked.
The audience's position is elevated be-
cause they are allowed to get in on the
whole story as it happens for the first
time. They aren't watching something
which has been edited in rehearsal.
Improvisation is risky and often re-
warding for the performers. It is a way
for performers to move on with work,
not to stick with an image or sequence
Volume 1 2 1987
"What did you think of what you saw, asked Miss Stein . ..
Only that Picassos were rather awful and the others were not.
Sure, she said, as Pablo once remarked, when you make a thing,
it is so complicated making it that it is bound to be ugly, but
those that do it after you they don't have to worry about making
it and they can make it pretty, and so everybody can like it
when the others make it."
Gertrude Stein
which must be repeated several times.
The formal qualities of contact make
it a focused and grounded improvisa-
tional technique. There is a basic
agreement between performers which
provides a continual springboard into
the dance. A performer of contact has
a lot of freedom.
I'm sitting here writing about how
good a performance improvisation
makes, and resisting tooth and nail the
detractions. However, there are some.
There is no overall structure to control
the impact of the performance. It's
like having several small dances linked
together. Dull repetition can be a
problem. When this happens, it's like
watching the same rehearsed dance
several times in one piece. This is one
of the main limitations in previous
dance work which many of us are try-
ing to avoid by investigating improvi-
sation. An ideal piece has few seams
showing. There are a lot of ways to
approach this, but they mostly have to
do with pre-planning. It's difficult to
achieve in an improvised performance.
There also are some beautiful possibili-
ties in pre-setting a piece; it can have
long sections of synchronized move-
ment, it can be a story, it can have
special timing, or it can deal with the
personae and not the personality of
the performer. The search for these
qualities is improvised but their presen-
tation is, for the most part, set.
Safety is important in contact.
This results in contact being playful,
game-like, and positive. There is a
utopian communal quality to contact-
a great experience but not the whole
picture. This one-keyed quality can be
limiting for an audience, and make
performing frustrating. There is little
room to explore subjects like discord,
tragedy, or events which are fantastic
or grotesque. Sometimes I want to
entertain/be entertained with these
rich images. Sometimes I want the
purity and abstraction of contact.
When I perform contact, I like to
feel that we, the performers, are shar-
ing the performance with the audience.
Among the most satisfying perform-
ances in this respect was a series done
at the Paula Cooper Gallery in New
York City [1978). We performed in
the afternoon for three days, three
hours per day. The audience was free
to come and go. Those who stayed
wanted to be there. As the perform-
ance progressed, the size and closeness
of the audience increased. Neither the
audience nor the piece was set. There
was a cumulative effect from perform-
ing for a long time for three days in a
row. Our dancing became very sure,
and many audience members returned
two or three times to watch. By the
end of the third day we all felt like
good friends. We felt like the home
team.
In the last year, I've been doing
contact wearing different costumes.
The costumes put a context into the
dancing. Nina Martin and I did cos-
tumed contact as part of a living sculp-
ture exhibit at the Susan Caldwell
Gallery. Every half-hour or so, we
changed clothes. We danced in paja-
mas, swimming suits, winter clothes,
formal attire, and disco polyester. The
costumes interest me because they
provide a lasting image, however weird,
of what the dancing is about. A sub-
liminal story unfolded with each
change. (Paul and Nina went to the
prom all dressed up. They felt good
and started rolling on top of each
other. Nina's hat got squashed, so she
_,.
CQICI Sourcebook 117
[Statement written by Steve Paxton, ca. 1977]
There is currently some controversy in
Canada: is CONTACT IMPROVISA-
TION a performance art?
Contact Improvisation is determined
by two people at once, who both im-
provise freely, trying not to impede
the work of the other. Spectators
watch a physical conversation which
may range from stillness to high athlet-
icism. The reasons for the change in
scale of movement or any other
changes can be analyzed from emotion-
al, physical or psychological levels. ?r
many levels at once. The spectator 1s
watching real people pressed by events
and time. It is improvisation. Neither
participant is totally in or out of con-
trol. The spectator does not look
through the performers to a mind and
its aesthetics. One looks squarely at
the performers and their choices, reac-
tions, and instincts, to what happens
between them.
What is revealed is mutual under-
standing, a basic system, a mode of
communication. Touch. The fast and
subtle skin processing masses, vectors,
emotions, giving the muscles the in-
formation to correctly move the bones,
so the duet can fall through the time
and space of demonstration, neither
partner hurt, hampered, subjected, ob-
jectified.
Is it performance art? Performance
has several levels of meaning. So does
art. No one is defining their terms.
They seem to mean, "I didn't enjoy
the way it went," or, "If Tharp is, this
ain't. " No matter. Go to it, Canadian
Critics. Get it on conceptually. Or
you could take a way out devised by
those who perform Contact Improvisa-
tion .... they call it an art-sport.
Steve Paxton
118 CQ/CI Sourcebook
threw it away while climbing on Paul's
back. They both fell into the buffet
table, pensively. When the music
started, Paul rolled up Nina's body and
she supported him upside down with
his jacket wrapped around his head.)
Someone else may be having other
daydreams as they watch. Our only
plan was to do contact after putting
on the different clothes, not to inject
the movement itself with any plot or
meaning. The dancing did feel very
different each time we changed, mostly
because the physical sensations of each
outfit were so different. Winter
clothes to swimming suits was a big
change. As at the Paula Cooper Gal-
lery, the audience could come and go.
We felt very connected to the audience
that stayed with us. Long perform-
ances are satisfying. We had a long
time to allow the dancing to grow and
change. Where we got the stamina tq
do a three hour duet, I don't know.
Solo space is something I want to
see and do more of. It's a way for in-
dividual eccentricities to come out.
Voice work, props, or other movement
forms are possible without being filter-
ed through that great equalizer, the
contact duet.
Contact ideas have been used as
parts of different dance performances
very successfully. Often I think of
contact as a technique, or resource,
rather than the thing to be performed.
Here are just a few of the ways I liked
contact being incorporated. In Simone
Forti's Planet,.Terry O'Reilly and
David Taylor did a low, slow, slither-
ing contact duet with intermittent
pauses based on reptilian movement
while other animal-inspired dancing
was happening at the same time. In
Meredith Monk/Ping Chong's Chacon,
Eli Pollock did a contact solo as the
basis for his role as a drunken or crazy
villager. Mary Overlie's The Figure has
a section of "spatial contact"; dancing
mostly without touching, but reacting
to external sensing, and developing a
flow with a partner(s). Last year, at
the Contact Quarterly benefit in New
York City, about twenty different
contact pieces were shown. The varie-
ty was tremendous. There was a lot
of experimenting and potential.
Contact is democratic. Performers
have equal opportunities in perform-
ance which, for me, is one of its great
attractions. This equality shows in
performance. The round robin form,
and the freedom to start and stop
dancing when you want is politically
healthy for us humans. All contactors
have a decision-making role as they
dance, becoming their own directors.
I have performed frequently in
companies with directors, especially
with Meredith Monk. What the direc-
tor/company situation offers is greater
specificity and depth of vision. The
work process in The House (Meredith's
company) is interesting. She frequent-
ly sketches out what to work on. We
improvise and then set the most suit-
able material during rehearsal. Then
work is further edited, or expanded
and arranged with the other material
in the piece. There are often sections
of structured improvisation. In creat-
ing and performing, each performer
has some responsibility as author,
director, and actor.
My thoughts on performance are
open ended. Performing contact is
rewarding, and there is an audience
for it. It's reminiscent of a sports
event minus the competition; the
focus is on the moment to moment
interplay of individuals rather than the
outcome. A set piece offers other pos-
sibilities, both as performer and viewer.
The two don't exclude each other. I
hope the' multiplicity of performing
possibilities continues to thrive and
cross-fertilize. 1::

CALIFORNIA
HARBIN HOT SPRINGS
CONTACT JAM
The first CALIFORNIA HARBIN HOT SPRINGS CONTACT JAM was real good.
I hod a nice time. A lot of people come from where /live-the Bay Area-but some from
Chico, one from Boulder and a few from other West Coast towns.
Being a newcomer to the contact community, I felt unfamiliar with what a jam
was supposed to be. You know, I hod read some earlier issues of CQ, taken a couple
classes from N. S. Smith at Wesleyan U., even did some Cl in NYC at PS122. But, going
away for a long weekend with complete strangers to bathe in hot pools and dance didn't
exactly make me feel at home, or at ease.
What was the purpose of a jam, besides a reunion for those who hod already been
practicing the form for awhile? I could tell there were some of us who were new to this
kind of situation and didn't know precisely what was going on. This initial awkward
time reminded me of my first high school dance.
About everyone agreed that music and dance should be present (but separate
some of the time) during the weekend. A lot of us, including myself, were interested in
not setting particular times for dancing and drumming or even bedtime. Someone re-
marked that the time might be felt organically.
At first, not a lot of time was spent collectively breathing or arriving. "Plunging
in" is what seemed to be the desire of many. Maybe the hot springs pools provided the
springboard for this desire. I couldn't join the flow, though, because plunging in is a
fear of mine. I needed to toke time to sit and think and tune into myself for what I
wanted from this jam.
We were in a completely new world at Harbin, a rustic 7000 mile woodland,
mountainous tree-lined areas volleying and rising oil around our enclosed dance shelter.
A small brook ran by with clear water from the Napa Valley (wine country here in
California). But with oil that beautiful natural environs, it was still troubling to me not
knowing the jam boundaries. What were the rules of the game, as they soy? I sow possi-
bilities in group sex, a homosexual and heterosexual couples' scene, raucous contact,
soft-eye dancing, rolling and breathing.
All this stuff I felt Friday night or so. Then, on Saturday morning, things opened
up. Some of the senior members of the group taught half-hour mini-courses in Contoct-
esque basics and new discoveries. I went up to the pools in the afternoon. It wosn 't so
bod there. It was softer and quieter than in the dance shelter. Hazy and lazy, strong
and light, mole and female, folks were just chatting and healing. Four of us novice con·
tocters huddled our arms together in the pool and floated around like a spinning wheel.
We talked about what we sow Friday night. To my surprise, we shored a lot of common
observations. Then, we steamed in the hottest tub. We walked out ofter\\·ords, my
bones felt heavy and my muscles suspended around my bones.
On Saturday night, .the jam got together in a circle to discuss the moi•ement of
the weekend. While people talked, someone tossed small coloriul ploybolls bock and
forth in the circle. It looked like a game of marbles. You would toss a boll into the
center and someone would try to knock yours out of the center and onto the periphery.
Then, I forget who rolled herself into the center as a human marble. People giggled and
rolled her around. Others joined her. That beautiful stock of 64 pillows by the \\·all
become the next marbles, and a huge and monstrous pillow fight ensued. Finale: a dive
bombing affair.
Pillow rumble, it was real good. A lot of heavy stuff. Straight, bold and big
strides over obstacles into the pile of tossed pillows. The rest of the weekend was fun.
I think we, as a group of disparate individuals, discoi·ered our challenge: a common
ground. I think we also discovered a commitment: on organic message. To me, it was
on unfurling of the foll, a movement outward and down across the floor. As I spoke
with someone on Sunday afternoon on the trail bock from our lost dip in the pool, I
felt a return.
Andrew Clibanoff

CQICI Sourcebook 119
One of nine studios at the Theaterschool (TMDD) in Amsterdam.
by Aat Hougee
translated by Leab Jacob
It is roughly 5 years ago that in
the curriculum of the Modern Dance
Department of the Theaterschool
(TMDD) in Amsterdam, Holland, the
subject of composition was replaced,
without too much discussion, with the
concept of 'improvisation'. It was not
just a matter of altering the name, but
a conclusion brought about by the
department's gradually changing vision
of the function and content of the
process of composition within dance
education.
In the department's eyes, the con.
cept of 'composition' had become too
static a hea:ding to apply to a subject
that actually is at the core of its teach-
ing-the search for new forms. As it
had recently been taught in our school,
'composition' appeared to take for
granted a correct and incorrect way, a
belief that compositional truths already
existed. Thus, for educators, it seemed
artistically more interesting to busy
oneself with the process that leads to
a form than with the results of a pro-
cess undergone by other people at
120 CQICI Sourcebook
other times.
'Improvisation' as a concept, it
seemed, (though we were at that point
unable to comprehend all the implica-
tions), had more to do with this ex·
plorative organic approach. In fact, in
the course of time, it came to embody
a rich variety of aspects and possibili-
ties, challenging the whole teaching
field with its far-reaching influence.
Mostly as a result of Pauline de
Groot's work, the department takes as
its starting point the view that develop-
ing into a dancer means developing in-
to an artist. This creative development
can in no way be set apart from the
young dancer's growing physical capa-
bilities. This premise means that in
every movement class the student is
being asked to arrive at a physical
shape by tapping his/her own creative
resources, even in those classes where
the movement form is provided by the
teacher. The idea of making use of
the student's own potential is in fact
a very obvious starting point, but it
does demand a certain attitude from
the student, a state of being that is
open. Especially in this area of attain-
ing a 'state of mind' enabling further
Volume 12 1987
physical and creative development, a
variety of improvisational forms can
play an important role in teaching.
The department views as a cohesive
whole the making of work, the train-
ing that leads to this, and the person's
physical/creative development. It is a
complex process of growth, with the
achievement of form through essential-
ly investigative methods of working as
its continual starting point. It is also
true, within the process of composi-
tion, that methods arising from moving
are more efficient and achieve more
logical results than most that stem
from a more static and dogmatic view
of composition. Improvisation repre-
sents an approach applicable to almost
every aspect of teaching: it is investi-
gative; allows, while practicing, an
overview of what is possible; and
teaches one to make choices. This
weaving of improvisation methods
through the whole of teaching-across
a much wider area than simply compo-
sitional techniques-has, in a way, led
to some confusion: which improvisa-
tion methods should be employed,
when, and to what purpose?
The TMDD has tried several times
to plan more effectively by drawing up
a list of approaches and goals. These
arose on the one hand out of the vari-
ous approaches to improvisation
launched, in a somewhat intuitive
way, by the department directors in
their choice of certain teachers over
that last few vears, and on the other,
out of the ai~s of the teaching faculty.
Looking down this list, it very quickly
becomes obvious that what can be
designated by a general heading is
limited: most improvisation forms
(still) seem to be intrinsically bound
with the particular teacher, and this
would seem to be more or less charac-
teristic of the phenomenon of improv-
isation. At first, wc tried to make dis-
tinctions along the lines of improvisa-
tion forms that had already been
taught by the TMDD faculty and
various guest artists, with the ultimate
thought that from such a list it should
Volume 1 2 1987
be possible to arrive at a logical frame-
work which would remain, as far as
possible, the same through successive
groups of students and over a period
of four years.
In theory, we could make the
following distinctions: (1) physical
improvisation, (2) associative improv-
isation, (3) improvisation from struc-
tures, ( 4) improvisation from creative
imagery.
Although such a list can certainly
stimulate interesting discussions and a
theoretical elaboration of it would un-
doubtedly yield material for further
inquiry, for the purposes of education
this approach has not contributed
much more than the conclusion that,
to a greater or lesser extent, the
aspects listed are intrinsically part of
most improvisational working methods.
It isn't yet possible to distill a clear
program description of classes.
Another type of categorization
would probably provide more starting
points, namely one in which there is a
distinction between improvisation as a
means to an end and improvisation as
an end in itself. Here too it isn't a
black and white distinction that is be-
ing suggested but reference points on a
sliding scale: at the one end, improvi-
sation methods used as a bridge
towards developing physically/creative-
ly; or towards working choreographi-
cally; and at the other end, improvisa-
tion as ultimate artistic goal.
It seems possible to establish a pro-
gram for working with improvisation
over a 4-year course, making a distinc-
tion analogous to that above and con-
structed according to this sliding scale.
But looked at more methodically, it
still defied completion. Especially
with regard to 'improvisation as ulti-
mate goal' there seems to be little
systematic material available. Probably
because, in this area, the personality
and vision of the teachers concerned
seem to have to play a decisive role.
Earlier in this article it was men-
tioned that improvisational working
methods have been slowly impregnat-
ing all aspects of teaching at the TMDD.
Through experience, we realized in the
department that in order for this inte-
gration to occur, two important condi-
tions had to be in place.
In the first place, this work requires
students who are willing and able to
take as a starting point in the learning
process their capabilities and limita-
tions, just as it requires teachers who
take as a starting point in their teach-
ing not the form they have chosen,
but the process leading to a form. For
students and teachers alike it is impor-
tant to have the courage to keep on
searching for new truths which in the
course of time will not appear to be
true anymore. Without an under-
standing that one's end goal shifts the
further one explores, working using
improvisation does not seem to be
possible. Those necessary changes in
attitude, with their accompanying
conflicts, have characterized the recent
development of the TMDD and will
probably characterize the development
of any institution that makes this edu-
cational, artistic (and political) choice.
In the second place, on the level of
day-to-day experience with the process
of improvisation, an open 'state of
mind' is needed. (Of course, this
openness is also required when work-
ing from more form-oriented methods,
but in that case if one's concentration
is broken, the form of the material of-
fers something to go by, if unsteady.)
Although at first the department took
the view that this 'state of mind' has
to be built up anew at every lesson,
the conviction has recently grown
that by structuring one's teaching in
a certain way, one can work towards
a physical and psychical condition
from which one can improvise without
having to go through a long prepara-
tory process each time.
CQICI Sourcebook 121
Should the above give th<;.impres-
sion that improvisation within dance
education is gradually becoming a
known quantity, the following para-
graphs point out a few areas in which
my opinions urgently need further
exploration.
When working with improvisation,
the student's personal contribution to
the material becomes central. In other
words, this is an approach that recog-
nizes the necessary connection be-
tween being an artist and being free as
a person. But, at the same time, this
starting point also carries a limitation:
by stressing personal involvement,
there is the danger that the limitations
of one's own vocabulary will be in-
completely challenged and broken
through, or that the initial physical
sensations resulting from working with
improvisational forms will become the
end goal: the stereotypes of the new-
est form of dance. For that reason,
teachers try and set themselves the
task of developing methods which will
encourage the student through contin-
ual exploration to break through and
widen his/her own vocabulary.
Where improvisation is handled as
a choreographic methodology, the
dancer/choreographer tries to make the
material found through improvisation
repeatable. This process of developing
physical memory seems to be protract-
ed and intensive. If one really tries to
use improvisation as a form for per-
formance then one is going even a step
further. In making such a perform-
ance repeatable, it should be possible
to anticipate those decisions which
enable the form to be repeated, while
ensuring that the process of change
continues.
When using improvisation as a work
form, one should pay attention to its
compositional aspects. With many
improvisation approaches, the empha-
sis seems to be on physical experience
and its consequent personal expres-
sion. In itself a logical first step, it is
also obviously necessary to work on
an awareness of 'form' over a long
period of time, so as not to have to
fall back on conventional composition-
al laws.
Finally, there is the problem of
assessment: when teachers chose not
to use known forms in teaching move-
ment and composition, we had to ask
ourselves how to give the student
satisfactory feedback about his/her
progress. A judgement in terms of
good or bad usually turns out to be
difficult and also undesirable. In
practice, a judgement seems either not
to be forthcoming, or to be closely
bound up with the vision and experi-
ence of the teacher concerned.
Working with improvisation means
taking risks, by both student and
teacher, and continuing to search while
treading a path which over the first few
yards is possibly smooth-but each
time a stretch is covered, the effect of
each step turns out to be incalculable.
And so finally, it would still be pos-
sible to distill a 'system' from all this
material on offer, except for the fact
that we have to consider teachers
whose teaching methods are necessarily
bound to their own creative develop-
ment, and this is just as unpredictable
as that of the student. Furthermore,
I would like to suggest that in fact
improvisation within the framework
of teaching dance depends on a mutual
interaction in physical/creative devel-
opment between student and teacher,
which is an area that cannot be system-
atized. And the processes and methods
which evolve from these interactions
can only be partly objectified.
A healthy dance teaching profession
should not be unhappy to belong to
this aliveness. *
Parking in front of the school.
122 CQ/CI Sourcebook

2nd EUROPEAN CONT ACT TEACHERS CONFERENCE
a report by Christine Gaigg
2nd European Contact Teachers Conference
November 18-21 , 1986
Theaters.chool, Modern Dance Department
Amsterdam, Holland
Participants: Meret Schlegel, Christine Goigg, Doniel Trenner, Lindo Forsman, Jacqueline
de Vries, Wolfgang Grof, Mork Tompkins, Howard Sonenklor, Moniek van der Look,
Doniel Lepkoff, Andrew Horwood, Pierre Riou, Bob Rease, Pauline de Groot, Pierre
Serophin, Suzanne .Cotto, jean Bloch-Laroque, Stephen Crowford, Lucio Walker, Koren
Nelson, Alita Alessi, Bruno Stefononi, Elinor Rodeff, Margot Rijven, Nancy Stork Smith,
Kirstie Simson, Renate Muller, Wolter Weiler, Cathie Caraker, Gonne Kooy1 Nien Mari Chotz
The first meeting on Tuesday evening
was impressive. After introducing ourselves
and talking about the schedule (prepared by
Elinor, Renate and Cathie, who had organ·
ized the conference), we came together for
dancing. I was happy in the very plain
meaning of the word. Happy, because I
could dance with people without demon-
strating anything in terms of teaching, with-
out thinking about the next step in leading
a class. There is this underlying structure
I am dreaming of, there is this language
spoken so well, that you can make poems.
The room was filled with the atmosphere of
light and easy dancing.
We began the next morning with a warm·
up led by Nancy, focusing on the questions:
What do I need to get ready to dance with
another person? How do I know what? Do
I teach the other what I need? (The motiva-
tion for dancing: just a topic for teachers in
their daily life.) The day got heavier, guided
by a dense schedule we were not really able
to fulfill. On the evening before, there had
been at least ten people interested in teach-
ing at this conference; now there was nobody.
So, instead of co-teaching (which was set on
the schedule), we gave brief personal reports
of our work, one by one in the circle. Many
issues were presented, but at that time we
were not ready to pick them up for further
discussions. (For example: falling as a need
for giving up the image of yourself; realness
of Contact; the help of aikido, tai-chi, medi-
tation and body-work; to look what is
beyond the form; relationship between
ground and space.)
The afternoon was spent with CO-
TE ACHING: three people share the time
(two hours) with or without connetting
their material. It does not mean to find
something new, but to give the chance to
look at how other teachers teach. This time,
Bruno worked with sounds leading to Con-
tact. Pierre treated the left side of our bodies
including our vision and ended up (surpris-
ingly) with cart-wheels. Moniek then went
from personal movement into group-improv-
isation, followed by open dance. People
were kind of uneasy or ungrounded. Later
we tried to find out why. Someone observed
that we were "bad" students. We felt that
we were confused by Pierre's sudden change
of direction and by the different teachings.
Moniek got nervous when she realized that
her intentions did not work out. A small
"accident" happened, but nobody was hurt,
and the open dance was exciting to watch.
{There was no "feedback" scheduled but
we found time for it in the very last discus-
sion on Friday, and got to some interesting
points .... One was the issue of how to deal
with accidents in class: to pay attention to
the hurt person, according to the situation;.
to pay attention to the partner as well, be-
cause he might feel guilty; a teacher can
get feedback for their teaching by remem-
bering the energy in the room when an
accident happened.)
We left for dinner on the first day with-
out a clear plan for the evening activity-
somehow confused and very tired. Many
people thought it was because of the talking
(which was actually listening), or, because
of the dense structure, or what? Some peo·
pie began to already participate only part-
time. Other people found this attitude dis-
turbing. This evening almost nobody came.
The second day began with co-teaching.
Karen and Alito led the warm-up: first
taking turns watching your partner's breath
(who is lying on the floor), then doing a
warm-up dance together and talking about
it afterwards. Danny Trenner then led us
through exercises where we sang contra-
puntal jazz rhythms in two groups while
doing contact, to learn to work with an
external rhythm while improvising, without
being disturbed by it.
After tea-break, we started our first dis·
cussion: the essence of teaching; different
cultures; contact in performance; problems
of teaching. The suggestion of splitting into
smaller groups was made but not accepted:
there was either no need to split at that
moment, or, more likely, there was the fear
of missing something being discussed else·
where. We got into a constructive discus-
sion about TEACHERS' AND STUDENTS'
ROLES. Unlike the martial arts, where the
master-role is very clear, the teacher-role in
Contact Improvisation can be confusing.
But, as a teacher, instead of being over-
whelmed by a student's fantasies and pro-
jections, you can receive their energy and
give it back. Also, if you don't take a stu-
dent's aggression or resistance too personal-
ly, you can break the psychological cycle.
On the other hand, you do have to find a
way to work with difficult students. You
cannot avoid conflicts. The whole psycho-
logical situation in a class can be like Con-
tact Improvisation itself: give and receive
weight and transform energy.
The after-lunch co-teaching was done by
Stephen, Mark, and Kirstie. In the begin·
ning, Stephen worked with being absolutely
dead and then responding to your partner's
leading. Mark talked about one's habits {Do
they work with this partner?) and how de-
cisions are made in the dance. Kirstie had
us centered and did some Ki-testing. At the
end, they experimented with teaching their
material simultaneously to the whole group,
leaving us in a chaos.
This evening, after having dinner together
at the school, we went into the NETWORK-
ING DISCUSSION, an important part of the
conference. The discussion was dominated
Volume 1 2 1987
by finding a place and an organizer for the
next conference. Will there be a next one?
How do we prepare it? It was a hot discus-
sion, at least for those who participated
verbally. For many of us, the English was
too fast and the statements were not related
enough to each other. Some were interested
in just a jam and others were not interested
in open dancing at all. But to find out what
was most important in a teachers conference
for the whole group was just impossible.
Some quarreling gathered around the
question of how structured a conference
should be in terms of prepared topics, etc.
Looking back, I think that we somehow felt
that the "teaching exchange" focus of this
year's confe"rence didn't really work, the
co-teaching was not convenient to every-
body, there was not enough open dancing,
and not enough time to meet people person-
ally, all at the same time. And the confer-
ence will be over within the next day! But
we could see clearly that a group-process is
a group-process and cannot be compressed
into one evening of talking about the sched-
ule, but has to take place.
By a vote we defined: all want another
conference, three-quarters want more un·
scheduled time. We agreed on two days of
jam to get to know each other and to arrive,
and three days of conference. We define
ourselves as a support-group, but we can deal
with a technical conference too. Also, the
spirit of a conference is dependent on the
organizer.
Another conflict arose because of Euro·
peans and Americans discussing European
networking. One difficulty of this mix {in
other aspects quite constructive) is the dif·
ferent ways of communicating: the strong
output of American participants vs. the
European's preference for listening. More-
over, we Europeans have to stay in our cities
to keep something going on, (and are not
travelling around Europe giving workshops
as much). We are still relying on each other;
we have to. This is why the next day we
made a gathering of European teachers
(people who are settled in Europe and teach
here) to talk about practical network.
In that meeting, we agreed that it had
been necessary for the first two conferences
to invite somebody (i.e. Nancy) who is very
well known and experienced. Her most
valuable function has not been so much her
attraction to get people to the conference,
but her catalyzing presence. Yet, we will try
to make it without special invitations next
time and try to share the responsibility
equally among everyone. On the practical
level, we decided to meet next year not in a
city but in a house in the countryside, which
means meeting in the summertime. Three
people volunteered to take responsibility, to
ensure a gathering would happen. [See
'Notices' in this Newsletter for details about
next conference.] The networking was
understood as a real supportive one, not only
in terms of teaching, but also as an exchange
of performances and projects, ideas. This is
the formal structure, the rest of it has to be
developed in personal relationships.
The European communication is still
arranged through the NEWSLETTER, co-
ordinated each time by a different person.
We ended the conference with a final
round robin and a party. We felt we have
awakened potential connections all over
Europe, seen that teaching problems get
less dramatic when discussing them, and
inspired each other through our dance~.
See you next year.

DOLPHIN DANCE
by Lisa Gottlieb-Clark
The first time I danced contact it was in a dream:
filtered light, heaviness in my lungs, another's weight and
breath rolling against my back, blood pulsing in my ears.
Seven years later, my dream came true.
Cold and shivering in the murky water of a small lagoon I
dive and turn, hearing my breath in my ears, diaphragm
constricted, straining to see a long grey form swimming
lazpy below me. I come to the surface for air, my breath
exploding in a burst. I breathe in deep and dive below
again. After a few minutes of swimming I feel my skin
beginning to warm. I stretch my limbs in the water, let-
ting the physical movement calm me. The energy moves
down out of my head and into my heart. I feel my heart-
beat regulate and my breath softening. My attention
turns to the sounds around me, mostly clicking and tril-
ling. What I sense is that sound in the water has a great
deal of movement. The noises bounce and vibrate below
the surface. Suddenly I feel a nudge against the back of
my knees, and turning I look into the eyes of a large
smooth skinned dolphin. He opens his long mouth in a
wide grin and the sound that comes from the blow hole on
the top of his head is surprisingly like a human laugh. In
turn I laugh back, amazed, and find myself with a mouth-
ful of salty water. I surface again for air and quickly dive
below again. Moving slowly towards him under the water
I keep my arms at my sides to appear less threatening, and
brush against his smooth back with my chest and belly.
The dolphin gently grabs my arm in his.mouth. It is an
odd sensation, having this huge mammal hold me between
bis 88 teeth (same as a piano, but that's a different story).
Knowing this is a sign of friendship I try and hold still un-
til he releases me. His mouth feels like the grasp of a small
child's fingers. Feeling like it is my turn to make a move I
reach for the dorsal fin in the middle of his back and hold
on tight to catch my first dolphin ride. The dorsal fin
feels very firm. It is like a smooth, rubber covered, trian-
gular shaped tree branch. It isn't slippery at all; on the
contrary, my hand feels like it is touching a wet piece of
solid rubber. We are side by side, under the surface, as the
dolphin pulls me through the water. We both come up for
air, dive again and then I notice on my other side a slightly
smaller dolphin. I reach over with my free hand and grab
the dorsal fin, arms extended, racing through the water I
124 CQ/CI Sourcebook
feel like I'm flying. Feeling the water's friction pulling
against my skin I imitate the undulating motion of the
dolphins. This second dolphin is a female, and she is hap-
py to join us. We twist, dive, nudge and explore the
similarities and differences ·in the way we move.
When I first danced contact I dreamed about it often. At
first I thought it was because I was learning something new
and it was occupying my thoughts in my waking hours.
As I remembered more of my dreams I realized dreaming
contact was a way for me to dance contact without the
usual limitations of a wakeful dance. I could move and
dance in complete 3-dimensional space. In my contact
dreams I had no horizon to limit or guide me.
I feel this freedom in the water with these two lovely,
graceful creatures. I am aware of a dream-like quality to
our water dancing. We spin and chase, touqb and push,
arching and tumbling, ignoring for once gravity and its
constant demand. I feel an exhilarating freedom in this
original and most basic liquid. They are home in it. I am
just a visitor, very aware of my smaller lungs and lack of
endurance, yet they welcome me and honor my limita-
tions. I fill my lungs again and dive, following their
shadowy forms swimming ahead of me. They turn and
wait, and as I catch up they separate slightly so that I am
in the middle between them. We all come up for air, and
they stay, bobbing in the water next to me. My husband
and daughter ca11 a greeting to me from the shore and I
start to swim towards them. The male dolphin quickly
moves in front of me, blocking my way, while his female
friend nudges me back into deeper water. I am mostly
surprised, a little frightened to find myself in a dolphin
sandwich without a lot of movement possibilities. How
do I convey Time-Out? Breathing deep I center my
weight and push outwards with my arms as I duck below
the surface and head out into the direction they were
pushing me. They surface next to me, one in front and
the other behind me. I tread water and laugh outloud.
I'm incredulous and pleased that they want to keep me in
the water. I feel like a dolphin play-thing. I reach out
with my arms, twisting myself slightly, and grab bold of
their dorsal fins. We all take a breath and we're off again,
a dolphin on either side of me. We make a large circle of
the lagoon and then they start towards shore. We are
diving and surfacing, diving and surfacing, sunlight shining
on the water, the salty breeze, my daughter laughing and
clapping her little bands. Diving and surfacing, two
dolphins and a woman making brief contact, making a
dream-like dance. -ti
rubber-stamp by Gottlieb-Clark
