Steve Paxton performing. London, 1980.
by Steve Paxton
The arts can be related to the senses, roughly
speaking-music for the ears, painting for the eyes, dance
for the body. But dance suggests an exception, because
in the West it has become a spectator art, and it is through
the eyes that the audience begins a kinetic response, or a
physical empathy with the dancer.
The way the arts relate to the senses gets more and
more complicated to describe when we consider the senses
as interrelating. We notice that the muscles of the eyes
move the visual apparatus to scan and focus. The neck and
torso muscles move the head to expedite the eye move-
ment. We move the whole body through space to look at
sculpture. And we are able to dance on time to music by
virtue of the fact that hearing, which is one of our fastest
sensing systems, drives our kinetic response.
In their creation or execution, the arts are deeply con-
nected to specially trained kinetic systems which, in a
painting for instance, produce not only images, but charac-
teristic strokes and lines. The 'touch' of a musician is as
characteristic.
These are ways of regarding the senses which are far
from the '5 senses' model, which is the way children are
taught about the senses and their perceptions of the world.
And it seems that little is added to this initial picture in
Volume 1 2 1987
CQ/CI Sourcebook 125
the adult popular mind. I have read newspaper articles
about "the 5 senses and the mysterious 6th sense" just
this year. I am not right up to date on the current materi-
al analyzing the senses into all their component bits, but
they were up to about 25 separate senses (each with iden-
tifiable independent nerves) in the late 1970s, which is a
much more interesting number. If you are interested in
other models, read J. J. Gibson's The Senses Considered
As Perceptual Systems, read Bonnie Bainbridge Cohen's
work on Developmental Movement Systems (in past CQs),
investigate the premises of yoga, acupuncture, or any of
the Oriental physical systems which have been imported
into the U.S., and read the provocative and heart-moving
The Man Who Mistook His Wife For A Hat, by Oliver
Sacks. I think we can't have too many models of the
senses and their operation when considering a topic like
improvisation.
It is a bit self-referential to say that the model I'm
working on in this essay is about the effect of models
upon the mind, but I can't see any way around it. I
should mention in this regard that Buddhist thinking in-
cludes the mind as one of the senses. I am fond of that
thought for the questions which arise for the analysts (how
fast do we sense our thought?). If this is an amusing poser
to throw at the sensoral analysts, it is because most of
their work on the senses relates to those of the surface,
disregarding questions about our sense of gravity, our feel-
ing of the muscles of the body when they are quiet, or the
sense of 'being', if I may propose such a sense.
Yet the analysts' labor to discover the special nerves for
each sensoral aspect is of interest, if only to explain the
mechanism which allows us to dance to music, a most
popular form of improvisation. If we can bop at a party
because our perception of sound is faster by 4 milliseconds
than our perception of our limbs' relative positions, we
may surmise something about how the brain interrelates
the senses. We could also look at time itself as a thing
modified by each of the senses, instead of being an objec-
tive measure of duration. In other words, in trying to
describe the sensing of time, we must refer to the percep-
tion of times. These times then must be collated into how
long we think an event took, which is a very complex
computation involving which of the senses experienced
the event.
F9r instance, if we drop an object from a known
height, we have the math to calculate how fast it will be
moving when it hits the floor. This formula indicates that,
barring air resistance, any object increases its rate of falling
constantly. With this mathematical tool, we feel satisfied
that we comprehend the event. It is objectively clear.
However, if we drop a soapy dish and before it smashes
on the floor, manage to catch it, we can appreciate the
sensoral complexity and precision of another point of
view-the subjective feeling of gravity's effect on the dish,
which enables us to save it. We treasure this facility in
sports-eye-hand-body coordination of time, space and
posture getting you there to snag the pop fly.
126 CQICI Sourcebook
I was deeply impressed by seeing a blind woman drop
and catch a plate she was washing. For once, the eyes
didn't have it. It 'makes sense', as the phrase goes, that
our bodies are completely attuned to the gravitic effect-
that the rate a body falls is abundantly obvious to our own
bodies; that any discussion of subjective time should men-
tion the gravitic factor in tuning the human time senses.
And we might as well put in right here yet another
factor, the state of our endocrine system, which can speed
up or retard our experience of these times-just to indicate
how extraordinary a job the brain is doing and to get some
glimpse of how quickly it does it.
"Quickly," compared to what? Ah, "quickly" com-
pared to how long it would take for the conscious mind to
do the job. (This is my conscious mind writing, and I just
wish you knew how long it's taking me to describe the
little I can deduce about what my unconscious mind is
doing.)
a word
Language, a medium in its own right, is deployed to
analyze experiences. Language is either written or spoken.
It is either for the eye or ear, but finally it is intended to
be sensed by portions of the brain which decode sounds or
symbols into meanings. We rely extensively upon it to
communicate what we feel and think. We use it to qualify,
quantify, and confirm and in general to hold our societies
together. It is the major medium, I would estimate. Julian
Jaynes has speculated (in The Origin of Consciousness in
the Breakdown of the Bicameral Mind), that consciousness
itself arose with the advent of the written word.
The link of language to consciousness suggests many
avenues for speculation. (It might explain the relative
slowness of conscious thought, for instance.) I think it is
well understood that there is much we experience which
language cannot touch and should not be used for, but it
is such an extraordinary medium that we keep applying it
to all sorts of things, in hopes, perhaps, of contriving a suc-
cessful new formulation; bringing the unconscious, un-
'knowable', or unspeakable into literate consciousness.
I would bet that no dancer ever reviewed, however
positively, has ever felt their dance captured in print. Yet
language, used to describe other arts, forms a very impor-
tant part of what we think about a work of art. It can
certainly influence our point of view and may even suggest
what can be thought about-that is, limit our perception
or experience to the forum encompassed by language. It
does seem to me that if we spend much time communica-
ting with others via language about a painting, music, or a
dance, we accustom our minds to the language version of
the experience.
There are sensible reasons for this. Human societies
deeply appreciate evaluations which, like mining for gold,
mean eliminating dirt and concentrating on what is con-
sidered valuable. Our actual experience of something can
include much that is irrelevant to its evaluation. An apt
Volume 12 1987
evaluation of a work will likely be repeated by others to
their friends. The further it goes from the source of the
experience to a verbal or printed version, the less recourse
we have to elaborations or answers to our questions. We
put such a rendered experience into our own version of a
context. It is an idea we flesh out with our own images.
As such, it has become a fictionalized picture, it has be-
come un-true, but we do it continually.
Much of what we know about ourselves is described to
us via language. The very idea of the 'imagination', for
instance. How did you learn it existed? In many such
respects, we 'imagine' ourselves according to categories,
descriptions, and names of aspects of whatever we are told
(or read) it means to be a hu;nan being. I have a soul, in
other words, because the Bible tells me so. I have an ego
and an id thanks largely to the writings of Freud, and an
anima and animus courtesy of Jung. Not to belabor the
point, we (think we) are conditioned to a considerable
degree by our language.
Not that the particular names, categories and descrip-
tions we apply to ourselves have always been the same nor
will they be used always. They reflect a consensus and
will change or vanish as that consensus changes. Jaynes is
fascinating as he tries to describe the state of the human
mind prior to consciousness. But wc don't have to reach
into the past to grasp some idea of how arbitrary our
notions about ourselves are, we can examine ideas of cul-
tures paralleling our own and feel the disconcerting void
of (our own) recognizable terms of existence.
Language is not only prominent, but it can be coercive.
We may opt to disregard experiences which don't work in
language.
Tom Wolfe wrote a scathing article called "The Painted
Word," wherein he suggests that painters in the NY School
of abstract expressionism were in fact led by the writings
of a few prominent critics to paint as they did. He seemed
to feel that this was a degenerate situation which devalued
the art produced.
It could be said however that this was an exceptional
case of painters and writers developing the ideas of a move-
ment in concert-exceptional in that it was a very intimate
scene with a mix of artists, musicians, dancers and critics
in close communication, and with regular forums for dis-
cussion. Language could then be developed to account for
the paintings at the moment of their creation. What Wolfe
and the rest of us are accustomed to is a 5-to-10 year lag,
to give time for critical language to be invented to describe
the work. When this has been accomplished it has the ef-
fect Wolfe was describing: people learn to imagine in and
with the new concepts, and artists and student artists else-
where paint in the described manner.
One medium may support another, rather like the sen-
soral model: one sense may support another. However, I
have begun to think that one medium cannot accurately
describe another. We are used to the attempt to make
films of novels, and then read critiques in which, to no
one's surprise, the film is found a more or (usually) less
successful 'adaptation'. Films are now 'novelized' to simi-
lar effect. Critiques do not adequately portray the art,
either: it all seems to boil down tCJ "you had to be there."
There are nothing like the real things, whatever version of
'real' we happen to start with.
Analysis of perceptions shows us the realms of 'reality'
we are able to tap with the senses. Seeing, hearing, touch-
ing, smelling, imagining arc, to me, each so very different
from each other, seen subjectively, that I am apt to think
Volume 12 1987
of them as sensoral dimensions. Again, we adapt one di-
mension to another; 'loud' colors, for instance, is an
'auralization' or a sight. But the point here is to notice
how the sensoral dimensions are different, since we are so
used to confirmations of events through their blending.
Watch a carpenter drive nails at close range-eyes see and
ears hear the hammer. Then, move into the distance-eyes
see first .... then ears hear. What we originally felt as one
experience has been divided between the two senses. We
are mildly bemused by the organic 'analysis' we experience.
for somethiNG
In the 1960s there arose an artistic movement called
"mixed media" in which one saw dances, films, language,
etc., used in the same work. Now that I understand the
senses and the mind as 'mixed media', I am very surprised
that the movement got this name. It seems to me that
what was really going on was 'unmixed media', since the
words usually did not relate to the dance or film which did
not relate to each other in familiar ways. The audience
was challenged with an event occurring in several sensoral
dimensions, and the effect was similar to that of the lag
between the visual and aural hammer-at-a-distance. The
mind's habit of synthesizing perceptions was confounded.
This was for me an agreeable aesthetic experience, because
it illuminated ordinary perceptions a bit. It served to train
the mind to examine different modes of sensing, and crea-
ted in the midst of life a new game to play.
As I recall, during that time no one mentioned that the
theater, opera and dance were already 'mixed'. No one
noticed that film had been mixed. Perhaps that was be-
cause people were excited over the possibilities for further
mixes. Sounds emanated from sculptures. Words emanat-
ed from dancers. New mixes occurred even within the
CQICI Sourcebook 127
Steve Paxton teaching. Rome, 1982.
same media: for instance, Bach and Rock together within
a dance performance. Paintings became 3-dimensional,
sculptures were made with new materials such as cloth or
compressed cars. Painters made happenings which were a
kind of theater loaded with colors and shapes and textures
and people and costumes and props; in fact, they were
paintings moving freely into the 3rd and 4th dimensions,
using 'in reality' what before would have been the models
for 2-dimensionaf works.
It was a period of radical interface. We were confronted
with unaccustomed juxtapositions, and of course came to
see things that way.
It was rather different than our former way of seeing
and from this contrast came little sparks of information.
That is how I evaluate it anyway; or value it. When we
have two things to contrast, it seems to cause one to
notice, and to learn. It was difficult to appreciate exactly
what we learned in the complex midst of a happening; I
suppose it all got synthesized in the mind, and one began
to notice things that had escaped notice previously. It led
me to consider the process of learning, and I came to re-
gard the overall experience as a rather powerful conceptual
tool with which one could cause change in experienced
128 CQ)CI Sourcebook
photo ©Carlo Maria Causati
reality.
The question arises, why did we not notice media mixed
before? That is what is so interesting. John Cage had been
doing it for decades, and Duchamp's work suggested it
decades before that. The surrealists seemed to be concoct-
ing mixed media images, and Picabia and Picasso began to
collage-to contrast painted images and real objects, to
sculpt images from found materials.
that can't
Perhaps the answer lies in language. Most folk couldn't
'get it' because they were still quite busy trying to 'get'
what had happened in impressionism and pointilism.
Cubism caused folk further consternation. And who
could includemelting clocks or a urinal or a prepared
piano or a bus ticket in the same conceptual frame? The
critical thought and conceptual tools of the day were
already busily employed elsewhere, and the media did not
dispense images or rationales as quickly as they do now.
Volume 12 1987
The arts, in those days, had begun to change far more
rapidly than our language could accomodate.
During this century it became clear that changes were
occurring on every level more rapidly than before. But
this experience needed time to be finally understood, and
those early examples, such as the more radical examples of
Duchamp, were put aside to accumulate a kind of critical
mass. By 1960, with enough hindsight, the times'-changing
had provided us with the model for the mix, and many
artists were at once ignited. By then the popular media
and critical establishment were ready, and the mixed medi-
um event was considered seriously and widely announced,
after more than half a century gestation period.
Apparently an object doesn't have a life of its own, an
objective meaning, but is imbued with values by the way
we, with our changing minds, evaluate it. For many peo-
ple, an object such as Duchamp's birdcage filled with small
white cubes of marble was not illuminating, but only
absurd. The mix can appear quite empty and gratuitous
unless the mind has some context in which to work with
it. So during the 1960s when so many artists began to
mix images, terms were arrived at to convey the impres-
sions. Then the Duchamp objects were re-evaluated and it
was possible to see them, not as obscure Dada jokes, but
as objects with a particular luminosity, which operate in
some other dimensions of the mind than the linguistic one.
They resolutely resist meaning anything; and as it turned
out, this quality became highly prized in that period of
intense critical and artistic interchange. The laughter
changed from dismissal to delight, and th<i last laugh was
probably Duchamp's.
The special gift of the mixed media was to provide a
contrast to our conventional use of media and the senses.
We had to admit that we did use conventions to convey
our thoughts and feelings, and to run our society at large.
The new mixes indicated that, once in place, conventions
can dictate what the mind will allow itself to think. It
seems to be the nature of the mind, the senses, the body,
and of the society made up of these, to fall into habits,
which are necessary for continuity, aids to individual sur-
vival and collective civilization. Individual habits and col-
lective conventions prod us to adapt what we perceive into
the most convenient shareable mental construct.
From the music we are accustomed to hearing, we form
an idea of the intervals between notes. As our ears become
more educated, we can become quite accurate in reproduc-
ing these intervals. Confronted with music in a different
scale than usual, however, this aural education works
against accurate reproduction. The unfamiliar intervals
will be 'adapted' to the nearest known intervals. This
adjustment is so automatic and unconscious that the person
attempting to reproduce the new intervals will probably
not even realize their mistake.
Given another level of training in the logic of different
musical scales, reproduction becomes possible, if difficult.
For those who don't have such training, being confronted
with an unfamiliar system can lead to confusion. Indian
Volume 12 1987
music, for instance, may remain opaque and possibly even
disturbing; the intervals will seem wrong compared to the
scale we are familiar with. In addition, it will be very dif-
ficult for the person to say why it seems wrong. The result
may be a kind of unease and possible retreat from the un-
familiar system, a sensation of being lost. Not just lost,
but without knowledge of how we are lost, for there are
many dimensions in music where it is possible to go astray.
Under these conditions, a little information about the
nature of the systems is very useful for re-orientation.
However a very different sort of aid is an appreciation
of the feeling of being lost.
Getting lost is possibly the first step toward finding new
systems. Finding parts of new systems can be one of the
rewards for getting lost. With a few new systems, we dis-
cover we are oriented again, and can begin to use the cross
pollination of one system with another to construct ways
to move on.
Getting lost is proceeding into the unknown. To reject
the familiar, so rooted in our nervous system and minds,
requires discipline. The difficulty is that we have to know
so much to understand what it is we do and why we do it,
in order to know what to avoid. We are not attempting to
simply eliminate the known systems, but also to realize
how we have adapted to those systems. It is the habits of
adaptation which will keep us reproducing the system.
The system itself is not the problem, but rather this
human capacity to imprint unconsciously a new system
upon our old system-or to embody the maps of our
acquaintance, however tenuous.
We are close to improvisation here. When lost, we will
have to relate appropriately to unknown and changing
conditions. The dictionary explains that improvisation
means 'extemporare', or 'out of the time'. I suggest that
we interpret "out of the time" in two contradictory ways.
I suggest we discard all notions of clocks, and the half-life
of cesium, or celestial mechanics, none of which are
capable of improvisation, and equate time with human
experience of duration, which is to say the experiences
accumulated in life, so that "the time" will mean who we
have become. "Out of the time" will mean that, out of
experience (conscious or not) there is material for making
something.
Simultaneously, "out of" should be construed as 'aside
from'. We have to use what we have become in such a way
as to not be so controlled by it that it is automatically
reproduced.
Improvisation is a word for something which can't
keep a name; if it does stick around long enough to acquire
a name, it has begun to move toward fixity. Improvisation
tends in that direction.
Dance is the art of taking place. Improvisational dance
finds the places. °"'
-to be continually continued.

At this point we are pulled inro the sparking space

130 CQ/CI Sourcebook

she drops her head toward t~e floor, she may suddenly

horizon, the wood grain flooding her screen. Like the

This makes her appear as a mind that has just dropped
into a body and is finding out how it all works. For this
she needs feedback through the consequences of her every
move, and perhaps this is what also gives us the feeling
that she is never dancing alone. She is dancii;ig a duet with
the entire con ten ts of her consciousness.
Within minutes we feel the spirit of her work, but it
is as challenging to actually read her dancing as it is James
Joyce's Ulysses. Both artists take consciousness as their
content, and in order to anastomose across the many levels
of its structure, they must work with a multi-faceted
mind. Joyce was often originally misunderstood as writing
stream of consciousness while in a subconscious state; and
Lisa can be similarly misinterpreted by one who thinks she
is simply doing the next thing that comes along. But
closer examination reveals distinct, unbroken threads of
sensory perception, reactions, personal and cultural mem-
ory, imagery ranging from archetypal and hallucinogenic
to geographical and anatomical, and the dialogue of self-
questioning and self-discovery. Lisa has the added chal-
lenge of weaving these elements into a multi-dimensional
tapestry-with rhythm, cadence and echo-on the spot.
Imagine that Ulysses was never put on paper, and that you
had one crack at understanding what Joyce was doing by
hearing it read aloud.
The way time is shaped in both artists' work has
a lot of connections to experimental musical forms.
Joyce, trained and admired as a singer, composed language
as music, and was the first writer to illustrate the natural
tendencies of human consciousness to break down lang-
uage into its phonetic and rhythmic elements, rearranging
them in the processes of intellectual and emotional diges-
tion. Lisa's unique orchestration of consciousness also
draws on her musical background. Something of a prodigy
on the classical guitar, she was asked to teach it when quite
young, and for a while considered, like Joyce, a musical
career. Identifying with the inspiration at the source of
pre-industrial tribal music, she was the first dancer I ever
saw who used such songs not as an exotically primitive
element but with the projection that she was of the people
who sang them. In 197 5. while teaching video to dancers
at Bennington College, she taped the entire ethnomusical
library and has studied and used it ever since, constantly
adding to it. See11: as a rebel in Daniel Nagrin 's perform-
ance group for bringing her voice into improvisations, she
has continued to develop her own technique of vocal im-
provisation. In performance, she uses her voice at times
to find an evocative image which carries through in move-
ment; conjuring yearning, haunting, and funny characters;
dismantling language into burping hoots and reedy chords,
or following one phrase into a timeless primal clearing. It
may be her experiences in this territory that endow her
non-vocal dancing with an almost tragic muteness, holding
an epic that cannot be told.
Volume 1 2 1987
And cannot be danced. We are moved by a woman
willing to dance the undanceable; to stand in the form
pointing "There" beyond it. In this we see our own willing
struggle with the limits of the senses, when what we really
want lies beyond them. To Walter de la Mare who said,
"Language is the banging on a cracked kettle for bears to
dance to, when all the while we long to 1nove the stars to
tears," I would show Lisa dancing. It is "precise! y the inade-
quacy of the medium to be what it inditates, that moves us
when she dances. She leaves us with what we thought we
saw, and the reality of that, despi_t~ithe failure of the
senses to prove what we have feltf' ·- ·
Perhaps this is the answer to a qliestion I have had
for ten years. Why did Lisa stop her experimental work
in environmental performan4 in 197 5 after the success
of The Green Dream. ip which she and Cathy Weis
orchestrated a vast surreal epic performed simultaneously
throughout a transformed Victorian mansion? Did the
centerpiece of that epic-a large mirror jig-saw puzzle
painted with Michelangelo's God touching Adam. which
the viewer-participants helped put back together-foretell
a restructuring of her own ideas about the creative act?
Did questions arise which led her away from performing,
into video as a medium of examining perception, then as a
performance tool, and back into performing in a new way?
Did she decide that making environments, or using sets
no matter how evocative, only reinforced the illusion of
an outside world? That it was more to the point to per-
form seeing than to make things to be seen? Did she, like
Joyce, reject the premise of an epic requiring "external"
adventures and redefine it as the adventures of a mind dis-
covering its own language? Whatever the answers are. Lisa
Nelson is an epic dancer who has become. for many of us.
the paradigm of improvisation; the moreso against the
backdrop of a dance scene that is increasing! y geared to
spectacle.
I would love to see the return of the mustachioed
figure in the Hawaiian shirt from PA RT (with Steve
Paxton, to Robert Ashley's Private Parts) who, like one
of Chaplin's characters, seems to have become a classic
now permanently living in the hearts of hundreds of us
who watched (and not always with dry eyes) her/his?
development. Who but Lisa could throw open her arms as
if to keep the stars from falling, shift her gaze to become
a conductor about to start up the orchestra, and move her
fingers to become a villager reading a newspaper, all in one
breath? Who else could give you the feeling of falling
down in rush hour in Grand Central Station, then drop
you in the wilderness howling at the moon? Now that she
has built her own studio on a mountainside in the North-
East Kingdom of Vermont, who knows what she'll come
out with. She has always been a rebel against the predict-
able, lighting up things that lie in shadow, the only true
meaning of the avant-garde.
Why do I want to see her on an opera house stage
making the million dollar sets disappear, evaporating the
mountains, holding the reins of the elements? Why do I
want for her what she doesn't want for herself? I think
we only get one every now and then, and sometimes not
anyone, and she is one. -Ct
CQ/CI Sourcebook 131.
by Carol Horwitz
THE POLITICS OF
IMPROVISATION
Of all the dance forms,
improvisation is probably the most
political-because it contains the
urgency of immediacy. Improvisation
forces people to make decisions on
their feet and to live with those deci-
sions. It helps people to see how their
decisions affect others. When done
by groups of people, it has everything
to do with learning to work with
others-not competing but working
with the idea of the total picture and
the individual's small but significant
place in that overall picture. When
working in duet form, as in contact
improvisation, one is forced to really
listen to what the other body is say-
ing. In contact, movement choices
are based upon forces of nature such
as gravity and the spiral plus a direct
response to the movement of the
partner's body. This ability means
survival in the contact form. While
improvising, we learn again and again
that to listen to another's movement
is to come to a shared meeting ground.
This is the politics of improvisation.
With this in mind, I conducted an
informal survey in the fall of 1984 to
see how improvisation was being used
in colleges and universities through-
out the United States. Because of
financial constraints, I wrote only to
dance programs which offered at least
a masters degree in dance. There were
60 in all; 31 responded. Here follows
the information gathered:
25 of the 31 schools who respond-
ed had improvisation courses. 5
schools offered it only as part of
another course such as composition.
15 schools offered one level, 7 offered
it at 2 levels, and 3 schools offered
three levels. 10 schools awarded 1
credit, 9 schools awarded 2 credits,
4 awarded 3 credits and 2 awarded
4 credits. 27 of the 31 schools be-
lieved it should be a required course,
16 believed one semester was enough,
4 felt that 2 semesters sufficed, 2
thought 3 should be required and one
believed 4 semesters should be re-
quired. 15 schools allowed it to be
132 CQ/Cl Sourcebook
Volume 12 1987
repeated for credit.
Accompaniment for class ranged
from voice to sounds, to canned
music, piano and percussion, the
range generally found in technique
classes. The music was primarily
provided by the instructor with occa-
sional visits by musicians. One college
always had live accompaniment, but
this was the exception.
It was felt that a wide variety of
movement experiences should be of-
fered in improvisation classes. These
should include experiences with an
aural, visual and art emphasis. Stu-
dents should be exposed to the ele-
ments of dance in their most funda-
mental forms: space, rhythm/time,
dynamics, body articulation, effort
qualities, line, emotional qualities,
gesture, sound, texture, sensory aware-
ness on all levels. Other important
aspects to include were phrase devel-
opment, chance [methods], environ-
mental inspiration, examination of
personal idioms, group dynamics,
elements of contact work, awareness
of inner/outer space. Through these
experiences, students should show an
improvement in performance skills,
a greater ability to think on their feet,
greater trust in themselves and in each
other, have an improved ability to re-
call, and generally be able to view the
total picture within the framework of
the dance, and dance within the frame-
work of the arts.
Most of the schools had members
of their full-time faculty teach the
improvisation classes. The appropri-
ate teacher just seemed to surface.
Some of the notable improvisation
teachers mentioned were Daniel Nag-
rin, Claudia Melrose, Kathryn Karipi-
des, Irini Nadel [Rockwell] , Barbara
Dilley, Shirley Ririe, Joan Woodbury,
Loa Mangelson and Ann Riordan,
among others. On only one occasion
did a Teaching Assistant teach improv-
isation.
Qualifications for the "ideal" im-
provisation teacher would also qualify
a person for sainthood, things to
which all dance teachers aspire. He or
she should be experienced, sensitive,
have an ability to work from a prob-
lem-solving base, be able to create an
Volume 1 2 1987
environment safe for exploration, be
constructive, kind, open, have a sense
of humor, empathy, extensive experi-
ence in improvisation performance,
be able to handle emotional blocks,
have an ability to work with imagery,
be able to create a group dynamic, be
able to structure problems that lead
to meaningful solutions and, probably
most importantly, be able to change
plans mid-stream.
The effects of improvisation ex-
perience upon students were noticed
in their ability to experiment, explore
and develop bits of material. They
had a wider movement vocabulary,
improved creativity, were more in
touch with their own movement
sources, took greater risks, created
more authentic, less stylistic work,
had improved technique and became
more sophisticated self-aware students.
They were more comfortable dancing
with others. Students often experi-
enced an evolution from fear to de-
light and it was a turning point in
many of their lives.
It was felt that improvisation had
value in and of itself. Besides the ob-
vious benefit of helping students
learn to choreograph, it is an aid in
learning to see and understand dance
movement as it relates to all the arts.
It allows the uniqueness of the indi-
vidual to be celebrated. It teaches
students to consider the value of their
own movements before setting judg-
ments. Improvisation enhances crea-
tive thinking and problem-solving,
heightens kinesthetic perception,
creates an awareness of group dynam-
ics, fosters a personal viewpoint about
dance, deepens awareness of move-
ment logic and helps students to live
creatively.
No one mentioned the importance
of improvisation in preparing students
to teach children or members of the
"very special" population. Also, the
use of improvisation in dance therapy
was not mentioned. I was surprised
by this omission as I feel that improv-
isation is the very basis for working
with these groups, which many danc-
ers will do at some point in their
career.
Improvisation is seen as an art form
separate from more "conventional"
dance. At one school, students have
to perform a structured improvisation
before they can receive their dance
degree. It was felt that there are some
who are brilliant at improvising and
they should perform. There were
mixed feelings about the way it should
be presented. Many saw it as having
an important place within convention-
al choreography or within structured
choreographic works. Some felt that
it shouldn't be mixed with traditional
choreography. Some felt that it stood
more strongly in an intimate setting.
The University of Utah was one
place that received recognition as a
place to learn about improvisation.
Naropa Institute was another. Some
of the other schools mentioned were
Ohio State University, UCLA, Arizona
State University, University of Texas
at Austin, Wesleyan University, Univ-
ersity of Arizona at Tempe, Sarah
Lawrence, NYU School of the Arts,
and Southern Methodist University.
I think it is obvious from this
survey that improvisation plays an
important role in the training of
dancers. I know there is a place in
dance for the highly competitive class-
es that train the super-tech dancers
who are so popular today, but I feel
this survey supports the belief that
there is still more to being a dancer,
and that it can be uncovered through ·
self-exploration. An improvisation
class can become the laboratory for
exploration of the form and of the
self. It can be a forum for sharing, not
competing. It can be a place to cele-
brate our differences while helping us
to understand that we share the same
roots. "Self-exploration," "nurtur-
ing," "sharing," "celebrating," "life-
enhancing"-to me, these words rep-
resent a very strong, positive politics.
It's this approach that keeps dance
vital and strong.~

SMALL DANCE POEM
... with thanks to Chogyam Trungpa
you start out
to find your little dance
first move, best move
you don't even know you are beginning
you think perhaps
you are a vegetable
of course, that is true
you begin and begin
you don't even know you are beginning
you stand up
you start this way many times
to find this little dance
you've heard so much about
and this time
you are standing on a patient turtle
you start out
to find your little dance
this time you think
you are the great ballplayer Sadahara Oh
but you work hard to keep
both feet on the ground
you forget about slashing your mummy
and race home for dinner
without going anywhere
you find more in your little dance
than there was the time before
and think about it less
you are a little dance
but you are not alone
you are hanging in the closet
in between a black sealskin cape
with Betty Davis shoulders
and a green striped bowling shirt
that once belonged to someone named Marty
you are not harassed by gravity this time
and the next time you will not even care
history proclaims to each
a little dance
bare! y a step is taken
but the changes compound
you start out
a little dance
you are called "de born born"
fidgety rhythmic pulse
you can travel anywhere you choose
there is not a country you can not em brace
and you never leave your little spot
you start out to find your little dance
first move, best move
best way, only way
only way, one way
and music
or no music
or no music
with music
no way, wrong way
first move, best move
- Terrie Yaffe
[Text performed with Karen Nelson and Nancy Stark Smith
at Breitenbush jam performance, 1986, Eugene, Oregon.}
134 CQ/CI Saurcebook
Volume 12 1987
Editor Note
In Banyoles, Spain, two hours north of Barcelona,
there is a 16th-century stone farmhouse with a horsebarn
made into a spacious dance studio. Three large windows
open directly onto manure-rich hay fields. It's harvest time
and the old Spanish farmers throw the bales one by one
onto simple wooden carts pulled by old-model tractors.
The first night of our workshop we meet only briefly in
the studio. In the evening's waning light, we 'arrive'-from
Denmark, Germany, America, Switzerland-we stretch and
breathe, arriving f;om the road, arriving into our bodies,
into the room, smelling the fields, hearing the pigs squeal
and sing altogether in their pens, feeding, down the road.
Without much plan, we dance in the deepening blue light
that obscures faces but leaves a rich glow that's inviting to
dance in. We stand back from the windows, putting what's
left of the light in front of us, and dance and watch each
other in silence. I like this beginning very much. It's un-
adorned and mysterious. Spacious and intimate. We're
ready already. It's nice here.
Two weeks later. A lot has happened. As usual with an
intensive workshop, it's been intense. We seem to have
risked a lot, been satisfied and confused, tired and exuber-
ant, alone and together here by the ripe fig tree in northern
Spain, and now it's time to go. Some don't want to leave,
others are ready, curious to see what new directions these
weeks will produce in their work. Soon the goodbye com-
mittee, waving the others off, whittles down to a solitary
few. Bill and I decide to stay at Arlequi for an extra day,
to not rush leaving, and-very unusual for me-to stay not
only to the end, but after the end.
I found Bill in the studio after everyone was gone, sitting
in the windowframe watching the farmer turning over his
field in the evening light. A brush fire was burning up on
the hill, and it all seemed very still. The light was just as it
had been the first night we came-blueblack, highlighting
surfaces. The room looks small, and so empty, I thought.
How incredible that so much can happen in a place and it
stays empty when you go. That you can fill it so full and
leave and it's still empty.
What I mean is, Yolanda cried out when we changed from
fire to earth state and Ole held onto her until her writhing
passed. She was Spanish, he was Danish, and ... days later
Ole crashed heads with his friend J orgen, both rushing out
of the studio dazed and confused and coming back in later
laughing. And Marie, living through the special dance she
tells of in this issue's "Still Moving" column. I guess it's
really so obvious, especially to dancers, that so much can
grow out of thin air-that just a bunch of people moving
and thinking and talking and seeing can generate such ap-
parent substance and then be gone without a trace.
The ending continued. As soon as we waved the last
person off at the bus, the weather changed dramatically. It
rained all the next day as it hadn't the whole two weeks we
were there. New tenants arrived, invisibly dividing the
house into theirs and ours. It was as if some Red Sea that
had parted now closed over, making the clearing we had
lived in for two weeks not just changed but completely
gone. The figs were still there, ripe and heavy in their
teardrop sacs hanging from the tree. You could run your
fingernail down the side of the soft thick brown mottled
skin, open the fig and find deep red fruit moist and
delicious, so satisfying, so perfect after dancing.
Nancy Stark Smith

Letter to CQ from Daniel Lepkof f
QUESTIONS NOT TO ASK:
Why is the aerial lift a signature of an accomplished Con-
tact dancer?
W!ly are complete beginners so beautiful to watch, and
experienced Contact lmprovisors often so boring?
Why is there a hierarchy of Contact skills?
Why were many of my advanced Contact students un-
consciously resistant to learning?
Why can someone become advanced in a form based on
communication?
Why do I expect to get "danced on" at Contact jams?
Why are Contact-based performances so devoid of vision
and self-examination?
Why does the allure of gymnastic skills overshadow the
exploration of making contact?
Why do experienced Contact dancers get frustrated with
beginners?
Why does a form rooted in the senses give rise to a
generic look, when life itself is so varied?
Why did I overhear an audience member say during a
C.I. performance, "How can adults do all of that and
not seem to feel anything?" 1r
DANIEL LEPKOFF has been instrumental in the development of
the teaching and performance practice of Contact Improvisation
since its inception in 1972. He is currently based in New York
City.
Volume 13 1988
ANSWERS NOT TO BE QUESTIONED
Response to Danny Lepkoff's HQuestions Not To Ask "
in "Still Moving," CQ Vol. 13:3, Fall 88
by Karen Nelson
"Why is the aerial lift a signature of an accomplished Con-
tact dancer?" [Lepkoff]
A. It does take some physical skill and listening ability to com-
municate timing, impulse and subtlety of moving weight and
support in order to accomplish the aerial lift. I'd say it's more
like the ink than the signature. [Nelson]
"Why are complete beginners so beautiful to watch, and ex-
perienced Contact Improvisors often so boring?"·
Beginners' Luck-we are watching an exploration of totally
new territory, the unknown. With experienced contact im-
provisors we see expanding knowledge of an infinite unknown.
Yet often a stage of doing contact involves a kind of automatic
response, everything seems predictable for the viewer and doer,
even if it still feels great to do, it can be personally stagnating.
Learning new senses of disorientation and new choices of im-
provisation is challenging to the imagination.
"Why is there a hierarchy of Contact skills?"
Safety; some semblance of order is useful. To engage the
senses, gross and subtle, is an overwhelming field of discovery.
A teacher's personal "hierarchy" of skills is probably
something very alose to their own dancing of contact.
P.S. What are yours?
"Why are so many of my advanced Contact students un-
consciously resistant to learning?
"Why can someone become advanced in a form based on com-
munication?"
How do you as a teacher read a person's unconscious? Is this
an advanced form of communication?
"Why do I expect to get 'danced on 1 at Contact jams?"
Personal history.
"Why are Contact-based performances so devoid of vision
and self-examination?"
Because it is an unprecedented performance form and a study
of performance values doesn't come along with basic learn-
ing of and therefore showing of the work. It's a style of its
own, still evolving.
"Why does the allure of gymnastics skills overshadow the ex-
ploration of making contact?"
Could be because everyone loves and marvels at the ability
of gymnasts-like when you watch the Olympics-and it's ex-
citing to see these tricks performed live. Maybe it stimulates
childhood memories.
"Why do experienced Contact dancers get frustrated with
beginners?"
Because beginners are better at not knowing what could come
next.
"Why does a form rooted in the senses give rise to a generic
look, when life itself is so varied?"
Why do human beings look alike, distinguishable from
kangaroos?
"Why did I overhear an audience member say during a C.I.
performance, 'How can adults do all of that and not seem
to feel anything?'"
How can we make this incredibly rich and varied inner world
of sensation visible to outside eyes? *
KAREN NELSON is co-director of Joint Forces Dance Company
which produces the annual CI JAM at Breitenbush in Oregon where
she lives. She has been practicing CJ since 1977, teaching and perfor-
ming in North America and Europe.

STILL MOVING (cont'd)
THE BERLIN JAM
by Keith Ormand
Living in Berlin for the last seven
months I have had ample time to
ponder. As a foreigner I am often fac-
ed with a certain level of culture gap,
and when this carried over into Contact
Improv I found some issues which I
would like to share and invite comment
on. Can Contact Improv cross these
gaps? Does Contact lmprov fit into the
German society? And, more pertinent
for me, into the Berlin Society?
Contact Improv is not absent in
Berlin, nor has it been for the last eight
years. Every week at least five different
classes are taught drawing more than 40
students. Workshops are sponsored
throughout the year with fair success,
and there are even a few performances,
performance groups here which have
utilized Contact Improv. Clearly Con-
tact Improv has precedent here. And yet
no jam has been established. Why not?
138 CQ;CI Sourcebook
Previously I lived in Seattle where a
jam has developed, especially over the
last three years, from a small group in-
to a large society, unhierarchical, and
very supportive. The jam there draws
from a community of about 100 people,
and hosting between 15 and 30 people
each Saturday. In the last year the Jam
sponsored six or seven informal perfor-
mances, and as a group we consumed
a lot of beer and time. The jam is a place
to see people that I might not during the
week, and a place where I can get
together with others and make ar-
rangements for the following week. It
has been a place for learning contact in
a way which the classroom cannot pro-
vide, and for helping other people to
understand something which they can
not figure out by themselves. It is a
place where I can see some of the best
dance in Seattle, and get to dance with
some of the best dancers. And all of this
exists with a m1mmum of
government/bureaucracy.
So why has there been no initial in-
terest in a jam in Berlin? Is it that in
Berlin there is no interest in this sort of
interaction? Is it that my level of interest
comes across as pathological? Or do
people here only participate in contact
classes and workshops for the physical
training? Yet the jam also provides ex-
ercise for those of us that like that sort
of thing too. Is it the challenge of dan-
cing without supervision? I have ex-
perienced the desire to be critically
observed at times. Could it be that all
of Berlin feels this way all of the time?
No. Actually for the last six months
there has been a jam in Berlin, and it
has had a significant participation.
Those of us who do come have
developed into a society which I find
reassuringly similar to the one I left in
Seattle. And they have created some of
the most enjoyable dance I have ever ex-
perienced. And with one exception not
one of them felt experienced enough in
the past to participate in a higher level
course. In other words noone who has
been involved in the preexisting contact
society (note earlier exception) has par-
ticipated in the jam.
The jam has been publicized only by
word of mouth, yet quite a few people
have found it with very little contact to
the established Contact Improv society.
Further, when I have talked to those
dancers whom I know have danced
Contact Improv previously they are in-
variably aware of the jam and pertinent
information regarding it, but they won't
participate. First they want to know if
there will be music, and will someone
be leading, and how many people will
be there. There used to be music, but
now that the weather is warm I bicycle
and can not carry the tape deck. I used
to teach for 15 minutes or half an hour,
but now it cuts into our time to dance,
too much. If you come there will be one
more person than I expected. Is this ar-
rangement too risky for the experienc-
ed dancer?
The real question is "what is 'being
taught?" How is it possible that a large
group of dancers can be involved in lear-
ning something and have no interest or
incentive to use this knowledge? Can
you imagine studying German for eight
years and never trying to speak or read
it except in the classroom? Let alone
teach it without encouraging your
students to practice somewhere? Just
what was the experience of these ex-
perienced Contact Improv dancers in
Berlin? Does this kind of problem exist
elsewhere? What is in the future for
Contact Improv? Is it possible that jams
will no longer be visited by experienced
dancers? Is a Hierarchy developing? Is
it possible, that in today's society tradi-
tional values are becoming invalid, and
the sort of environment encountered in
a jam is no longer of interest? What
aspects of Contact lmprov have you
seen change in the last few years? Could
it be that Contact Improv is no longer
a vehicle for ideas, but merely a style of
movement?
Contact Improv has existed in Berlin
for nearly a decade. Now a society, in
its infancy, has developed to support it.
Communication is opening up. The jam
welcomes all new participants (See let-
ter in this issue's CQ Newsletter).
Perhaps in the future we will be able to
invite you here for an international Jam.
In the meantime, come and visit for
your next vacation. *
Volume 13 1988
THE BERLIN JAM II.
by Keith Ormand
The Berlin Jam has now been in
existence for over a year, and is
showing no signs of coming to an
end. Many of the problems en-
countered a year ago have vanished,
and the Jam is entering a new stage.
As it grows, new problems come to
the surface, and with time these also
will be replaced. At least three of the
four teachers offering contact
courses regularly here support the
Jam by informing their students of
its existence. As a result, there is a
regular attendance of 12-16 people,
and usually one or two are there for
the first time. In return, information
is exchanged in the Jam regarding
the classes and workshops which are
available. Furthermore, The Berlin
Contact Ballet has been formed by
participants of the Jam, and has
three performance dates in the up-
coming months. On the other side,
many of the people who have taken
contact courses since before the Jam
began have never been to the Jam. I
hear that they do not have time since
Saturday is the only day during the
week to take a day off from dance.
Those of us who enjoy dancing on
Saturday respect this, and appreciate
it. I have also heard that some of
the more advanced dancers would
come if more of the other advanced
dancers were there. Contact
Hierarchy?
The Berlin Jam is predominately
male, with a ratio of 2: 1. This is not
surprising in a city where 75% of the
contact teachers are male. Last
February I had the opportunity to
spend a weekend in Cologne, where
Volume 13 1988
a jam was also organized. It was a
pleasant change to encounter a jam
with 10 women and 4 men. I also
participated in a workshop in
Brussells, in which I was the only
male for the first two days, and one
of three by the last day. Last
December I participated in the Euro-
pean Contact Jam in Angers,
France, where the proportions were
about equal, something like 18:17,
and I am not sure which gender had
the majority. Now, in Angers, there
was one woman who was as
fascinated by the gender question as
myself. She asked questions such as
"How does it feel, you men, to be
dancers?" and made statements such
as "I really like seeing two men
dance together ... It is such a dif-
ferent energy than when two women,
or a man and a woman are dancing
together." Being one of the men
dancing at the time this last state-
ment was uttered, I was brought
abruptly to the realization that men
and women are different. Well, of
course I won't presume to say how
we are different. I do not often have
difficulties in telling men and women
apart, and if there is any lingering
doubt, I can always contrive to
dance with the one in question and
take a gender energy reading. But
precisely what this difference is,
eludes me.
Have you ever been told by
another dancer that he/she prefers
dancing with men? Have you ever
told another dancer that you prefer
men? Have you ever told your
parents that you prefer men? I hear
this preference often these days,
much more often than a decided
preference for dancing with women.
And generally, the assertion of a
preference for women partners in
Contact Improvisation is often ac-
companied with the assertion of a
specific woman. I do not want to
imply that this is a problem. It is not
a problem, unless you are not one of
the "preferred dancers." After all,
there are men, too, that I do not
want to dance with.
Many dance forms have been
dominated by men. In Kabuki, and
Keith Ormand & Andi Lucas in the Berlin Jam.
Peking Opera, women are tradi-
tionally excluded. Although not a
dance form, Shakespeare's company
had no women on the stage. Contact
is much more lenient. Although even
in ballet, men acquire recognition
more easily than women, actually,
ballet is not a good comparison,
since, in Contact a very good female
dancer may be regarded as outstan-
ding, while a good male dancer may
be regarded as standard. I believe it
is rather the opposite in ballet. And
of course, I do not want to use Con-
tact Improvisation as a metaphor for
society. That is up to you.
Actually, I am interested in heirar-
chy. Who is good enough to dance
with? Who should be allowed to par-
ticipate in special jams and con-
ferences? Who should be excluded?
At one point someone at the Berlin
Jam said that we should not tell
anyone else about the jam, because
there might not be enough room,
and we might not like the
newcomers. He conceded that
anybody who had the energy to fit
the jam into their schedule probably
would not be so disagreeable, and
probably could find enough room to
dance in. In Angers, the question of
who should be allowed to participate
in the jam, and in turn, in the con-
ference, was not easily solved. Well,
I know that there is nothing I hate
more than being forced to dance
CQICI Sourcebook 139
STILL MOVING (cont'd)
with bad dancers, or listen to th~
opinions of unqualified, nonfeachers.
(Of course I know that space is often
a problem here, but I prefer to deal
with the problem of exclusion which
occurs when there is enough space.)
When is a dancer good enough to
go to jams? Over here, many people
start contact in classes. But how do
they know that they are good enough
to go to a jam? How do they know
that they have reached the point that
they can participate in workshops for
experienced dancers? If they are not
part of the dance crowd, they may
never know. When they come to the
jam and find that none of the dance
crowd from the classes is there
regularly, they have no standard to
measure their own progress until
they have established themselves
either in the jam, or in the class. It
is easy to think, as a newcomer, that
the really good dancers do not come
to the jam because they do not want
to dance with the people there. It is
easy to come to the conclusion that
the dancers in the jam are not as
good as the dancers not at the jam.
This may be why it is so easy to
dance in the jam; no challenge. Is it
that the good dancers find the jam
not challenging enough? or are they
afraid of being hurt by dancing with
someone who is unexperienced? I
have heard these excuses. But how
can the beginning dancer get enough
experience to dance with a good
dancer so that he/she won't hurt
him/her? Are we going to support
the beginning dancers? or is it a
justifiable aim to keep contact
among ourselves, now that there are
so many of us. It would be safer,
perhaps, to dance only with people
we know. There may still be a little
room here for taking risks. Or
maybe you take risks by offering
classes to beginners. Are your
students good enough to go to a
jam? Contact is in no danger of dy-
ing out, but is there any possibility
for growth?
In Berlin, the space problem is left
alone, but we keep a careful eye on
the quality problem. When a bad
dancer shows up, we change the
standard. In this way, we are sure
that all the dancers participating in
the Berlin Contact Jam are good. 'fr
KEITH ORMAND established the Berlin
Jam a few months after having moved
there from Seattle where he had been ac-
tive in the Contact community for many
years.

The following piece is an excerpt
from Cynthia Novack's doctoral disser-
tation, "SHARING THE DANCE, an
ethnography of contact improvisation,"
written in 1986, soon to be published.
For more information contact Cynthia
at 46 Warren Street, NYC, NY 10007.
EGALITARIANISM AND HIERARCHY
IN CONTACT IMPROVISATION
by Cynthia Novack
Anthropologist Louis Dumont
has suggested that:
"The ideal of liberty and
equality follows immediately
f ram the conception of man as an
individual. In effect, if the whole
of humanity is deemed present in
each man, then each man should
be free and all men are equal . ..
By contrast, as soon as a collec-
tive end is adopted by several
men, their liberty is limited and
their equality brought into ques-
tion."'
Dumont continues an analysis begun by
Toqueville of conflicts in American
society, arguing that "to adopt a value
is to introduce hierarchy, and a certain
consensus of values, a certain hierarchy
of ideas, things, and people, is indispen-
sable to social life" quite independent-
ly of "natural inequalities or the
distribution of power." Consequently,
the ideal of equality, "even if it is
thought superior, is artificial."'
Dumont is proposing that the concept
of equality among individuals will in-
evitably be challenged by hierarchies
arising from social roles and social ac-
tion defined by the very concept of
equality. His argument does not defend
the use of power and control in hierar-
chy, but rather attempts to separate
hierarchy from the automatic associa-
tion with power and control which it has
in Western culture. Contact improvisa-
tion embraced values of individualism,
equality, and anti-hierarchical relation-
ships, but, if Dumont is right, one
would expect contradictions to appear
between these values and the differences
developing from social roles and social
action, even if no one takes power.
In the initial formation of contact im-
provisation, an egalitarian ethos main-
tained itself, with Steve Paxton as the
teacher. As the contact improvisation
movement developed, the looseness of
organization within the movement con-
tributed to an atmosphere of fluidity.
As Steve Paxton notes, people were free
to come and go; for those who chose to
be involved in contact improvisation on
only a casual basis, not much was at
stake. So long as no one was concerned
about the outcome of a performance
other than to demonstrate the exciting
new dance form of contact improvisa-
tion, and so long as the group of peo-
ple participating in the dance form re-
mained a small group of friends,
everyone could be viewed on an equal
basis. Contact improvisation could be
practiced in both a social setting and a
performance setting by the same people.
However, for those people who
became involved with the dance form on
a professional basis, teaching and/or
performing to earn their living, a great
deal was often at stake. As the years
went by, many of those who had been
doing contact improvisation since the
beginning became teachers, and some
began to form performance groups.
There was a spate of local group
organization from 1977-79, as well as an
increase in touring by individuals gnd
by ad-hoc groups. Both teaching and
performing established distinctions of
roles within the movement. Differences
between amateurs and professionals,
between the less skilled and the very
skilled, and between the social dancers
and the performers developed. Lisa
Nelson, after talking about the "folk"
nature of early contact improvisation,
commented, ''Eventually a lot of peo-
ple became teachers and that started to
change things ... if you'd been perfor-
ming, that kind of became a habit and
you wanted to keep performing ... after
a while, it took you away from just be-
ing 'a folk' and spending time with 'the
Volume 13 1988
people."'
Inevitably, the ideqlogy of
egalitarianism and spontaneity was
challenged by the appearance of implicit
hierarchy and overt planning. The
demands of performing and operating
within the professional dance world-
promoting one's work, applying for
grants, seeking students-involved these
dancers in somewhat different concerns
from people who were participating in
contact improvisation for social and
recreational reasons.
Dancers were often differentiated
from each other on a more informal
basis. Some emerging differences were
simply based on the level of skill of in-
dividual participants: everyone began to
know who the most able dancers were.
Elizabeth Zimmer recalled her percep-
tions of the contact improvisation jams
in Vancouver:
"There was a hierarchy. Peo-
ple tried to pretend there wasn't,
but people who could really move
got to dance with the people who
could really move . .. like if I
would ask Andrew Harwood to
dance, we would dance for a
while and then he'd say he wanted
to stop and then he'd go on to
dance with somebody who could
really dance. So I'd consider it a
good day if I got a couple of
minutes with Peter [Ryan] and a
couple of minutes with Andrew.
So there were people you didn't
want to dance with because they
couldn't, other people you were
reluctant to ask because they were
so good, and depending on how
you were feeling about yourself
on any given day, you took cer-
tain kinds of risks."
Those who were very skilled or very
involved in developing the dance form
often found their interests diverging
from the increasing numbers of new and
relatively unskilled contact improvisers,
as well as from those long-time im-
provisers who were primarily interested
in socializing. For example, in 1979, two
large gatherings of contact improvisers
took place. "Current Exchange," a
month-long meeting at the Western
Front artists' cooperative in Vancouver,
was organized by Steve Paxton for
"serious students" of contact im-
provisation. Those wishing to attend
had to submit letters to Paxton describ-
ing their activities and interests because
Paxton did not want to teach or be in
a position of responsibility for the
group. The conference involved danc-
ing, discussion, writing, and video-
making, a full-time endeavor for peo-
ple with a strong involvement in the
dance form. "Country Jam," an event
held that same year on Vancouver
Island, was, according to Peter Ryan,
''exactly the opposite of Current Ex-
change." Ryan, who attended and
helped organize both events, laughing-
ly described Country Jam as "dance for
the proletariat ... it was homespun,
there was organic food and jamming
every day."
Ellen Elias, who attended Current Ex-
change, felt that it was "elitist" because
of the requirement to write a letter, even
though everyone who wrote was ac-
cepted. Among contact improvisers, she
says, "there was a way of being elitist
that was subtle ... there was definitely
a sense of in-group, out-group, but it
was supposed to not be there." Like
Zimmer, Elias noted the social conven-
tion of assumed equality which overlay
the existence of hierarchies.
What is particularly notable about the
contact improvisation community is not
that hierarchies existed but that many
participants were so conscious of them
and disturbed by them. As mentioned
earlier, many contact improvisers active-
ly promoted the idea that an experienc-
ed dancer should be able to dance with
anyone and learn something from it, as
well as vice versa. At the 1980 Dance
Guild Conference ["Dance As Art-
sport," in Minneapolis], many of the
remarks at a gathering to discuss
"Politics and Contact Improvisation"
were addressed to the problem of
"elitism" and the "exclusivist at-
titudes" of the "contact
heavies."' People felt strongly that the
egalitarian aspects of contact improvisa-
tion needed to be maintained.
As the years passed, cultivating a
spirit of trust and caring between part-
ners remained a central component of
contact improvisation classes and jams,
and extended to the social ethos of com-
munal organizations like Dance New
England. On a social basis, it was
strongly felt that the participation of
everyone in contact improvisation was
not to be sacrificed to the special
abilities of a few people. But this ethos
was very difficult to maintain as a part
of presenting the work publicly. *

STILL MOVING
''FALL
AFTER
NEWTON''
"FALL AFTER NEWTON" is a videotape
that traces 11 years of development of Con-
tact Improvisation from Steve Paxton's
seminal work Magnesium in 1972 through
consecutive years of Contact performances
including Contact's 11th Anniversary Con-
cert Series at St. Mark's Church in NYC in
1983.
Steve proposed the documentary concept
for "F.A.N." after editing the videotape
"Chute" with Steve Christiansen and Lisa
Nelson in 1979 from performance tapes of
the first Contact concerts in 1972. The great
fortune of having video coverage of perfor-
mances from the very beginning suggested
the possibility of examining one dancer's
development and looking for corresponding
growth of the dance form itself.
In 1980, the editing team plus Nancy
Stark Smith, who was asked to be the focal
dancer for the tape, began pouring through
9 years of video documents of performances
(approx. 50 hours) and made a first 20
minute edit from the material. Seven years
of sporadic editing sessions, script writing
and revisions resulted in a 23 minute
videotape in color and black and white with
the following narration by Steve Paxton.
Lisa Nelson
["Fall After Newton" received partial funding from
the National Endowment for the Arts, the Judson
Church Dance Project of Bennington College in
Vermont, and Videoda, a project of Contact Col-
laborations, Inc. "F.A.N." and other Contact tapes
are available through Videoda, Box 22, E.
Charleston, VT 05833.}
142 CQ/CI Smtrcebook
TRANSCRIPT
by Steve Paxton
When an apple fell on his head, Isaac Newton was inspired to
describe his three laws of motion.
These became the foundation of our ideas about physics. Being essen-
tially objective, Newton ignored what it feels like to be the apple.
When we get our mass in motion, we rise above the constant call of
gravity toward the swinging, circling invitation of centrifugal force.
Dancers ride and play these forces.
Beyond Newton's third law, we discover that for every action several
equal and opposite reactions are possible. Therein lies an opportunity for
improvisation.
We are looking at performance of Contact Improvisation, a duet move-
ment form. This tape takes a sweeping look over 11 years of practice by
Nancy Stark Smith, from her first exposure to the form I was working on
when it was new in 1972, through moments chosen from consecutive
years of performing with me, Steve Paxton, and with others.
Nancy solos by testing her movement, playing with gravity and the
floor, which are, in this work, not taken for granted, but are considered
constant partners. She doesn't direct herself-she starts moving and then
lets it happen, finding ways to cope with momentum and gravity.
Here, in 1976, Nancy and her partner Curt Siddall move to follow the
point of touch as it moves-a basic focus for their improvisation. In the
play of moving and being moved, specific movements are unpredictable,
but they occur within a knowable field-of gravity, centrifugal force, sup-
port and dependency.
Human touch unites the forces which act upon the body, with the sen-
sations they provoke within the body. This interaction makes it possible to
keep all the parts of both their bodies harmonizing.
They were not so adventurous 4 years earlier. In this first performance
of Contact Improvisation, they were getting used to moving by touch. By
feeling their way, they brought back clues about the form and about
themselves and their perceptions.

Volume13 1988
The performance which attracted Nancy to this form
was a high energy study called Magnesium. I made
Magnesium tor a group of men at Oberlin College in_
1972. It investigated the reflexive quality of touch, with
momentum, falling, rolling and colliding. .
Magnesium ends with five minutes of standing, to
draw a great distinction in the scale of movement.
Standing still is not actually "still." Balancing on two
legs demonstrates to the dancer's body that on~ moves
with gravity, always. Observing the constant ad1ustments
the body makes to keep from falling calms the whole be-
ing. It is a meditation. It is watching the reflexes at .
work, knowing they are subtle and dependable-not 1ust
emergency measures.
Standing became one of our disciplines, keeping the
mind attentive to the body's present moment. This sim-
ple practice was preparation tor the complex intera_ctions
that would arise with a partner. As were the throwing
and catching practices that called for instant response,
introducing ourselves to adrenalized states.
At first, there seemed to be only two options-trying
to follow the flow of communication or resisting it.
Each dance is a series of on-the-spot decisions. And
they are on the spot. The soft skin is alert to the points
of contact, signals telling the dancers where they are,
orienting them to their partner and the floor. .
Their perceptions are stretching. They are adrenal1zed.
Inside their minds, the many touch events and constant-
ly changing relationships blend i:ito a continuity _of mov-
ing masses which creates a logic reached only in the
heat of the dance. A logic as secure as that found when
standing alone and watching the reflexive dance of
one's bones.
Higher momentum brings new areas of risk. In order
to develop this aspect of the form we had to be ~ble to
survive it. The support needed from the unconscious
reflexive parts of the brain is more present when the.
conscious mind is not afraid. The calmness of standing
is extended into the fall.
There are hazards. One of them is thinking ahead.
What the body can do to survive is much faster than
thought. .
It is useful to re-train the reflexes to extend the limbs,
rather than contract them during a fall. During this very
disorienting fall, Nancy's arms manage to cradle her
back, and this spreads the impact onto a grea_ter area.
And she doesn't stop moving. That helps to disperse the
impact over a slightly longer time. She doesn't seem
bothered.
Taking a brief scan through time, we begin with. the
first duet in which I danced fast with Nancy, dancing
with abandon and thrust to get into momentum. It seems
rough and disorienting for her, but she was up for it.
One year later, the sensation of movement was more
continuous, the flow of movement longer. There was
more time to feel what was happening.
While in contact, we attend to our own reflexes, which
have been stimulated by the other's movements. Our
reflexes move us, and this causes our partner to move.
This cycle of movement responses is continuous and
forms the basis of the dialogue.
Volume 13 1988
When physical safety could be assumed at a more in-
stinctive level, we were free to play with time and the
subtleties of touch. Phrases of movement could be ex-
tended indefinitely when we no longer needed to stop
and check our situation.
By 1978 we had examined the timing _of the body on
its own for six years, and began searching for ways to
change our habitual patterns. Using music was one of
these ways. For this performance, Collin Walcott pl~yed,
incorporating the random sounds of our movement into
his drumming. We danced to his rhythm, and he played
to ours.
During periods of extended soloing, we each expect
physical contact at any moment. We wait for the mo-
ment of decision to be taken by our partner or by
ourselves.
Over the years, the principles underlying our move-
ment remain the same. As the senses expand into
spherical space and the muscular system learns t~ res-
pond to touch on any surface of the body, any point can
be the fulcrum tor leverage and continuity.
Nancy and I have danced with many other partners o'.
varying backgrounds and skills. Each partner posed the1r
own kinetic puzzles which were danced through, con-
tributing new elements to the body of work, the network
of Contact Improvisation.
The main focus of training is retuning the senses. It
isn't just the sense of touch which must be expanded,
but all the senses must become elastic enough to
navigate through spherical space, to handle any posi-
tion, any change of acceleration.
It seems that what has emerged is two bodies acting
as one within the domain of the physical forces. Newton
proposed ideas about the forces and their intera?tion. In
addition, Contact deals with ideas or images which are
sensations first, then felt by the mind.
Every person who has danced Contact Improvisation
taught their variation to their partners. This network
spreads information among us.
As a member of the first generation to study Contact
Improvisation, Nancy not only learned it but helped to
define it by the direction her training took her. When she
began, no one knew what sort of body would result from
the practice, nor what properties would be developed.
We have seen the first Contact event Nancy saw,
Magnesium. This piece must have left an impression
upon her, which in turn must have been altered by the
development of the duet form.
We have been able to see Nancy's physical change in
the early years of her study, and the development of her
performance style. In looking at performance footage
mainly, we have seen little of the daily work, but have
seen the public forums in which Contact Improvisation
became widely defined. In looking at Nancy's develop-
ment over 11 years, we've seen one strand of the
physically rigorous and interwoven structure of Contact
Improvisation.
An important aspect of Contact Improvisation is the
pleasure of moving, and the pleasure of dancing with
somebody in a very spontaneous way. It happens in a
framework which considers the body in all its variety as
the primary focus from which the mind can draw. With
this, the dance is refinable to ever more precise relation-
ships with the physical forces. *

THE ECSTATIC POETRY
-Searching for The
Today, like every other day, we wake up empty
and frightened. Don't open the door to the study
and begin reading. Take down the dulcimer.
Let the beauty we love be what we do.
There are hundreds of ways to kneel and kiss the ground.
Jalaluddin Rumi
We have a huge barrel of wine, but no cups.
That's fine with us. Every morning
we glow and in the evening we glow again.
They say there's no future for us. They're right.
Which is fine with us.
When I am with you, we stay up all night.
When you're not here, I can't go to sleep.
Praise God for these two insomnias!
And the difference between them.
Sometimes I forget completely
what companionship is.
Unconscious and insane, I spill sad
energy everywhere. My story
gets told in various ways: A romance,
a dirty joke, a war, a vacancy.
Divide up my forgetfulness to any number,
it will go around.
These dark suggestions that I follow,
are they part of some plan?
Friends, be careful. Don't come near me
out of curiosity, or sympathy.
I would love to kiss you.
The price of kissing is your life.
Now my loving is running toward my life shouting,
What a bargain, let's buy it.
by Martin Keogh
Imagining the whirling dervishes,
brings alive blossoming robes, hats
resembling beehives, beards like bees in
pursuit, and arms floating weightlessly,
the lone clouds in a clear summer sky.
The twirling faces focus on nothing as
they turn, peering into the betwixt and
between, into the limenal world.
The original whirling dervish was
Jalaluddin Rumi. Born on September
30, 1207 in Balkh in what is now
Afghanistan, Rumi's family settled in
Konya, Turkey after being exiled by the
Mongol invasions. During the thirteenth
century this was a meeting place at the
western end of the silk road for Chris-
tian, Islamic, Hindu, and Buddhist
worlds. Here he succeeded his father as
head of a medrese, the dervish learning
community. This fairly conventional
career ended when he met the wander-
ing dervish, Shams of Tabriz. Shams
took Rumi's books, the sum of his in-
tellectual brilliance and tossed them
down a well to show him that he need-
ed to live what he had been reading and
writing. With Shams, Rumi met the "in-
ner friend," "the beloved." However the
mystery is named, he found it with
Shams and out of their parting came
Rumi's poetry.
The poetry is described in the in-
troduction of Open Secret by John
Moyne and Coleman Barks:
"The essential power of Rumi's
poetry is ecstasy, an ecstasy
melting the confinement of the
ego into a larger, elastic, cross
pollinating dance of Selves. His is
not the ecstasy of Union, not in
the poetry. It is a love-ecstasy
mixed with some brokenness and
much longing.
"Rumi's odes and quatrains are
the personal, human records of a
man's being given a sharp, clear
consciousness of the divine, and
enduring it."
Rumi died in 1273 and his funeral
was attended by members of all religious
groups including the different Islamic
sects.
Rumi was a Sufi, poet, dancer, and
144 CQ/CI Source"book
