or JALALUDDIN RUMI
Roots Of Contact
We don't need wine to get drunk,
or instruments and singing to feel ecstatic.
No poets, no leaders, no songs,
yet we jump around totally wild.
though it is immodest to claim, his acui-
ty puts him in the geneology of Contact
Improvisation. Though some of Rumi's
poems were written, most were im-
provised and came out spontaneously
metered and rhymed.
It's difficult to find a comprehensive
description of Contact Improvisation.
There are adequate explanations of the
physical and functional aspects of our
dance, yet there is always that element
which can't be embraced with language.
Seven hundred years ago Rumi recited
poetry that to this day speaks to the soul
of Contact.
Here is a sampling of Rumi's poetry.
In the second group are poems that I feel
have a connection to C.I. In the Sufi
tradition, another name for the medrese
is the night school, following the idea
that we have a day mind and a night
mind. The day mind does the laundry
and pays the bills. The night mind is for
poetry. These poems are ideally read
aloud, at night.
Think that you're gliding out from the face of a cliff
like an eagle. Think you're walking
like a tiger walks by himself in the forest.
You're most handsome when you're after food.
Spend less time with nightingales and peacocks.
One is just a voice, the other just a color.
The clear bead at the center changes everything.
There are no edges to my loving now.
I've heard it said there's a window that opens
from one mind to another,
but if there's no wall, there's no need
for fitting the window, or the latch.
D
Contact Improvisation has always
been sheltered under the aegis, the pro-
tection, of the founders' predilection for
not creating certifications, degrees, pro-
fessional associations and strict defini-
tions of the form. From a broad variety
of sources, imagery is created, related
disciplines explored, points of view ex-
pressed, but absolutes are ultimately
dismissed. How easy it would be to con-
cretize into a set of exercises and rules
this protean dance form which at its best
is fluid.
It is accepted but only whispered how
Contact is a metaphor for our lives and
that it has therapeutic value. Less talk-
ed about, and rightly so, are the spiritual
elements, what we contact, or more
likely what contacts us during those
dances that are out of our constraint,
where a third body is created. Contact
Improvisation, like Rumi's poetry, is
limenal, neither in balance nor over the
edge. His poems express the dances that
are not predetermined, those rare
moments when the dancers disappear
and there is only the dance. *
Let the lover be disgraceful, crazy,
absentminded. Someone sober
will worry about events going badly.
Let the lover be.
Out beyond ideas of wrongdoing and rightdoing,
there is a field. I'll meet you there.
When the soul lies down in that grass,
the world is too full to talk about.
Ideas, language, even the phrase each other
doesn't make any sense.

DANCE WITH DIFFERENT NEEDS
a report by
Alito Alessi, Alan Ptashek and Karen Nelson
THE WORKSHOP
DANCE WITH DIFFERENT
NEEDS was a two day, JO hour
workshop which brought together
disabled and non-disabled people to
learn and practice contact improvisa-
tion. The focus of the workshop was to
explore contact as an accessible form for
people of all physical abilities to meet
on equal ground and experience the
movement possibilities that exist bet-
ween them. The workshop, which was
attended by 60 people, was facilitated
by Alita Alessi & Karen Nelson (Joint
Forces), with special invited guests
Kevin Finnan and Louise Richards of
England, and Alan Ptashek and Bruce
Curtis of the San Francisco Bay Area.
Earlier in the year, Karen and Alita
worked with Mobility International
U.S.A. to develop an intercultural
dance workshop with disabled and non-
disabled people from Germany and
Michael Goldhammer (left) said, "It has to be fun
to interest me."
Oregon. Alan, an able-bodied mover,
and Bruce, a quadriplegic mover, are
both from Berkeley and have been prac-
ticing contact improvisation together for
the past two years. Kevin and Louise,
from England, have been working with
different populations including dancers,
students, old, young, able-bodied and
physically alter-a bled, and have found
contact improvisation to be an accessi-
ble and engaging form to teach. The
workshops (which were free) were
wheelchair accessible, and sign inter-
preted. [Alito]
As no two people are identical in
their expression of Contact Improvisa-
tion, styles and approaches toward
teaching Contact are equally diverse.
Collaborative teaching is a challenging
situation where the exchange of facilita-
tion, structure and spontaneity is form-
ed by the communication between the
teachers. The six facilitators of the
"Dance With Different Needs"
workshop had all been previously work-
ing in their separate home areas with
people of varying physical disabilities.
With the exception of Bruce Curtis's
relatively new practice of Contact Im-
provisation, we collectively had many
years of experience in teaching Contact
to a wide range of students. As this
workshop was our first teaching col-
laboration with one another, we had
questions, but more so, an excited
curiosity about each other's ideas and
approaches toward shaping the teaching
material.
Day One
We began the first of the two day
workshop with an extended period of
personal entry and exploration of the
very large gymnasium. Spatial and
peripheral alertness was gradually
developed. We encouraged individuals
to improvise their personal warmup. At-
tention to breath and the forces of gravi-
ty upon the body were emphasized.
There were a few people with previous
experience in Contact Improvisation,
disabled people in both manually-
operated and motorized wheelchairs, a
146 CQ/CT Sourcebook
Naomi and David find ways of support in the chair.
blind woman, a deaf woman, and others
with lesser physical limitations or injury-
related conditions. Many others in the
workshop of approximately sixty peo-
ple were able-bodied and interested in
Contact as well as in learning within this
particular situation. There was a high
level of enthusiasm and willingness to
develop playfulness and physical con-
tact. The speed was faster than we had
anticipated. We began to slow the
overall pace and look toward a deepen-
ing of the process through which we
move into contact with greater sensitivi-
ty and awareness. Those of us who
could fall and enter the floor did so,
while others stayed in their chairs, relax-
ing into the "small dance" that can be
felt in a sitting position. The forms of
contact between able and disabled
movers slowly revealed the ways in
which a person in a wheelchair often
must teach and become the initiator
when their partner is feeling awkward
and tentative. It seemed to be equally
important to allow that partner time to
get in touch with their awkwardness.
As we moved with one another in an
increasingly more mindful and physical-
ly alert manner, our specific strengths
became more apparent. When Michael
Goldhammer explained to me that his
head and neck muscles were the most
powerfully developed extension of his
body, we both found much pleasure in
feeling the exchange of one another's
strength and support.

Later in the morning, we had people
combine in small groups where each
person was gently rocked and rolled
along the floor. Particular focus was
placed on the head-neck joint, inviting
restful and fluid movement to be pass-
ed through the body. Counter-balancing
with a partner was also explored in
various positions, each person using
whatever degree of movement and flex-
ibility they had in their arms. A person
in a wheelchair could lean their weight
off the side of the chair so that they
wouldn't be pulled too far forward or
out from their chair by their partner's
leverage.
In the afternoon, Kevin and Louise
introduced a different form of a
gauntlet (two parallel lines of people
facing each other) where one person
slowly walked, wheelchaired, or crawl-
ed through the gauntlet from one end
to the other while everyone in the lines
gently blew upon their clothing and
skin. Sensuality was a powerful and
common feeling for us all. We held a
round-robin toward the end of the day.
There was little reluctance to enter the
circle. We ended the day with a circle
for feedback and discussion.
Day Two
Sunday included quite a few new peo-
ple. We began the morning in a quiet
and more reflective mode. Karen led an
extended warm-up that brought us into
light qualities of contact with one
another, maintaining a feeling of warm-
up without further ambitions. Later, we
covered one half of the space with mats
and introduced "log-rolling." Everyone
lies on their stomach, lined up next to
each other with heads pointing in the
same direction, arms extended
overhead. The contact between the sides
of bodies is tight but flexible. One by
one, a person places him/herself in a
perpendicular direction across the
bodies. Before beginning to log-roll, the
joined bodies gently nudge and ar-
ticulate some small movements upward
to the person they're supporting to give
them a feeling of buoyancy. Then, the
person atop is carried along by the roll-
ing bodies until he or she reaches the
other end. For those people who left
their wheelchairs, this exercise was an
exhilarating way to experience the sup-
portive combination of body, ground,
breath and momentum, essential ingre-
dients of Contact Improvisation.
Staying on mats, we moved into part-
ner work, what we call "low table"
positions. Here, one person is on hands
and knees, while the other partner
148 CQ/CI Saurcebook
drapes him/herself horizontally over the
back of their partner. Gradually, either
partner can initiate some movement,
changing shape and position in whatever
way they are able. Relaxed but alert, the
bodies can feel the force of gravity
through muscles and bones. Higher sup-
port positions were also introduced,
allowing skills for falling and rolling to
be expanded upon. The level of trust,
communication and safety was becom-
ing very apparent as we entered another
round-robin. The dancing was accom-
panied by some friends who played
acoustic and synthesized music along
with vocal improvisations. The
workshop closed with a deep apprecia-
tion for what we all created. The desire
for this work to continue was echoed
throughout the group. [Alan]
IMPRESSIONS
Through the workshop, I learned
about a deeper level of being supported,
and opening my body to trust the sup-
port more, in more directions. This
came after working with Jessica, a girl
whose arms and legs have sensation but
cannot bear weight without additional
bracing or support. Her spinal and tor-
so movement is fluid like a fish and we
had a great time cruising the space by
rolling.
We discovered a way she could wrig-
gle her weight onto me while working
close to the floor. Once I felt the center
of her weight directly on top of me, I
could play with rocking her and shifting
the position of my support under her,
raising the level of the support to hands
and knees. For her it was slightly
delightfully terrifying-"Don't! Don't!
DO! DO! STOP! STOP! GO! GO!!"
So I stayed within her limits of how
much permission to take. Her fear was
of falling on her head without any pro-
tection from her arms while hanging
forward across the bench my body made
for her. Then we played with shifting
her weight towards her feet, just enough
so she could feel the floor through her
flat feet, with most of her weight still
into me. From here I crawled and she
had the sensation of walking, feeling the
floor pass under her feet while she took
steps.
There was something in her courage
to trust my support and to follow her
interests that affected my dancing.
Later, during a round robin, I had a
dance with an able-bodied man I'd
never danced with before, David, where
I gave my center in situations that are
usually slightly delightfully terrifying for
me-up high on a shoulder, hanging up-
side down, the support moving with
momentum, no hands-and I felt deep-
ly relaxed.
I just realized in rewriting this story
that I was in the same situation with
David that Jessica had been in with me.
We truly do instruct each other through
the dancing. [Karen]
"
In reflecting back on what I now
prefer to call the "Dance with Different
Abilities" workshop rather than
"Dance with Different Needs," I ex-
perience an immense gratitude for the
opportunity to not know, to not assume
mobility habitually and to appreciate
more deeply contact in still frames.
Leading a class with physical assump-
tions or habitual statements like "Stand
up," "See the whole space," or giving
any verbal instructions for that matter,
could at times isolate. A good sense of
humor was quickly cultivated. Verbal
interpreting for the blind, sign inter-
preting for the deaf, and a multitude of
modifications allowed for a moment by
moment experience. Along with
everybody else, I seemed to learn a lot
about replacing the use of one sensory
perceptor with another. Seeing with
your skin could not become cliche. My
perceptions began to expand through
the transmission of experience in this at-
mosphere of diverse abilities. Consider-
ing the weight of a partner with a near-
ly total absence of muscular resistance
gave me a profound experience of the
"small dance" and enlightened me
about the sense of an abandoned body
moving through space where my will-
ingness and ability to support was essen-
tial to the pleasure and safety of the
dance. I also did a solo dance with a
wheelchair, lifting it, rolling it over my
back, balancing it on my shoulders. This
allowed me to experience not only the
spirit of the chair but how it was an ex-
tension of its owner's body. The
anatomy of a wheelchair, with or
without a partner in it, was a great
education in mechanics, motion and
caring. The idea that contact improvisa-
tion is an accessible movement form
continues to expand in all directions
regardless of one's physical abilities. A
sense of common ground pervaded the
space. [Alito]

CONTACT WITH
SPECIAL NEEDS
by Louise Richards
& Kevin Finnan
Whenever we tell people that we
teach contact improvisation to people
with severe physical and/ or mental
disabilities they look at us like we're
bonkers. A dance with somebody who
is paralyzed would appear to be a con-
tradiction. How can you dance if you
can't "move"? The study of contact
teaches that there is always movement
in the body, the continual falling with
gravity, and for somebody prostrate
there is always the movement of the
breath. Where there is movement, no
matter how small, there is the possibili-
ty of a dance. It sounds easy to say, and
obvious, but it took a while for us to
prove it to ourselves. We are Kevin Fin-
nan and Louise Richards and we are
members of New Midlands Dance, a
British community dance company with
a commitment to new dance.
In early 1985 Lesley Hutchison
became Artistic Director of the
Midlands Dance Company which was
until then a traditional contemporary
rep touring company receiving an an-
nual revenue grant from the Arts Coun-
cil. Lesley transformed the company in-
to a community dance resource for the
region. In order to reach the widest
possible range of people within the
region, there would have to be a change
in the company's way of working. New
forms, New methods, New audiences,
New dance!
In the summer of 1985 we joined
Lesley in forming the New Midlands
Dance Company. We decided to focus
on youth and special needs groups, sec-
tors of the community with little or no
access to the arts. Our work involves
both performances and workshops in a
residency format. We have just com-
pleted a year long project with special
needs groups and are about to embark
on a similar project with prison/custody
populations, men, women and children.
Whoever we work with: dancers,
students, old, young, able-bodied or
paralyzed, we have found contact im-
provisation to be an accessible and
engaging form to teach.
Our first real experience with a
physically handicapped group was dur-
ing a week long residency at a school for
children with disabilities in Nottingham-
shire. We had expected to be working
initially with mentally handicapped
groups but to our horror our first
residency of a year of touring was to be
in this school. Residencies are usually
one week long and we work with a core
group plus staff; teaching intensively
Volume 13 1988
CQ/CI Sourcebook 149
and performing once or twice during the
week. We had done a lot of preparatory
work, particularly with mentally han-
dicapped groups but none of this really
prepared us for the first session with fif-
teen wheelchair-bound children. The
group was nervous because they didn't
know what to expect. We were nervous
for exactly the same reason. We started
very timidly, introductions and other
warm-ups in a game format to break the
ice, introducing the notion of touch ear-
ly on.
The group was resistant. They were
handicapped so what could dance
possibly be for them? On top of that
they had all the usual teenage hang-ups
about looking stupid, touching each
other and associating dance with pop
records or ballet. Most of them had up-
per body movement, few of them could
move legs or feet. We worked a lot with
contact through fingertips and heads. In
pairs they started to use their weight
through counterbalance and easy
pushing and pulling. At first there was
a real feeling of them doing it to humour
us, but gradually over the week the pairs
moved into duets. They worked on
shared "journey" dances. For example,
two people in one chair supported
through each other's laps or backs, us-
ing their weight to initiate movement in
each other.
Strong bonds developed within the
group, with each other and with us. The
school is in a beautiful wooded environ-
ment so there were countless invitations
to accompany them on walks on the
special wheelchair trails. It was hard not
to form real attachments with two girls
in the group who took me (Louise) for
regular lunchtime strolls. Going up steep
hills they (in their chairs) left me (on my
legs) standing.
I (Kevin) was invited to play soccer
in the playground with pupils and staff.
I was amazed at the speed and vigour
of the game, wheelchairs and crutches
flying past me. Receiving a great pass
from a young lad in a wheelchair on the
wing and filled with sudden enthusiasm
I turned and volleyed the ball
goalwards, only to see it ping off the
head of a passing lad on crutches-
sending him flying. I was mortified,
what had I done? I could see the
headlines: "Dancer assaults crippled
child." I began apologizing to
everybody. To my surprise this pro-
moted gales of laughter at my expense.
I was learning another seminal lesson:
Just because people are less able in some
way it does not mean that they need to
be wrapped in cotton wool and touch-
ed at arms length. The children were
physically adventurous and were
150 CQ/CI Sourcebook
prepared for physical risk.
Over the week, through the dance ses-
sions we became more confident and
they became more adventurous, taking
full weight, spinning their wheelchairs
and supporting themselves through new
body surfaces. We were successful in
challenging their notions about what
dance was and showed them they could
create their own.
During the project we have worked
with a range of disabilities. With the
chronically disabled we start from relax-
ation, rocking, listening to and work-
ing with the breath rather than impos-
ing movement, allowing ourselves to be
with another body, picking up breathing
rhythms and amplifying them into
shared movement. We don't do to peo-
ple. Even the most handicapped person
can move someone else-we lay our
heads in the bowls of their abdomens
and let their breath move us. A shared
responsibility.
We had a dance with a boy called
David who has head movement but no
other body movement. He was propped
on a cushion, we put our heads together.
We explained to him about listening to
the moving point. Slowly it began to roll
around our heads. Sometimes it would
slide, we'd explain to him that we need-
ed it to roll so that we were moving and
being moved by each other. This
developed into a twenty minute dance
of amazing intensity. David had a huge
grin which never left his face.
We don't just work with children.
Similar work at an adult residential cen-
tre developed much further due to our
increased experience and confidence at
that time. We began with everybody out
of their chairs. Finger dances which roll-
ed down arms and into torsos happen-
ed early in the residency. We taught
about weight and momentum and they
learned that their seemingly immobile
weight could affect change in someone
else. A man called Ian resented being
moved and responded with "I'm not
helpless, you know." We told him he
should move us too and he could do this
by letting his weight fall back, thus pull-
ing us up. We really gave our weight in
these counterbalances. By further using
counterbalance and shared balances
back to back or side to side, people
could support themselves on unfamiliar
levels. By using the natural ledges on
our bodies, backs, hips and thighs, we
would feed their weight through our
bodies in the passage from up to down.
We used a lot of "body surfing" and
"log rolling" and developed ways of us-
ing table work both as supporter and
supported. For example, people with
upper body movement were able to use
their arms as levers to get on and
balance on our backs, and were often
able to sit on their folded legs and use
their arms as main supports to take our
weight through their backs. With
careful regard to the weight, this
developed into a dance, transferring
support between us, swapping roles and
using flow and momentum to move.
Early rocking developed into tumbl-
ing, throws and catches where people
could experience spatial disorientation.
It is impossible to explain people's joy
in this work. For more nervous people
we made physical structures to give
movement experiences. For example, in-
terlocked human "tables" became a
mobile support structure. One woman
lay on the backs looking at the ceiling
and said "I've never been upside-down
before."
It is not always possible for people to
communicate verbally as to whether or
not we are achieving anything, par-
ticularly when mentally handicapped.
However we believe the dance com-
municates on a very deep level. In one
session a man got quite violent. He was
working with one of our helpers and
started to lash out with his fists. In-
tuitively I (Louise) grabbed his wrists
but rather than blocking and restrain-
ing him I began to move with him.
Gradually his energy changed from con-
frontation to flow.
Sandra was a group member who
gave no sign of recognition from one
session to another. She didn't seem to
understand any of the work we were do-
ing. One of our warm-up exercises in-
volves passing a touch point from finger
to finger around the circle. On the very
last day I (Kevin) made a point of say-
ing goodbye to her. As we were leaving
she came up, did a wonderful "finger
dance" and touched her finger to the
end of mine. I was speechless, shocked
and deeply moved.
There are many of these stories and
we've chosen these few to try to convey
at least a feeling of the work. What
seems to be a daunting task is incredibly
rewarding. We are not therapists but
dancers who teach dance to people
whatever their abilities because we
believe that dance is for all. We haven't
space here to discuss our performance
work in this context, although it too is
an integral part of the work. Maybe that
will be another article. Certainly we plan
to write another installment on our ex-
periences teaching contact in custodial
settings. tr

BEGINNERS DANCE
by Marie Mannschatz
It is my first contact class after a
seven year break and the first dance at
all after months of painful resistance
against any engagement in the moving
arts. A friend has convinced me to par-
ticipate in this Contact Improvisation
workshop taught by Nancy Stark Smith
in Spain, north of Barcelona.
Very slowly I settle back into the
body, keeping my vital forces way down
inside. The air around me stays empty
and my heart aches in consequence of
neglecting its basic needs.
The teacher tells us to kneel down and
connect with the crowns of our heads.
A tall young dancer with sea-blue eyes
slips towards me and offers his head.
Our bodies hardly move while we sit
quietly tuning to each other. Through
my skull I feel the pulsation of his spinal
liquid, the rhythm of his blood, feel my
hesitant expansion towards him and
decide carefully to open up a little bit
more.
"Let's roll across the floor, keeping
in contact with the top of our heads."
Gliding across the wooden surface we
look for a synchronized movement. His
nose reaches already up into the air,
mine still sticks to the ground.
"Now roll back to the other side of
the hall. Allow yourself to go slow;
when you feel stabilized in your connec-
tion you may want to develop a more
open dance with each other, from time
to time coming back to the head con-
nection."
We exchange no opinions about our
way of working together. His attitude
is serious and alert. We both focus on
our task, intending to align our abilities.
Like siamese twins we are rolling
now, grown together at the crowns of
our heads. My brother starts to guide
me through the space, passing arms,
feet, legs and torsos of other dancers.
He calls me internally and waits for my
answers. His touch is delicate and firm
at the same time. I try to catch an ex-
pression of emotion in his face. His gaze
is relaxed and empty, while he is diving
deeper and deeper into the realms of my
intuitive self.
Reaching a rare level of trust I allow
him to hold me, carry me and let go of
me. For a tiny second I am as happy as
a toddler. But as my mind is realizing
the long lost feeling, it vanishes under
the weight of memory.
My partner keeps moving. Inhaling
the fresh smell of his sweat I curl into
his lap and want to stay there, losing my
sense of self, ready to slip back into the
liquid warmth and timeless security of
a womb.
He keeps moving. He is obviously an
experienced dancer, unlimited in his
gentle presence, clear in his direction,
while my perception is flooded by im-
ages, feelings, rhythms and vibrations.
Like a little boat I am rocking across his
body waves, feeling his bones and soft
Volume 13 1988
stILL G
NfOVI
CQICI Sourcebook 153
tissues communicating with mine. By
now my lungs empty completely. Each
exhalation goes further and is
sometimes accompanied by an un-
conscious sound of agreement. Our
breathing patterns overlap, we lose
culture and age, but I stay always aware
of being a woman.
For the glimpse of a moment a silvery
net is woven around us by dashes of
sunlight, spread all over our bodies. The
next minute we are blades of grass in the
summer wind, intermingling with the
group energy. The two of us are not
alone in this space-we touch others
flying through their territory, grateful:
ly catching the arm that supports us the
moment we lose our balance. A little
laughter jumps out of my throat. Hey,
babe, life can be fun!
No reaction on his side. He always
keeps moving.
Finally we discover our spiral spheres
and now I am expanding completely.
Yes! Fun! Love! Spirals up and down
and up again in different speeds and col-
ours. Spirals towards a subtle
completion.
Our bodies agree to finish the dance.
Gently we unwind the energy threads
which carried the codes of body
language. A few more touches and
swirls and thanks to the floor. Then:
Gravity and empty space.
As soon as the dance is over, the com-
munication system is fallen apart. Here
I am with onehundredtwentyseven ques-
tions I never dare to ask: Is a dance like
this daily life for him? Was the level of
contact my own projection? Did he en-
joy the fluidity of our movements as
well? Which images arose for him?
What did he learn?
The class continues, other dancers
come, but this first dance stays with me.
My questions unfold into an investiga-
tion of relationship-patterns. Something
has been healed inside of me, has been
replaced by soft openness, curiosity and
a need for creative expression.
In the dew of the next morning I sit
down to continue the dance with my
type>,yriter. Across the printed letters my
eyes are wandering towards the milky
bluish mountains in the distance. Birds
busy with being surround me and the
heavy sneeze of a Spanish farmer echoes
through the valley and is answered by
angry barking dogs. *

DEEPENING
by Tom Trenda
Deepening combines elements of
work that I've been doing in The
Tragertm Approach, Authentic Move-
ment, Re-Evaluation (CO-)Counseling,
and Contact Improvisation. This work
was first presented in a class that I
taught with Danny Mack and Susan
Brokaw at the 1987 Breitenbush Jam.
I listened to literally hours of personal
testimony and feedback by highly ex-
perienced contactors after this class,
which indicated to me that I was "on
to something."
This work is not about the advanc-
ing of Contact Improvisation skills ex-
clusively, although from the feedback
I've received, it seems to be very effec-
tive in that way. It can be applied in
various movement contexts, from dance
improvisation to dance therapy. It is a
very specific structured form which
demands a high amount of commit-
ment, attention, and focus.
What is Deepening?
Listening to another while they move
is at the heart of this experience. Like
Authentic Movement, this form is about
one person putting their undivided at-
tention on another, without judgement,
comment, or intentionally shaping their
process. Simply being there with that
person with total commitment to
whatever they do, as "witness" to their
process. Unlike Authentic Movement
however, the "witness" uses not only
their eyes to "pay attention to," but
also their hands and (later) their bodies.
Why touch? Because touch gives a
kind of information that cannot be ob-
tained through just observing. I am not
only looking for the information that I
can get simply from how I see your
movement. This experience is deeply
physical. This kind of connection, this
kind of "being with," is not only
through a visual mode, but includes the
kinesthetic/tactile as well. It is the ex-
perience of seeing, touching, sensing,
and moving with, on all levels of so-
meone's body, from periphery to core.
Feeling experiences which often arise
out of this kind of deep, intimate shar-
ing are those of profound personal
validation and support.
Preparation
I first have people pair up and I teach
basic Trager hands-on skills. With one
person laying on their back, the other
places their hands somewhere on their
partner's body and "settles in." The
focus is on deepening the level of con-
tact with one's hands through the sur-
face structures of their partner's body
such as skin and body shape to the
deeper structures such as skeleton and
organ. Once this kind of contact is sens-
ed, the active partner begins to lightly
rock the body of their partner, sensing
for the movement of the skeleton and
the pathways this movement is travel-
ing on. This works beautifully to drop
people into their body's core, and to
engage them in focusing deeply into
another person's physical structure.
Through the work of Milton Trager, I
have learned that by developing sen-
sitivity in the hands along with the
focusing capacities of the mind, the sur-
face of the body can be penetrated
allowing core structures to be contacted
and moved.
This kind of "penetration" or depth
of touch is necessary in the Deepening
experience. The intent, once this is ob-
tained, however, is not to "do"
anyt~ing with those deeper structures,
but simply to contact them, to listen and
to follow what is happening in there
while that person is moving.
The basic Deepening structure
The basic structure begins with one
person in the pair moving on their own
and the other person observing.
Throughout this whole experience the
person moving is focused on referenc-
ing everything to their own center, edge,
flow, timing, weight, momentum, inten-
tion, etc. In other words, they are solo-
ing. The person watching is following
with their eyes, the whole or any part
of this person's body moving-wherever
their attention is drawn.
Next, the person observing moves in-
to the other's kinesphere and begins to
follow the movement of any part,
[touching it] with their hands. Here it
is helpful for the person moving to work
at a slow pace. This facilitates contact
from hand to body. It is also important
to note here that the person "soloing"
is not initiating or "leading" the other,
but simply moving while someone
moves with them. They are paying at-
tention to their own movement process.
Their partner is also paying attention to
the movement process of the person
soloing, with their eyes and hands and
whole being. This is an experience of
"one-way attention." In other words,
both partners are paying attention to
one person's dance. It sometimes re-
quires an active intention on the part of
Volume 13 1988
the person following to not initiate,
limit, or direct their partner's move-
ment, especially if they have had a lot
of Contact Improvisation experience.
Noticing the subtle ways in which we do
this in Contact Improvisation is one of
the benefits of practicing the Deepen-
ing form. This means that when a move-
ment is going outside of one's reach
space, the follower must simply let it go.
To pull the motion back towards oneself
by changing the pressure would inhibit
the full movement of one's partner.
It is helpful for the kind of deep con-
tact I spoke of earlier that the person
Applying Deepening in a
Contact lmprov context
If I am working with a group of Con-
tact Improvisors, I will have people
focus on bringing the experience from
the edge (hands) to the center (torso),
and throughout the whole body. The
person "paying attention to" their part-
ner (while that person continues to
"solo") begins to use the rest of their
body to follow, track, and read their
partner's movement in just the way that
they were using their hands earlier. Here
I make it clear that it is not about "go-
ing after contact," but is about allow-
0
0
_c
0.
Tom Trenda & Bev Rackoff
following does not ,jump around with
their hands, but rather rests on one body
part, "reading" as much information as
is possible about the whole organism
from that one moving part. There is
everything to be learned about a whole
from any of its parts.
Of course, it is a lie to say that the
person moving is "soloing." As soon as
one is aware of being watched there is
interaction. And when one is being
listened to through touch, there is cer-
tainly a presence there to be reckoned
with. But just as one can do a "solo"
dance piece with many people watching,
one can also solo while being touched
if the person touching has a presence of
respectful attention.
ing any body part that is convenient to
come into this role, as the movement
flows through one's kinesphere. This
prevents people from throwing their
centers into contact. It is O.K. to be in
the way of your partner's motion-you
are a part of the environment in your
partner's dance.
Issues of weight support invariably
come up at this point. Torsos weigh
more than hands. If I am following a
Volume 13 1988
movement with my hand I can be above
or below it, without much change in
weight. But if I follow someone's move-
ment with my torso to a place where I
am on top of them, they are supporting
my weight, but I am supporting their
movement. Similarly, if their movement
takes them to a place on top of me, I
focus on supporting the movement, not
the weight. The focus is not on giving
and taking body weight. This is often
what makes Contact Improvisation so
static: you give me your weight, I'll give
you my weight, and [thus] the dance of
all the signals and responses associated
with this. Weight sharing is one by-
product, just something that happens to
happen, in the process of reading so-
meone's movement with certain parts of
your body in the Deepening form.
At this point, I might suggest the
soloist begin playing with moving faster,
changing levels, and jumping. Or the
partners can allow the roles to dissolve,
keep moving, and take from this struc-
ture anything they feel [like] integrating.
I also structure sessions for specifical-
ly working on connecting in this
Deepening way in running, falling, and
jumping. This seems to be very
challenging-to come into contact with
a quickly moving body without shaping
the course of that jump, run, or fall.
The outcome
Some of the benefits of doing
Deepening are rooted in the fact that it
is about taking turns. How often do we
get to receive such undivided attention
to such a major part of ourselves? To
be supported in whatever we are doing?
Receiving this kind of attention from so-
meone enables one to go deeper into
their own body/movement process. This
form creates a high degree of safety for
people to explore their own movement.
As the person moving, you get very
committed to your own movement
without a lot of ego investment. This
comes from someone else's commitment
to your movement without ego. As the
person following, focusing one someone
else enables you to let go of any of your
own stuff about your own movement.
It takes the focus off yourself and how
well you are dancing.
Through practicing this work a new
relationship to "form" can be
discovered. It could be said that "form
follows following." Awareness of form
grows organically from the inside.
Pathways begin to emerge through the
skin and extend out of (and back into)
the body. It comes unself-consciously
from the mutual commitment to follow-
ing each other's internal movement
pathways. *
CQICI Sourcebook 155
EXPOSED TO GRAVITY
What follows are two essays, one
by each of the initiators of The Ex-
posed to Gravity Project, a col-
laborative organization specializing
in movement arts and education for
physically disabled people.
For more information on their ac-
tivities and videotapes, write to:
The Exposed to Gravity Project,
c/o Alan Ptashek, 5863 Birch
Court, Oakland, CA 94618; Tel.
(415) 653-7098 or 644-8301.
by Bruce Curtis and Alan Ptashek
L
by Bruce Curtis
Often when I would con-
template dancing, and look at all
the wonderful bodies swiftly spinn-
ing and flashing across the floor, it
would seem to me that I was
another species of Human. It felt
much the same way to me upon
traveling to Guatemala, going to the
end of the road in the rain forest,
and then wandering among the
village people that live there. The
people stopped what they were do-
ing and stared at me while I was
staring at them, everyone filled with
curiosity.
Since my body looks different and
moves differently, I've often
wondered, how I can participate in
this dancing when it doesn't seem
that we share a commonality of
movement. I would wonder how
· they ever learned to move like that,
what was their secret? Lots of
classes? Born with rhythm? Survival
of the fittest? It seemed pretty clear
to me, whatever the reason, that for
me to dance, the method would
have to come from my personal ex-
perience of how my body moves,
not from copying how non-disabled
people move.
When I first began to explore
moving my body in rhythm (before
I knew of Contact Improvisation),
I wanted to dance like everyone else
with grand gestures, lots of wiggles
in the right places, relaxed and cool.
But a body in a wheelchair doesn't
seem capable of moving like that
and it was pretty scary to experi-
ment in front of people. So I would
move my shoulders while shifting
my weight to the rhythm of the
music, dancing inside my body with
grand gestures and lots of wiggles.
At some point it occurred to me that
this micro-movement was just as
much dancing as what everyone else
did with their whole body. It may
not have looked the same but the
rhythms flowing through my body,
unlocking the grip of my mind and
fears, came from the same primal
experience. With this realization, I
began to lift my hands, lengthen the
movement of my arms and deepen
my reliance on the validity of the
movements that were emerging
from my body. I found my body
starting to move in patterns that did
not exist in my mind or in my
previous experience. When I would
dance in this manner, and look in-
to the eyes of someone dancing next
to me, a smile would spread across
our faces from the joy in our hearts.
The dance was within us.
I would like to explain very
generally how Spinal Cord Injury
(SCI) affects most bodies. To injure
the spinal cord in the back or waist
can result in paraplegia, with no
muscle control in the waist or legs
but normal use of the arms and
hands. An injury to the neck can
result in quadriplegia. Most
quadriplegics are unable to move
any muscles below a mid-chest
156 CQ/CI Sourcebook
Volume13 1988
level. Their shoulder, bicep and
wrist muscles are often good with
triceps weak and finger muscles
nonfunctional. In those muscles that
are affected by SCI, sometimes a
movement will create a spasm
which can start a leg or the whole
body shaking or momentarily to go
rigid. Breathing is done through the
diaphragm with each breath being
shallow. There is usually no surface
skin feeling below the mid-chest
level so it is possible to cut or bruise
this area without being aware that
it has happened. (A good visualiza-
tion of how a body like this moves
is to look at a newborn baby. Its
mind does not control how the
head, each limb, or the body as a
whole responds to gravity or posi-
tioning. If the head is not sup-
ported, it will immediately fall.)
Each person's degree of function
varies, depending on the severity of
Volume 13 1988
the original injury, their body tone
before and after the injury, and the
person's willingness to experiment
with their body and environment.
What is most important to
remember is that each body, disabl-
ed or not, is unique and presents
another opportunity to explore
what movement is possible.
I am a quadriplegic but my lower
body is able to feel pressure when
touched and therefore I can usually
guess where my legs or feet are posi-
tioned. Since my feeling is minimal,
I often use my eyes to tell me exact-
ly where my body is. My body is
long (6 ft. 2 in.), lean, limber and
I love it! I have used a wheelchair
for 20 years now, living, working
and playing around the world. My
chair has become a part of my body.
It has movements, sounds and
rhythms that describe the texture of
the ground that I am moving across,
"O
:;,-
0
....
0
@
Alan Ptashek and Bruce Curtis
defining the space that I am passing
through.
Gravity is very noticeable to me
since my muscles cannot stand in
opposition to it. When sitting, my
chair supports me above the
ground. If I lean in any direction too
far away from the center of my sup-
port, gravity will bring me down. I
really don't mind falling down since
it's never that far. My body will
sense a lack of support immediate-
ly and since my muscles can only
delay the fall, I usually don't think
about how to keep from falling, but
rather, how to use the momentum
or a quick push off a nearby surface
to help me arrive on the ground in
a safe manner. Most of the time, I
CQ/CI Sourcebook 157
(cont'd)
just prepare to meet the ground.
Gravity is very constant and
dependable and I use it often to
move my body when my muscles
cannot. When sitting in my chair,
the ground is rarely level under me
and I will feel gravity moving me in
some direction. Since my arms are
not awesome, I am always looking
for ways to ride the flow of gravi-
ty, by analyzing the angle of the
plane of the surface in front of me
and then moving to a point that op-
timizes the motion in the direction
that I want to go. For example,
climbing up a hill against gravity is
made easier by tacking diagonally
across it. Often I will stop to rest by
finding that place of balance where
momentum is briefly suspended bet-
ween possibilities, and then with a
slight push, I will join with the flow
once again. This is how I play while
moving through the world, riding
gravity and looking for cheap
thrills.
I was first introduced to Contact
Improvisation while on the March
for Peace in Central America, 250
people from 30 countries traveling
overland from Panama to Mexico
City. We had stopped for a rest at
a coffee plantation high in the nor-
thern mountains of Nicaragua.
Another marcher, Holly Hamilton,
and I went for a walk and
discovered an abandoned coffee
drying courtyard made of concrete
with a view of the countryside for
30 miles. Since I had a cassette
player with a tape of Andreas
Vollenveider in my backpack, I of-
fered to demonstrate how I could
dance with my chair. To my amaze-
ment, Holly began moving with me
without my having to explain much
at all! After we danced, she told me
about Contact Improvisation and I
decided that this was an important
discovery for disabled people. A
dance form that uses movement,
gravity and their relationship to
each other as revealed through
158 CQ/CI Sourcelx>ok
changing points of balance, seemed
ideal for people like myself whose
daily interaction with their environ-
ment is grounded in the same
experiences.
My first experiments with Con-
tact Improvisors Holly Hamilton
and Arthur Hull involved moving
with my chair in spirals, using the
momentum of my partner as the
force that moved me. Through the
point of contact, I would feel the
potential for movement waiting to
be released, the intention to move
shifting between me and my part-
ner, and for the first time I felt tru-
ly equal in the creating of the dance!
At this stage, I was primarily look-
ing at my dancing like a ballet.
Grand sweeping movements of my
chair without my having to push it.
For me the dance was to move
across the floor in a graceful, fluid
manner, while making interesting
patterns and smooth transitions.
This early work was videotaped and
after showing it to Alan Ptashek, a
great teacher of movement, I began
to learn with him how to have a
physical conversation through the
movement form Contact
Improvisation.
I went through what must be
familiar issues to anyone beginning
to learn the language of Contact Im-
provisation. Keep your eyes open,
don't get lost inside your head.
Look for opportunities to support
your partner, move in underneath
them. Don't give or take weight
without listening for the agreement
of your partner's body. Let each
move evolve from mutual agree-
ment, rather than having an image
in your own mind dictate what hap-
pens next. Mutual trust is based on
uncompromised attention, so stay
in the present moment.
In the beginning, my interest in
Contact Improvisation was always
oriented towards performing a
choreographed dance by stringing
together all the fascinating moves
and deleting the "dull" spaces in bet-
ween. When we began practicing
for our first performance, I would
always be asking Alan what move-
ment we would start or finish with.
It wasn't until the last rehearsal,
while feeling totally nervous, I
realized that without a
choreographed plan of action, our
practices were always full of ex-
citing moments of improvisation.
Since Alan and I were creatively and
unhesitatingly exploring our
possibilities, what emerged was a
fascinating conversation of move-
ment. I also learned how important
it was to remember to have fun
while dancing. So, I started playing
with Alan the same way that I
would play off the wall in a hallway
or moving through cars while cross-
ing the street. Being spontaneous
and playing together with gravity.
When I· first started practicing
Contact Improvisation, it was
necessary for me to have music
playing in the background. It was
a strong habit- if you want to
dance, put on some music. Initial-
ly, Alan discouraged this because
the body tends to move to the
rhythm of the music, instead of to
the internal rhythm of shifting
weight, sensation and the com-
munication that goes back and forth
between the dancers. As I listened
more intently to Alan's body
through the point of contact,
minutely increasing or decreasing
pressure against my body, I would
notice afterwards that I couldn't
remember hearing the music while
we danced. The attention of my
mind was so focused that music, au-
dience, monitoring my self-image,
everything external to the next
movement vanished. Now when we
practice, we sometimes turn off the
music to listen to the sounds of our
breathing or the squeaks and
grunts. At performances we used
taped or live music to help fill in the
environment for the audience as
well as set up an emotional mood.
The first time Alan lifted me out
of my chair, I had no idea what was
happening until I was looking down
from his shoulders. For a person in
a wheelchair, control over your own
body's safety is never easily relin-
quished to someone else's judge-
ment. My life is full of people who
want to help me by grabbing my
body or my chair and dragging me
somewhere in an unsafe manner.
On top of Alan's shoulders I could
feel the ground through his body
solidly supporting me against gravi-
ty. Whether sitting in a chair or
wrapped around someone's body,
when I feel supported against the
Volume 13 1988
flow of gravity, it isn't scary to let
go of holding on and explore new
possibilities. Once after a perfor-
mance Alan said, " ... when we
dance together, I always feel that
when I offer you two different op-
tions, your response is Yes-Yes. I
rarely feel that you resist what I of-
fer you." One reason for this is
because I explore my movement by
playing off of Alan's movement, any
movement, they all have
possibilities. Another reason is that
you don't have to say no to someone
who is listening to your body.
In the beginning, I was concern-
ed about how I looked while I was
dancing. Was I sitting up straight or
slumped down. If my leg began to
spasm, jumped out straight and
started shaking, what would people
think? In our discussions about
these concerns, we agreed that these
were not problems but oppor-
tunities. They were not to be ig-
nored, feared or repressed. The
point was not to clamp down and
control my body but to listen to it,
accepting whatever movement was
inherent. If a spasm did occur, we
would just keep on dancing and let
it happen, allowing the spasm to
become part of the dance.
Sometimes we would play off of the
spasm, with Alan taking a rigid leg
and using it as a pivot point to move
my chair in a spiral. When we don't
make fences around our bodies, all
movements become acceptable.
Two weeks ago, I began practic-
ing Contact Improvisation with Pat-
ty Overland, a disabled woman
who uses a wheelchair. She had very
little prior experience with Contact
and I had never tried to teach it to
anyone. So while we were trying to
figure out how two people in
wheelchairs do Contact, she was
learning how to improvise her own
ways of playing and shaping our
duet. Even though music helped her
to be more comfortable, (Patty was
used to moving her body in rhythm
to rock-n-roll), we had to turn off
the music so that she could easily
listen to my body through hers. It
is difficult to stay in close contact
with each other because the chairs
are rigid and don't wrap around
each other like bodies do. Both of
Volume 13 1988
us have limited use of our waist
muscles, (and her right shoulder is
weak), so, it has forced us to experi-
ment a lot. We have to lean out
away from our chairs to remain in
contact while allowing enough
space for our chairs to move off the
momentum from the shifting
balance between us. We have also
started working off each other's
chairs like they were bodies. Since
we feel that our chairs are part of
ourselves, it feels natural to grab
hold of or push off of a chair if it
is in front of us at the moment. Last
week, I discovered that she could
get out of her chair, down to the
floor and then back into her chair
fairly easily, so we started incor-
porating this into our dancing.
We feel it is important that the
dance comes from the realities of
our bodies and our chairs, rather
than trying to move like non-
disabled dancers. We have a rela-
tionship with our disability that has
three parts-how our bodies move,
how our chairs move and how they
both move together. There is a great
joy that comes from coaxing a sub-
tle, minute movement from a part
of our bodies that we do not con-
trol and then using the momentum
of the changing point of balance
between us, to slowly begin turning
a wheel in a sweeping arc. Since Pat-
ty and I did not know each other
personally before we started danc-
ing, or the specifics of each other's
body, it has been emotionally scary
to explore how to combine all of
these variables, while trying to be
relaxed and comfortable enough to
encourage our creativity. Our will-
ingness to expose our awkwardness
could become stifled because on the
way to practice, someone on the
street has once again violated our
personal space or negatively af-
fected our self-image so that when
we arrive, our bodies aren't really
open to being vulnerable. If our
fears don't get in the way, the
mechanics of moving can make it
difficult for us to keep our com-
munication tightly focused without
interruption. For example, when
our bodies are not centered in our
chairs, there is a strong habit to stop
dancing for a moment, straighten
ourselves, and then continue. Yet,
working within the physical limita-
tions that we have sometimes re-
quires us to break physical com-
munication long enough to make
the next move possible.
When you are in the present mo-
ment and listening to your partner's
body, the point of contact can
bridge the empty space between
your bodies until the dance brings
you both home again.
So what do I share in common
with all those wonderful dancing
bodies gracefully spinning and
flashing all over the floor? Another
wonderful body filled with the
movements of the glorious life that
is within every part of me, just try-
ing to let those movements come
out in all their joyful wiggles and
spontaneous jumps. Another danc-
ing body that is laughing and play-
ing with the rhythms that join my
body with someone else's body,
communicating in the most intimate
way our willingness to open our
hearts and be vulnerable to each
other. We are all dancing bodies,
playing together to ignite the light
that shines from our eyes and our
smiles to touch the souls of all who
can see us dancing.
I am a disabled person who has
spent half of my life trying to open
more possibilities in society for
myself and other disabled people
through legislation, civil rights
demonstrations, community
organizing and the Independent Liv-
ing Movement. Yet all of these ac-
complishments have not caused the
able bodied stranger that I would
encounter in my work to open their
hearts and let their eyes show how
happy they were to see me. I feel
that it is just as important to talk
about what we as human beings
share in common as it is to show it;
through the joy that we have in each
and every one of our physical
movements, through the
fearlessness of our hearts which has
been.forged by the daily journeys in-
to the dark of our souls, and
through the beauty of ourselves that
we are willing to reveal to the
ablebodied and the disabled
strangers that we meet. As disabled
people, we have shown that we can
survive in this world, now it is time
to show that we can dance too. The
world needs our joy, our dancing.

EXPOSED TO GRAVITY
II.
by Alan Ptashek
In the SUllllller of 1986, Bruce
Curtis called me and introduced
himself as a physically disabled man
who had become very interested in
Contact Improvisation. He describ-
ed his previous experiences with this
movement form as well as his
travels within and outside of the
United States. Without having met
him, I was already seeing Bruce in
terms of the mobility and curiosity
that had led him to explore Contact
Improvisation, a dance form that I
have been practicing, developing
and teaching since 1974. I was also
thinking of how I might interact
with a person who has spent more
than half of his life in a wheelchair.
I knew that within myself and most
able-bodied people, there exists a
wide array of pre-conditioned
responses and behavior that has
limited and confused our interac-
tions with disabled people. At the
extremes of one's discom-
fort/ awkwardness with disabled
people (and vice-versa), we may un-
consciously be physically distancing
ourselves, avoiding direct or sus-
tained eye contact, or behaving in
an overly protective manner.
As I viewed tapes of Bruce's
earlier dances, I felt excited by the
possibilities for developing a
physical vocabulary between us,
one that could challenge and ex-
pand previously held ideas about
the limits of movement for those
with spinal cord injuries. Bruce and
I acknowledged that there was
much more ground to explore, and
we began a regular practice of Con-
tact Improvisation, a collaboration
that held equal amounts of en-
thusiasm, caution and wonderment
for both of us.
Learning where and how one's
Contact partner senses and carries
their center of gravity is essential to
the development of trust and
physical rapport. Bruce's wheelchair
was an immediate part of that lear-
ning for me. The man and his
wheelchair are definitely not in-
separable, and a constant awareness
needs to be maintained for intuitive-
ly and· reflexively determining the
boundaries and shifts of balance
Alan Ptashek and Bruce Curtis
between Bruce's body and the struc-

ture of his chair. In another sense,

Volume 13 1988
Improvisation duets, the high
velocity of movement and risk bet-
ween Bruce and myself rriay sudden-
ly be experienced as a heightened
state of alertness and precision, our
awareness of time and space relax-
ed and expanded, moving safely
through our movement possibilities.
Bruce's wheelchair is surprising-
ly light and responsive to subtle
degrees of weight and momentum,
although it is Bruce's receptivity to
those subtleties that determines how
my body and movement can shape
itself with his chair. I have leaned
and thrown my weight on Bruce
and his chair from many angles and
impulses. We have occ"asionally
fallen onto one another's body and
the floor, with the chair somehow
cooperating for our safety and con-
tinued contact with each other.
Neither of us have ever been injured
in our dancing.
Our warm-ups include taking
weight across one another's back
and hips, counter-balancing
through the extension and grip of
each other's arms and hands, slow-
ly stretching and releasing tension
from muscle and joints. We focus
deeply on internal awareness of
breathing and finely-tuned balance.
Where certain areas of Bruce's
spinal and muscular functioning is
limited, I have developed an in-
tuitive capacity to locate those parts
of his limbs and torso where he can
initiate movement, and direct the
needed amount of pressure to sus-
tain our surface of contact without
slipping or losing our communica-
tion. Whether this limited physical
functioning is within his fingers or
around his pelvis, there is always
enough time and attention for us to
kinesthetically scan through our
bodies for other areas of strength,
support and balance. It is this "scan-
ning" that creates the movement
vocabulary which is shared between
Bruce and myself.
Our work together has been
observed and commented on by
movement and somatic educators,
dance and physical therapists, per-
forming artists and other people
with physical disabilities, both less
and more severe than Bruce's. We
have performed before a variety of
audiences within the San Francisco
Bay Area, most recently through
The John F. Kennedy Center's
Special Arts International Program,
at Grace Cathedral Church and at
The New Performance Gallery.
University and cable TV stations
have produced and aired tapes of
our work. Exposed to Gravity:
Two Men Dancing, a video edit of
our work, has been screened at
various educational video festivals
in the U.S., Mexico, Brazil,
Nicaragua and China. Our practice
sessions and video tapes have been
viewed by individuals and groups
who are interested in movement and
body awareness for those with
spinal cord injuries.
Bruce and I do not look upon our
dancing as a particular form of
rehabilitative or therapeutic work,
although those aspects and benefits
are acknowledged and may be ap-
plicable in working with other
disabled people and those who are
interested in movement education
and disabilities. In our collabora-
tion, there isn't any specific condi-
tion that we are judging or attemp-
ting to change. Contact Improvisa-
tion is very much of a self-informing
movement practice in which the
participants are continually
recognizing and adapting to the
changing circumstances of their
mutual dance. Bruce and I are con-
scious of the kinesthetic and pro-
prioceptive experiences that are for-
ming our individual patterns of
movement, as well as the quality of
the contact between us.
New movement capabilities in
spinal-injured people is an issue that
Bruce and I have discussed with
great interest and enthusiasm,
especially after we've danced. These
discussions are filled with the im-
ages and sensations that linger long
past the actual dancing. While
Bruce cannot outwardly move his
legs, I know that he can feel a cer-
tain amount of pressure from my
weight resting or rolling across the
bones in his legs. A greater amount
of my weight across the same area
may create some sensation or feel-
ing of contact with the floor beneath
him, as well as that same pressure
sending sensation, feedback, im-
pulse and action to other areas of
his body. We do not have to stop
and reflect on these sensations, as
we are continually listening through
the natural momentum that carries
and sustains our dancing. What re-
n;iains important to my participa-
t10n and learning is that I maintain ~

Bruce Curtis (left) and Alan Ptashek
an active and attentive relationship
to all areas of Bruce's body
throughout the time of our dancing.
There are no "dead" or unfeeling
parts of Bruce in my relationship to
him as a moving human body who
is as fully involved in the life of our
dance as I am. Comparisons bet-
ween our abilities are limiting and
too predictable if based solely on
physical skill and functioning. Our
minds, hearts and creativity join us
in deep and powerful ways, that in-
tegrate and often transcend the ob-
vious physical differences in our
bodies.
In a Contact Improvisation
dance, there are somatic activities
taking place on many levels of con-
sciousness and perception. The
glandular and neuromuscular
systems are being filled with sensa-
tion, becoming a direct source for
the communication between still or
moving partners. What is happen-
ing while we are rolling, falling and
162 CQ/CI Sourcebook
guiding ourselves and partner via
the feedback exchanged through
skin, nervous system and internal
organs? As our body spins and sud-
denly shifts from standing or sitting
in a chair to balancing on arms or
head, what accompanying changes
are ignited through the body as
cerebra-spinal fluid or adrenal
gland activity pulses through the
moving partners? To what extent
does emotional excitement play
upon the ability to feel movement
and expand ourselves motionally?
In my movement with Bruce, these
questions and interests arise and
provide direction for our future ex-
plorations. I'm reminded of a par-
ticular image described by Bruce
after one of our practice sessions.
He referred to parts of his pelvis and
legs as having "black holes,"
whereby reaching through his senses
he goes "fishing with the flashlight
of my awareness" into those black
holes, in a sense, providing the
pathway for sensations to be felt.
Movement can't be forced in any
"O
::;,-
0
0
@
body. Desire and risk are called
upon if there is to be a deepened and
more fully conscious movement for
any human being.
As we continue our collabora-
tion, Bruce and I are interested in
moving with other disabled and
able-bodied people, and in offering
lecture-demonstrations and
workshops. We want to work with
other individuals, groups and
organizations who support the
development of movement
arts/ education as a valuable and in-
spiring basis for play and com-
munication for people of all levels
of ability and disability. In these
ways, Bruce and I hope to further
explore and share with others a
dance and dialogue that we have
delighted in. 0
Volume 13 1988
Yes, a group of us have been contacting
regularly here in metropolitan RICHMOND,
[VIRGINIA] and s!owly rousing the curiosi-
ty of others. "ls it a sport?" "Oh, it's
choreographed." Anyway, recently DENNY
SORENSON, ED CLAP, KEN
MANHEIMER, SUZANNE NAY and I per-
formed publicly and in my attempt to
understand, at least translate my experience
into words, I've written some reflections
from the day:
Contact in an Alley
I had noticed the fluorescent flyers stapl-
ed to the walls of Fulton School. How could
I miss them amid the otherwise drab chip-
ped paint on the walls? I picked up an orange
one from a table in one of the halls, studied
the collage which incorporated the Rich-
mond skyline atop a pile of rubbish, then
read its only words, "Ram Cat Gallery," and
wondered why I had never heard about this
gallery before. Was this to be a new display
space for art?
Several weeks later, Suzanne Nay, one of
the members of the group I'd been doing
Contact lmprov with, asked me whether I'd
be interested in participating in Ram Cat
Gallery. To her I addressed a barrage of
questions.
Downtown Richmond is laced with a net-
work of alleys. Usually you only know the
one behind your apartment and the one
behind your workplace. Although I'm
familiar with many more (they're great for
evening walks), I had never encountered the
alley called Ram Cat. It's a typical cob-
blestone alley, out of the way, unknown but
to a few, and like every alley, used regular-
ly by sanitation engineers. This section of
town like all of historic Richmond had its
peculiar sites and stories. At one end of the
alley stands a three story milk bottle,
in 1913 the home of a dairy, and at the
other end, the studio of Blab TV, a local
cable show featuring philosophers
er ... um ... publicly blabbing.
Several artists, Suzanne one of them, had
chosen Ram Cat as a site for visual artists,
musicians and performing artists to showcase
their wares. Did I want to partake in this
event by doing Contact outdoors? I said yes
and several days later, a few days before the
show, investigated the space which I quick-
ly found to be full of glass, parts of pipes,
strips of wood and a hodgepodge of other
items that would make dancing much more
than a challenge. After envisioning myself
rolling across the ground, rising with my face
and limbs bloodied by what I had en-
countered en route, I considered renegging
on my previous agreement. Art for art sake
is fine but blood is blood.
Despite promises of a cleanup, we who
had agreed to perform continued to enter-
tain the idea of merely playing spectators that
day, a much safer role. Then the carpet was
rolled in and once unfurled, five gleeful
dancers pou need on it to begin to ex-
perience Contact in the wilds of a Richmond
alley.
Relatively new to Contact, I found alley
dancing invigorating. How could I not res-
pond to the fifty-year-old, keyless piano (us-
ed as accompaniment for another dance)
found amid a pile of Coke bottles, men's
Volume 13 1988
shoes, t-shirts, tires, used condoms and other
artifacts of American culture? Beneath two
trees growing out of brick walls, we tumbl-
ed, pushed, pulled, and leaped in response
to each other but also in response to the sun,
the fresh air and to the angles of the buildings
unique to Ram Cat.
Whether we were conscious of it or not
at the time, our dance was influenced by the
environment. We all later agreed how the
quality of our moves differed from those per-
formed in our usual wooden floor, high ceil-
ing dance space. The cushion the carpet pro-
vided made our more pedestrian moves such
as walking and crawling more springy and
made falling less threatening. The slight in-
cline of the ground sometimes contributed
to the momentum and direction of a roll. Be-
ing outdoors, reminiscent of children on
recess, allowed us to be more playful.
Although we more or less stayed within the
confines of the carpet, a space much smaller
than what we were used to, the diminished
size did not diminish the breadth of our ex-
pression. With the idea of performance in
mind, we had earlier given ourselves a time
limit, but while dancing it was obvious that
we didn't want to stop.
The rawness of the alley suits Contact.
Contact is not sterile or refined, a manufac-
tured product assuaging a public that wants
art to be more or less predictable and
beautiful, pleasant enough to be displayed
like souvenirs from last summer's trip to
Europe. Contact is more primitive, disregar-
ding etiquette and incorporating a full range
of human expression. Bodies intermingle in-
timately continually. Bodies graze and press
against each other with the ease of a chat
and the impassioned eloquence of
discourse. The aim of Contact is not to create
forms, to reach toward lofty ideals or vulgar
ones, but to sustain a gestural communica-
tion with another. The product, then, is the
result of dancers exchanging their weight
and support with integrity, innocence and
responsibility. The power of the form
originates in its authenticity, in presenting the
dance in its moment of becoming.
This, it seems, is where the element of im-
provisation comes in. Improvisation stems
from a source that is not solely intellectual
but integral. Not only the mind but the body
too generates the composition. The Carte-
sian problem of dualism disappears, neither
mind nor body attributed with greater heirar-
chical importance. The composition is a fu-
sion of the self as whole. Out of this concor-
dance, spontaneous movements arise
naturally and last as long as the dancers are
able to sustain its many evolutions.
More than any other dance form, Contact
resembles life, at least my life with its cons-
tant flux and need to make decisions within
a moment's notice. The improvisational
movements are organic, not polished time
after time in the studio, and are influenced
constantly by whomever I come in contact
with.
The alley has contributed to the group's
general knowledge of Contact. I know also
that it affected some of the spectators.
Several days after we had danced, a friend
of mine mentioned how she had gone to a
museum and left after ten minutes. Her
reason: the controlled and lifeless environ-
ment contradicted the intention of art. She
had been touched by the vitality of the alley.
[Cheryl Pallant]
CQ!CI Sourcebook 16.3
... WITH AWARENESS
by Darshana Ellis
I've played with dancing and teaching
C.I. for about 8 years and something
hits me continually-if your own
awareness isn't with your self before
you touch someone else, it won't feel
half as nice and will appear "not to
work." We all know the contrast of a
dance that culminates in luxurious bliss
versus one whose consequence is bruis-
ed body or soul.
So now, how?: Bringing conscious
presence into anything creates medita-
tion (and some very nice dance
material).
Starting with the rudimentary (thin
air), some breathing techniques that
help remind us what we're doing:
1) On your own, lying on the back (or
front), using the floor for resistant
reference, explore the breath's travel
into the lower belly, throughout the
ribcage and high into the entire lung,
chest cavity. This exercise is best ver-
bally guided: anatomical reminders
are great, e.g., open around the
clavicle, deep into the armpit,
ar.ound the sacrum, etc.)
2) In partners, one partner lying on
their back, other lies over them
perpendicularly, face down, belly to
belly. Partner on top may have to
take some weight on their elbows and
knees for comfort's sake, to begin.
Partner on bottom initiates a low,
deep, slow belly breath, partner
following. Try breathing in opposi-
tion, Jetting your partner push the
breath out of you then Jetting gravi-
ty create space in the belly for the in-
coming breath.
3) Coming back to the breath. During
any contact exercise or warm-up
(anytime) if the dancer(s) get side-
tracked, lost or sleepy-stop, to come
back home, breathe first.
4) Running, one of the best reasons in
the world to breathe. I will run 5
miles sometimes just to give my lungs
an excuse to breathe. Running
backward, downhill, relaxed and
breathing softly, is meditation. In-
doors, if your space allows, even
short runs with the breath slow, deep
and relaxed help the body become
softer and more quiet in motion. Try
sideward and backward relaxed runs,
fast and slow, alone and with
partners.
Like the ultimate in love brings you
back to yourself, the ultimate dance
form takes you home to your body,
heart and awareness. -tr
DARSHANA ELLIS is a dancing foal and
a teaching cantactar in San Diego, Calif
Karen Nelson and Alito Alessi

LIST OF EVERYTHING (PARTIAL)
When I mentioned the List
Issue to Susan Schell this morning,
she smiled sheepishly and reached
into her shirt pocket to show me her
list for the day. Lists are everywhere,
especially when you're looking: menus,
road signs, people at this breakfast
place, conversations overheard, things
to do, ways to begin.
Last fall, in Banyoles, Spain, for my
second workshop there, I sat on the
stony terrace thinking about what
material I'd present in the classes. The
notebook I'd brought was nearly empty
and I decided to make a list of all the
exercises, concepts, images, activities,
principles and structures I could think
of that I'd been working with during
the past 15 years.
I made the list over several sittings
and kept coming back to add more. It
was amusing to try to think of every-
thing. Some forms that I remembered
were still alive, others just a record of
where life once was. Many of the new-
er ones were still too unformed to be
co.ntained in a few words; I didn't know
them intimately enough to give them
names. In reading the list over now, I
can feel myself read dimension into
the words. It's like a class list: before
the class begins it's just a list of names,
afterwards it's a list of people.
So here it is, played from move to
mind to hand to mouth to word to
word to word, part (space permitting)
of the partial list of everything:
falling in the 6 directions
rolling up the body
joy kicks
jump start
slow dive to the center of the earth
round robin
domino falls
sloughing
surfing
safari roll
nose as a measure
3 minute class
spinal rock
the wringer
throws and catches
empty/full
high posts
low tables
rolling into the sunset
3 by Ss, 3 by 1 Os
5 by 5s
solo duet solo duet
dance while considering: your mother,
the ocean
oceanic state
time states-slow, med, fast, faster
pre-character states
pre-contact states
the elements-earth, air, fire, water, ether
shakedown
come as you are
alternating current
headstand, handstand
jog and fall

calling the end
here! here!
