"Dance with Different Needs" performance, Eugene, Oregon, March 1988.
A RESPONSE
from Susan Schell
I have recently become very ex-
cited by developments in Contact
between ablebodied and disabled
dancers. At A Cappella Motion
workshop this past summer there
was a v.ideo that Karen [Nelson] and
Alito [Alessi] brought from the
[Dance With Different Needs]
workshop that took place in Oregon
and then the report about it in the
last issue as well as the articles by
Bruce Curtis and Alan Ptashek.
This new growth in the form
seems to have the potential of shed-
ding light on some of the questions
that have surfaced through the years
about elitist tendencies in Contact. I
have never felt the limitations of the
form to be carried by any one per-
son in a dance; rather, it is determin-
ed by the willingness of those par-
ticipating to commit to the realities
of their exchange instead of what
they wished it would or would not
be. I think this kind of attending to
oneself and each other can be ex-
hausting or the assumption is that it
would be, so an easier duet with
''somebody who can really dance" is
often the option taken. As a result,
some of the most potentially
challenging and possibly growth pro-
ducing duets are aborted or avoided
altogether. Perhaps it has something
to do with our perception of what it
means to be challenged. Our skills
can be even more limiting than
another's lack of skill if we do not
find ways to make our experience
available to those we dance with.
Otherwise, all our much sought after
skills begin to isolate us and become
the sign posts of exclusion rather
than routes of access.
This kind of attentiveness to one
another and the dancing was
palpable for me in both the video
that I watched of the workshop and
in the writings of Bruce, Alan, Karen
and Alito in CQ. I will be very in-
terested to see/ experience the effects
of this work on all our dancing in
the future.
One parting question-what is a
"bad dancer?" u
SUSAN SCHELL has been teaching Con-
tact Improvisation and performing since
1977. She has worked in Authentic Move-
ment for the past six years and is current-
ly co-directing a 9 month intensive pro-
gram in the form.
166 CQ/CI Sm1rcebook
HIER- VISIBILITY
by Steve Paxton
Let me see. I gather egalitarianism
and hierarchy are opposed in the title
of Cynthia Novack's columns in the last
"Still Moving" section ["Egalitarianism
and Hierarchy in Contact Improvisa-
tion,'' CQ Vol. XIII no. 3]. I fear a trip
has been laid on us by some free-
associating dance critic who once
described CI as more democratic than
other dance forms. I sort of remember
that line getting picked up by subse-
quent reporters-freecwheeling, dance
for folk, etc., that kind of image aris-
ing uncensored, unexamined and
perhaps unverified. In fact, until
Novack questioned me recently, I don't
recall any of the many dance writers
asking about this point.
Egalitarianism resulted in democracy,
our 'form' of government. This was op-
posed to monarchy-Geo Washington
was offered the monarchy for the states
of America, I once learned. So that sort
of hierarchy was avoided by democratic
government. But a hierarchy, a tern~
porary one, is what we use to run the
country.
I believe I agree with Dumont's thesis.
Agreeing on values creates constraints
on individual directions. And humans
are a communicating animal-we get
jerked out of our path by far more sub-
tle events than agreeing on values.
Sometimes for good, sometimes for ill.
A pause, a glance will do it.
I have noticed that hierarchies are
noticed by those outside them. This sug-
gests that those who make them up are
to some degree ignorant of that fact, or
even that they are projected on a group,
or faction, or gathering, or team, or
committee by someone who perceives
the association as hier-. Or rather,
higher.
Volume 14 1989
Now no one in these stories is dead;
Zimmer, Elias, Ryan, Harwqod or
myself; it would be possible to ask some
direct questions of all of us. For in-
stance, who organized the situation
Zimmer attended? I'll bet Ryan had a
hand in it. Harwood and Ryan are both
movers and shakers in the Canadian
Contact Improv. scene and it seems to
me no Contact scene could exist without
deep bonds existing-I mean people
must enjoy dancing together in order to
have it survive for over a decade. Zim-
mer probably wasn't the only beginner
who wanted to whet their skills on those
two, and probably not the only one to
emotionally defer to their skills.
Elias says there was definitely a sense
of in-group and out-group at the Cur-
rent Exchange gathering [in Vancouver,
B.C., summer 1979]. I can vouch that
there was such a situation. The in-group
photos: Natanja den Boeh
consisted of any one who had a long
association with the idea of CI and had
toured and/or taught with the others.
The out-group were in such a group for
the first time. They were a bit at sea.
They didn't know how to act and since
there are no rules as such, no one told
them. The nice things about rules,
schedules, etc. is that they limit the
number of choices one has to make. If
one may act any old way, a bewilder-
ing situation may exist. Those who
weren't bewildered were in. Those who
were bewildered saw themselves as out.
If Current Exchange was elitist, it was
Volume 14 1989
only in the sense that the entire U.S. and
Canada was not invited. In fact, living
conditions restricted the size. Word was
quietly put out to a few people who told
a few other people. A letter of intent
from participants was required to insure
they had their own reason for using the
facility (Western Front
Lodge/ gallery I studio/ video) and that
their reason resonated with the facility
and harmonized in practical ways with
the rest. Every one of us paid for the
rent of Western Front and pitched in for
food, cooking and cleaning up.
I fear that the article, in using these
two examples, will give elitism a bad
name. Surely just being a small group
or being buddies is not cause to bring
out such a big gun. To be elite is to hold
some plum and restrict its dispersal. To
use this word to criticize those who go
to the trouble of organizing classes or
who attempt to specialize and concen-
trate is to disparage a kind of grass roots
community work.
Zimmer and Elias will be amused to
learn that they have been placed in an
elite, at the top of a hierarchy, recent-
ly. In Berlin at the 4th annual Contact
Conference (they decided to drop the
word 'improvisation', to my despair).
A European delegate asked if all Euro-
peans could do with Contact was repeat
what the first generation had done. So
it goes. Zimmer and Elias are, to
Europe, first generation contactors.
And this is an example of a hierarchy
being perceived from without.
Truth be told, CI is a dance form. It
does have and, as far as I am concern-
ed, teach certain values; mostly to do
with physical safety.
CI has always abounded in logical
contradictions, foremost to do with im-
provisation. If improvisation springs
out of each moment in each individual,
then how to get people to improvise-
to discover their own initiative? And I
point this out in classes-this cannot be
taught, but it can be learned. I can help
with the physical part. I can provide
useful (to me, at least) images and
meditations. I can point out glaring er-
rors in partnering. I can't know how so-
meone feels or protect them from their
insecurity, fear, dislikes, associations,
boredom, or their elation, warmth,
sense of achievement, for that matter.
Contact Improvisation deals with
gravity, momentum, human bodies, etc.
Humans form or perceive hierarchies.
It is egalitarian. The folk who aren't in
Contact Improvisation, either don't
know about it or choose not to do it.
To pick at words just a bit more, Con-
tact Improvisation could not have "em-
braced values of individualism, equali-
ty, and anti-hierarchical relationships,''
because CI cannot do anything. It is
something to be done.
And in Dumont's remark some
strange loose ends, which perhaps
should be brought to the attention of the
U.S. government and of course, its
boss, the people: "If, in effect, the
whole of humanity is deemed present in
each man, then each man should be free
and all men are equal. By contrast, as
soon as a collective end is adopted by
several men, their liberty is limited and
their equality is brought into question."
So apparently even a government
formed on values such as freedom and
equality will trample on freedom and
equality. How embarassing. Yet even
such an experiment in good intentions
is of some interest, compared to the in-
tentions of other governments, then and
now. If liberty and equality are chosen,
then at the moment of choice, they ex-
ist. What happens the next moment is
something for all of us, anthropologists
especially, to ponder. *
STEVE PAXTON is an inveterate Contac-
tor who is interested in improvisation.
CQ/CI Sourcebook 167
The European contact community
came to Berlin for its annual
teachers conference under the banner
"Issues for the Nineties"-but more
of that later.
The merry folk of Europe had come
together, oh joy! There was also in
attendance an extremely large con-
tingent of American gentlefolk, "our
cup floweth over" was the thought
on everyone's mind. Scurrilous ac-
cusations that certain visitors were
treating the event as a trade fair
were roundly dealt with-thank
goodness!
At the first gathering the eager and
the faithful were presented with a
handy information pack and a con-
dom. The completely untrue rumour
that this was in response to previous
years' discussions was treated with
disdain. Instead, the conference
grasped its prophylactics and awaited
"Deepening the Form."
Days and days of dance and inter-
course, the cut and thrust of a
modern art movement, here, in the
very crucible of 20th Century
politics-Berlin. Rich, poor, east,
west, Berlin-the divided city. This is
extremely handy as any fellow tourist
will tell you! What would be an in-
surmountable tourist problem, is
divided into easily touristable
sections.
The East, ever mindful of its respon-
sibilities has erected a handy wall
and effiCient turnstiles, very similar
to the Disneyland model. One always
knows where one is. Everything ap-
pears to be produced by the same
company and everything is so ex-
tremely cheap. So cheap in fact one
has extreme difficulty cashing in all
those cute little chips that allow you
to go on any of the rides.
Sadly however, the West does not
show the same sort of responsibility.
The American, British and French
sectors are almost indistinguishable.
No walls to keep out the non-paying.
No efficient tour guides. I for in-
stance, was completely unable to
find the German sector. A sad fact
of the times, I mused.
''THEY CAME THEY SAW THEY DID
A LITTLE SHOPPING"
Berlin-A personal view by Kevin Finnan
I digress, there had been fears that
there might be a language problem,
but luckily everyone spoke English
and anyone who did not, did not
speak. This compromise was working
wonderfully until a frustrated Ger-
man listener, unable to contain
himself, spoke out somewhat testily
at the first panel discussion. Luckily,
he did so in German. Thus all the
English speakers, completely unable
to understand him, could continue
on without noticing. "Vive la Dif-
ference," whatever that means!
By now the conference was in full
swing. The spartan life of eating,
dancing, eating, dancing, eating talk-
ing, eating, eating was taking its toll.
In the case of yours truly the old
adage "free food makes Jack a fat
boy" was not heeded-alas! Thus
several unsuspecting partners were
crushed to a fraction of their normal
volume when dancing with moi, and
several sackloads of health food.
Allegations of a covert operation by
German dance to elevate its Euro-
pean status by reducing the rest of
us to bulging bean bags were hotly
denied.
At the second panel discussion,
which was on the topic of perfor-
mance, a furor broke out. Members
of the panel were apparently attemp-
ting to perform during the discus-
sion. Order was only restored when
the panel was persuaded to return to
the sedentary you-listen-we-talk for-
mat. It had taken a courageous lone
voice to bring back sanity to the pro-
ceedings. It has long been known
that performance is not a viable
method of communication after all!
I, mercifully, was spared the whole
indignity as I had a pressing engage-
ment with a dinner table in another
part of town.
A quote that rang clearly through
my mind came on the last day. So-
meone, who shall remain nameless,
(after all who am I to point the
finger) but who definitely was not
me, observed dryly that "the con-
ference had been entitled 'Issues for
the Nineties', but as far as anyone
could see it was "Whatever happen-
ed to the Sixties."
168 CQ/CI Sourcebook
I awoke with a start! Which surpris-
ed me as I had been so pleasantly
lulled as to not realise I was un-
conscious. Someone had mentioned
the future-too bad, we were all go-
ing home. Oh well, next year
perhaps?
What had been the questions that
had concerned us? Well, as far as
yours tTuly could recall, they had
been-What is contact? What is
teaching? What is performance?
How can the contact community deal
with itself? Well, pearls indeed! Fun-
damental, I know, but this type of
examination tends to give the same
satisfaction as tap dancing in quick-
sand. For every apparent resolution
there are a dozen new questions.
This is required listening for insom-
niacs but tres yawn for the razor wit-
ted artist of the '80s such as your
good self dear reader. We all agree
"What is contact?" is a nugget, but
so is "What is God?" (There also
seems to be a curious correlation bet-
ween the answers by the way, could
it be ... ) No I digress, still, pressing
on to the next paragraph ...
Attending these conferences is rather
like being engaged in conversation
with someone who is permanently in
the state of just waking up-Who
am I? Where am I? What am I?
repeated endlessly. In the end, driven
by frustration one is moved to say
"I don't know, why don't you tell
me?" or (in the case of those less
charitable than my saintly self)
"Who cares?"
In order to break free from the iner-
tia brought about by these questions,
it is necessary to make the assertion
"I am here and this is where I am
going." This is obvious enough to
those who made it from the first
bleary eye open to boiled egg this
morning, but the conference, I am
afraid to say, still had its head firm-
ly in the pillow!
It seems that the world is such a nas-
ty place these days that anyone who
continues sleeping will get their py-
jamas stolen and wake up with their
bottom showing in public. Questions
such as "What is contact? etc." will
never have completely definitive
answers. If they do I believe the
form will be dead. Therefore it is
necessary for everyone to have their
own "working" answers which they
modify as they proceed along. These
are constantly changing in response
to our own development and the
work we meet.
Volume 14 1989
At the conference I sensed a fear
that people felt their answers might
not be the "right" answers and thus
they needed to compare and discuss
these questions to reconcile the
doubt in their minds.
Firstly, I feel that there are no
"right" answers. Secondly there is
no one to confirm or deny the
"rightness" of your answer. The
thought that someone can do this is
what creates a hierarchy. Finally it is
this sense of doubt which really fires
the work and forces the challenge.
The sense of doubt is to be en-
couraged not feared.
During the conference, many people
stated, almost incredulously, that the
most rewarding part of the con-
ference for them had been the infor-
mal discussions they had been able
to have over lunch etc. They be-
moaned the fact that the organisa-
tion had not offered more facility
within its structure for this to hap-
pen. I feel that this is wanting the
"conference" to do it for you. The
informal discussions are so fruitful
because you yourself have initiated
them. This is to be encouraged.
Looking for ways in which the struc-
ture can make these discussions hap-
pen may be beneficial but I feel
there is a danger in so doing of
returning the responsibility of mak-
ing them happen to the structure,
thus relieving yourself of the burden.
The conference as a whole is to
discussion what oil tankers are to
slalom sailing. "Unwieldy" is me at
my most charitable.
Still, a jolly good time was had by
all and heartfelt thanks must be ex-
tended to Tanzfabrik for the sterling
work done in hosting the event. Well
done everybody!
And so with hearts uplifted and in
the spirit of Perestroika we look for-
ward to 1989 in Finland where, I
suspect, the sheer effort of getting
there will motivate the participants
to get what they want from the con-
ference and perhaps raise our eyes to
the future.
Toodle Pip. u
KEVIN FINNAN is a performer and
teacher living and working in the
Midlands region of England in collabora-
tion with performer/teacher Louise
Richards.

BASIC CONTACT LIST--------------
A random list of elements-concepts, exercises, skills and attitudes we thought should be included
in a Basic Contact course. In no particular order, these include:
Alignment
Body-work, massage
The Stand
The spiritual or inner sense of the work
Compassion
Being rather than Doing
Falling
Play, playfulness
Spatial Orientation
Warm-down
Neutral Condition
Entering & leaving the Dance
History of Contact
Breathing
Safety
The Small Dance
Back Stretch
Counterbalances
How to listen to the Body
Ease of movement
Positive feedback modes
Uses of Imagery
Contact Jargon
Kinesiology, Anatomy
Relaxation
Following the Contact Point
Connection
Trust
Posting
Rolling
Balance
Stuffing
Warm-up
Arriving
Ambiguous instructions *

HARBIN HOT SPRINGS NOTES-----
California Contact Jmprov. Jam
Harbin Hot Springs, Nov. 88
Home, I sit at the breakfast
table, overwhelmed with grief. Leav-
ing Harbin and the incredible sense
of community that we gradually built
was very sad for me. I didn't
acknowledge it until Monday morn-
ing. I was listening to Whales Alive
and the singing back and forth of
the whales made me intensely aware
that our gathering was over and the
migration back to the working
grounds, like whale feeding grounds,
was a very lonely process. Fortunate-
ly, we have a contact community in
San Diego now. Three of us went to
Harbin: Darshana Ellis, Bruce Gard-
ner and myself. So we have each
other as a support group. Two
others are regulars in our communi-
ty: Annete Frei and Laurelia Barton.
We will be processing the learning of
this Harbin for quite awhile. Some
ideas that come up follow.
Mixer: The round robin is ineffi-
cient for giving everyone a chance to
dance. It is common for many of us
to have an inner dialogue that goes
like this: "They look so good," "I
don't want to interrupt," "I'm not
good enough," "I'm tired,
cold ... , " "Neither of them like
me," and "They haven't had a long
enough turn." This kind of taped
message goes through my mind over
and over again. I have to contradict
my mind to get into a round robin.
This becomes less of an issue when I
begin to know I'm accepted. Then
again sometimes the inner messages
are confirmed.
In a mixer format, everyone is en-
couraged to dance with someone
they have never danced with before
for a fixed time interval, then
everyone switches to another un-
familiar dancer. This happens in
"class" settings regularly but I think
it should be used to introduce an
evening of dance. There are no "I'm
interrupting" or "long enough turn"
messages to deal with. And this pro-
cess helps to overcome the "I'm not
good enough" and "I'm cold and
tired" excuses. A set time interval is
very important so that everyone
switches at the same time. Perhaps
the use of a bell or music to cue
people to the switch process is
appropriate. 0
172 CQICI Saurcebook
Volume 14 1989
(cont'd)
There are those who rigidly oppose
structure at all costs. I agree that im-
provisation in its purest form should
dominate. But I have strong feelings
of compassion for the newcomers to
contact who are overwhelmed by the
beauty of the more experienced
dancer and the close bonding and
subgrouping that is common in con-
tact communities.
Nude Dancing: For the first time
at Harbin, we had a nude contact
session that occurred spontaneously
on Friday night. I was overwhelmed
by inner dialogue, "How will my
body react to even greater sensuality
than the already incredibly sensuous
contact dance form?" The beauty of
this session finally overcame my in-
hibitions and I truly enjoyed the
freedom. It was like the first skinny
dipping at the beach. Although I'm
thankful for this experience, I have
mixed feelings: (1) what will out-
siders think, (2) will the wrong kind
of people be attracted to our com-
munity if nude dancing becomes
commonplace, (3) many community
members stopped dancing when the
nude dancing began, (4) many people
were subtly criticized for objecting.
Some thoughts for future occur-
rences: (1) I will not encourage nude
dancing at Harbin with any regulari-
ty, (2) if the community agrees to a
nude dance, I will strongly encourage
a non-prime time, contained setting
that will not disrupt the community
function, (3) this should never be
advertised in any way because of the
potential disruption from outsiders.
Notes on my nude dance ex-
perience: It was like velcro and silk.
Initially I felt glued to my partner
like velcro. This permits some
unusual perches and unexpected
locks. Then the perspiration set in
and I slide around like sliding the
length of the high school shower on
my butt. It wasn't that bad but I
never knew when I would stick or
slip. I perspire heavily so I became
concerned over others slipping on my
slicks so I stopped, but not before
rocking back on my back for a long
slide across the floor. My dances
with men were shorter than usual
and I had some inner reservations
like those I went through when I
first started dancing with my com-
munity brothers long ago.
Renewal (Welcoming) Ceremony:
Initially getting together for the first
community meeting needs more
grounding spiritually. We seem to
get too cerebral with names. I would
appreciate repeating some of the
closing rituals from the last Harbin
so that we can honor the continuity
of the community and show
newcomers some of the results of the
last community gathering. When the
Native Americans joined for the Sun
Dance, they began with ceremonies
that evolved in the course of cen-
turies to honor the community and
to ritually bond each tribe member. I
don't think this needs to be anything
elaborate but something like a line

CONT ACT CLASS TITLES AND
DESCRIPTIONS from flyers I picked up
outside the Saturday Berkeley Jam on
April 15, 1989. [N.S.S.]
THE WINNING CIRCLE (Martin
Keogh).
For those with contact experience. We
will start with the fundamentals of Con-
tact, encouraging poise at the center of
it all. The second month, we will surprise
ourselves in flight and extended follow-
through. The third month will be in the
worlds of paradox and talks with the in-
ner dictator who always demands
resolution.
THE FL YING WORKSHOP (Martin
Keogh).
For those with well developed landing
gear. This workshop is the weekend
following the Bay Area Contact Festival.
During this day-long intensive we will
work on finding rhe openings for aerial
play. We willforns on what the dance of-
fers us rather rhan "tricks." We will work
on support, lightness, S(~fiening in the air
and relinquishing extra psychic weight.
CONTACT IMPROVISATION/The In-
stinctual Dance (Carol Swann).
The primary de1·elopmental patterns of
rolling, crawling, falling, walking and
, running form the basis of a movement
vocabulary. Through a detailed and in-
depth forns on these patterns we will
develop an understanding of the body's
structure and the movement offorces act-
ing upon this structure. These principles
will help us to gradually include explora-
tions into the mm·ement form of Contact
Improvisation which is primarily a duet
form. Dancers, sustaining a physical rela-
tionship, move in and out of contact,
uniting their masses to create a mutually
supportive and dynamic interplay of
weight. IYe do not strfre to achieve results
but rather to participate openly in a con-
stantly changing physical reality. Open to
all with or without mm•ement experience!
I look forward to hm·ing a fully physical
and exploratfre time with you!
174 CQKJ Sourcebook
where each community member
touches every other community
member is appropriate. (See the
Closing Ritual.)
Closing Ritual: We closed this
Harbin Jam with a Sufi chant, the
Thunder dance, and a line hug. The
Sufi chant was:
I open my eyes to you,
I open my heart to you,
Together we raise our arms
to the sun.
And together we are opening
our loving hearts as one.
The Thunder dance begins with one
person rubbing their hands together.
Then the hand rubbing progresses
around the circle until it reaches the
first hand rubber again, then hand
clapping begins and progresses
around the circle, then knee slapping
and foot stomping. Then the steps
are reversed: knee slapping, clapping,
rubbing, then silence.
Lastly we passed each community
member through a double line,
showering them with hugs, kisses,
rubs, lifts. As wonderful as this was,
it took too long with a fifty person
tribe. At Naropa Institute last sum-
mer I solved the problem in my own
way by writing a personal message to
each one of our 35 member class and
a poem for the three teachers; this
took me 3 hours but that was a
meager expression of the compassion
I felt for our Naropa Dance
Community.
With the help of Bruce Gardner, a
San Diego contacter, on the plane
back to San Diego from Harbin, we
came up with the collapsed circle
dance. It begins with a circle. Each
participant hugs the person to the
left with only their left arm, leaving
the right arm free. Then collapse the
circle inward so that it is more like a
long hollow worm. As the collapsed
circle cycles around, each tribal
member greets every other member
by touching them with their free
hand. It ends up being a community
contact dance where the physical and
spiritual integrity of the community
takes on an actual form as opposed
to an amorphous soup.

WII,DNESS AND GRACE/Creative
Movement and Contact Improvisation
(Kirk Andrews).
Each Class begins with a welcoming ritual
and warm-up to unify and ground our
energies. We start with "traditional" solo
and duet skills and then expand into
"non-traditional" creative expression,
using the emotional tone and potential of
the group to guide the movement process.
This may lead us imo emotional qualities
ranging from active and joyous all !he
way to depressed and passive. We will use
mulliple channels of sensory perception
10 fill 0111 and enrich the dancing. Our
goal is to dance with the Gods and God-
desses, and maybe even a litrle bir with
the devil. This is more than jusl a dance
class. Bring your dreams and your
judgments, your wildness and your car-
ing, your sensi!ivity and your impulses.
Movement experience and 1111 ounce of
courage required.
A PEER COUNSELING CLASS for the
CONT ACT COMMUNITY (Tim Hart-
nett and Karen Gee).
This will be a special class offered to the
Contact Improvisation Dance community
(dancers and friends of dancers). We will
be teaching the theory and techniques we
have learned from our study and practice
of Co-counseling. In addition, we will in-
tegrate body-oriented therapeutic work
drawn from other sources. Our purpose
is to help you become skilled at
facilitating yourself and others through
the healing of emotional pain. The
material covered in this class can be use'1
for your personal benefit or integrated
into your own work as a healing artist.
Each meeting there will be discussion of
counseling theory, demonstration of
technique, and time to give and receive
attention with each other. Class members
will meet in pairs to practice the techniqes
outside of class once per week (we will
help you arrange this). We hope to draw
on what Contact Improvisation has to
teach about trust and support, add aver-
bal component, and create an environ-
ment for profound emotional release.
You are welcome to join us.
Volume 14 1989
(cont'd)
After considering the complexity of a
tribal connection, I realized why the
number of people we dance with·
seems so limited. To dance for 5
minutes with everyone in a 50
member tribe would take an organiz-
ed 4 hours. I think we can touch
every member of our tribe but the
dance time is inherently limited.
Although I have appreciation for the
difficulty, that will not stop me from
enjoying the challenge.
Track-to-Closure Witnesses: Each
community gathering needs someone
to sense the closure of a process. On
our nude dancing discussion and
subsequent dancing, we did not have
closure. At our final meeting, the
tribal memory led us gently back to
that sore spot and we brought it
closer to completion. Our witnesses
helped us all; we can all use our in-
ner witness to the benefit of the tribe
as well as ourselves.
Co-Counselling: This is relatively
new to me but I sense great value
for some of us in this process. I saw
groups get together to co-counsel;
defined for me as active listening and
closure assistance. All members
would listen to one member for a
focused time interval until that
member reached closure on their
process. Another part of this is
assisting with continuity or tracking;
the speaker being gently counselled
to stay on track.
Kinesthetic/Touching: Another
need I see in the community is for
safe touching. Many, many
subgroupings form at Harbin for
stroking and petting and purring.
This could use some organized en-
couragement for newcomers.
Oldtimers are like old cats rubbing
against each other's legs, backs,
heads, etc. Like co-counseling, touch
group networking for newcomers
could be helpful.
Masks: I realized at this Harbin
Jam that I wear a mask that
unknown to me may put people off.
I work in a factory where there are
plenty of robots. And I think I may
be reflecting the mindless gaze that I
frequently get at work. I will be
playing with my personal masks and
artificial masks so that my face
becomes more open and joyous. I
saw this in myself after seeing it in
some Harbin folk. I will be looking
as carefully in the mirror as I look
at some of my friends. *
BARRY HYNUM is a contactor from San
Diego, California.

CONTACT: PRACTICE and PRO-
CESS/ An Advanced Training Group
(Alan Ptashek).
Contact Improvisation engages our fullest
attention to our bodies, minds and emo-
tions. Time, discipline, humour and trust
in ourselves and one another is the pro-
cess and the practice that strengthens our
dance. Contact Improvisation has been
an integral part of my personal and pro-
fessional work for fifteen years. As
teacher and group facilitator, I am offer-
ing this advanced training group for com-
mitted practitioners of Contact Im-
provisation who are interested in a group
process structure where we can deepen
our practice in ways that are specific and
meaningful to how we are moving, think-
ing and feeling.
We will strengthen our physical and in-
teractive skills, finding greater confidence
and risk-taking abilities in our dancing.
We will look at and challenge our belief
systems about Contact Improvisation and
ourselves, as well as how we develop and
perpetuate those beliefs. Other areas that
we will explore include: control and loss
of control, intimacy and distance,
vulnerability and power, gender issues,
sexuality and sensuality, conflict and
resolution. This is an opportunity to prac-
tice Contact Improvisation in an in-depth
and contained environment where extend-
ed dancing, personal feedback and discus-
sion will form a rich meeting place for
men and w·omen.
My intention is to create an on-going
group training with a committed core of
participants. A minimum 2 month com-
mittment is required.
CONT A CT IM PRO VISA TION /The In-
stinctual Dance (Sue Stuarl).
Through rolling, crawling, walking, run-
ning and jumping we will explore the
physics of body weight, gravity, and
momentum. Reconnecting to these early
infant movement patterns can help wake
up animal instincts lost or forgotten. Sen-
sitising to internal movement will
establish a grounding from which we
move into contact with one another. We
will make the initial connection slowly
through partner warm-ups such as shared
stretching and massage, opening our
senses more deeply to both our own and
our partners movement and rhythms. We
will practise ways to lift, fall, jump, and
pick each other up, taking the dance in-
to the air. In this enlivened state, dancers
play with moving in and out of contact
exploring ways of supporting each other.
Some of the classes will be taught by
visiting contact improv. teachers from the
Bay Area.

THE BERLIN CONTACT CONFERENCE 1988
a report by Juliet Shelley (excerpted)
About eighty five people came from
Europe and the U.S. to take part in the
Fourth European Contact Teachers Con-
ference in Berlin, W. Germany from June
8-13, 1988.
About a year ago, the Tanzfabrik (literal-
ly, "Dance Factory") was asked to par-
ticipate in "Werkstatt Tanz: Issues for the
Nineties" as Berlin is the European City of
Culture for 1988. DIETER HEITKAMP,
BRUNO STEFANONI and BOB REASE col-
lectively organized this conference with a
theme of 'New Influences and Crossovers'.
"Werkstatt Tanz: Issues for the Nineties"
was organized around a theme of artistic ex-
change and some of the events of the con-
ference were open to the public.
June 8th and 9th were days for registration
and open dancing as well as reunions with
friends, and acquainting oneself with parts
of Berlin. Most people had arrived by the
evening of Thursday 9th for an official
welcoming and introductions at the new
studio at Tanzfabrik. One studio had been
put aside as a 'Video Cafe' and quiet room
where, throughout the conference, videos
of contact-related work were being shown
informally.
Friday 10th June began with OPEN JAMS
in three studios at the Tanzfabrik. On Friday
afternoon, NANCY STARK SMITH and
ROBIN FELD led two separate
WORKSHOPS-"Developing the Form"
and "Contact and Martial Dance."
On Saturday afternoon, ISHMAEL
HOUSTON-JONES led a workshop on the
Politics of dancing, while PAULINE DE
GROOT led one on improvised dance and
music.
On Friday and Saturday evening after din-
ner, there were two PANEL DISCUSSIONS.
The topicfor Friday was 'Teaching' and Nan-
cy Stark Smith moderated, assisted by Steve
Paxton [see excerpts from this discussion in
this Newsletter]. People on the panel includ-
ed ROBIN FELD, BOB REASE, LOUISE
RICHARDS, BRUNO STEFANONI, BERND
KAMMERER, and GILA ROGERS. The
discussion looked at questions in teaching
such as the definition of a teacher, language
and Contact, sexuality and aggression within
sessions, and Contact in Therapy. Bernd
spoke of his difficulties in the translation from
English into German, of the concepts and
practice of Contact. He had learnt Contact
in English and was teaching it in German.
Bernd was voicing an experience of teaching
which must be common to all those teachers
176 CQ,K'I Sourcebook
of Contact for whom English is not their first
spoken language. It was added that it helps
to use language which is part of the lives of
the people being taught, to teach concepts
of Contact in ways which will make it ac-
cessible for them.
Steve Paxton said that his first concern
when teaching Contact was how to teach it
without people hurting themselves. Robin
Feld described how she uses the breath to
encourage the release· and flow of weight in
her teaching. Using imagery such as melting,
softening and folding at the joints all helps
to encourage a safe physical environment for
doing Contact. Louise, who teaches Contact
with Kevin Finnan in high security prisons,
said that changing from a dispersed to a
focussed activity can also prevent injury in
a session.
The question "what are you teaching
when you are teaching Contact?" was link-
ed with finding a definition of a teacher.
STEVE BATTS spoke of sharing an investiga-
tion with his students. He directs but may
not know the outcome of the activity. He
brought up the element of improvising in
teaching, so that possibilities may be ex-
plored by teacher and learner together.
"What about feeling and emotion in Con-
tact?" This brought up some important
issues. Steve Paxton talked about how emo-
tion may or may not be acknowledged in
dance. He spoke of how Contact arose in
a dance context, within which people such
as Martha Graham and Jose Limon were
dealing with emotion, and making it into
form. Paxton was drawn to Merce Cunn-
ingham, because Cunningham's dancing
ethic was more abstract and he dealt with
shape, form, space and time-the physics of
dancing. This was more intellectual and
there was less drama, less expression of emo-
tions. In physics there is gravity, momentum,
shape, angles and as physics is present in
dancing, so are the emotions, "but we don't
know how to deal with it, teach it, make it
into a form." This was difficult for Kevin Fin-
nan who explained that he taught Contact
to criminals who have killed children and
adults, including their relations, and these
'students' learn Contact and talk about it
afterwards. I think there was some confusion
here between finding forms for emotional ex-
pression in daily life, and finding emotional
forms for dance, although the distinction can
sometimes be difficult. Contact in therapy
was an issue which was also discussed, and
Gila Rogers, a dancer who is also a
psychotherapist, explained how she combin-
ed Developmental work with Contact in her
treatment of emotionally disturbed people
and how effective Contact can be in her
work generally.
On the following evening, the topic for the
panel discussion was 'Performing
Contact-Working with Personal Material'.
Steve Paxton moderated, assisted by Nan-
cy Stark Smith. The discussion began with
an infqrmal jam for the panel who were
PAULINE DE GROOT, LAURIE BOOTH,
BOB REASE, DIETER HEITKAMP,
ISHMAEL HOUSTON-JONES, MARK TOM-
PKINS and JAAP KLEVERING. When the
dancing had subsided, some people found
seats to sit on, while others continued im-
provising while the discussion took place.
After about an hour, there was a break and
the panel rearranged their chairs closer to
the audience and remained seated. Steve
began by asking each person on the panel
to say a little about themselves, and their
work. He also asked them whether they had
danced in a Broadway show or musical,
which had run for more than three nights,
or if they had danced on a boat, and whether
they performed set material or improvised
material. I was occupied with taping the
discussion and taking photos so my notes are
scarce. However, I have down that Laurie
Booth was interested in aberrant behaviour
as movement in performance, describing
aberrant as marginalized or unusual activi-
ty. He recounted an incident which he was
witness to at the zoo that morning, where
a monkey had made history by imitating a
film cameraman to the point where the
monkey was making a film of everybody
looking at him inside his cage. (I think this
is what he meant, although the cameraman
may have given the monkey the equipment
to make a film). Ishmael had never danced
any set material, and Steve was alone in dan-
c~ng in a sh~w which repeated itself for many
nights running.
JORGEN TELLER asked whether im-
provisation can become a collection of anec-
dotes from learned forms. Steve replied, "Be
careful of what you're taught, every step
you've ever learned is stored in the body,
and pieces will emerge when you're least exJ
pecting it." There was a lot of discussion
around defining the form of Contact Im-
provisation. A definitive statement of Steve's:
"Forms exist to follow, but you're less than
human if you don't try to break them." This
panel discussion began at 8pm and was go-
ing strong at 11 :30pm when I left. There
were tapes made of it which would hopefully
be obtainable at the Tanzfabrik.
The NETWORKING COMMUNITY
MEETING began at lOam on Sunday mor-
ning, with sunshine streaming through the
window of the studio. We decided that the
next European Contact Teacher's Con-
ference would take place from the 10th-17th
August 1989 in Finland, and Jaap Klevering
and Jaana Turunen would be organizing it.
Volume 14 1989
in Cyprus and Walter Weiler offered to
organize one for Zurich, Switzerland.
Dieter Heitkamp was interested in com-
piling a kind of journal to document
responses to and feelings about the con-
ference in Berlin. In the last part of the
meeting we all shared something of this with
the rest of the group. Here are some of the
things that were said:
How to begin dancing with strangers.
Was this a conference or a symposium.
The founders of Contact dance don't know
the definition of it.
The conference was part of Issues for the
Nineties and we spent most of the time
talking about the '60s and '70s.
At no time did anyone say OK that's what
Contact is.
To make a group at this conference to talk
about personal, social and political issues
surrounding Contact dancing.
That a large group of people should learn
how to listen to each other.
It is a political act to go and see how the
Berliners live instead of attending the
conference-this is as important as be-
ing 'in the stars'.
There is no personal communication after
such intimacy.
To create more opportunities for personal
work to develop.
The psychology of the outsider-a
celebration.
To sustain a network between conferences.
Wanting to have nothing to do with the 'hip-
py, pseudo cult' which Contact could
become and to keep Contact available
for all people.
The "BERLIN JAM" on Sunday afternoon
was an opportunity for open dancing among
people outside of the conference and those
in it, and new energy and faces meant reviv-
ed dancing, for me anyway. It was a
welcome change from the highly structured
events of the conference, and reminded me
of the two days open jamming before the
conference began. I enjoyed the Sunday
afternoon novelty of that jam and was glad
of the opportunity to dance with new
people.
That night was the party, and Dieter
transformed the biggest studio with some
spotlights and a sound system into a disco.
On Monday morning a group of us went
to a cafe together. At the Contact Network-
ing meeting Mark Tompkins suggested that
people get together and talk about the per-
sonal, social and political issues that they ex-
perienced in dance and particularly Contact
dance. It was a small group and there was
the chance to talk quite personally which
was a relief. The group was small enough for
some close personal communication to take
place and I feel that this is what the con-
ference, by virtue of its size, failed to pro-
vide for, except in this group and outside of
the structured ti me.
The PERFORMANCE MARATHON was
from 1 pm to 6pm that afternoon. This event
was open to the public and everyone paid
15 marks toward it for the Berliner Aids Hilfe.
In the first half there were solos, duets and
one or two group pieces. This half was quite
organized, in that there was a predetermin-
ed order to the pieces. There were im-
provisations and set pieces in this part of the
marathon, including new collaborations.
After the break, the space was basically open
except there was a structure which Pauline
Volume 14 1989
ignored or forgotten as time w;nt by.
When one of the musicians left, sound
became the domain of the dancers who ac-
companied themselves from around the
space or within it, talking and singing, or us-
ing tapes or playing the percussion in-
struments that were available. The space was
nearly always pretty well full of activity and
there were one or two solos and at one point
there were about twenty people in the
space.
The marathon was the last organized event
of the conference, although most people
would be attending the PERFORMANCES of
STEVE PAXTON and LISA NELSON, and
LAURIE BOOTH that evening in another
part of Berlin. But it was the end of the con-
ference at the Tanzfabrik and so those of us
who were there sat in a circle together for
two minutes of silence to acknowledge an
ending with Dieter, Bob and Bruno.
I would like to finish with some quotes by
KERIAC from an article called "Politics and
Contact" in an old issue of CQ:
. whv was this form named Contact Im-
provisation and not Steve Paxton Technique?
In the entire modem dance world, every new
approach except Contact is based on a cult
of personality: Graham technique, Cun-
ningham technique, Hawkins technique,
etc . .. to me, that name is revolutionary-it
reflects a whole new value system in
dance. . I understand that Paxton was a
teacher at Bennington at the time he began
exploring Contact, and that his co-workers
were mostly students. And yet, if two peo-
ple try to do Contact while maintaining the
typical student-teacher relationship, I think
those two wouldn't end up doing much Con-
tact at all. Both the name and the form itself
assume that Contact is a collective creation
that is bigger than individuals. There is a let-
ting go of ego, and a letting go of traditional
role expectations, that is unprecedented in
dance.
.. Contact began with the progressive, anti-
elitist values of its inventors. But third and
fourth generation contacters, who may have
little or no contact with the originators, now
outnumber the originators as teachers of the
form. It's quite possible to teach someone
'Contact' in the physical dimension only,
without any conscious reference to values,
or even in relation to values that conflict with
the form. To me, it's important for teachers
of Contact to cultivate in themselves and in
their students a heightened awareness of the
political and spiritual richness of this form of
dance.
.. I think we in the Contact community
have a unique opportunity to foster the
revolutionary model that was generated by
the Contact originators. We can validate the
activities that do not conform to the tradi-
tional expectations, because our own ex-
perience in Contact has given us intimations
of another type of 'good quality perfor-
mance' . .. we can perceive performers as real
people in real time, making choices that have
physical results neither good nor bad, rather
than applauding 'stars' whose image would
be ruined by a 'mistake'."
[from "Politics and Contact," interview with
Keriac by Mark Pritchard, San Francisco
1979, CQ Vol. Vil no. 7. Fall 7987.]
JULIET SHELLEY lives and works in Oxford,
England and is a member of Jointwork Dance
Group.

TEACHING CONTACT
IMPROVISATION
-An abstract of the panel-discussion with
Nancy Stark Smith, Robin Feld, Bernd Kam-
merer, Steve Paxton, Bob Rease, Louise
Richards, Gila Rogers and Bruno Stefanoni
(excerpts)
At the Contact Teachers Conference in
Bedin, June 1988, the first public evening
was a discussion about Teaching. I tried to
put the four hours of tape recorded discus-
sion into a "short form," which is quite a
subjective selection and not always in the
original turn, so I hope I didn't distort too
much of what people were saying.
With this abstract I want to say thank you
that I could join the conference and get a
feeling of what the community is. This was
a "big thing" for me with a lot of highlights,
fears and new contacts, because I didn't
have so much experience in Cl and dance
at all. It helped me a lot in writing my thesis
of Cl for my exams in physical education.
[Ulla Brinkmann]
SAFETY
STEVE: When I realized how many people
get hurt in their own kitchen, I thought Cl
is very safe. But it was that preoccupation
with safety that made me try to find some way
to make it teachable. Was there a wav in
which safety could be taught in what looks
like a very dangerous situation?
GILA: I feel I can't take the responsibility that
people don't hurt themselves. I only see what
kind of people do I have, how much trust do
they have in their own body. So, if I see that
they are scared with their body, I work a lot
with the floor and the surface of the body,
so that they start to develop the feeling that
they can trust their own body and that there
is always the floor. It doesn't matter, whether
it is a physical or an emotional situation, but
that every cell of the body starts to learn there
is support for me.
BOB: In terms of individual movement, it's
not ordinary in our daily life to use our hands
for support on the ground. It is a very rich
experience for people when they learn how
to break their fall with their hands. So there
are certain skills and safeties to learn at the
same time [as having] a really moving
experience.
ROBIN: For me, developing a released body
is to have a very responsive body, that
responds quickly to the different situations,
whether you go up or down. The floor is also
a big answer for me. I guess first going inside,
working with gravity and working with the
connection to sensation, the sensation of
weight, the sensation of releasing that weight
into the floor. Also, the bones seem to me
important, a kind of folding and unfolding
of the joints so that the weight can draw in-
to the center and then spill out of it.
BERND: You also have to develop a reaction
or a kind of awareness in speed.
BOB: There is also a commitment to merg-
ing with the partner. This is something that
I couldn't have learned alone. There is an
underlying trust, commitment to each other
as human beings. Even if you don't like
somebody you are dancing with, even if you
hated him on that night. you still have this
underlying commitment to get out oi that
stuff and commit yourself to the task at hand.
Volume 1 4 1989
EMOTIONS
NANCY: Let's go on to emotions in
teaching. I think there is an attitude about
teaching Cl and doing Contact, that there
is a kind of "mask of neutrality" that goes
with it. Steve, in the way you've worked
and taught, it seems that as emotions arise,
you don't get involved, you let it go, you
don't work with that. What would you say
about working with the emotional energy
that arises in dancing?
STEVE: The first problem was the context in
which Contact arose. It arose in a dance con-
text in the States in the modern dance styles.
Most of them were quite dramatically emo-
tional, or a romantic style . .. except for Cun-
ningham, really the most important abstract
style, and that was what I was drawn to. He
wasn't dealing with the emotions. He was
almost looking at a kind of physics of dance
and to get to that in a pure way, the way
couldn't be dramatized. He says that we are
human beings and we have emotions, ... but
we are not expressing and we're not
dramatizing it.
In Contact, the other stuff occurs. This
could be like and dislike, hate and love and
sexuality and all that. So there is a kind of foot-
note underneath all the physics. I don't know
how to teach it or make it into a form or
enclose it in the art, except working personal-
ly with the people. But the physical reality
is like a water for the fish. We all have it and
we most of the time ignore it. For me it is
always the time of looking at that and finding
more and more about it and how are things
connected. But so far physical reality seems
to be quite a rich entity in teaching.
ROBIN: For me it seems that people in their
dancing now recognize a moment, a feeling,
and it is almost like a sidetrip, like a play with
that moment and then going back into the
physical. It is just a perception I had in my
own dancing, it is maybe like a tender mo-
ment with someone, as an example. And that
you not only feel it, but you might exaggerate
it, the feeling, for the moment, like show it.
Let it come out.
STEVE: If you find yourself in Contact sud-
denly pushing and fighting as a joke, then
there is aggression in it. I had someone who
said, don't be aggressive, only be loving, but
I didn't want to do that. We couldn't find
that form unless we got rid of social
uneasiness. So what do I do instead of say-
ing don't be aggressive, I try to say "isn't
momentum interesting?"
GILA: One word which I use a lot is "emo-
tional body." It is not about emotions in the
dance, because the people I work with are
not dancers and they don't live with their
body and their emotions. So my approach to
Cl is to give them a laboratory where they
can make the experiences that I am at home
in my body and that I don't have only a
mechanical body. I don't need to evaluate
whether an emotion is good or bad, strong
or not strong enough. And I can give them
a lot of possibilities from Contact to ex-
perience it. Like in walking and feeling the
difference when somebody gives me support
just by putting their hand on my back and
then walking alone again finding my own
way. And in this very simple movement there
is so much experience to feel my emotional
body. When I own it, I'm responsible for it.
DANNY LEPKOFF: For me personally, the
best dances I had rode on not physics, they
rode on connections which had to do with
trust or feelings of aggression, connection or
love. This gave me the energy to dance. But
this was only in my mind . .. But I didn't feel
that this was taught at all and the whole ex-
perience for me was opening to come into
connection with people and share.
BERND: To use emotions for skill, for exam-
ple: this problem of closeness, this problem
of touching the sexual area. I use it sometimes
in a very funny way like "bump-dance," and
people giggle and they have fun and after-
wards they notice this thing (pelvis) moves
much more than they thought. For me, it is
a preparation for a lift to warm up the pelvis,
because before they don't have an idea how
to get them round, how to get them under
the pelvis of the partner. And this I need for
a pure physical dance.
PIERRE SERAPHIN: I'd like to speak about
an issue of technique. I think that at the same
time a teacher creates his technique, they
must create their "anti-technique." At the
same time a certain kind of knowledge is in-
troduced, the idea you have to give what you
cannot know. -tr

a bottle of wine, a cane, a cloth
by Martin Keogh
In the ancient fairy tales there is often a character walking in the woods where he meets a man (often named
Mercurius, one of the names of Hermes) who gives three gifts. These might include a bottle of wine that is never
empty, or a cane that will open any door, or a cloth that will heal any illness. A gift that is often given is the cloak
of invisibility. One of the images I often wear as I'm dancing is this cloak. I try not to interpret the image or figure
out how it applies to my dance, I simply wear it, and the dance changes.
In teaching Contact Improvisation, I have found images to be invaluable for getting at the soul of our dance.
It is possible to give physical corrections and teach techniques but I have found that it is the imagery that brings
down mountains and sets the oceans ablaze. The images are sometimes descriptive and sometimes prescriptive.
I will often flesh them out with a story or other images but they don't come with an explanation. Here is a list
of images, some that I have used in my own dancing and some that I have given in my classes:
- sleeping beauty after the kiss
- the sand in an hourglass
- sausages for bones
- an empty scale
- seeing through fish eyes
-Atlas after putting down the world
- think of yourself as a timeless piece of literature
-hunter after big game deep in the jungle and the lions
have just come into view
- bread rising and overflowing the bowl
- resplendent robes, jewels and crown of a king
- at the bottom of the exhale is when the lights go on
- pancake batter spreading as it's poured
- patina cracking and peeling off
- greenhouse for winter blossoms in the chest
- head and shoulders like a mop
-you have no forehead
- an old crone who has seen it all
- the reverence, standing in a grand cathedral with
sunlight streaming in
- flowing skirt, longgggg hair, dangling earrings, dozen
bracelets, emerald in bellybutton, tattoo of a rose on
the thigh
- filled with cooking garbanzo beans
- surprising thunder claps in the head
- set up house at the bottom of the exhale
- the view at the top of the inhale
- spaghetti as it's dropped into boiling water
-you have no arms or legs
- jellyfish in the joints
-the fall of the Roman Empire
- tumbleweed
- your spine is a pollywog (tadpole)
- your ribs are fingers
- pads of butter between each vertebra
- bird bones that are lighter than feathers
- a lover hand feeding you
- granite mountain with crags and ledges and trees
growing from rock
- a leaf in the wind
- mane of a lion whipping in the wind D

Volume 14 1989
25 Questions on
Improvisation
gathered from teachers and students
during the Summer Dance Course "On Improvisation,"
June 1985 at the Theaterschool Modern Dance Department,
Amsterdam, Netherlands
1. Is improvisation one thing or is it many different things?
2. Imagination and structure, where are we free?
3. How does imagination relate to form?
4. Is improvisation in dance only the lack of set material?
5. How can you use feedback (video, audio, discussion) in working on
improvisation?
6. Is improvisation a technique?
7. Do animals improvise?
8. How do you develop skill in improvisation?
9. How does improvisation apply to physical training? to body work? to
technique?
10. How does the activity of research relate to the performance situation or
can we continue the sensibility of research in performance?
11. What does limitation, structure and rule do to improvisation?
12. What do we want the audience to know about improvisation?
13. How do you relate to the audience while improvising?
14. Creative process and improvisation. The same thing?
15. What are the perceptual skills of improvisation?
16. What are criteria for evaluating improvisation?
17. Can the same standards be used to evaluate the success of an improvised
performance as are used to evaluate a performance of set movement?
18. Is improvisation a political choice?
19. Are there specific skills, either physical or creative, that facilitate the learn-
ing of improvisation?
20. What do you do as a teacher of improvisation, if your students understand
the assignment in a way it is not meant?
21. How can there be such a difference between the experience of doing an
improvisation and the seeing of an improvisation from the outside?
22. Is improvisation anarchism?
23. What is the relation between improvising and life?
24. Is a cathedral built over a period of 200 years of improvisation?
25. How do you know the ending of an improvisation? *

CQ/CI Sourcebook 183
A DEFINITION
The natural forces that govern the movements of the human body
on the earth are the same for all bodies. External forces-gravity,
momentum, friction, centrifugal, centripetal. Internal forces-
breath, bones, muscle condition, tension level, habit patterns.
Environmental conditions-floor, weather, size of space, time of
day, time allotment. The play of these forces together are THE
SCORE. Testing the limits and the range is the challenge. When
two bodies, moving with this score JOIN FORCES, the variations are
endless. The combinations test the flexibility, imagination, courage
and intelligence of the bodies involved in the play.
photo: Doug Winter

Some Considerations When
Structuring an Improvisation
(to be seen by an audience)
by Jaime Stover
'" What sort of structure do I want in terms of spatial rhythm?
" Do I want to create a mechanistic Newtonian framework, sitting over the
dance like a compressional structure, OR
... do I want a relative structure which reflects changes which have occur-
red as part of the unraveling of the dance in time and space?
" Is my intention in providing this structure: to give a formal framework which
allows the viewer to better see the action taking place, to set up a program
of interrelated parts and then observe the phenomenon which occurs, to
couch dramatic, virtuoso, or other purposely expressive elements (no mat-
ter what the aesthetic), or possibly to create a movement event with no par-
ticular purpose?
" Do I want visual markers that act as stabilizing markers which arrest the flow
of actions and space?
" Do I want my 'game rules' to be clearly seen, only indicated, or deeply
hidden?
" How much do I want to rely on the actual rules I set or on the formalistic
and otherwise significant sensibilities of the dancers?
" How does my dance fulfill or not fu Ifill existing contextual aesthetic expec-
tations and parameters for dance, for performance, for improvisation,
for ... art? *

those that are trained, id) suck-

fabulous ones, lg) stray dogs, (h)
those that are included in this
classification, (i) those that trem-
ble as if thev were mad, (j) in-
numerable ones, (k) those drawn
with a very fine camel's hair
brush, (/) others, (m) those that
have just broken a flower vase,
(n) those that resemble flies from
a distance.
-Jorge Luis Borges

186 CQICI Sourcebook
Final Notes on
Improvisation
by Nathalie Rousse
written for Improvisation en Danse, a course given by
Dena Davida at the University of Quebec. Translated
from the French by Dena.
(A conversation with myself.)
Do you like to improvise?
Sometimes yes and sometimes no.
Why not?
Because I'm afraid that my body lacks ideas.
Why yes?
Because when the improvisation is over I discover that I've
enjoyed it and learned something as well. Improvising for me
is like eyeglasses for someone who doesn't see clearly.
Why are you afraid that your body lacks ideas?
What I mean to say is that when the improv begins, I do have
ideas, but feel too timid or incapable of carrying them
through; and that is why now I try to move not from
preconceived ideas, but rather in function of the present mo-
ment and the things around me.
What things?
First of all, the space-also, the other movers and objects.
While improvising, what do you find most stimulating?
I am rarely stimulated from the inside (maybe later on).
Specific places motivate me enormously, certain pieces of
music, occasionally themes and images, and also other
people. Maybe it's because I'm new to improvisation that I
need outside stimulation. I sometimes feel insecure because
I'm concerned with the quality of the final product, and with
improvisation you never know. On the other hand, I've
discovered how process can be as interesting as the final
product and is not necessarily complex. I've always had the
impression that discovery comes only by digging long and
deeply but, for now, I need first to look for those things close
to the surface and near to me. *

Amie Dowling and Catherine Sands in rehearsal. Washington, D.C., 1987.
MAKING SAFE CONTACT
by Diane Elliot
I have a recurring dream that I
am teaching dance to a large group
of students who aren't interested and
keep leaving the room until only one
or two remain. In the dream, I feel
confident and strong about my
material but am struggling to com-
mand people's attention. Different
characters people this dream, both as
students and observers. Sometimes
my former dance teachers from New
York, Murray Louis or Phyllis
Lamhut, appear and criticize me.
One time my mother was one of the
two students who remained at the
end of the class. Sometimes I feel
bad about everyone having left,
sometimes relieved. The last time I
had the dream, I knew that the peo-
ple who stayed really wanted to be
there.
Recently I lived out this dream.
During the winter I taught a Sunday
morning series called "Making Con-
tact," open to new and experienced
contacters. In the class flyer I
described Contact Improvisation as a
movement form integrating
"elements of dance, play, martial
arts and developmental movement in
a concrete way that allows us to
celebrate moving and to explore both
our individuality and our in-
terdependence with other people and
our environment." During the past
two years I have incorporated into
my Contact classes my growing
understanding of developmental
movement patterns and the interrela-
tionship of body systems, cognitive
information and deeply felt
knowledge gained through my study
of Body-Mind Centering•m. With
each new level of discovery in my
Volume 15 1990
CQ/CI Sourcebook 187
own learning process, I amend my
class descriptions, wanting to attract
people whose personal discovery pro-
cess will mesh with and expand upon
my own.
Last winter's series began with
about 12 people, some with a fair
amount of Contact experience, some
trained dancers with minimal Con-
tact experience, and some without
any Contact background at all. The
first-timers were scared and said so
in our first check-in circle. They
weren't quite sure why they were
there, what would be asked of them.
The only man in the group, a novice
contacter, worried about being the
only man. Others wanted to be more
present in their bodies, but were
daunted by their sense of vulnerabili-
ty in the group or their fear of in-
timacy in working with a partner.
Some people could only sign up for
part of the series, a situation I un-
comfortably allowed because I felt it
was the only way to fill the class.
Nevertheless, the first class went
well. People candidly verbalized their
excitement as well as their discom-
fort. We worked deeply and gently
on cellular awareness, yielding into
the floor and through a partner to
the floor. We practiced thrusting in-
to the resistance provided by a part-
ner's body. By the end of the class,
people seemed satisfied and expand-
ed, more confident about their abili-
ty to take care of themselves and to
learn in this environment. I relaxed
about the class, feeling that the
group's energies would mix well and
that the more experienced would
both encourage and learn from the
beginners.
One woman never came back. On
the phone she told me that the class
was too scary for her. Over the
weeks more people came and went.
Never knowing exactly who or how
many would show up, I felt bullied
by the inconsistency. I wanted to be
open to the energy at hand, but was
subtly angry that I couldn't count on
people at the very least to be there. I
felt unmet, frustrated that I couldn't
generate momentum by building on
previously taught skills. It was like
teaching a perpetual first class.
By the last class of the series only
six people remained. And during that
class, even the few remaining people,
one by one, began to leave. Someone
slipped out of a group improv in
188 CQ;Cl Sourcebook
which people had the options to
move, watch or draw. When I went
to tell her good-by, she "playfully"
punched me in the stomach, saying
she had left a drawing for me. I
looked at the drawing and found it
full of anger about how I had led
that day's class.
I could hardly focus on the three
remaining women, still entwined in a
tender, playful dance. When we
finally- gathered in a circle, I shared
how difficult it had been for me to
remain present with the dancing
while others left the class. They said
they had been so engrossed in what
they were doing, that they had not
noticed the others leave. They
assured me that they had had a
wonderful time, told me how much
they had learned through the whole
series, and encouraged me to keep
developing the work. As they spoke,
my little-girl feelings of shame and
abandonment drained away. I realiz-
ed that I had been paralyzed by
"shoulds," voices in my head like
the voices of my former mentors in
the dream, criticizing my choices as a
teacher, telling me how I had failed
to deliver.
In the weeks that followed, people
who had disappeared from class and
some who had seemed ambivalent
called to tell me how important the
work had been to them and were
eager to sign up for the next series. I
could see that in allowing more parts
of myself to be present in class, I
had opened the door for more parts
of everybody to be there. However, I
didn't know how to fully support
this process which, while sometimes
allowing people to tap into nurturing
and delightful movement, at other
times brought them up against their
deepest patterns of discomfort.
I have always understood Contact
Improvisation as a healing activity.
Through my study of developmental
patterning, I now understand more
specifically that I was drawn to Con-
tact not only by love of movement,
but also by my vast unsatisfied need
for nurturing, non-sexual touch,
especially for compression, the kind
of pushing into the body that, as in-
fants, tells us where we are, gives us
our sense of self. Pushing against
resistance allows us to develop the
strength to reach beyond ourselves
into our environment. While modern
dance training helped me reclaim my
body as a source of learning and ex-
pression, Contact Improv satisfied
my need to connect with others
directly, through bodily sensation
and the physical laws of motion that
affect us all. Having the weight of
my adult body met, I rediscover
baby joy, trust and expansiveness.
Yet, offering ourselves these
pleasures may restimulate old hurts,
fears and unresolved conflicts that
live in our tissues, in the very pat-
terns of our movements. The people
coming to my classes these days are
not necessarily dancers or even
"movers." They are people on a
pathway of personal growth, in-
terested in expanding the range of
choices in their lives. Many have
mapped their emotional, spiritual or
energetic terrain, but still experience
their bodies as largely unexplored
territory. They want to understand
how inner movement translates into
movement in the world of form,
space, objects and relationships.
They may appreciate the importance
of hanging out with emotional or
spiritual discomfort in order to free
up old patterns, but may never have
experienced this process in their
bodies, challenged by gravity,
momentum, and other people's
physical presence.
In my classes I had created permis-
sion for people to move with their
bodies' desires. But at some deeper
level in myself, I was still taking
responsibility for the "success" or
"failure" of the class. Judging that
success (and myself) by how unified
the energy of the room felt, how ful-
ly people disclosed their experience,
how much they said they had learn-
ed, what the dancing looked like, I
was still not comfortable with each
person having their own experience
of the class. Because my sense of
self-worth depended on my judgment
of the feeling in the room, none of
us were completely free to enjoy the
sense of individuality which would
support our interconnectedness.
Gradually, I have come to ap-
preciate in my teaching what I ex-
perience in my dancing: meeting
resistance, physically and emotional-
ly, creates support. Defining a
boundary-where you end and
something or someone else begins-
empowers you to reach beyond your
own kinesphere, past the edge of
your known experience. Yet, at
another level of perception, what ap-
pears solid and static can be perceiv-
ed as spacious and moving: a wall or
a person that seems an impermeable
VolumelS 1990
barrier may be viewed on the
molecular level as many small
vortexes of energy with vast spaces
in between. At this level, the very
idea of "boundary" loses meaning,
the "resistance" shifts, perhaps
disappears!
Contact work engenders such
perceptual shifts; to integrate them
requires a safe environment where
we can learn to listen to and trust
our bodily experience without fear or
self-judgment. Recently, my learning
process has included the ReParen-
ting'm work of Constance Abbott.
Body-Mind Centering taught me that
support precedes movement.
ReParenting encourages people not
only to understand and feel what
messages we needed and deserved to
receive from our families at different
stages in our development, but to
create situations in our adult lives
which can provide the support we
missed then and need now. It is, in
some ways, a reframing process: we
learn to look at our experience dif-
ferently and can then choose to act
differently, to not perpetuate pain.
Contact Improvisation, Body-Mind
Centering and other body-based
educational and therapeutic modes
take us beyond a mental reframing
into the physical experience of how
messages-both past and current-
are encoded in our bodies. When we
can feel movement patterns, we can
allow them to dissolve on a cellular
level, a tissue level, and reclaim the
power of active choice, balanced by
receptive flow, in our movement and
our lives.
In last winter's classes, I had
created permission for my students
and myself to follow inner desires, to
enjoy movement, to meet resistance.
But I hadn't known how to establish
enough safety for all of us to hang
out at the edge of our comfort zones
and to let movement happen from
that edge.
For many people, the mere fact of
participating in a group is unsafe.
Groups are often places where our
bodily experience must be repressed
or denied. As the main model for
group interaction in our society, the
nuclear family provides a setting-
echoed in schools, workplaces, and
community groups-where members
are often ignored, stifled or actively
abused. We have few models for get-
ting our individual needs met while
supporting others in the context of a
group. Contact work, which asks of
us the vulnerable acts of giving and
receiving support with our bodies,
can easily remind people how they
have been wounded time and again
in relationships and group situations.
It requires courage to return to the
place where one has been hurt and
to open oneself to the possibility of
a different outcome. This is a heal-
ing potential of Contact.
My job, then, I realized, as a
teacher, is to create a safe structure
for the learning process; and that job
includes, but is not limited to, the
teaching of physical skills. The need
for these skills, it seems to me, an-
nounces itself as people trust enough
to push their own limits. To reach
that place requires an initial set of
boundaries or clear parameters for
the class. Feeling safe is not the
same thing as being comfortable.
Safety entails trusting a situation
enough to risk trying out the new,
the uncomfortable. In order to re-
main at the threshold of discomfort
where I can let go of old patterns of
movement, thinking, and being and
make other choices, I have to know
where the support is, where the
Tom Thoreson and Teri Carter, Milwaukee.
photo: George Johnson
Volume 15 1990
· CQ/CI Sourcebook 189
boundaries are, from which dir~ctfon
gravity is pulling. Being safe is
knowing where the edges are, not
deciding in advance what I'm going
to do about them.
This spring I decided to write
down all the things I needed to feel
safe teaching my Contact class. I
realized that the first person for
whom I must create safety is myself,
that if I took the initiative, then
those in the class would feel freer to
create it for themselves. When it
came to actually saying these things
to my class, I got scared of sounding
rigid, authoritarian. I remembered
the exhilarating bodily and emotional
freedom of my first Contact ex-
periences (with Mangrove in the '70s)
which exploded me into new dance
territory. Yet, those early experiences
were frightening too; I felt unsup-
ported, jerked around. I hurt myself
by pushing too far and not knowing
how to let go. Perhaps my desire
now for clear parameters is not so
much a betrayal of the '60s spirit of
anarchy that attracted me to Con-
tact, as a need for a less violent,
more knowledgeable doorway to the
freedoms it allows us to reclaim.
In the first class of my spring
Making Contact series, despite my
misgivings, I took the risk of sharing
everything I had written. Not only
did it feel good, but people thanked
me for stating my boundaries so
clearly. They went on to work for
two hours, even the first-timers, in a
deep and inspiring way, and to share
amazing insights for a group that
had just met. As the series progress-
ed, people remained present, their
focus, mutual support and honesty
in sharing their experience deepening
throughout the weeks. My own
energy and commitment to teaching
expanded with each class.
I offer these Guidelines for Con-
tact Class, understanding that each
community and its needs are dif-
ferent, that they reflect both the
direction of my personal work and
the kind of students that work is at-
tracting in Minnesota. I offer them
in the spirit of sharing information,
in the hope that they will be helpful
to other practitioners of Contact
who are encountering similar issues.
J

• This is a movement class not a

charged because it deals with the

involves touching. For this reason I.

basic guidelines for our work
together.

If an emergency comes up and
you're going to be late, please give
me a call at home before 9:30 am to
let me know. ·

miss class.

desire. Sometimes the check-in may
come after the individual warm-up
time. We will then do partner work
exploring an idea, image or par-
ticular skill, and then may do a
group dance structure.

DIANE ELLIOT is an independent
choreographer and movement teacher
based in Minneapolis. She has enjoyed
watching, doing and teaching Contact for
