STILL MOVING, , . after all these years
by Jerry Zientara
In my life as an animal, Contact
Improvisation has been one of the
brightest illuminations, helping me in
the search for wisdom, happiness
and the meanings of life. Contact
has been a catalyst in my relation to
the world, opening my dancing and
giving me a world to dance in.
Since I was a small child I have
surfed on the urge to create little
worlds of my own. I have been pro-
ducer and director, writer and per-
former, designer and clean-up crew
over and over in order to dwell in
the magic reality of an altered, im-
agined society. Contact, in its all-
absorbing concentration of attention
and array of options, gave me hope
and an example of physical/meta-
physical freedom. I hungered to ex-
pand the inward state of being in the
flow of movement, turn it inside-out
and make an arena of total
involvement.
In 1982 I began going to jack-off
parties. Lessons about weight and
?u~render, momentum and timing,
mt1macy and respect for the partner
vital in Contact were also vital
amidst hot, horny men eager to play
together. In both Contact and the
j/o scene, trust, good humor and
sensitive touch invite the interper-
sonal rapport, and generate above
and beyond the physical sharing an
atmosphere of generous welcome.
Unwilling to be merely a partici-
pant, I felt my dictatorial drives
push me to become an organizer of
the San Francisco JACKS in 1984
and the next year I was one of th~
circle of radical artists who aimed to
conquer the epidemic of FADS
(Fear, Anger, Doubt and Sorrow) by
becoming the Healing Order of
Kthar Sissies. The party animal
within me emerged as Dogtor Woof!
Semiremide and I began to ad-
ministrate, mediate and realize
clinics, parties, rituals and
ceremonies dedicated to healing of
the self and the planet. Dance had
become movement, theatre had
become paratheatre, my Contact
group had become a support net-
work and dining club; as the '80s
rolled along, religion, money, art
and sex twisted and turned into
something almost coherent,
something like a big jewel lighting
my way.
For the last four years, my
endeavors have included mixed
events for men and women, of gay,
lesbian, bisexual, heterosexual, wild,
shy, s/m and other persuasions
widening my outlook and expa~ding
my provincialism beyond the ex-
clusively gay men's milieu.
Recently I went to a Contact
weekend where I discovered many
folks deeply embroiled in anxieties
about intimacy, attraction and other
psychoerotic phenomena within the
context of our movement form. At
last, I thought, my studies might
apply to the world of Contact, where
I got so many good ideas in the first
place. Currently I am looking for
ways to offer some of the lore I
have won to contactors who once
again surround me. I am meditating
on drills, exercises, articulations of
some of the attraction/repulsions,
curiosities, fears and delights movers·
report that verge on and merge with
the psychoerotic. In private practice
as a planet therapist, I use breath,
mas~age, movement and counseling,
seekmg to heal psychic wounds, as
many of us have been doing all
along, directly and indirectly. The
duet of the skeleton and the breath
once my vision of the Contact dan~e,
has another dimension, crisscrossed
and united with the duet of the per-
sonality and the aura.
Attractions to certain partners
r~sistances that lead to challenge~,
difficulties leading to triumphs, the
darknesses required for stars to shine
in: these are among my current con-
siderations. How these notions apply
to Contact, how Contact applies to
me and others, how by dancing our
best we can heal ourselves and the
planet: these are among my favorite
thinks.
Found in a spin, loving the spin
I'm in, I remain your admiring stu-
dent and pal, relaying radiant
blessings!
Woof! *
JERRY ZIENTARA was part of Frxx Movix

whose works include "Fur on Fire" and

dancing again. He lives in San Francisco

WHAT'S IN THE BAG?
Issues that lie in the shadow of Contact Improvisation
transcript of a discussion at the
2nd California Contact Festival, 6/10/89,
facilitated, edited and revised
by Martin Keogh and Kirk Andrews
In the issue that had "Questions
Not to Ask" [by Danny Lepkoff, CQ
Vol. 13:3], you put out the call for some
"meaty discussion." I think this will
provoke just that.
Over the months of working on this,
I have gone through feelings that range
from elation to alarm. Looking at the
form has reflected back on myself and
a lot of my own issues that are crying
out for attention. I hope that by putting
this material out to the community at
large, it will evoke the same for others.
[M.K.]
Keogh: A few months ago I had the idea
of having a discussion on the shadow
of Contact. I told Kirk, and he said he
was thinking of doing a panel on sex-
uality and Contact. We agreed they were
related, and decided to put them
together. We spent a lot of time
reaching into what we thought the
shadow was for ourselves personally,
and for the form as a whole. We came
up with an image that poet Robert Bly
uses to describe the shadow. He sees it
as a big bag that we drag behind us.
When something is too painful to deal
with, or there is something we don't
want to look at, we throw it in the bag.
When we were children, we threw a lot
of who we were into the bag from
parental and teacher disapproval and
their desire for us to be good little boys
and girls. And as adults we continue to
fill the bag. For some of us the bag is
so long that they're all blocked up in the
doorway.
Kirk asked me, "Why do you want
to delve into this shadow material?" I
thought about this and realized that it's
because I feel married to this form of
dance. It would be easy to trance out or
go on automatic and not confront the
stuff that over the years of dancing I've
put into the bag. Sometimes when I'm
in a duet and a beautiful moment opens
up, an opportunity for a spontaneous
ride, I say, 'Wait a minute while I get
my bag', and then the moment is lost.
Hopefully, by mucking around in the
muddled and uncomfortable aspects of
the shadow, we can get some stuff out
of the bag, so the dance can continue
to open up.
Andrews: The term 'shadow' has been
used for thousands of years as a literary
metaphor for the dark side, the under-
world of life. Carl Jung, of Analytic
Psychology fame, then picked it up and
used it as a more poetic way to describe
information that we repress and keep
down. There are a lot of things that we
have inside us that we don't show to the
outside world. Jung said we have a
"persona" character that we like to
show, and then a bunch of "shadow"
characters which are kept hidden. The
shadow is full of these characters. A lot
of them are quite creative and power-
ful (i.e., the thief, the witch, the tyrant)
but we are taught that they are unac-
ceptable. When we work on integrating
the shadow characters, by talking about
them, and bringing them into conscious
awareness, we release a lot of energy
that has been trapped there. The annoy-
ing thing is that just when we think we
have the shadow uncovered and figured
out, it changes into another form. In-
tegrating the shadow therefore becomes
a lifelong process of looking within
ourselves.
We hope this discussion today will
help us to bring out some of these
shadow issues that are very real but also
very difficult to talk about.
Keogh: One aspect is my pers~nal
shadow in the dance: "What am I bnng-
ing that I'm not wanting to look at?"
Then there is the shadow that is inherent
in the form itself. I've noticed as we
have looked at some of this, that
sometimes the material is offensive. The
ones I take offense at, the ones I get
defensive about, the ones where I say,
"No, that's not what Contact is about,''
are the ones I need to look at most
carefully.
We want to start by shining a bright
light at Contact so the shadow can
192 CQ;Cl Sourcebook
become nice and long and clear. Take
a moment to think of what you like best
about Contact, what brings you to the
form?
Participants: • The incredible
playfulness of everybody• Trust•. Con-
nection • Release • Cooperation •
Resonance• Total contact with some-
one • I feel like I get to be more fully
me than in anything else I do • Because
it helps bring out the shadow side of me
• Confronting my fear and my sense of
limitation • Physical and emotional risk
• Surprise • Touch • Freedom to push
• The accepted intimacy • I love the sen-
suality • The vitality • Inspiration to be
creative in all of my life • Getting energy
from non-energy• It's a metaphor for
all my relationships • Spontaneity •
Athleticism• Sportsmanship• Medita-
tion • Recognition •
Keogh: These statements demonstrate
why we love this form. Because we. l?ve
it we think it's healthy to turn a cntical
e;e towards the form and our participa-
tion in it. One way that Kirk and I have
been working on this is to look at what
we like then delve into what's behind
that. If.I like how playful everybody is,
does that suggest we have a group of
people who refuse to grow up? If this
idea bothers you, you might be near the
shadow. Today, let that be a guide.
Here are five questions to work with
in your small groups:
• What do I deny or repress to do this
form?
• What do I like too much about this
form?
• What do I experience in this form that
I don't like to acknowledge?
•What's missing for me in this form?
•What's missing in the Contact
community?
(The group splits into eight small groups
who talk with different levels of anima-
tion. We get back together.)
Keogh: We would like to hear a few
statements from each group. The rest of
us will try to hold back the impulse to
make it all better, or say you're wrong,
or you're ok, or that's not my ex-
perience. We will just try to hear it.
Group one: We talked about two main
categories. One was sexuality and the
other, community. There is a blurred
distinction between sensuality and
eroticism, and the lines often blur in the
dance. . . One person said that
sometimes women he'd be dancing with
spoke about feeling aggressive sexuali-
ty from their male partners, and they
didn't know how to deal with it, and
Volume 15 1990

usually would excuse themselves to go
get water.
Participant: Uh oh, you blew thefr cover
now.
Group one: With community, the in-
dividual often judging what the com-
munity wanted from them. It came back
to not knowing who you are yourself,
changing how you act to fit the
community.
Group two: Honesty vs. niceness, how
do you let someone know you dislike
them and don't want to dance with
them. Is it better to be honest and just
tell them so, or be nice ... Culture came
up. A lot of us seem to be from the same
background ... Everyone in our group
is involved in academic institutions, and
really get into our heads, is this a haven
for our bodies to express themselves?
Group three: What do I experience that
I don't like to acknowledge?: fear of
physical danger, of rejection and
vulnerability. How do I judge who I
dance with or don't dance with and how
do I tell them? Men holding back their
weight from women ... What's missing
for me in this form?: The completeness
of the intimacy. This sense that it is ok
to be intimate within certain parameters
but it doesn't extend even to sometimes
saying goodbye to someone after class.
One of the most frustrating parts of this
form is describing it to people who don't
know it. A definition! ... What do I like
too much about this form?: Again, in-
timacy, sensuality, letting go of the
mind, just sort of checking out or
o.d.ing on the experience, not knowing
when to stop just because 'it's there' ...
What do I deny to do this form?: fear
of physical danger. Sexuality: the fear
of being exposed, fear of rejection, of
not looking good, the judgement that
I don't know how to do it. The judge-
ment that I am not trained enough to
do it. .. What's lacking in the com-
munity?: a lack of professionalism, in
presentations and facilities.
Group four: We talked about feeling
vulnerable about approaching people
and feeling insecurity around skill
levels ... Someone had discomfort dan-
cing with the opposite sex, and had a
desire to know them better to feel more
trust before dancing with them ... The
difficulty of making eye contact while
dancing, which seems paradoxical in
such an intimate situation ... Someone
was disturbed by the lack of blacks and
minority groups.
Group five: Somebody asked does

anybody feel grief or other strong emo-

had some intense experience, either
remembering something from our

that this is only Contact, we're not

homogeneous. We are mostly white,

Group six: We talked about talking too

talked about fears of intimacy, liking

Group seven: We talked about snob-

Group eight: Injuries; people feeling

Participant: Contact also has a
smugness about it, vis a vis the modern

we often say modern dance is very
mechanical, and something is missing in
it. And about Contact, their's is a
thread about a lack of discipline, a lack

refuge in our own spontaneity and struc-

work with crafting things.
Andrews: Any time you put out that
you are better than someone else, one
has to wonder if the shadow part is say-
ing, "We feel less than ... "
Keogh: Kirk and I have come up with
some statements, or "judgements"
about Contact that we hope will bring
some of the shadow out into the light.
Some are more serious than others. We
would like to put these out, and then we
can make the impossible jump of try-
ing to at least resolve our talk today.
After we came up with this list we split

•Power •
•The Form
• Gender & Sexuality
• Personal (a catchall for those that
are hard to categorize)
We decided not to put these down as

start with the Power category:
•We are always in a supportive role
with one another, seldom in a conflict
role. There's very little conflict in this
community.
Participant: ... little that gets express-

Keogh: • We are all big fish in a little
pond and there are no predators.
Because the form is about support and
flowing and acknowledging, the form
attracts people who aren't very
powerful.
• We are people with a complete lack
of boundaries.
• There is no overt competition, there
are no levels of attainment, no accolades
to be reached. So you can do this form
forever and never get any acknowledge-
ment for it.
• We are a group who tends to have a
failure streak. (heated reaction)
Participant: Is there any group that
doesn't?
Keogh: Remember, we don't have to de-
fend the form right now.
• No one in our community is outstan-
ding or excels in their own field. (more
heated reaction)
Participant: What are these????
Keogh: These are statements.
Participant: That someone else made,
that you made, that you believe, that

Keogh: These are possible shadow

tact, some are our own speculation,

are from people looking at the form

are in your shadow, we usually don't

like, and are critical of, what we hold

put them in an "I" form.
Andrews: That's a good thing to say but

Keogh: We decided that because the
form is so supportive and "I" based and

Andrews: They include us, but I can't
take responsibility for them all because
I don't believe them all. This is to fuel
the discussion. We are not saying, this
is how you are, or how the form is, but
these are possibilities of our shadow in
this form. If these statements don't
apply to you, just let them go by with
a laugh. If they draw a reaction from
you,_ it is worth reflecting on your reac-
tion. That is a part of our purpose.
Keogh: Under the category of The
Form:
• There is no connection in this form
between discipline and art. It makes no
artistic statement.
• There's a hierarchy based on who gets
the highest rides, who shows off the
best.
• It is a form for latent gays and
perverts.
• We suffer from naive idealism, with
ideas of egalitarianism and "organic
process."
Andrews: In the Gender and Sexuality
category:
• We're getting off on breaking the
incest taboo. We get to have a kind of
sexual experience with lots and lots of
people who are more family than lovers.
• Men are better at Contact than
women. (oohs and laughter)
• It fosters an androgynous sexuality.
It turns men into women and women
into men.
Keogh: Look at our apparel.
Andrews: • The emotional and the sex-
ual get cut off to do this dance.
Under the Personal category:
• One thing I don't like to acknowledge
is that I'm good at this form. My brag-
gart has been kept in shadow.
• Contacters are slaves to their sensa-
tions and feelings. If we gave as much
weight to our ideas, we would be called
Volume 15 1990
fanatics. (whew/ no, no/ again!)
• Contacters are slaves to their sensa-
tions and feelings. If we gave as much
weight to our ideas, we would be called
fanatics. And possibly would be much
more powerful in the world with that
force behind our convictions and beliefs
rather than our physical bodies.
• We are trying to fill the hole where we
don't feel loved.
• We are all leftovers from, or resent
having missed, the '60s and the
touchy/feely/group-gropes. (moan)
• This issue of everyone being so playful
and not wanting to grow up. The Peter
Pan syndrome.
Participants: Hallelujah! I Let's just

some of this stuff doesn't sound like

Andrews: Like we said, there's a lot of
creativity and pow:r in the shadow.
Participant: Whenever we talk about

I feel when we talk about this, it's as if

Andrews: That is true, and, for right
now we are here to dig up the muck

Participant: I like that. I'm not saying

Participant: We have to dig them up to

Participant: I see the focus on two
forms of shadow. There's our own in-
ternal blockages, that may get in the
way of the dance (or help the dance).
And then there is this cultural stuff that
we grew up with. IN essence, we are
here to turn that stuff upside down and
explore the side of it we never got to see
the first 20 or 30 or 40 years of our lives.
The culture is driven by fanaticism on
a certain level, the fanaticism of
dominating the world and repressing
our feelings about that. Here we are tur-
ning it upside down: it's not our place
to be powerful out in the world in a
driving way. We can't challenge what's
out there with the same behavior as that
which we are challenging. So, it's very
appropriate, at this stage in the evolu-
tion of the form and the community to
be very introspective and visible.
Keogh: I feel we're trying to elevate this
material. I don't think we are ready to
do that. We haven't been down in the
shadow long enough yet.
Participant: I noticed my emotional
responses to some of these statements.
Some went right by and others got stuck
in me. Like hearing an old judgement
I used to have about myself from the
culture. Boy, there was a lot of reaction
happening among us in response to
many of these. I would encourage
everybody to look at the ones you had
the most inner discomfort with and ask
what's happening here. Clearly there's
a lack of resolution around a lot of these
issues. I appreciate the statements be-
ing read the way they were because they
were spoken the way my internal judge
speaks them.
Andrews: One of the benefits of look-
ing at the shadow is you get a lot of
energy back. If we are powerless, the
power is in the shadow, if we are
playful, the adult is in the shadow, if we
are shy and afraid, the confident one is
in the shadow. It makes it worthwhile
to go there. Secondly, I think it's im-
portant for our community to look at
the shadow and look at what's missing
in order to be a real community and not
a pseudo community that's superficial.
Call it community maintenance. We
can't say, "Oh, we talked about that
last year," and then let it go. We have
to keep working with it. One of the
things that I want to acknowledge is that
our community does a good job of talk-
ing about issues. That has been especial-
ly true at some of the Harbin jams.
Hopefully today will have raised up
some questions that we can continue to
work with. This is an ongoing process,
seek out a friend if you need to talk with
someone. We need to close for now.
Keogh: Before we get active, let's sit
quietly for a moment and review what
this discussion has evoked. *
Special thanks to Dawn Weiss and Dan
Matson for transcribing the tapes of this
event.

The following is an article about
my career in contact improvisation.
During the time I was doing Con-
tact, I often felt unappreciated and
shut out by other contacters because
Mark Pritchard.
I wouldn't toe the line on Cl's rela-
tion to New Age issues. This article
looks back on that period and traces
my involvement in the form. [M.P.]
MY RISE & FALL IN CONTACT IMPROV
by Mark Pritchard --------------
1. In 1974, after seeing a visiting
dance company during my senior
year in high school, I sign up for
modern dance P.E. as a freshman in
college. Astonished that I had made
such an effeminate choice, my
parents make me switch to
basketball-which, since I'm only
5 '4", I hate.
2. Three years later, a fellow
writer on the college newspaper in-
vites me to a performance by
Deborah Hay, who has just moved
to Austin. "She's kind of a hippie,
but you may like it anyway," my
friend predicts. I hate the first half
of the show, and then love the se-
cond. A few months later, I take the
first dance classes of my life with
Hay. One of the core members of
the Judson Dance group, Hay's
classes during the late '70s are run
with a kind of New Age anti-elitist
philosophy, in which everybody is
allowed to think they can dance.
Fortunately this includes me.
3. During the next two years, I
learn a philosophy of movement and
performance from Hay. At the same
studio in Austin, I also take modern
dance and contact improvisation. I
get a crush on every one of my
196 CQICI Sourcebook
teachers. I even become brave
enough to take modern dance at
other studios. After two years, I'm
in the best physical shape of my life.
More important, the realm of dance
has been opened to me. Best of all I
love contact improvisation; learning
how to do it is like relearning a
language I had once spoken and then
forgotten.
4. After a terrific summer, the
core group of contacters that began
in my class slowly fades away until
I'm literally the only one left who
wants to do contact anymore.
Frustrated with this and other
evidence that Austin is full of space-
outs who don't really want to do
anything, I move to San Francisco.
It's 1979.
5. One of the reasons I moved to
San Francisco is that I knew I could
do contact improvisation here.
Mangrove, the most successful of CI
groups, is at the peak of its career,
and there are several other active
groups. As soon as I get to San
Volume 15 1990
Francisco, I head up to Vancouver
Island to take part in a two-week
long jam. I'm excited to think that
at last I'll be among people who like
doing contact as much as I do, in-
cluding all of Mangrove and most of
Cl's biggest luminaries from across
the continent.
6. Unfortunately, these stars seem
mostly interested in dancing with
each other rather than with nobodies
like me. I'm not the only person
who feels this way; three days into
the event, there is a general discus-
sion about elitism. The CI stars pro-
test that they don't have to dance
with anybody they don't want to.
7. Intimidated by Mangrove's
self-preservation stance and feeling
insecure, I link up with the only
dancer at the event who's really
friendly to me, a contact teacher
from San Francisco named Keriac.
When I get back to the city, I join
her group, Walkabout Dance
Collective.
8. Walkabout had been formed a
couple of years before by en-
thusiastic participants at a big CI
conference of some kind in Berkeley.
As a new nobody in town, I'm
grateful for how they welcome me
and the skills I learned in Texas.
Three months after I arrive, I do my
own performance, in which some of
Walkabout's members participate, a
mixture of poetry, film, music and
dance. It's a fair success.
9. I also start attending contact
classes taught by Alan Ptashek, who
had been one of the only people will-
ing to show me a few things at the
event on Vancouver Island, and I go
to all the CI jams. There are a lot of
friendly, enthusiastic people to dance
with, and I'm really glad I came to
San Francisco.
10. But there are some currents
that I don't understand, even in this
non-elitist group. During rehearsals
for my piece at Walkabout, it
became clear that there was a
misunderstanding about whether the
performance was going to be a col-
lective process or mainly my crea-
tion. Ten days after the show, I visit
one of the performers. At first she
tactfully criticizes my creative pro-
cess, but then gets angrier and
angrier when it seems I don't under-
stand. After a long tirade, in which
she calls me sexist-which deeply
disturbs me, since in Texas I seemed
to be one of the most feminist men
Volume 15 1990
around-she throws me out of her
apartment.
11. In November, seven months
after I arrived in San Francisco, I do
my second performance, in which
the main piece is a long apology for
all the things I've been criticized
about, like being macho, walking too
heavily, not melting sincerely
enough, and not trusting my feel-
ings. Afterwards, the woman who
threw me out of her apartment
comes up to me and says, "Mark,
Mark, I loved your piece."
12. I like Walkabout because we
have our own studio, and a
twice-monthly performance series, so
I get plenty of chances to do my
own work and interact with others in
the community. Even better, we all
support each other on our projects,
even if we privately realize it's not
very good art. It's great to be ac-
cepted and to belong to a real
creative group.
13. In addition to my involvement
with Walkabout, I help produce the
Bay Area Contact Network (BACN)
newsletter, and I take it upon myself
to show up on time at every single
Sunday jam in Golden Gate Park, in
case someone new comes on time
(everyone else is always late). I go to
performances by Mangrove and
other groups. I conscientiously
develop my skills in Alan's class and
in workshops taught by visitors like
Andrew Harwood. I collaborate with
others on several shows. I get fired
from a job for reproducing the pro-
gram for a show on the office
copier.
14. Eventually, through sheer ef-
fort if not dance ability, I begin to
belong. I stop being a nobody and
become one of the people closer to
the center of the CI community. I'm
always ready to do the door at my
friends' shows; I produce a couple of
large contact improvisation concerts,
one to benefit CQ, the other for
abortion rights. I do my own pieces
at Walkabout shows every two or
three months. The peak of this
period is mid-1981.
15. All during this time, however,
as I practice contact improvisation
for performance and as a pure move-
ment form, I'm utterly bored by the
aspects of CI that have to do with
health, chiropractic, "healing,"
various New Age therapeutic
"techniques," and mysticism. A title
of a typical article in CQ at this
time, when it seemed that the only
way to salvation was to become as
healthy and "natural" as possible,
was "Can the Body Ransom Us?"
[by Maxine Sheets Johnstone, CQ
Vol IV:3 I 4; Summer 79].
16. In reaction to all this, I write an
article entitled "New Age Shuf-
fleboard," in which I warn that an
emphasis by contacters on all this
"healing" will lead CI away from
art and performance and into some
post-hippie suburb of the soul. My
article is published in the BACN
newsletter and reprinted in CQ [Vol
VI:l, Fall 80]. Some people like it,
although they don't agree with me.
The number of people who are will-
ing to approach CI as a performing
medium rather than as therapy, and
to collaborate with me on CI pro-
jects, gets fewer. I start to feel like a
lone voice crying in the wilderness.
17. By mid-1981, the people of
Walkabout and I have gone about as
far as we can go together; I've
started doing theater and music and
they aren't really the people I want
in my pieces anymore. I leave the
group and start another CI group
called Tangent Dance Collective with
some friends from jams. We do a
one-night "tour" to a small town
100 miles away, and plan a trip to
Santa Fe, NM. One week before the
Santa Fe trip, the other male in the
group decides that it wouldn't be the
best thing for his process. We cancel
the trip and the group breaks up.
18. The moves I like to do most in
CI involve jumps and carrying. Me
and some other males start practicing
these at a jam one evening. Flying
through the air and throwing each
other around, we're having a terrific
time. Then some women start teasing
us, singing "Macho, macho men"
after a currently popular disco song.
"Mark, the jumping is fun, but you
should really learn to melt more,''
one of them tells me later. "Get in
touch with your receptive side."
Around the same time, a contact im-
provisation teacher tells me
mysteriously, "You need to realize
your beginner's mind more." I don't
have the slightest idea what he's talk-
ing about.
19. At some point in 1982, the at-
tendance at jams really starts to fall
off. The jams in the park fade away
and eventually it seems that no one
except me is interested in doing CI
as performance, although you can
CQICI Sourcebook 197
see CI from time to time in
choreography by some local modern
dancers. The BACN newsletter stops
publishing. Mangrove breaks up into
two competing groups. Meanwhile
my performances start to contain
much less dance and much more
theater and music. There are fewer
and fewer CI events to go to, and
my attention is taken by my writing
and theater projects.
20. I still believe in CI that has an
artistic emphasis. I write a couple of
articles for CQ, but they're rejected.
I organize a symposium of con-
tacters, with a panel made up mostly
of people who were the bigwigs when
I arrived in town 5 years before. We
talk about what we think the future
of contact improvisation is. There is
no consensus, not even towards
therapy. In fact, everybody sounds
really depressed about the future of
Contact. This is the last CI event I
contribute to. Although there are
still a few classes going on, the Con-
tact scene that was booming when I
arrived in town seems dead.
21. In early 1983, while the roof
of Walkabout's building is being
repaired by a landlord whom we
suspect wants all the artists out
anyway, there is a huge
thunderstorm. Water destroys the
dance floor at Walkabout's studio,
and the collective seems ready to
disappear. I feel glad to have gotten
out when I did. Meanwhile, my non-
dance performing is going strong. I
also go back to school and become a
high school English teacher.
22. By 1986, I've spent several
years doing only theater and music.
With two dancers who know nothing
about CI, I form a group called
Short on Attitude, which is dedicated
to doing dance and performance art.
I choreograph a wildly energetic
dance piece that has almost no con-
tact improvisation. There's nobody
around to tell me I have to melt or
be natural, or do things according to
a party line, but I am learning about
melting in another realm. I get in-
volved in s-m and learn, in a sub-
missive role, what being receptive
really means.
23. I go to Japan and teach
English for 2 years in a stultifying
provincial city.
24. When I return to San Fran-
cisco in late 1988, I attend a sort of
Walkabout reunion. It seems they
sued the landlord for the ruined
198 CQJO Sourcebook
dance floor and won a settlement;
we go out to an expensive dinner
with part of the money. It's fun to
catch up on what people are doing
these days. One of the people at the
dinner used to be a big wheel in the
CI community. I ask him what he's
doing these days. "Holding safe sex
orgies," he replies. I get his phone
number.
25. Keriac, whom I also see at this
dinner for the first time in years, has
become successful teaching contact
improvisation in Europe. She makes
enough there in six months to live in
San Francisco for the other six
months.
26. I resume doing pieces with
Short on Attitude. Meanwhile, a
group called Contraband, made up
of people who are artistic descen-
dants of Mangrove, is becoming
well-known using CI, along with
other kinds of postmodern dance, in
choreography. Another rising com-
pany called the High Risk Group
does the same. In fact, although
there are no performances anywhere
of plain contact improvisation, you
see scraps of CI in almost every
modem dance performance and even
in rock videos.
27. Short on Attitude sponsors a
low-tech performance series. To my
surprise, a group of contacters signs
up to do a piece, and I eagerly put
them on the bill. When they per-
form, they make all the worst
mistakes that I made, and learned
not to do, ten years before. I feel
disgusted, as if all the lessons we had
learned in the early '80s, all the pro-
gress we tried to make, had been in
vain.
28. I later learn, from an ex-
Walkabout member, that CI is in-
deed making a comeback, especially
in Berkeley. There are lots of classes
and well-attended jams. Hearing this,
I don't want to go at all. I feel I
gave Contact the best of my energy,
enthusiasm and support, back in the
days when I was young and impres-
sionable, and that all my efforts
were wasted because my priorities
were different from other people's.
If I went back now, I'd probably get
ripped off again. Also I'm older and
fatter and wouldn't dance well. It's
not worth it.
Moral: Contact Improvisation,
when I discovered it, held a great
promise. It let me do things with my
t- THE CALIFORNIA
"HARBIN" JAM
A Brief History
by Sharon Tomsky ----
The California Contact Im-
provisation Jam at Harbin Hot
Springs continues to be an amazing,
delightful, challenging, evolving
phenomenon. The first one was held
in the Fall of 1986. We're now
working on the eighth Jam, which
will be the end of May, 1990. The
organizers are: Julie Oak, Penny
Paulus, David Russell, Robin Tren-
da, and myself. On occasion, due to
uncontrollable circumstances or by
choice, an individual organizer has
taken a Jam "off." For the past
year, Robin has discontinued his role
as an organizer, because of the vast
commitment he has made to
establishing and directing the new
Wall Street Center for the Arts in
Chico, CA.
In the Spring of '86, the 5 of us,
plus several other Bay Area Contac-
tors were at the Breitenbush Jam [in
Oregon]. Robin proposed a meeting
for exploratory talks for anyone in-
terested in starting a California jam.
During the week we must have had 4
or 5 meetings and the groundwork
I
oz
body I never thought I could; it
taught me valuable lessons in sexual
and social dynamics. But by insisting
that contacters follow a party line on
the relation of contact to health and
spirituality, and by neglecting
esthetic issues, the CI community
alienated me (and who knows how
many others?). If it's true that there
is a new upsurge of interest these
days, I would like to urge the CI
community not to do this again.
Don't spurn the priorities and in-
terests of new converts who love
contact improvisation and have a vi-
sion of what it could be. It's too late
for me, but it's not too late for you.
*
Ex-contactor MARK PRITCHARD is a
writer, performance artist, and publisher
of "Frighten The Horses," a magazine
about the sexu_al revolution.
Volwne 15 1990
was laid. We went home, fresh from
the excitement of Breitenbush, made
phone calls, decided on Harbin as
the site (mostly because of the
spacious Conference Center, the
mineral hot tubs and pools, and its
2Vi hour proximity to the Bay Area),
found a caterer, chose a date and the
ball was rolling. We had a solid ex-
ample from Breitenbush to draw
upon, and our own personal ex-
periences there helped shape our
ideas about how we wanted our jam
to be. Thanks to Alito Alessi and
Karen Nelson for creating the event
that inspired us.
We had the advantage of having a
large regional Contact Community as
a base, but still we decided to
minimize our risk and start small.
The first one was from Friday night
., to Sunday night, and was truly one
§1 of the most joyous and exhilarating
c3 weekends of my life. Everyone seem-
ed to feel that way. It was like giv-
ing birth, except that the labor had
seemed so easy! We decided not to
wait a whole year to do it again. The
2nd Jam was about 6 months later,
©
0 in May '87, and was expanded to 3
] days and nights.
c.. We've been doing them twice a
year ever since, one in the Fall (late
October, early November) and one in
~ the Spring (late April, early May).
!:'-- The 1990 schedule, however, has
·g, been changed drastically. The Con-
..: ference Center we use at Harbin
c (where we have a beautiful large,
iJ airy room, bathrooms, showers, a
~ small "guru" room, and hot & cool
15 pools all to ourselves) is in high de-
0' mand, and this year is not available
tf at our usual dates. Also, for years
;; people have been wanting a longer
E jam, and there is an ever-expanding
~ list of folks who want to come. We
c. always have a waiting list, though
·5 we've increased our maximum from
v B 40 to 55. So, we decided to try
gf' something different. This year we're
i having THREE Jams-all occurring
over holiday weekends [see Notice in
"CQ Newsletter"]. We will resume
our "regular" Spring/Fall schedule
Volume 15 1990
in '91.
What has been the effect of all
these Jams? Besides being a tremen-
dous source of enjoyment and op-
portunity for exploration and
development of our Contact, there
has been much bonding and inspira-
tion for other Contact activities. The
"Community" I speak of has grown
dramatically during these last four
years. It is still diverse, and not
everyone involved with Contact
comes to the Jams. But a strong,
vibrant and more organized group
continues to spawn, expand, create
and develop. The annual Contact
Festival emerged from an initial vi-
sion and discussion at one Harbin
Jam, spearheaded by Andrew
Clibanoff, who now lives on the East
Coast. The Contact Teachers Net-
work has taken shape-we now
cooperatively list all our classes,
workshops, jams and performances
together on one joint flyer. There
was an energetic effort to create a
Contact Community Center. Many
people devoted much time and
energy to this endeavor. Because of
the lengthy process we were pre-
empted in getting the studio we had
hoped to rent. For now, the 8th
Street Studio serves as our Berkeley
hub. There have been various perfor-
mance groups and series, as well as
Contact camping trips and picnics.
A 40-minute edited videotape titled
"Thank You For This Dance," has
been created by videographer/film-
maker Brandy Brawner and me. It
contains footage shot at the '87 and
'88 Harbin Jams. Copies are
available from me for $25 [see 'Con-
tacts' for address].
Although the majority of Harbin
J ammers do come from the Bay
Area, many have come from
throughout the West Coast, notably
Seattle, Portland and San Diego.
We strive to set up an environment
where everyone feels welcomed, in-
cluded; and that each person's feel-
ings, ideas and feedback are impor-
tant. We have daily group "sits"
and meetings (though we prefer to
call them "gatherings"). We have a
"dream pool" where people put out
their dreams and fantasies for things
they would like to have happen at
that Jam. There are group scores
and presentations. A memorable one,
initiated by Darshana [Ellis] from
San Diego, involved nude women
and paint. After much heated debate
about whether, where and when it
was going to happen, it turned out
to be an exquisite dance ritual per-
formed outside shortly before the
Sunday closing. Last Fall, Keriac
and others constructed a thoughtful
score which we all participated in,
processing our many deep and mixed
feelings following the Earthquake
("The Pretty Big One") which we
had just survived 1 Vi weeks before.
There is careful attention given to
group process and how Jam policies
and decisions are made. Participants'
written feedback at the end of each
Jam has proven invaluable. As
organizers, we have developed our
skills as facilitators-a role which
has proven to be both challenging
and rewarding.
And of course there's lots of Con-
tact. Dancing, jamming, classes,
more jamming. But I think what
makes the experience so rich is that
rather than being an isolated 2-hour
episode of Contact (like at a weekly
jam) it goes on 24 hours a day for 3
or 4 days. We live it and breathe it
together, as a group. We eat
together, sleep together (accommoda-
tions are either camping or com-
munal in the conference center), hot
tub together, hike, dance, talk, play,
drum, argue, sing, cry, laugh, hug,
etc. It feels so natural. I always feel
somehow more fully "me" at these
Jams than at practically any other
time. And I always feel so grateful
for this form which has so im-
measurably enriched my life; and for
the opportunity to serve, grow with,
and be part of this special Communi-
ty, which I know extends beyond the
Bay Area, beyond California,
beyond the U.S., even beyond
Europe and into at least parts of the
rest of the world. *
SHARON TOMSKY is a dancer, con-
tacter, and bodyworker. She teaches, per-
forms, & organizes contact events in the
San Francisco Bay Area.

EDITOR NOTE
Last spring, in the North of
England, I felt the network working.
I'd never been to Scotland before, and
being so close, I wanted to go. I'd
heard there was a small group in Glas-
gow that did Contact Improvisation and
I was curious to meet and move with
them. A little asking around produced
a name and phone number for John
Macinnes (now in 'Contacts') and I
called to see if I might invite myself up
for a jam and when was it. As there
wasn't one planned for that week, one
was generously and quickly organized.
Within 20 minutes or arriving in
Glasgow, I was slipping into my dance
clothes in an old gymnasium where a
dozen people were already in motion.
Finding a spot on the side of the room,
I lay down, dropping into my weight,
my breath, enjoying a stretch, taking
time to let time decompress and sensa-
tion come forward. I could hear the
others moving around me as I took
more time to stretch and breathe,
beginning to find in my body the
deep circulation I call dancing.
Relaxing my weight further with
each exhalation, I locate the center of
the earth directly below me down
deep. In whatever city or continent,
the center of the earth is always right
there. I move on, mobilizing inside
my "skinesphere," opening channels
through my body's tissues and joints
Volume 16 1991
for the passage of movement, impulse,
energy, feeling, idea. As my kinetic
energy builds, my appetite for space
expands, first into my immediate
kinesphere then into the whole room.
Pathways through the moving ensemble
appear and I take one.
Circulating among the others now,
I'm passing them by, not yet ready to
physically engage. I'm glad to be
among them and feel a language coming
that I love to speak.
When touches meet and weight is
exchanged, two deep breaths can often
be heard. Dancing is its own land and
language and in a short time a profound
level of communication can be reached.
I loved the "conversations" I had that
day in Glasgow-from abstract philo-
sophical exchanges to detailed techni-
cal explorations, through emotional
landscapes that swelled then gave way
to some physical fun that was played
out through increasingly subtle permu-
tations and so on until the dance was
done. Leave-taking takes its own form
each time. Introductions after dancing
often seem other-worldly, as ifthe
dancing was the "real world" and our
names a familiar fiction.
I was moved that day by the net-
work. I found people tremendously
generous with their time, space and
spirit. The moving body can hold forth
on a variety of subjects, revealing how
she feels, what she sees, who she is,
what she's thinking, where she's going.
But just to be dancing, improvising,
even touching, doesn't insure that
there will be a moving experience, a
quickening of life, and not just a
pleasant physical or mental workout.
Movement forms are good vehicles,
but there's something else, some other
level of engagement that happens
within a form-a release of spirit or
perceptual vitality-that inspires inven-
tion and deepens the contact with
oneself and partners. These improvisa-
tions can feel effortless but not without
exertion-demanding discipline to stay
within the bounds of the form, courage
to veriture past them, wisdom to decide
when to, humility when your decisions
lead you astray, compassion when it
hurts, elation when it works, and inten-
tion to stay with it through it all. To
dance in this way with others is to
communicate and discuss through
touch, movement, weight, tone, and
timing, bits of the mystery that only
our bodies can describe .
Since its inception in 197 5 as the
Contact Newsletter, Contact Quarterly
has been a vehicle for the exchange of
news and views about Contact Improv-
isation among an expanding network
of movers.
The first formal discussion in the
magazine of work other than Contact
was "Dartington March" in the summer
1976 issue, where Steve Paxton talks
about Mary Fulkerson's classes in Ana-
tomical Release and David Woodberty
reviews Lulu Sweigard's book, Human
Movement Potential-Its Ideokinetic
Facilitation. Since then, CQ has had
the opportunity to follow many over-
lapping currents of new dance develop-
ment-various approaches to improvisa-
tion, Bonnie Bainbridge Cohen and
Body/Mind Centering, Ruth Zaporah's
Action Theater, Contemplative Dance,
Irene Dowd 's neuromuscular training,
several branches of "Release work,"
Authentic Movement, and the work of
many contemporary choreographers/
performers.
Next issue's focus-Dancing With
Different Populations-will bring us in
contact with new currents of moving
people, and further our dialogue with
the "disabled" community-a highly
developed network in itself. In the
issue you now hold, Tom Giebink and
David Koteen preview this focus with
writings that stem from their experiences
with "differently-abled" dancers. There
is a lot to be learned from the mingling
of these bodies of work and organiza-
tions of people. What will come of this
inspired crossfertilization remains to be
seen-in the mind and in the moving.
Nancy Stark Smith
CQ/CI Sourcebook 201
DANCING WITH THE
DIFFERENTLY-ABLED
by Tom Giebink
Empathy, an elusive word,
means ... dancing in another person's
skin? If empathy is the ultimate
form of love, then dancing contact
improvisation, too, is a form of
love.
This idea took on new meaning
for me in Eugene, Oregon at the
1991 DanceAbility workshop.
Disability is not monolithic.
Cerebral Palsy is not the same as
Multiple Sclerosis is not the same as
Paralysis due to nerve damage is not
the same as Blindness. Even within
the "category" of cerebral palsy,
one person can communicate by
speech, another by pointing at pic-
ture symbols, yet another spells
words by looking through a clear
plastic plate covered with the
alphabet while a "reader" speaks the
words the "speaker's" eye
movements have indicated. Each per-
son is affected by their disability in a
singular way, yet they are routinely
lumped together as people "who
can't get along without help" in our
(able-bodied) world. Instead, each
has struck a different balance with
the physical world, making
themselves differently-abled.
Gerry Overington, who is totally
blind, told me that I might close my
eyes but would still not enter the
world he lives in since my "mind's
eye" would still see the scene. He
does not live in a picture that's been
temporarily obscured. For him the
balance of his senses has shifted
toward attention to touch, sound,
smell, and memory.
Bruce Curtis lost the use of his
legs, and hands partially, to his
spinal cord injury at 17. Since then,
his wheelchair has become an exten-
sion of his body. (He's definitely not
"confined" by it.) If I say, "Wheels
have replaced his legs and feet," that
seems obvious yet far too simple.
202 CQ/CJ Sourcebook
Rather, his body has become dif-
ferently shaped because it integrates
his chair with his flesh and bones so
that his movement is an ever-varying
combination of the capabilities of
both.
In dancing with Bruce, I was the
one learning to move. I couldn't res-
pond in habitual ways-weight and
balance were different. For instance,
"taking weight" in an able-bodied
duet often involves supporting the
other's weight through spine, legs,
and feet. However, in one duet with
Bruce, I felt "taking weight" as a
balancing in which our arms were
like the spokes of a wheel transmit-
ting weight back and forth between
me as the hub, and him as the rim,
our weights alternately stretched
away then compressed towards each
other. Bruce might lean/ extend into
me in such a way that my weight,
anchored through the floor, would
provide a resistant center which he
could alternately push off from or be
pulled in by, creating a momentum
and continuous change of direction
that rolled his chair around me.
Gravity seemed to act horizontally as
he pivoted around me. No hands
were required to turn the wheels of
his chair, the spiraling thrust created
was the almost effortless product of
the lateral interplay of our weights.
(The image of planets orbiting each
other comes to mind.) Seen from the
outside, one might think that the
able-bodied person was always in-
itiating, but Bruce said his partner's
active weight supported him by pro-
viding a resistance he could work off
of, amplifying his initial impulses so
that his range of movement was ex-
tended. In fact, our dance was
unrestrained by his chair whether he
was moving in it or carrying the duet
out of it to the floor.
Emery Blackwell cannot walk or
feed himself. When I helped him at
the Breitenbush Jam my hands were
momentarily his: when they fed him,
while brushing his teeth, while clean-
ing up after being on the
toilet. .. standing or sitting was a
game of balance and support with
me or some well-anchored object.
Yet he bicycles 30-60 miles a day,
alone. He talks of his dance of get-
ting into and out of the bathtub,
dancing his way out of bed, into the
bathroom. He has a joy in moving
that I realize I've long given up to
the habitual shorthand of getting
from here to there in the world. He
also dances for audiences-solo, and
in a remarkable partnership with
Alito Alessi, continually dancing
through what I'd imagined to be his
limits. He bought ballet shoes for
feet which, although they will not
support his weight, have been
poetically aligned by his cerebral
palsy into a permanent en pointe
position. He is a truly graceful,
whole human.
Dancing in EMPATHY, what does
it mean? Bruce told me a story, say-
ing, "Suppose I drop a coin on the
ground. Now, it usually takes me
longer to pick it up than an
able-bodied person and it's curious
to see if someone will pick it up
before I do. There is a certain
amount of time which society allots
to a given task and if I take longer
than that I am seen to be having a
problem." He's not. Long ago he
accepted his own frame of reference.
Time has become very personal. He's
not having any trouble but able-bod-
ied people often don't see that. Their
discomfort sets in when, "it takes
him too long" and sympathetically,
sometimes with pity, the coin is pick-
ed up so that he doesn't have to
"work so hard at it." Bruce said he
does appreciate help, but it almost
always comes with that attendant in-
sistence on the able-bodied frame of
reference. Sometimes that means he's
been judged, subconsciously or not,
to be less than competent.
Perhaps he's seen to be in physical
pain? For a short while I was captive
of those feelings, even when I knew
they had no basis in fact. In my
case, the pain and fear associated
with old, and temporarily-very-disab-
ling skiing injuries were being dredg-
ed up, leading to momentary irra-
tionalities like, "He is in pain, and
unhappy. It's temporary, he'll
heal ... " Or, sometimes emotions
can link disabilities with aging and
dying (I've had a close friend and
parent die recently) and so sadness
and grief, as well as fears around the
loss of physical functioning, can in-
appropriately become attached to
disabled people. It's awkward when
Volume16 1991
photo: Rochus Schmuecker
Gerry Overington getting a ride with
Emery Blackwell in Eugene, Oregon.
the experience of the different-
ly-abled is felt through an able-bod-
ied filter, but it's not surprising. We
spend so much time and attention
dancing around our own discomfort.
So to dance in empathy is to
dance in the frame of reference of
one's differently-abled partner, mov-
ing on equal terms ... which is true
of contact improvisation in general
but now one stretches into new ter-
ritory (as Chinese or Arabic are a
much greater stretch for our English
ears and tongues than German or
Spanish).
Sometimes the contact of the im-
provisation is other than touch.
Other times conflicting touch im-
pulses are felt. Emery told me that
an able-bodied person bends an arm
by signalling muscles to contract
while simultaneously suppressing
those signals to muscles opposing
that movement, but the area of his
brain which controls the "suppres-
sion" function has been damaged by
cerebral palsy so both bending and
straightening muscles contract at the
same time. It's a standoff that he
can overcome by conscious thought
but there's often a "lag time" of 30
seconds or more which, even then, is
accompanied by the jerkiness of con-
flicting muscle messages. In dancing
Volume 16 1991
with him, I found I had a tendency
to arbitrarily choose to follow one
impulse or, conversely, none. Either
way, I was stuck in my own perspec-
tive, doing my own dance, drowning
out Emery's initiatives. Waiting with
Emery meant operating in his time,
listening for his intention to sift
through his nervous system, allowing
my senses to shift into line with his
without losing my own center. Danc-
ing with that lack of knowing was
differently-dancing, since for those
dances I was living as if all muscle
movements required conscious in-
tervention. I felt the awkwardness
like a never-ending struggle to
juggle.
Rarely have I been so conscious of
the body as the container of the
spirit. But then that's the content of
these dances: the movement of the
human spirit transcending the con-
tainer it's been given by birth or
chance. In contact improvisation,
both movers are extending through
limits, not the least of which are the
limits of empathy ... going into
another world and allowing another
into one's own. Enabling each other
to dance with those risks. *
TOM GIEBINK works as a sculptor,
painter, performer, and video artist in
Austin, Texas where he is currently
organizing a DanceAbi/ity workshop with
Mary Ganzon to take place hopefully this
coming fall. He spent 6 years on the
alpine ski race circuit until he moved to
Austin where there's no snow, but there
is Contact.
A QUESTION OF
PERFORMANCE
Some questions I brought to
the performance portion [of the
Breitenbush Jam experience in
Oregon, specifically the post-Jam
performances in Eugene] were never
addressed and are still burning a
groove in my brain. What is the
criteria for deciding to perform, and
do we have a responsibility to the
audience to give our best? I was very
happy to see that everyone who
wanted to got to perform. There was
no sense of elitism, or judging a per-
son's worthiness to perform. But I
think it would serve us all to help
each other bring what is unique and
special about us to the stage.
At 44, I think of myself more as a
mover than a dancer. 'Performance
artist' is a convenient term
nowadays. I think of a dancer as so-
meone who spends a lot of time dan-
cing, practicing, focussing on their
body as an instrument of expression.
As a performer, I try to focus on the
areas in which I can offer something
unique, something that makes a con-
tribution. For people with limited
dance training and limited contact
experience to perform in either of
these areas doesn't seem to make
sense, for sure to the audience, and
probably not for the performers. My
wife, who has a strong ballet
background, found it hard to relate
to most of the performances. She
was reminded of when she was a lit-
tle girl, and the kids on the block
would put skits together, and the
parents would watch. I guess this is
fine, if the audience feels related
enough to the performer that they
really don't care what they do; they
just love seeing their friends on
stage. What is the criteria for
deciding to perform, and do we have
a responsibility to the audience to
give our best?
Scheduling only one day [at the
end of the Breitenbush Jam] for a
Performance Conference seems an
anomaly to me. I think it would
make more sense to have the jam on
the weekend, and have the con-
ference last much longer. There was
barely enough time to figure out
what we wanted to do, and there
was little if any time to take advan-
tage of the people present who spend
their lives as performers. The range
of commitment to performing varied
greatly. Should anyone be allowed to
come to the Performance Con-
ference? It was nice to feel like we
were all kindred spirits, but the day
seemed identical to me to many im-
provisational workshops that are of-
fered to the general public. I missed
being able to pick the brains of Steve
[Paxton] and Nancy [Stark Smith]
and Alito [Alessi] and Karen
[Nelson]. It would be interesting to
know why we all came to the Perfor-
mance Conference. I have the sense
that it's partially an ego thing. I get
to go home and say I'm a per-
former, I get to make believe I'm of
the same caliber as people who focus
their lives on performing. I get to
avoid all the deeper questions, all the
demons that squelch my creative
drive the other 51 weeks of the year.
Namaste,
John William Johnson
Portland, Oregon
CQ/CI Sourcebook 2IB
Nevon Clark and Lisa Gottlieb-Clark
BIRTH DANCE
Birthing my baby boy was the essence of a contact
dance. For me, the key to a satisfying improvisational
dance means abandoning my analytic mind and embracing
the center of my intuitive self. This intuitive center grew
in size and strength through my pregnancy, and bloomed
as my labor began. I trusted my body in the dance of bir-
thing, and my body freely opened.
In this age of high-tech births, in cold, sterile hospitals,
my home birth was instead a celebration of spirit and
nature.
I danced my baby out into this world, and he danced
with me. Now, when we hold each other and move
together through our days and nights I feel the continua-
tion of our original improv on the day he was born.
Lisa Gottlieb-Clark
LISA GOTTLIEB-CLARK: "I live in Ann Arbor, Michigan with
my husband Ed, my daughter Zoey and my new baby Nevon. I
· am a visual artist, and also have a part-time body work prac-
tice."

FIRST TOUCH
by Christina Svane
It has been said that gravity is
the expression of Mother Earth's
love for her children. When I im-
agine that hug ending, I feel the hor-
ror of being lost in space, alone.
When my baby had the hiccups in-
side me, I yearned to comfort her,
but how could I hold in my arms
what I already held in my womb?
My blood in her veins, my body her
world-yet I could not say to her, "I
am here. You are not alone." That
which loves us surrounds us yet can-
not contact us; we must contact it.
Perha~s I could say that culture is
the collected effort of our species to
·;nake contact with the unseeable
totality in which we are developing.
It can not pick us up when we cry.
But its blood runs in our veins.
Sometimes, in dreams, we see what
we later will call a face.
I find it tragic that Contact Im-
provisation has so often a
therapeutic effect on a person. That
implies that everyone out there who
isn't experiencing it is probably suf-
fering. That doesn't sound quite
logical. But surely it has spread
because it answered a collective need,
quenched a collective thirst.
I have tasted my own milk and it
is sweet. I cannot remember, but I
imagine, that as a breast-sucking
newborn, I enjoyed nursing not only
Volume 16 1991
for the sweet sustenance it poured in-
to me like sunshine, but equally for
the contact improvisation. We could
call it our first conversational part-
ner, speaking and listening through
the skin of mouth, hands, arms, face
and chest, to a partner who is warm,
responsive, fluid, round and featur-
ing a special point of access. The
circle with the point in the center.
Symbol of the sun, the mother. (In
Tibetan teachings, the solar principle
is feminine-the power to ripen and
sustain life-and the lunar principle
is masculine. It was affirming to see
this interpretation.)
What effect has it had on us to
live in a society in which our most
important symbol has been deemed
obscene? I am not advocating a
"topless" society, though I see
nothing wrong with that concept, so
long as it is left up to each in-
dividual to decide. But I believe in
doing the obvious, ·or at least
recognizing it, and it is obvious that
the human body is a long way yet
from being liberated. Especially the
body of the mother, once worship-
ped, now persecuted through taboos,
harsh birth practices, a medical
establishment that discourages nurs-
ing, and ignores the fact that skin-to-
skin contact with the baby is the key
to its well-being and development.
In accepting the Nobel Prize, and
again in addressing the Italian
Government this spring, His Holiness
the Dalai Lama of Tibet reminded
his audience that every member of
photos by Janet L. Cox
the human race is born free of
religion or culture, but shares univer-
sally with all human babies the need
for affection. Physical contact with
the mother is the single most impor-
tant factor in the well-being of the
newborn for the first weeks of life,
he said. It reaffirmed my feeling that
this is the topic of the hour. If we
allow the generation being born now
to grow up with their need for con-
tact satisfied instead of scorned, the
problems of the world may be in-
herited by sane and compassionate
people who have the natural ability
to do the obvious.
I wonder how the contact com-
munity can assist in the process of
restoring to childhood the possibility
of communicating with parents or
others through touch-a language
already functioning at birth, years
before speech is perfected. Perhaps
the goal of Contact Improvisation
should be to render itself obsolete.
My hope is to stimulate dialogue
in "Still Moving" regarding the
language of touch between parent
and infant. I would like to hear ac-
counts of what you've been doing
privately and/ or publicly with this
relationship. *
CHRISTINA SV ANE was one of the
founders of Movement Research, Inc. in
NYC. She recently returned with her
newborn girl to Vermont after 2 years
teaching dance at the Theaterschool in
Amsterdam, Holland.
CQ/CI Sourcebook 205
STILL MOVING (cont'd)
On Watching "Continuing Contact"
Oberlin College Dance Company Concert
Spring, 1990
Tumbling socks ... That is what the performance of
Continuing Contact brought to mind during the concert.
The eight dancers appeared in loose white costumes and
took turns bumping each other out of duets. Have you
ever sat and watched your clean clothes rise and fall
through the spin of the drying cycle? A pair of white
socks thrown into a dark load will stand out vividly and
will look like they are tumbling alone in the dark. I can
easily unf ocus my eyes from the looping pattern and con-
centrate on the intimate movement of socks in contact
with each other. The socks wrap, roll and slide across
each other. Theirs is a continuous falling and rising.
There is no "muscling it" in this duet, the socks rise in
response to their incredible momentum. The dancers on
stage also did a duet of wrapping, falling, sliding, rising.
There was continuous motion; none of the moments of
contact resulted in weight colliding and ending in dead
stops. Nothing ever remains stationary in the dryer either.
Kim Monis
When the dancing began I was absorbed in the details;
where the point of contact was, where it was traveling
and how; placement of weight. Soon, I felt m)'self
responding in, as they call it, a kinesthetic way, feeling
physical empathy with the movement. Yet I, for the first
time so removed from the dancers, was deeply moved by
the spontaneity of their responses to each other; the in-
finite variety, and the incredible (and necessary) sensitivity
of those responses. I had not been separated enough from
it before to see the miraculousness of these movements in
terms of just daily human interaction.
SylviaChwe
Watching Contact and doing Contact are experiences
further apart than most audience/performer relationships.
Contact is like Pop Rocks. If you just watch surface ac-
tivity, it's difficult to understand what's going on. Get
Close, pay attention; maybe you'll hear it crackle. But to
really experience it, you've got to pop it in your mouth
and feel the explosions.
Betsy Cutler

"Continuing Contact" was a performance Ann Cooper Albright directed with her students at Oberlin College, Spring 1990. Using
contact improvisation, it was danced on a proscenium stage in front of a backdrop of enlarged 'hieroglyphs'.
The performance frame in which contact improvisation
was danced in the ODC concert trivialized this movement
form. On a proscenium stage, the Continuing Contact
dancers were formally separated from the audience,
removing the personability and the three-dimensionality
available at closer viewing. Unlike a regular jam, the
dancers wore white costumes which made it difficult to
distinguish between bodies and hence watch the par-
ticulars of what one body was doing, save dancing solo,
at any particular time ... The audience found the dancing
amusing if not hilarious and I became increasingly more
uncomfortable as the people around me laughed harder.
What for me is a beautiful and moving dance form had
become a comedy act.
Chloe Stamas

Volume16 1991
CONFESSIONS OF"A LAYMAN DANCER
Dedicated to Anyone Who Ever Danced With Me
by Barry Kirsh
Would you pass up free tickets
to an ice hockey game at the Forum
in Montreal? Seats at ice level, with
the Canadiens playing the defending
Stanley Cup Champion Calgary
Flames? I did. And my father was
baffled when I declined his invita-
tion. I'm sure he was shaking his
head at the other end of the
telephone line and wondering about
me.
Don't misunderstand. I love
hockey. But, on Wednesday nights
this autumn, I have been attending
dance classes. In fact I have been
dancing Contact Improvisation on a
once weekly basis for the better part
of 4 years now. My father, on the
other hand, has been bowling every
Tuesday night for the last 40 years.
Yet, he would grab the hockey
tickets at a moment's notice. Why
didn't I? Could it be that 'the World
of Dance' has occasioned more for
me than bowling has for my father?
Three and four years ago I
remember that the dancers talked a
lot about technique. Many of us in
Contact courses and occasional
weekerid workshops wanted to im-
prove our skills. I felt that if I could
stand on my hands and flip over
someone else's back, then I would
improve tremendously as a Contact
Dancer. Or at least be in a position
to register for a new course entitled,
"Flying," an eventual step from
Contaet Dance, I believed.
Luckily Montreal is the home of
several fine Contact Dance teachers.
Andrew Harwood, the leader in
three workshops I have attended, has
helped me. My feeble attempts to
walk on my hands were not ridicul-
ed. Instead, my immediate needs
were recognized. He suggested poetic
polarities to frame my precarious
perches. We pulled for the earth and
pushed to the sky. We differentiated
shadows amidst images of
light/dark. With weight/lightness, we
intimated the profundity of Being
and Nothingness it seemed, as I grew
into my body more fully. The poetry
did assuage my fears of disorienta-
tion. Though I still had to accept the
reality of lurching acrobatic postures
and an instinctual begging for form.
I kept on. Contact Dance became
a paradox to me. It intrigued me as
a mystical endeavour in the guise of
physical movement. It was exciting
that people around me seemed
uninhibited about their bodies. So
spiritual communication through
bodily movement became the enigma
I sought to express each time I danc-
ed. To hell with handstands! If I
could listen to the impulse, the magic
of Contact might glow in the intense
corporeal kinship on the dance floor.
It was better than erotic behavior
because it drew from many more
human emotions. And I took delight
in it.
My weekly course work with
Gurney Bolster confirmed the notion
that concentration was essential. The
long warm-ups, from the slightest
movement, gradually, into the sweep-
ing gestures of the entire body,
heating up and perspiring in solo
dance focussed my energy and
prepared me best to dance with
others. I think I wanted to become a
monk at one point. Cultivating the
meditative poise of a monk would
surely accrue the release of unfet-
tered movement. Then I went home
to my wife and kids to realize that
deliberate action often meant com-
promise. No recluse could I be.
Nevertheless, I wished to continue
my labour of love, regardless of my
certain limitations. But Gurney
became pregnant, and Andrew, like
most of the Contact teachers, taught
exclusively during the day. Spiritual
aspirations could not transcend the
realities of my daily life. I was a
layman whose feet could no longer
step into the rarefied world of dance.
Disappointed, I felt left out. I re-
bounded though. I ran. It was the
only way to move open to me. Run-
ning is movement with power and
grace and I worked on lengthening
my stride. I was also able to keep
fit. My mind, however, longed for
the concentration of dance.
This hiatus from dancing proved
to be a short one. Andrew decided
to offer an evening course in Im-
provisation. I was saved. And in
November, he offered a weekend
workshop in Contact Improvisation.
Volume 16 1991
I was in full swing once more.
The intensity of this last workshop
was refreshing; more, it was truly
creative. I was listening for the
poetic images to cleanse my mind of
disjointed details. The group
dynamic to sweep away personal in-
significance into the greater Zen
mind of truly spontaneous movement
was a beckoning goal. Yet, my ex-
perience that weekend is not readily
defined by poetic or religious im-
agery. The only word to suggest the
new experience is 'simple'.
Movement is simple when it is well
done. I discovered that simple
movements lead to other simple
movements. That kind of obvious
progression yields gravity and fluidi-
ty. A fully sensual presence, in sim-
ple dialogue with the world, occurs
in the best moments of dance.
I still can't do handstands, despite
my evolution and continuing enjoy-
ment of dance. My infrequent so-
journs in dance tempt me to stay
longer and dedicate to the discipline.
Though I'm forced to recognize a
layman's position outside the life cy-
cle of a dancer. Nonetheless, my
awareness of body as soul steadily
deepens. And that I believe is why I
baffled my father. *
BARRY KIRSH teaches high school
drama in Vancouver, B.C. where he
recently moved from Montreal.
photo: Sheri Cohen

STILL MOVING (cont'd)
CONTACT-SKIING
by Tom Giebink
If you imagine the path the Con-
tact point travels across a dancer's
body to be a continuous trail, like an
irregular ball rolling over soft clay,
embossing a bumpy curving track,
you've something of the feel for the
slope the downhill skier follows to
the bottom of the mountain. During
Contact Improvisation, the movers
alternate being "the mountain,"
sometimes very quickly ... creating a
trail, mimicking snowy terrain: over
bumps, sudden drop-offs, twisting
sharply, skittering over ice, floating
in deep powder snow. The Contact
point is the "fall-line," the shortest
path down the mountain according
to gravity but by no means the
shortest distance. The mountain sup-
ports the skier like thermal air cur-
rents do the soaring bird. Skiing is a
controlled fall. Gravity provides
momentum. Muscles are used like
those of birds, to guide rather than
propel, continually shifting the body
into elastic equilibrium between the
lifting push of air and pull of gravi-
ty. Sometimes the mountain drops
away so sharply it's all the skier can
do to react quickly and forcefully
enough to keep up with radically
fluxing terrain. Sometimes the slope
is so gentle that a wide arcing turn
requires delicate balance over edges
of skis that engrave a single curved
line in the snow.
Danger is clearly present, especial-
ly the faster you move ... because
things come at you faster. Or maybe
it's not "things," maybe it's that
space around you changes shape
faster than you can cope with that
change which prompts a survival in-
stinct that car crash survivors
describe as a near-death experience. I
know I survived a few 70 mph
downhill race crashes (no broken
bones, no sprains even!) that I
wouldn't have if time hadn't
somehow stretched out, become
elastic, enough to allow me to move
in ways that saved me from serious
injury. What happens "in the blink
of the eye" to the outside observer is
"more than enough time" to the
person inside the event. I'd name it
catastrophic awareness.
Such an uncanny state of being,
yet it can also exist outside of the
208 CQICI Sourcebook
trauma of impending injury. I found
that out during a downhill race that
has remained tatooed on my psyche,
a continual reminder of what's possi-
ble while moving. On that run, I
knew that I had "more than enough
time" to do whatever was necessary,
sometimes waiting as impatiently as
for an overdue bus lumbering, too
slowly, closer. Yet, at 50-75 mph,
my skis flapped like banana peels, a
clear indicator that a lot of ground
was being covered fast. "More than
enough time to be ready" was not
like film seen slo-motion. At race
speed, the landscape zips by as a
blur of shadowy shapes and bright
edges. It's very abstract. There's a
strong tendency to "hang back,"
trying to "hang on" each moment in
order to sort and bag it in the cor-
rect category of past experiences,
which only leaves awareness "hung
up" like a mail bag at the station as
the train hurtles by. With one's
psyche "stuck," stiff fear tries to
guide the body and a truly dangerous
situation arises. Yet during that par-
ticular downhill race each moment
was seen/sensed sharply ... no blurr-
ing, like extraordinarily fast film
shot at an unheard of shutter speed.
So many "sense photos" felt and let
go of with such quick clarity that it
was easy to deal with each moment
completely. Confusion and panic
were out of the question, so the
moving had a calm easy quickness.
My body seemed so much expand-
ed that I could touch snow twenty
yards downhill. The feeling was one
of being "present" in a lot more
space than the physical body oc-
cupied, and time had to stretch
around it like a balloon being in-
flated. "How much time does it take
to get from here to there?" makes
little sense when there's no separa-
tion between here and there ... so the
farther the "reach," the more space
encompassed by the moment. Maybe
that accounts for my sense of "more
than enough time."
Dancing Contact Improvisation
was the first time, off snow, I've
been able to approach that peculiar
state of awareness. Certainly it has
to do with unpredictability, especially
since Contact lmprov involves "two
mountains that think." The ease, or
lack of it, in dealing with the
unknown has to do, in ski racer
jargon, with the quality of "being
unweighted, or floating." Oddly,
Contact lmprov jargon describes that
sense as "giving your weight," yet
there's no contradiction. Neither
means weight as measured by a
scale, but rather an equalizing of
pressure, skier against mountain,
body to body. No matter the words,
the phrases imply a resilience in
response to the occurrence of an un-
foreseen possibility.
On the level of muscle tissue, I
think of resilience much like Steve
Paxton's description of "snap-
release" (the ability of muscle fiber
to change states, tensed vs. untensed,
quickly). A released muscle is long
and untensed; a relaxed muscle short
and untensed. The released muscle
contracts and an arm, leg, torso lifts,
twists . . . The relaxed muscle con-
tracts to resist compression, or lock
the bones in a structure. Relaxed
muscles allow gravity and momen-
tum to open joints with no work.
Released muscles shorten without
resistance. Just a few basic syllables
in the language of muscle tissue
make for novel choices of body
movement. A zillion bits (like the O's
and 1 's of computer thought) of
response taken as a whole reveal the
limits and shape of the dance.
And so the body is ready to
change shape suddenly, in unex-
pected ways. When a mogul (snow
bump) presses upward sharply, the
body has to compress quickly, absor-
bing the shape of the mountain, then
unfold as the mogul drops away, in
order to equalize the pressure of skis
against snow. Resilience allows any
choice of many how/when/where's
to be executed with an easy
Volume16 1991
quickness. However, to the un-
resilient skier a mogul is a synonym
for catapult and the one choice left
is survival in a wild ride down the
mountain.
There are patterns that make the
terrain feel familiar, yet every run is
singular, even if its the fiftieth down
that trail. Resilience seems to have to
do with paying acute attention to
idiosyncratic inconsistencies with past
experience and'using that awareness
as the impetus to act, rather than in
accord with "what you know is go-
ing to happen." Even in a slalom
course where the "gates" dictate a
tightly structured path, there are an
infinite number of lines through it.
Similarly, my most wonderful
Contact lmprov dances have revealed
that what might become routine or
habitual can be familiar and unique
all at once. Each dance seems to
create a new, big, human psyche.
Bodies link like bones, gravity the
muscle tissue connecting them, ben-
ding and twisting this larger body. It
falls apart, it falls together ... as it
steps and "catches" its momentum
on a common foot. *
TOM GIEBINK spent 6 years on the
national/international alpine ski race cir-
cuit and has found contact improv
remarkably similar in the sense of move-
ment. He works as a painter, sculptor,
video artist, and set designer in Austin,
Texas where he's currently working on an
installation and performance called "The
Anti-Gravity House."
