IN RESPONSE
STILL MOVING
It was in April '86 when I first
saw contact improvisation. Nancy
Stark Smith and Lou Danielson
Stewart were giving a demo [in
Austin, Texas] on the Friday evening
before a weekend workshop with
Nancy. I had an immediate
kinesthetic response and a sensation
of calling. I knew that my
body/mind was curious and longed
to explored this form. It wasn't until
the latter part of '87 that this prac-
tice was part of my life. I was truly
in love with it and was a little in-
credulous when some would say, "I
tried it, but it's not for me," or, "I
have a really hard time watching it."
I knew I hadn't been exposed to
another side of how people have ex-
perienced contact.
Then, in a group conversation at
the dorm during the A Cappella
workshop '89, someone brought up
how people had left the form-for
reasons other than family, other
movement forms, or some seemingly
natural reason. This piqued my
curiosity. My attention at people's
grievances during round robins,
jams, or workshops heightened, not
because I wanted to fix anything but
I just wanted to know. So, I have
really appreciated Martin Keogh's
and Kirk Andrews' "What's in the
Bag: Issues that lie in the shadow of
contact improvisation,'' ("Still Mov-
ing," CQ Vol. 15:1, Winter 90) and
Mark Pritchard's "My Rise and Fall
in Contact Improv" (CQ Vol. 15:3,
Fall 90). They have really broadened
my vocabulary of perception,
understanding, and allowance. I am
realistic enough to know that un-
criticized consensus is a sign of nar-
row vision and listening, not to men-
tion impending blocks.
Thank you everybody for the con-
tributions to "Still Moving"-for the
ones with continuing communities
and for those building ones. There
seems to be a common thread to
bridge and breach a loneliness. I am
happy to report on our own contact
group. We had our first all-day jam
this summer. [See "Newsletter" for
report.]
Mary Ganzon
Austin, Texas
I would like to comment about
Diane Elliot's article in the "Still
Moving" section-"Making Safe
Contact" /CQ Vol. 15:2,
Spring/Summer 90].
I was moved by her capacity to
put down the ongoing difficulties,
reflections, situations, conclusions,
etc. of the work. That was great,
because just recently in a workshop
in May [in Sao Paulo, Brasil) I felt
good about teaching, I mean in an
ordering way, a kind of organizing
in my mind together with people's
time or timing how to introduce con-
tact and all of its interaction with
energy, physics, dance .. , but it's still
so fragile that I'm not able to reflect
clearly, or even get at any conclu-
sion. It's like a necessary step of the
work and I'm in a very good
humour to at least recognize a subtle
change and understanding of my
teaching. Let's see: people here have
never seen the work of contact; to
feel better when putting together a
small woman with a big man or to
allow the men to be together at
another time or to let them choose
by themselves; to work only on the
floor for a few minutes or few days,
why? ... It's more intuition than
knowing. Well! Let's say it's not so
complicated, but on the other hand,
it is, otherwise I can't know where I
am, and it's hard for students to get
somewhere. I also feel that you have
to teach & practice a few movement
skills just like any language so you
have a minimum to be able to work
with and to allow the dance & prin-
ciples to come out and to develop by
themselves.
I think Elliot's right when she says
that the great thing is to create an
atmosphere for the work, (but at the
same time not to feel responsible or
guilty if things work or not), but to
create an atmosphere I need to be
calm. It means that I can be lost,
but if I'm not calm in my heart and
mind it's not easy. It's about having
love, to be in love and with love, but
love is not enough if I'm not clear
about what I want to do, to give, to
receive, to look for or NOT.
The way that she used to get her
experiences down on paper helped
me a lot. I've also appreciated her
openness and courage to put out and
share with people her fears and
angers.
Tica Lemos
Brasilia, Brasil

SHELF
LIFE
Sharing the Dance:
Contact Improvisation and American Culture.
Cynthia J. Novack. Madison, Wisconsin: The
University of Wisconsin Press, 1990. 114 North
Murray Street, Madison, Wisconsin 53715.
Sharing the Dance, by Cynthia J. Novack, is subtitl-
ed Contact Improvisation and American Culture. She has
posed her deep investigation of contact improvisation as a
lens through which she shapes her view of American
culture from 1960 onward. She holds that we can have a
perspective on what we do in contact improvisation by
contrasting it with other political and dance phenomena
from the period, and by measuring contact history with
cycles of other cultural events which are familiar to us-
the "demise" of the 1960s counterculture, the effect of an
affluent economy turning meager, the flings of youth
leading to more measured steps.
Cynthia J. Novack is an anthropologist, dancer/chor-
eographer and teacher. With striking intelligence and pa-
tience, she writes from all these perspectives in this book.
To save you any inconvenience, I quote the blurb on
the back cover of the book: " ... The book examines the
ways in which contact improvisers (and their surrounding
communities) encoded sexuality, spontaneity, and gender
roles, as well as concepts of the self and society in their
dancing. While focusing on the changing pr_actice of con-
tact improvisation through two decades of social transfor-
mation, Novack's work incorporates the history of rock
dancing and disco, the modern and experimental dance
movements of Merce Cunningham, Anna Halprin, and
Judson Church, among others, and a variety of other
physical activities, such as martial arts, aerobics, and
~rest ling.
"Novack concludes her study with a discussion of con-
tact improvisation both as an icon of the "end of the six-
ties" and as an ongoing dance practice which continues to
demonstrate the subtle ways in which movement
represents and creates culture."
Steve Paxton

Roll. Gerry Overington.
by Steve Paxton
Rolling on the floor provides a way to examine
structural forms in the body.
An example of such a form lies in the intertwining
spirals noticed in the play of left and right and forward
and back basic to normal walking.
photos by Kate Mount
Volume 16 1991
Right-left walk. Steve Paxton.
CQICI Sourcebook 211
(cont'd)
If you take a step and continue the oppositions in the
body to an extreme, you can see two spirals circling
around each other.
And if the next step is taken to an extreme, the spirals
reverse.
Left-right walk. Steve Paxton.
Right side moving around support of the left. Left side moving around support of the right.
212 CQ/Cl Sourcebook Volwne 16 1991
Helix legs. Gerry Overington.
When this form is taken to the floor the energy of the
extreme twist of the body promotes a coil which can be
rolled with no apparent effort. This is done with the
spiral initiated either at the top with the hands and arms,
or with the toes, feet, and legs at the bottom.
In these spiral rolls, a single initiation of energy flows
into the most extreme spiral the body can achieve and
the roll continues as long as the shape is maintained. The
movement stems from changes of weight inside the body
as different surfaces of the spirals are rolled upon.
The less extreme alternating helixes of walking also
permit movement initiation from different places in the
body, but in practice just a few such options are
integrated into an habitual walk. The other options wither
with disuse. Turning this basic form onto its side causes
many parts of the body to extend and take responsibility
for the roll.
Helix arms. Steve Paxton.
Volume 16 1991
CQ/CI Sourcebook 213
(cont'd)
Crescent roll: head lifted. Steve Paxton.
Head and feet lifted.
Middle lifted.
214 CQ/CI Sourcebook
Crescent rolls come in 5 varieties.
The premise: a body shaped like a
banana, arced from top to toe, and
maintaining this exterior shape
relative to gravity while rolling along
the floor. In this form the left and
right sides of the body stay closer to
parallel than in the previous spiral
form. The rolling choices are: head
lifted, head and feet lifted, middle
lifted. And two in which the whole
body is stretched along the floor:
rolling toward the points of the arc
or toward the bend of it.
These rolls are viewed as a set
because of their external form; the
coordinations for their execution are
different as the relationship of the
shape to gravity changes. The first
roll provides a good stretching for
the short muscles at the joining of
the sides of the pelvis to the upper
torso, and a good stretch for the
abdominals. The arms are used on
the floor, so great strength is not
needed in the lower back. The
second and third rolls are
outstanding whole-body toners, and
are fairly advanced work,
muscularly. The last two were
spotted in the dance works of
Simone Forti, the American
choreographer, and don't require as
much strength, but provide one of
the unusual coordination problems
in the annals of human locomotion.
Volwne 16 1991
Two moments in the 'Simone Forti' rolls. If coming forward, the roll is toward the points.
If moving backward, toward the bend.

CQ/CI Sourcebook 215
(cont'd)
Exploring discs.
Teaching by to11ch. Gerry Overington & Steve Paxton.
This article is excerpted from a forthcoming writing by
Touchdown Dance, a project working for extended mobility for
the blind, directed by Steve Paxton and Ann Kilcoyne, Devon,
England.
216 CQJCI Sourcebook
Exploring form in these nine rolls
has several advantages. Fear of
falling does not interfere with
learning. The sensations found in the
exercise are unusual: we rarely
notice these singular forms in our
daily movement, but in these rolls
we can hardly miss the basic
forms-the helix and the turning
crescent.
These forms give the spine itself a
workout, as well as the muscles and
attachments of the spine. The point
is that there are sensations in the
body of the spine itself, in the discs.
This rather dull lot of feelings along
the length of the spine can best be
identified in the lumbar, where a
vague pinching sensation may
accompany the backbend.
Once the quality of sensation in
the spine is found, it can be noticed
in the course of the rolls. It then
begins to make new sense to speak
of 'the spine'. A prerequisite to this
discovery is relaxation, as tension
masks sensation and maintaining it
requires wasted effort. Once the
feeling in the spine has been
located, one can more accurately
stretch the circling arms through the
upper spine at the center of the
back, or align points on the spine to
discover new postures.
Sighted people start to learn these
rolls by seeing them. For those
having difficulty in translating what
they've seen into what to do, hands-
on is most useful, with verbal aids.
Blind people I work with learn the
rolls by feeling with their hands and
are further assisted by hands-on and
verbal aids. When the blind then
direct the efforts of the sighted, they
tend to teach by hand. They convey
the principles, and the sighted
person can visualize what is meant.
In teaching, one can transmit the
basic spiral forms visually, verbally,
and kinesthetically. Each of these
transmissions is effective, but touch
is the keystone of the work. *

Volume 17 1992
EDITOR NOTE
CROSSING THE GREAT DIVIDES
The first time I experienced Ishmael
Houston-Jones's score, ''The Politics of
Dancing," was in Berlin at the 1988 Euro-
pean Contact Teachers' Conference. In his
afternoon workshop by the same name, he
asked us to imagine a railroad track run-
ning diagonally through the room from
comer to comer, dividing the room in half.
Gathering in the middle of the room upon
the invisible track, the group met for in-
structions: "All those people who have
both parents alive, form a group on the
right side of the track; all those who do not
have both parents alive, form a group on
the other side." To the image, he added a
fast approaching train and with it the need
to make a quick decision, one side or the
other, which way to go.
Once we were divided, Ishmael said,
"Look at your group." Eyes to eyes, eyes
to bodies, eyes to floor. Eyes certain, eyes
shy, eyes teary, eyes wide. After some
minutes he said, "Now tum and look at the
other group." Across the length of the stu-
dio, across the invisible track, we look at
"the others." What we see: how many of
them there are, who's there, the visual de-
sign of the group-heights, colors, postures;
mood. What we feel: anything, everything.
We are nervous, proud, curious, bored,
uncomfortable, sad, amused. It depends of
course on the categocy.
After several more minutes of looking
at the other group without talking (the
whole exercise was done in silence), we
are asked to move towards each other
slowly, to miX again in the middle and
wait for the next instruction: "All those
who were raised in a Jewish household, go
to the right side; all those who were not,
go to the other side."
Some categories were easier to respond
to than others, either because the answer
was indisputable or the subject less
charged. Sensitivities varied greatly from
person to person, and subject to subject,
whether questions of age (under 35/ not
under 35); social/sexual issues (homosexu-
al/ not homosexual; living with a mate/
not living with ... ); self-image (those who
consider themselves above-average intelli-
gence/ those who do not consider them-
selves ... ); money (those who make above
$20,000 a year/ those who do not ... );
politics (those who voted in the last
election ... ).
As I remember, we basically stuck to
the score in Berlin. There was, however,
always recourse in interpretation: some
people decided to lie, or at least play "the
facts" in their own way; other people ago-
nized over every decision, tcying to be
Volume 17 1992
absolutely honest: did having had an abor-
tion place you with ''those who have had
children" or "those who have not"?; and
then there were people who chose to get
run over by the train rather than make an
impossible decision or acknowledge an
unacceptable categorization.
''The Politics of Dancing" came back
around last summer when it was led by
Daniel Lepkoff, an old friend and former
CQllaborator of Ishmael's, as part of a
"group morning" at the A Cappella Motion
workshop in Northampton, Mass. The
exercise was conducted, as before, in a
very straight-forward manner-the instruc-
tions simple and clear, the categories rang-
ing from mildly to extremely sensitive.
This time, the response was stronger than I
remembered: more soul searching, people
crying, and more people choosing to stay
on the track, even for questions like "those
who are women on this side, those who are
not women on the other side." Gradually,
new spacial categories of response were
invented as people placed themselves in
the room on a continuum between the two
extremes, until on some questions the
group was completely scattered.from one
end of the room to the other rather than
divided into two distinct groups. Emotions
ran high, bonding among participants
strong, and by the end we were all a bit
dazed.
I remember going through a range of
emotions as the categories came and went
and I dutifully plumbed my depths for the
truest answers. Among my feelings were
suspicion and irritation, as I wondered
what made this The Politics of Dancing,
what this had to do with dancing at all,
and what it had to do with me. I went
away from the exercise stirred up and
confused, my consciousness obviously
raised, or at least on edge.
I am remindoo by this writing that I
have always loved facts about people. I
often like to ask workshop participants for
a fact about themselves. Anything: I ate
an English muffin this morning; I like to
play golf; I'm grieving the death of my
boyfriend; I learned something new about
leverage in my last dance. I don't exactly
know what it is about these details, the
particulars of their lives, that moves me. I
do know they add a dimension that wasn't
there before.
And what exactly did we mean by an
issue of CQ about "dancing with different
populations"? What does "different"
mean? If Ishmael had said, "All those who
are different go to this side, all those who
are not different go to the other side" who
would have gone where and why?
Common denominators among people
are many, yet differences remain. They
help to distinguish one thing from another,
provide contrast, and with it, dimension.
Using movement to communicate across
boundaries-cultural, age, experience, gen-
der, etc.-is a challenge that can teach us a
great deal, about ourselves, each other, the
nature of nature, and the power and com-
plexity of the language of movement.
Overcoming prejudice, ignorance, and fear
through dancing 'with "other" people is
awkward, outrageous, liberating and can be
quite a lot of fun.
There seems to be a first flush (does it
continue?) that accompanies this opening
to another person, where the reservoir of
human compassion and humor is tapped
and we are deeply moved by an unimpeded
flow of-is it love?-to someone once dis-
tanced from us by definitions and circum-
stance. At these moments we might feel
we are truly dancing beyond ourselves.
I honor and have felt this effusion and
glow. And now I wonder what will hap-
pen next, as the novelty of crossing borders
gives way to other curiosities aroused once
inside the new territocy. What will be
found as we continue to make our way
through this complex world, deciding to
tum right or left, following our instincts
and desires into the dancing we love the
most?
Nancy Stark Smith
CQ/CI Sourcebook Zl.7
GIANT STEPS
Contacting Children with Neuro-lntegrative Dysfunction
Judy Stone
From September 1989 until June
1990, I taught contact improvisation at
Giant Steps, the Canadian Institute for
Neuro-lntegrative Development. This
Montreal school is known throughout
North America for its innovative ap-
proach. The director, Darlene Beringer,
is open to many new ideas and was
daring enough to include contact dance
as part of the school curriculum. To this
end, Ms. Beringer assembled a team of
three experienced contact dancers: Ian
Talbot, who had been working at the
school for several years, Marie-Renee
Tremblay, and myself, Judy Stone. In
December, Ian was obligated to leave
because of other commitments, and he
was replaced by Fiona Meyer.
Giant Steps services children between
the ages of 4 and 13 with developmental
delay, sensory and motor dysfunction,
aphasia, autism, apraxia, genetic disor-
ders and specific syndromes.
Techniques of synchronization and
neuro-integrative patterning used in mu-
sic therapy and occupational therapy
form the basis of the school's approach.
Ms. Beringer recognized that contact
improvisation uses both vestibular move-
ment patterns and proprioception within
an improvised context that is readily
adaptable to individual needs and
abilities.
We began by establishing a rapport
with our students using massage, manip-
ulation and simple weight exchanges.
For the first few weeks we paired each
child with an adult As the months
passed and comfort levels increased, the
children worked with each other more
and more. Our sessions were structured
much like an adult's contact class with a
warm-up followed by directed exercises
around a theme (falling, rolling, support-
ing weight, following a point of contact),
then dancing (duets, round robins, solos),
and ending with a warm down.
Sixty children came to us in groups
numbering from three to nine. Each
group had very different abilities and we
adapted the classes accordingly. Two
adults would demonstrate an exercise
while the children watched. Some were
aided by our simultaneous verbal de-
scriptions while others could imitate our
actions without necessarily understanding
our words. Levels of verbal and physical
prompting that were necessary also
varied immensely.
There were enthusiastic students who
quickly took the exercises and made
them into their own dances with intriguing
variations. Even the children who need-
ed to be guided through each step ap-
peared to enjoy the new movement sen-
sations. Many children responded to
concrete imagery. We gave names and
a little story to each exercise (see exam-
ples). When we began to have round
robins, children would at times lose focus
and we would use the names of exercis-
es as anchors to recall their attention to
the dance. "Look at Emilie, she's being
a rock. How can you dance with that
rock?" "It looks like Francois is being a
house. Can you be a snake and slither
through his house?" These movement
suggestions helped to overcome the
impasses of short attention spans, moti-
vational lapses or perceptual disorgan-
ization.
Everyone involved in our dance pro-
gram was surprised and delighted by the
progress which the students made.
Children who were initially quite tactile-
phobic began to relish the physical con-
tact that they received in our class.
Passive or withdrawn children were soon
initiating contact and by the end of the
year joyous dances filled with mutual
respect and recognition developed be-
tween children who had previously shown
little interest in each other.
I myself was amazed at the wide vari-
ety of positive learning experiences that
came about through introducing the
children to contact improvisation. This
dance form has been an incredibly effec-
tive tool in reaching these sometimes
unreachable children.
A SAMPLE OF EXERCIS~
THE ROCK
One child would be the support, or
"rock," on hands and knees. Her partner
would find various ways to give weight.
We would offer suggestions such as:
sitting or lying on the rock; sliding off the
rock; being snow melting on the rock;
finding various ways to be on or near the
rock as it moved about the room; finding
ways to be supported by two rocks;
somersaulting from a rock then crawling
underneath your partner to become the
support for her.
Exercises would usually be introduced
with an adult partner, the adult in this
case being first the rock, then the weight
giver. When the children became com-
forta.ble giving and taking weight they
would then work with each other.
Judy Stone with students at Giant Steps.
218 CQ/CI Sourcebook
Volume17 1992
FOOT DANCE BRIDGES WITH
SNAKES
We did dances with different body
parts. Foot dances were a favorite that
children at any level could do. Snakes
would slither under a roomful of wavering
bridges.
THE RAIN MASSAGE
Fingers and loose fists were gentle
raindrops falling on the backs of children
resting on the floor. The teachers in the
school who gave so much to the children
were pleasantly surprised to be receiving
relaxing massages from their charges.
The children also enjoyed massaging
each other.
THE CHAIR
One child lies on their back, knees
over chest, feet up, forming a chair which
gently pushes into the air anyone daring
enough to sit upon it. Variations: leaning
against the chair with different body
parts. This exercise brought smiles to
the often impassive faces of severely
autistic children.
THE MONKEY IN THE TREE
A monkey climbs a human tree in
search of imaginary bananas. Many
earthbound children were motivated to
climb to unexpected heights.
THE HOUSE
Several children join together forming
a multi-level structure. Others take tums
entering, exiting and otherwise exploring
the house.*
If you have any questions or would like to
share your work with us please write or call:
Judy Stone, 4039 Des Erables, Montreal, PQ,
H2K 3V7, Canada; (514) 524-0280.
Volume 17 1992
A CONTACT PROJECT WITH YOUNG CHILDREN
Lauren Shapiro Raker
Improvisation is the most natural
form of movement for the young child
who is beginning to dance. They move
freely through suggestions of various
images ... readily becoming the "butterfly,"
"cloud," or "tiger" that is suggested.
Throughout my twenty years of teaching
experience with young children, I have
employed improvisation as a method to
enhance the children's modes of expres-
sion. Although they occasionally made
contact with another student (partner)
during the course of an exercise, it often
seemed uncomfortable, or took on the
appearance of social dance styles. The
movement then became more contrived
and less free.
Five years ago, I began to introduce
contact improvisation to the children. We
began gradually with very specific rules.
What evolved were some very elaborate
and interesting shapes, which we dubbed
"Pretzel" dances. The children loved the
procedure as well as the results. We
have continued to evolve these dances
over the years by adding and eliminating
the rules of design.
In the most basic form, a group of
youngsters takes independent space on
the floor. They must be close enough to
the other students to touch them. Upon
hearing certain musical cues, i.e., the
beat of a drum, they must contact a
neighbor with any one part of their body.
Upon hearing the second cue, they make
contact with another child or the original
child using a differen' part of their body.
This method continues until the children
are entwined in an elaborate design.
The design usually breaks apart with a
final musical cue (i.e., the ping of a trian-
gle), and then the group is ready to begin
a new design. Very often when I watch
their pieces evolve, it seems that I am
viewing them through a giant kaleido-
scope.
Recently we attempted a new version
of our contact "game." I decided to ex-
plore the use of a basic notation method.
A pattern might be written like this:
START: 1, 2, 3, ... 1, 4, 3, ... 2, 0, 2, END.
These numbers indicated how many
parts of their bodies should make contact
with another dancer or dancers. By
writing this series of numbers on the wall,
the children viewed them as they moved.
The contacts flowed more smoothly, and
the transitions occurred at the individual
pace of each child. They no longer only
made contact at the specific beat of a
drum. The results were less entangled
and intertwined, but far more interesting
and sophisticated.
The final step of these contact projects
at this point, has been to add our own
music. Using simple percussion instru-
ments, the children take turns being the
musicians and the dancers.
Although there seems to be a rigid
formality to the method, it appears to
have captured even the most reluctant
and inhibited of the children to participate
in the creation of joyful and creative
design.*
Students in Contact Project at Town School, NYC.

A COMMON SENSE

220 CQICI Sourcebook
by Anne Kilcoyne & Steve Paxton
Peter is big and burly and in his
fifties. He has virtually no sight. having
been badly injured in a traffic accident
His sight loss was instantaneous and
irreversible. He could no longer work
at his trade; he no longer had an income
and he felt very depressed He came to
the contact improvisation workshop with
some trepidation-he was not "artsy
craftsy" and thought dancers were a
peculiar and probably effeminate breed
However, he thought he would give it a
chance as a friend of his had spoken
very warmly about the workshop.
Having been athletic and interested in
sports when he was sighted, he experi-
enced no difficulty with the work. Over
the five days, he began to enjoy himself,
giving and receiving massage and learn-
ing to build up the skills that are basic
to contact improvisation.
Towards the end of the workshop
Peter was asked to contribute to some
video-based research. At the start of the
workshop, blind participants had been
recorded walking the length of a gymna-
sium, finding a chair, sitting on it. and
rising from it They were asked to go
through the same exercise at the end of
the workshop; thus we are able to re-
cord objective changes in posture and
gait
Peter had in fact missed the initial
session as he had joined the workshop
late. He is a very humorous man, and
realising the reasons behind the exercise
he treated us to his version of a "bad
walk" as he put it What he gave was a
hilarious Chaplinesque performance of
the exercise: holding his cane ahead of
him, he stumbled his way forward
through the vast dark territory of the
unseen gym, the blind cane then became
a fencing sword and he fought his way
through a duel with an imaginary foe.
Victorious, his stick transformed into a
horse which he sat astride and whipped
into a gallop to the far end of the gym.
Turning, he lurched drunkenly back
towards the chair which he could not
see but sensed the position of. Having
reached it. he failed to sit on it. somer-
saulting his way through falls; and ulti-
mately, threading his cane through the
Volume 17 1992
back of the chair and hoisting i~ aloft
over his shoulders, he trudged off into
the proverbial sunset. Peter had found
improvisation and performance.
When the workshop finished Peter
broke down and wept with deep grief.
It was the first time since his accident,
several years before, that he had cried.
His tears were not only for his loss of
sight but also tears of appreciation at
having found a new level of experience.
This experience opened up the future
for him. He has since entered the Royal
National College, in Hereford, England
and qualified to become a masseur. He
has found direction and now expects to
combine his new-found skills in mas-
sage with his old interests in sports and
sports injuries.
Peter is one of many blind people
who have come to contact improvisation
workshops that we have developed in
England over the last five years. These
workshops are designed to extend the
movement vocabulary and creative
experience of the visually challenged
and the professionals that work with
them. The blind who have danced with
us come from all walks of life. Some
have been blind from birth, others have
lost their sight in adulthood through
degenerative diseases, accidents and
diabetes. The professionals include
social workers, recreation officers,
teachers and dancers who work to make
dance accessible to disabled people;
most of the professionals are sighted but
a few of them are visually impaired.
If you are born without sight there are
profound implications for your physical
development as well as your social and
intellectual/cultural life. The blind
person is inhibited from freely moving
from one place to another by the fear of
falling over or bumping into things and
by a confusion in spatial orientation.
Those who are congenitally blind spend
much of their lives at home, sitting or
lying down. The paucity of the every-
day movements of walking, sitting down
and getting up means that the muscles
in their feet and legs do not develop
their potential. Through lack of use,
their muscles are underdeveloped, flac-
cid and poorly toned. These character-
istics are also reflected in posture; the
torso is often limp, the head hanging
forward. The essential movement needs
of the body are often met by a gently
repetitive rocking motion in the seated
Volume 17 1992
position. This rocking is generally
discouraged by family and professionals
as it is considered to be a 'blind-
ism '-i.e., a movement or gesture which
is not found in the sighted world and
which is seen as a stigma.
In those people who have seen for
some years but lost their sight in adult
life there is another set of bodily re-
sponses. These people do not generally
exhibit blindisms. Their gestural vocab-
ulary is consonant with that of the dom-
inant sighted culture, but their loss of
sight prohibits normal everyday move-
ment. The trauma and anxiety which
accompanies visual loss is reflected in a
chronic level of muscular tension which
is so habitual as to make muscular
relaxation a forgotten experience. they,
too, stand and wait for someone with
sight to guide them to another seat in
another room where they can safely
'park' their bodies.
Although there are some programmes
of physical education available to the
blind these are mostly purely functional
and often mechanised. For example,
there is tandem cycling, or jogging a
course that is marked out by a rope
which the blind person can hold onto, or
the treadmill or weight lifting machines
that are available in a modern gym.
Whilst a few of the visually impaired
make great efforts to keep themselves in
trim by going to the gym, they generally
report a sense of boredom with the
repetitive and socially isolated nature of
many of the exercise regimes open to
them.
Contact improvisation seemed an
ideal activity for the blind, based as it is
in the ·natural physics of movement, in
human communication, and focused on
a touch-based learning path rather than
a 'seeing and copying' mode endemic in
so many dance classes.
The inherently democratic nature of
contact improvisation makes it a particu-
larly good form for the so-called dis-
abled who deal continuously with dis-
abling social attitudes and relationships.
For example, Janee, who is partially
sighted, had left the workshop with
Rose, a sighted arts worker. They were
sitting in a car at a petrol station when
Rose was approached by a lady who
Anne traces the pathway on Peter, from sacrum, under shoulders, underneath arms
to the little linger. Sitting allows one to feel the sitzbones. Carollne (l.), is ·
extending ring and small lingers, feeling the connection from center-of-weight
(pelvis) along the same path. Peter's hands don't grasp the thought of extension
here.
CQICI Sourcebook .2Zl
]Letter from a Touchdown Dance
workshop participant
Dear Anne,
I will try to be articulate about my
perceptions of the Contact Dance work-
shop I attended in the spring, though I
don't consider putting thoughts into
words to be my natural way of express-
ing myself.
The workshop seems to have had quite
a profound effect on me, and perhaps
you will forgive me for starting by de-
scribing some of its after-effects.
For a few days I felt quite out of tune
with friends and acquaintances. I just
wanted to withdraw and relive the expe-
rience, and one of the first things I did
was to get out my sketch pad and draw
a complete sequence of all the move-
ments and partnerships I learned in the
workshop; I found that I had remem-
bered every single one vividly.
When I got to bed, at peace and very
relaxed, I had two strange experiences,
which I suppose were sort of dreams.
I was aware that I was lying inert in
the dark. I began to feel as though my
head was being gently drawn away from
my body; as though some kind of mag-
netic force was attracting me, and the
pull came first from one direction and
then another. The sensation was similar
to that which we did during the head
massage exercise and I felt very elated,
but was aware that the force controlling
this sensation was not human. I wasn't
afraid at this point, and the sensation
then faded and the sequence changed.
I was aware of standing in a bare
room with white walls. I was only con-
scious of my body from the waist up.
While standing in the same spot my body
started to make a circular movement
from the waist. As I did this I began to
get the same feeling as before; that a
magnetic force was drawing me upwards
by the head. I felt myself rising upwards
gaining speed and I could see the ceiling
getting closer. I remember feeling
slightly afraid at this point because I
wasn't quite in control and I thought I
might hit the ceiling, but I didn't, and
just floated round and round the edges
of the room as though on the end of a
222 CQ/CJ Sourcebook
piece of elastic, til the intensity of the
sensation woke me up.
This experience was all the more un-
usual for me because I am not religious;
haven't done any form of meditation;
hadn't had any alcohol and wasn't high
on anything.
It's only happened on that one occa-
sion so far and I would like to find out if
other people who've done the workshops
have had any kind of after-effects like
this?
There may be a connection between
this experience and a discovery I made
some years ago while undergoing some
formal mobility training with the long
cane, under blindfold. Much to my
instructor's bewilderment, I found that I
could sense objects from between 25 to
50 yards away without making contact
with the long cane. I did this without
sight or sound cues. I just felt a strong
sense of claustrophobia when there were
solid objects in front of me, and relief
and elation when there were wide open
spaces. Since my mobility instructor's
objective was to teach me to detect ob-
stacles with the cane, he was rather
skeptical of this ability, and set me many
challenges. He hadn't encountered this
in other clients, but did reveal that a
similar thing was referred to as a rare
phenomenon in his profession, and was
known as "Shadow Sound."
I disagree with this description: I
don't think it is a "rare phenomenon" ;
nor has it got anything to do with
"sound." I think it has more to do with
a primitive instinct for self-preservation
and dealing with the unknown, which is
dormant in most people, and only comes
into play when they are deprived of one
of the other senses.
Anyway, I return to my observations
about the workshop.
An element of the workshop that ap-
pealed to me was that the only "props"
we needed were human partnerships.
Through the variety of exercises and
partnerships I was involved in during the
workshop, I was able to initiate more
ambitious shapes and movements, confi-
dent that I would get the support to
carry them through as well as anticipat-
ing and supporting my partner's inten-
tions.
I think the basic structure of the work-
shop was sound, and there were only
two instances where I experienced diffi-
culty; though both of these were resolved
during the five day period.
The first was trying to respond, from
the perspective of a person with visual
impairment, to the mainly sighted imag-
ery used to describe some of the shapes
we were attempting to create, e.g., I
didn't understand Steve's reference to "a
double helix," which we were supposed
to achieve when we were doing the
rolling exercise. As I suggested at the
time, verbal description is necessary, but
could be more effective if it referred to
familiar tangible objects. (I have since
found that I have a sea shell which is
eroded by the sand, and forms the exact
shape I was trying to make.) I think this
point is important as I don't want to be
in positions where I have to rely too
heavily on a sighted partner's interpreta-
tion.
The second occasion I experienced
difficulty occurred when one of my sight-
ed partners matched me up with a visu-
ally impaired person for the next se-
quence in the workshop. I found that I
couldn't get any rapport or positive
contact going. This did shatter my con-
fidence for the rest of the session that
day, and although I did manage to re-
solve the issue with the person who
initiated it later, perhaps it might have
been avoided if the basic ground rules
were spelled out more explicitly at the
beginning of the workshop.
I enjoyed and benefited from the ex-
pertise of the professional sighted danc-
ers; and certainly didn't feel at all intim-
idated by their experience. In fact I
think it is proof of the effectiveness of
the work that both sighted and visually
impaired participants were in a recipro-
cal learning situation.
Finally, one last comment. I recently
read an article in the New Beacon about
Amici (a dance group working with peo-
ple who have a wide range of both phys-
ical and mental disabilities). I hope that
"Touchdown" doesn't go down this
road. The general public has two per-
sistent, and prejudicial stereotypes about
visual impairment; we are either genius-
es, or mentally retarded. I think mixing
disabilities as Amici does, often coefr.rms
rather than banishes the second stereo-
type in particular. I for one, don't want
any involvement in such an exercise.
Well, I hope all these comments will
be of some help to you. The fact that I
have written at such length ~s quite
unlike me, and you may take it as a
token of my enthusiasm and commitment
to Contact Dance.
Sincerely,
Megan Hughes

(cont'd)
was collecting for charity. "How old is
she," the lady asked Rose a8suining that
because Janee is blind she must also be
deaf and possibly intellectually retarded.
Janee answered her. "I am 24." The
woman continued to ignore her whilst
focussing on her. "Can't they do any-
thing for her," she persisted. "It's a
terrible shame... Still, labradors are
lovely dogs."
Even Fred Raffle, a man who has
risen to the top in the field of blind
education, and who was blind from
birth, revealed that sighted co-profes-
sionals could not stop their caring role.
This means that even though be is the
boss, he has to deal with paternalistic
attitudes which infantalise, patronise and
stigmatise. In the workshops however,
he found the politics more nearly equal,
feeling that through the contact improvi-
sation structures, he was met as himself,
rather than as a person with a disability.
The workshops which we have devel-
oped are always "integrated" in the
sense that they include both sighted and
blind people who work in an equal
partnership as they learn contact impro-
visation together. -
A class in contact improvisation for
sighted people involves ''pre-tuning" the
senses from the normal sighted domi-
nance to the sense of touch. In an
integrated class this occurs very con-
sciously because of empathy with a
blind partner. It can be an intense expe-
rience to assume a state of mind in
which the visual world plays no part at
all in the reference systems. This shock
is a most salutary one, giving a high
contrast to the differences between one
sense, vision, and the other, touch.
Usually the transition from the vistlal-
ly dominant world into the world of
touch is gradual, and the sighted student
senses the change less acutely than
when dancing with the blind. We have
seen students who resisted any empathic
connection however, leaving a dance
with a blind partner who then had to
stand in place waiting for aid. Or the
student who was so inattentive that after
a two week workshop, when asked to
run an exercise for the group, she real-
ized that she didn't know how to refer
to the blind group. In a loud aside to
Anne, she said, "What do you call
them?"
She had missed the point and the
opportunity of the workshop. The peo-
ple who have lost their sight have an
increased concentration in the other
senses, and working with a blind partner
is an education in touch 'density'.
Concentration of sighted students will
waver between touch and vision. The
concentration of the blind will not
waver.
Transmitting the principles of an
exercise to the class can be taxing. To
further the democratic ideal one at-
tempts to describe the positions in a
way that both populations can under-
stand. Even though an obvious point of
integration is for the visually able to see
the demonstration, to facilitate transmis-
sion so that each of the blind can have
an example from which to learn by
touch, at the same time, one strives to
present careful, clear pictures in the
language. It is astonishing how quickly
Tony, the visually impaired partner, traces the same pathway while sighted partner
does spiral roll (see "Helix" in ca last issue). No parson in Touchdown goes
untaught, untouched, even Anne and I. This reciprocity is informative and regularly
reverses the teacher-student heirarchy. Integration, reciprocity, intimacy, physical
care are the basis for Touchdown Dance workshops.
Volume 17 1992
CQICI Sourcebook 223
In order to form a circle, touch is used. This creates physical intimacy and warms
conversation. Those with partial or total vision are blindfolded.
the language itself flounders, however.
We have a personal reference sys-
tem-'right and left', and the more ob-
jective 'north and south' or other exter-
nal references. For the blind the most
important of these is the personal sort of
reference. If everyone is facing the
same direction and reacting at the same
time, then instructions go well. When
things get a little chaotic, we've found
that overly-general directions result in a
number of different interpretations
which, if not corrected, can lead to
confusion and loss of confidence. Even
when they are corrected, the time spent
and tone of voice may feel overly tech-
nical. This taught us to use methodical
steps, careful verbal introductions to any
exercise, coupled with an attempt at a
casual, conversational air.
For those who have no movement
training and poor muscle tone, the phys-
ical approach has to be clear and soft.
This has called for an analysis of exer-
cises to try to determine what exactly is
suitable in this situation. The propor-
tions and durations of things have to be
224 CQ;O Sourcebook
considered. Relaxation and massage are
brought in frequently. Exercises requir-
ing just one part of the body to relax in
isolation, such as 'floating the head' or
'arm drop', are very useful. Exercises
which can strengthen longer connections
between limbs and torso are used.
Apparently the real juice of the work-
shops which include the blind is the
contact improvisation itself. It is this
which produces the feelings of self-
empowerment, joy and satisfaction.
There are deeply felt, difficult emotions
and painful social configurations which
accompany the loss of a person's sight;
however, our working contract is physi-
cal rather than psychotherapeutic. In
these workshops, as in all other contact
improvisation sessions, the teaching
focuses on the issues of weight, mass,
gravity, leverage and movement design.
In the teaching, we acknowledge con-
nections between emotions and muscular
tensions, but formal space for the ex-
pression of emotions is reserved for the
closing process of the workshops.
Peter's catharsis was the most extreme
that we have seen and occurred within
the formal structuring of the closing
phase.
Although many others have moist
eyes and sometimes tears, the most fully
and frequently expressed feelings are
overwhelmingly positive. Objectively
we can see that the emotional landscape
is accompanied by a changed physi-
cality-improved complexions, relaxed
features, lowered shoulders, alert pos-
ture, more confident gait and increased
animation. The participants' own im-
provisations have provided the means
whereby these changes have taken
place.*
Touchdown Dance integrates visually im-
paired and visually able (usually dancers
and blind-care professionals) folk in Devon,
England. For information: Touchdown
Dance, Anne Kilcoyne, 34 Bridgetown Rd.,
Totnes, Devon, England TQ7 5AD.

STILL MOVING
BABY CONT~CT
by Sandra Jamrog
I am a Childbirth/Parent Educator
and a Teacher of Body-Mind Center-
ingTM. One of the aspects of my work
is helping babies and parents "bond."
I feel Baby Contact is a perfect way to
help parents attune with their babies.
I have been experimenting with
Baby Contact since my thirteen year
old daughter Jenny was born. During
the summer of '77 I was working at
the School for Body-Mind Centering™
and was fortunate to have had some
colleagues invite me to join in some
Contact Improvisation. I was fasci-
nated. I was pregnant for the third
time and had danced and performed
through all my pregnancies. Doing
Contact Improvisation while I was
pregnant felt like a trio (me, the fetus,
and the other adult).
In addition to exploring BMC and
Contact Improvisation, I was also dis-
covering the Kestenberg work, a meth-
od of observing movement and work-
ing with child/parent movement inter-
actions. Influenced by these three
bodies of work, I was experimenting
with dialoguing with the fetus in all
sorts of ways: moving in the same
rhythms that the fetus within me was
moving; initiating the movement;
following the fetal movements; leading
from the uterus; vibrating the amniotic
fluid with sound and movement; let-
ting the weight of the uterus or the
fetus adjust my relationship with grav-
ity; as well as anything else that hap-
pened as a result of the continually
changing dialogue within my body.
After Jenny was born, the natural
continuation was to keep dancing, with
her on my body, using the same dia-
logue-sometimes following, sometimes
leading; two energies creating one
ever-changing form, contacting each
other through movement and stillness.
My Baby/Parent group began exper-
imenting with Baby Contact, first the
parent lying and breathing with their
baby, just listening to their baby; then
feeling their baby initiate movement,
then responding in micro-movements
to their baby's movement initiation.
We began very gently and very care-
fully, combining initiating movement
and responding to movement, moving
and receiving, being motor and senso-
ry. Eventually we built the micro-
movements into larger movements that
moved through space. With Contact
one needs to be constantly feeling and
re-evaluating, ready to instantaneously
respond as well as instantaneously act;
one needs to be open to receiving as
well as giving; responding as well as
initiating. That balance of give and
take, in and out, is an important attrib-
ute for parents as well as babies.
In addition, in Baby Contact, babies
have the power to move the environ-
ment, of initiating movement through
space, of being responded to physical-
ly, of initiating change. They also
have the experience of incorporating
another person's rhythm, of internaliz-
ing another person's movement and
perhaps even developing it. They also
have the opportunity to see the world
from many spacial perspectives, stimu-
lating their vestibular system.
My students and I have enjoyed
Baby Contact and have learned things
we could have learned in no other
way. We have had.the opportunity to
blend our adult bodies with our
babies' bodies into one movement.*
Baby Contact.
photo: Sandra Jamrog

DANCES WITH WHEELS was the name of a weekend
movement workshop that occurred last May in Seattle, Washing-
ton. Organized by Charlene Curtiss of Light Motion and Whistle-
Stop Theater, Dances With Wheels brought together about 35
people of varying abilities to learn and practice contact improvi-
sation under the guidance of Alan Ptashek and Bruce Curtis of
The Exposed to Gravity Project from Berkeley. The workshop
ended with a series of perfonnances, opening into a "jam" for the
workshop participants, all of which was enthusiastically observed
by the audience.

Climbing into Charlene's chair, shaking with the unsureness of
my sudden new relationship to the ground, I had to figure out how to make
these two big wheels my friends. Charlene had adjusted her chair to make
it more maneuverable for dancing, and consequently extremely tippy. There
were now two unpredictable wheels deciding my motion. I felt completely out
of control. To sit up meant risking falling directly backwards, propelling my-
self forward meant risking a spill also. I was momentarily paralyzed Vel}'
slowly I began to find motion that did not threaten to unbalance me. J Jeamed
to find that teetering point, sort of like treading water. where you're hovering
between falling backwards and sitting straight up. Still shaking, I gradually
found a little more confidence.
I had put myself in a position that was vel}' foreign and where it took a lot
of effort to make myself comfortable. As an able-bodied person in an •able-body
oriented" world I don't have to do this vel}' often. I found myself feeling se/f-
conscious and clutzy. This experience has become a strong physical memo/}'
and a good reminder of the constant struggle to understand each other, our
differences, and to dispel any assumptions relating to these.

STILL MOVING
CAN CONTACT BE WHAT IT IS?
by Rick Knowlton
excerpt from a two-part essay
Perhaps Contact Improvisation
first arose as a neat idea rooted in the
experimental, freedom-loving '60s,
catalyzed in 1972 by Steve Paxton as a
situational structure for spontaneous
performance. Perhaps Contact is a
recreation or a choreographer's tool or
a means of therapy with difficult-to-
reach populations.
But to me, Contact is a key to
accessing a whole realm of human
interaction that most people would not
otherwise ever reach. A context in
which movement therapies can inte-
grate, chronic social attitudes can
relax, the rigid can become spontan-
eous, the playful can celebrate, the
skin's hunger can be fed.
There are so many states of body
and mind, qualities and depths of
touch, invitations for spirit to embody
the flesh which I can't imagine occur-
ring so naturally in any other con-
text... Consider, for instance:
11 In Contact Quarterly we often
read about dances with alter-abled
(i.e., "disabled") people. I'm thinking
also about the emotional and relation-
ship disabilities of "able-bodied" peo-
ple-emotional limitations rooted in or
augmented by physical habits and
rigidities. Do our Contact studies
consciously span the gap between
people of vastly different tempera-
ments?
Iii In therapeutic contexts, this
movement form can quite literally em-
body the Gestalt therapy model of
"psychological events occuring at the
boundary of the organism," (Fritz
Perls, The Gestalt Approach). Can our
work in Contact approach the more
profound boundary problems in our
society such as dysfunctional family
dynamics, abuse victims, sadomaso-
chistic disorders?
Rick Knowlton & Barry Hynum.
11111 Early in this century, F.M.
Alexander pioneered what have now
become schools of body/mind repat-
teming/re-education, on the premise
that we, as a society, incorrectly inter-
pret our nervous system's kinesthetic
information. Is there any better
ground for constant kinesthetic feed-
back, any better place to relearn and
re-evaluate our kinesthetic/proprio-
ceptive perception than the Contact
duet?
11 Contact Improvisation enjoys a
fun, multifaceted, international com-
munity of practitioners. There is
something special about a community
in which you can really touch every-
body. Now, what community wouldn't
benefit from such a direct invitation to
support and empathy? It may seem
silly to picture the executive board of
I.B.M. upside-down in sweatpants, but
what are the actual limitations keeping
Contact contained as a special interest
of us few?
11 Finally, we are just about to
see the first generation of adult chil-
dren of Contact dancers. Are they just
regular kids whose parents have a
weird hobby-or have they been ex-
posed to a world of free energetic flow
barely dreamed of by the likes of
Wilhelm Reich?
Ill If not, why not?
I'm writing perhaps not so much of
what Contact Improvisation is as of
what I feel it can be. Ten years after
first learning the form, I still have the
very highest expectations of what this
simple movement practice offers. On
the eve of Contact's twentieth birth-
day, where has it already gone? Are
there not countless frontiers still await-
ing this movement? *
