Volume 17 1992
MAKING CONTACT IN MEXICO
by John Marinelli
I recently had an opportunity to
teach contact improvisation (CD in
Mexico City, Mexico. "The Winter
Workshop Intensive for Dance Profes-
sionals," which took place at the
Instituto Nacional de Bellas Artes
(National Institute of Fine Arts), was
attended by professional dancers and
dance students from all over Mexico.
For three weeks I taught two 2-hour
classes a day, one with students and
the other with dance professionals.
The workshop took place from De-
cember 1-20, 1991.
Other instructors for this intensive
were Sara Pearson, Patrick Widrig,
Jeremy Nelson, Nancy Topf, and Dan-
iel Nagrin. Jaime Ortega and Marcella
Broughton, who are dancers from New
York City, were our translators. The
organizer of the event was Lidya Ro-
mero, Director of Dance at the Insti-
tuto. Lidya and our other hosts were
very gracious and the students were
extremely receptive to new ideas, both
in body and mind. They had been
exposed to only a bit of CI before and
were mostly trained in Graham tech-
nique, so I expected bodies that were
not malleable to CI, but I was wrong.
They learned the skills and form of CI
very well and were willing and able to
integrate this different movement
paradigm into their bodies.
The workshop setting presented me
with the opportunity to rediscover the
beginnings of the CI form for myself.
I decided to ask questions to help
focus my teaching: What is contact
improvisation? What is my point of
view of the form? How is it taught to
dancers who know very little about it?
What is the essence of CI and its basic
skills? What do I think are the most
important things to get across about
the form? Even though I have taught
CI to beginners many times, I felt
more responsible because this was
another country and the dance ideas
out of which CI originated had not
permeated their culture. Besides, it's
exciting to start from the beginning
again after so many years. I enjoy
a:;:king questions and gaining new
perspectives and getting fresh insights.
I divided the basics into different
categories: internal skills such as
awareness of inner space, including
gravity, mass, weight, and volume;
learning to give and take weight; and
following a continually changing point
of physical contact. There are also
several senses that need to be devel-
oped when learning CI, whether
learned consciously or unconsciously.
These are the sense of weight shifting,
the proprioceptive sense, the kinesthe-
tic sense, and the disorientation of the
vestibular sense of balance.
There are also a number of physical
skills that can be taught and practiced
including "nameable" skills such as
rolling, sluffing off, platforming and
draping, body surfing, falling, giving
and taking weight, and counterbal-
ancing.
Dancing contact improvisation is
about learning a new movement lan-
guage based on these senses and skills.
CI is a type of whole body dance
technique. You get the experience of
the body-as-a-unit. IT brings you to
an altered state similar to that of an
athlete. You become adrenalized and
sensitive to lower brain reflexes so
that you are able to make split second
decisions about falling, breaking falls,
and taking other physical risks. In
addition, you learn to follow impulses
from moment to moment and to com-
municate those impulses through your
body. You also become adept at lis-
tening to impulses from another body.
I allotted plenty of time for open
dancing so that skills and concepts
could be integrated into the dancing. I
also set up structures to allow for
discussion from the point of view of
the dancers as well as from the obser-
vers' perspective.
Volume 17 1992
I stressed that the form is about
dancing from the inside out. First you
become aware of and align with the
physical forces of nature. On the frrst
day, I began with a body awareness
exercise; while lying down, sense the
mass, weight, volume, gravity, and
points of contact with the floor. In
standing, I led the experience of the
small dance of tiny weight shift ad-
justments we unconsciously do that
keep us in the vertical plane.
The next day I introduced the skill of
following a continually changing point
of physical contact, as well as the
concept that weight shifting is a sense.
On the third day I did exercises to
introduce the idea of maintaining
balance while in disorienting relation-
ships to the ground.
To sum up, the concepts that I cov-
ered the first week are as follows: four
senses (proprioceptive, kinesthetic,
vestibular (dis)orientation, and weight
shifting); aligning with gravity, mo-
mentum, and movement impulses;
following a continually changing point
of physical contact; giving and taking
weight; learning the art of controlled
free falling; listening with the body.
After this first week of introducing
the concepts and doing some open
dance structures, I wondered about
which step to take next to get to the
dancing form that I recognize as CL I
CQICI Sourcebook 229
don't care whether people do ''pure"
CI or not I don't want to be pedantic
simply to maintain control, but to
insure safety. My interest is in shar-
ing the experience of freely falling
through space and aligning with what
Cynthia Novack described. in her book
Sharing the Dance: Contact Improvi-
sation and American Culture, as the
"third force" that is both of you and
neither of you.
Skills are fun in and of themselves
and they help you to find new places
in your dancing. However, while
dancing, you want to be feeling it
from the inside rather than looking at
it from the outside. CI is not only
about learning skills and doing them in
a dance. It is also about listening and
communicating. It is about sensing
where the impulses and motivation for
dancing originate. It is about discov-
ering the place where the impulses
come out of your physical relationship
to each other and the physical forces
that you share while in contact The
movement ideas arise out of this
shared energy relationship.
I had these thoughts during the
round robins, because although the
students were extremely open to the
form and the energy ran high, it dissi-
pated very soon instead of building or
growing. I wanted to show techniques
for letting the momentum of a dance
build and also for finding different
energy states within a dance.
Therefore, for a focus during the
second week, I decided to work with
the elements of air, water, earth, fire
and ether as originally presented in CI
by Nancy Stark Smith. In addition, I
wanted to expand on disorientation
concepts as a way of letting go of the
'egohead' of dancing and allowing the
body's reflexive responses to take
over. I also continued teaching skills I
had introduced in the first week.
The "elements" turned out to be a
good way to talk about and feel the
different states and qualities that a
dance can have. After a week of
230 CQICI Saurcebook
focusing on skills and taking them into
the dancing, the element of fire (un-
leashing wild and high energy) added
a quality of freedom to the dancing,
and the dancers' creative resources
seemed more accessible. It was also
helpful to use the polarity of air and
earth (levity and gravity) and I focused
on how to mutate between these two
states. Doing water-flow dances with-
out limb support was a helpful ap-
proach to changing movement patterns.
However, it was when I introduced
the element of ether (unpredictable
qualities) that the dancing really be-
came engaging, both to watch and to
do. Up to that point they had been
tied to a form based on a continuum
from skills to dancing, much like
ballet where steps are combined to
make a dance. With the ether concept,
there is a whole range of movement
qualities that are available to the danc-
er, and what I feel is the inherent
unpredictability of CI duet dancing
became accessible. Dance structures
like the round robin could remain
energized much longer and with more
variety in movement, qualities, and
dynamics. The personality of each
person came out which added texture
to the dancing.
For the third week I allotted plenty
of time for open dancing and discus-
sion. I feel that the way to get better
at CI is to do it (a radical thought
indeed) and discuss it I also taught
some more advanced skills such as
running and jumping onto a partner,
windmills and other dynamic lifts,
handstand techniques, and dancing
scores.
Each day, the dancing was getting
progressively more recognizable to me
as the CI I have seen in its many
permutations. The communication was
clearer and the dancing developed
from the contact instead of from the
willful mind. A feeling of trust within
the form and within the group was
becoming more apparent
To culminate the experience of the
three weeks, during the final day we
combined the classes and did a contact
jam with the students and profession-
als together. The dancing was highly
skillful and at an extremely high ener-
gy level. I was gratified to see the
dancing become more fully realized
within the form. It reminded me of
the early days of CI in the U.S. where
experimentation and an attitude of
testing one's limits prevailed.
There were limitations in having
only three weeks of teaching time.
For example, the slow, sensuous quali-
ty that CI can have was not present
enough to offset the high energy,
adrenalized dancing that dominated the
open dancing sessions. I would have
liked to have had more time to allow
the dancers to engage in many types
of dance energies and qualities, and to
explore extending the form in other
ways. However, I think that this will
all come when the physical limits have
been fully tested. The form will grow
from the unique combination of ener-
gies of these dancers in Mexico. Who
is to know or judge what new direc-
tions the form might take in this un-
tapped environment?
During the third week, I made Shar-
ing the Dance and four issues of Con-
tact Quarterly available during class
time and there was much interest in
these publications. I encouraged them
to write to let CQ know what is hap-
pening in Mexico regarding CI and
related dance. They expressed an
interest in having contact jams and
open improvisaiton sessions in Mexico
City. It will be interesting to see what
develops. In closing, I would like to
thank Lid.ya Romero, Instituto Nacio-
nal de Bellas Artes, the organizers,
and the students for making the work-
shop possible and a total pleasure. I
appreciated the dancers' openness,
high energy, and graciousness. I hope
this is the beginning of a dance dia-
logue with the rich culture of
Mexico.*
Volume 17 1992
3 DAYS
Steve Paxton
Joint Forces, a dance company founded by Alito Alessi
and Karen Nelson, has presented four annual DanceAbility
workshops, sharing Contact Improvisation dance with
hundreds of people, some able-bodied and some with vari-
ous physical disabilities. This year's DanceAbility, pro-
duced by Alito, took place for 3 days in mid-March, 1991.
Eugene, Oregon is a city designed to be wheelchair
accessible and has provided the Hilyard Community Center
for disabled folk as well. It is a handsome center, and as
an able-bodied person, I enjoyed the extra space and wider
doors which result when a building is designed to accom-
modate people who use wheelchairs. A problem for many
disabled folk is our society's commercialization of space.
It can be difficult these days to get out of a car without
chipping paint on the doors of three vehicles. Vans modi-
fied to raise and lower wheelchairs need the equivalent of
two spaces.
So, for those disabled people able to drive to the center
on Friday, it was easy to park, descend to ground level,
wheel into the building with its automatically opening
doors, and attend the orientation meeting for DanceAbility.
Originally, DanceAbility was to be held at the Center,
but Saturday and Sunday's workshops had to be relocated
to a more spacious high school gymnasium. The planners
had not foreseen the large turnout for the event; disabled
people are known stay-at-homes.
Some disabled folk objected to the concept of the pur-
pose-built spanking-new community center for the disabled.
The problem is how to integrate the populations, not segre-
gate them into separate facilities. Bruce Curtis noticed that
the men's toilet had no urinals, and though admitting it was
a detail, felt that it was a symptom of a lack of consider-
ation for the able-bodied, and as if all men with disabilities
were in wheelchairs.
Getting pissed-off about the pissing facilities expresses
the sensitivity of the activist Bruce was invited to Dance-
Ability as a facilitator. He is a quad-a quadriplegic-in a
wheelchair, from San Francisco. He has been active in
Volume 17 1992
Pete
teaching and performing dances with his able-bodied part-
ner, Alan Ptashek. Alan is a veteran of a movement form
called Contact Improvisation, a sort of partnered gymnastic
improvisation. He answered the phone one day and found
Bruce at the other end. Alan is a gifted partner and he and
Bruce, who has no control of his legs or fingers, began to
work out together.
Most forms of dance partnership suppose that one part-
ner leads, the other responds: in CI however, either dancer
may initiate or respond. After the movement starts, a new
possibility arises; to follow the momentums established and
to continue them while remaining in touch with the other's
body. This is the 'contact' part of the form. The 'impro-
visational' aspect of it allows either partner to be in any
position relative to their partner or to the floor. Essentially
it is old-fashioned touch dancing taken from the vertical of
the ballroom into the horizontals and obliques of wrestling,
with, hopefully, the ability of the snooker player to intuit
momental affects.
Bruce began dancing, I believe, from a political motive,
a desire to insure that the equality of the disabled folk was
addressed; as well as his own need not to be marginalized,
which included not letting his body degenerate in a wheel-
chair. Bruce came to Eugene without Alan this year,
which is a measure of their success, and drove up the coast
in a VW bus he had modified to cope with his chair and
the ways that his limbs work.
He is accepted as a performer, invited as a teacher.
Have we heard of a quadriplegic dancer-teacher before?
No, we have not.
Yet, at DanceAbility, Bruce was not unique. Charlene
Curtiss teaches wheelchair aerobics in Seattle, for another
instance. Emery Blackwell has recently resigned the presi-
dency of OIL (Oregonians for Independent Living) to
CQICI Sourcebook 231
devote himself to dance. He has cerebral palsy to a degree
which makes speaking difficult Words are stretched. tones
distorted. His vocal mechanisms and jaw, like the rest of
his body, reflect the aspect of CP which exaggerates the
motor nerve impulses, sometimes producing kicking and
quavers in the legs, large arm gestures, flexed (athatoid)
wrists and extraordinary finger movements.
For three years Emery has worked with Alito Alessi and
they have made a duet which uses the paraphernalia of the
disabled-wheelchairs and crutches. Alito recently had an
extraordinary accident A bottle of carbonated water blew
up and severed the muscles and tendons of the front of his
right leg above the ankle, requiring surgery and extensive
rehabilitation. So he has experienced a profound, if tempo-
rary, disability. He was no stranger to the disabled; his
mother was wheelchair-bound from an auto accident when
he was young, and his duet with Emery reflects his famil-
iarity in a playful way with the mechanics of the support
systems.
Emery has also produced a short solo, for which he
made the music on his synthesizer.
Bruce, then, is not alone in taking the creative initiative
in dance. It is an extraordinary thing they are all doing.
We have seen paintings produced by disabled artists; music
of distinction has been composed by disabled musicians.
However, dance uses the body as a medium. Unlike other
arts, when one looks at a dance made by somebody who is
disabled one is looking at the dance and also the disability.
This contravenes our society's protocol regarding looking
at the disabled. But theater exists in part to provide the
opportunity for frank appraisal of human beings and per-
haps it suits the agenda of disabled performers to use the
theatrical conventions to allow us to get used to their con-
ditions. Then they get on with the creation of dance under
those conditions, without necessarily referring to them.
1'1~: l
L--·--·-··----·-___.,...._.. ________ --./
These thoughts arose because we saw two dance works
featuring Emery at the introduction to the workshop. One
was the solo he choreographed in which he rolled across
the floor, rose to his knees, turned in place, and then de-
scended and rolled away. What we see is that the rather
simple format of the dance is taxing. Emery is working at
the edge of his capabilities, just as many dancers do.
He is a striking man. The CP exaggerates his movements
and features, but his long curly head of hair, his thin face
with goatee often wetted with saliva, his intelligent and fre-
quently merry blue eyes suggest a character of some flam-
boyance. Bruce once said that the disabled are either
ignored or viewed as martyrs. But I think Emery would be
extraordinary even if the facade of CP did not provide such
a lavish filter through which to appreciate his spirit and
thoughts.
Emery's spirit lights up his actions and face. His
thoughts are thoughts set in a new body language.
Emery has said that to get his arm raised above his head
requires about 20 seconds of imaging to accomplish.
232 CQICI Sourcebook
Extension and contraction impulses in his muscles fire
frequently and unpredictably, and he must somehow select
the right impulses consciously, or produce for himself a
movement image of the correct quality to get the arm to
respond as he wants. We observers can get entranced with
what he is doing with his mind. More objectively, we can
see that as he tries he excites his motor impulses and the
random firing happens with more vigor. His dancing has a
built-in Catch-22. And we feel the quandary and see that
he is pitched against his nervous system and wins, with
effort and a kind of concentration we haven't seen before.
He has mechanisms in his mind we able-bodied have not
had to learn. His facility with them allows us to feel them
subtly in our own minds.
The taste of this we get during the short solo is much
amplified in the duet with Alito. This dance is longer and
requires that Emery be where he needs to be in time to
coordinate with Alito. The choreography provides a new
filter to show us the man. The CP appears to mask the
logic and intent until events show us that what we see has
been carefully calculated by Emery and Alito.
Can these dances avoid the obvious trap of being curios-
ities, grotesqueries, or analytical, quasi-medical displays?
It depends on the artistry, of course, and the failures of this
new geme will probably be absorbed politely, just as the
theatrical misadventures of the able-bodied usually are.
Theoretically I see no problem. Usually we think of
dance as the development of human movement potential to
superhuman levels. Another way to look at dance tech-
niques is to notice that they exclude more movement po-
tentials than they develop. Ballet works in the realm of
extension of the limbs. It does not include the contractions
of Martha Graham nor the asymmetry and sexy pelvis of
jazz. Each of these techniques operates within its own
logic, and each may borrow or quote from other systems,
but at heart they are defined by how they are limited.
These definitions are aesthetic ones, though, and it is a
difference of kind when the limitations are the result of
mischance or malady.
But we quickly grasp the logic of the limitation, be it
aesthetic choice or physical disability, and this is the point
at which the mind and spirit of the performers take over to
convey us through the moments they are there to perform.
I have seen awful Balanchine ballets. I have seen balleri-
nas with superb bodies who could not hold my attention
for sixty seconds. And conversely, I have seen disabled
performers who, without evoking pity or morbid fascina-
tion, kept me riveted to their work.
Jose Limon, a famous modem dancer, once said he felt
most alive when on stage. I have similar feelings, which
may be hard to understand by those who don't perform
regularly. What happens is that the rigidity and self-con-
sciousness of stage fright change to fluid extra power and
deeper self-awareness. For that moment you have more of
you. I guess it is adrenalin and other hormones surging
through one's system, which mixes with one's own feelings
and the feelings of the audience--who are there to witness
feelings, after all. What a broth.
For Bruce and Emery, perhaps this performance high is
the same. I view performance as an empowerment, and a
Volume 17 1992
healing, or wholing. They may have found this as well.
There is something in it for them, in other words. And if
they find it, their transcendence will fuel our transcen-
dence.
Will we find it more natural to design the disabled into
our urban environments because they will be in/on our
minds? Or are we struck with novelty? Will it wear thin?
The audience at the Center absorbed the performances
with appreciation and then watched a video of last year's
DanceAbility workhsop so they could see what they were
in for on the weekend.
Alito
SATURDAY ...
We were all together from eleven until six p.m., about
60 people at any one time, and slightly fewer disabled than
able-bodied which is a comfortable ratio. Not all the
disabled needed help, mind you. Those with CP did:
Emery, Jeff, Pete, Dana, for instance, are wheelchair
bound, and also need others to be 'hands' for them.
Jan however is a single amputee who walks with crutch-
es. She teaches skiing and could sit to the floor on her leg
and arise as easily. She needed no assistance.
Rhoda was my partner for an initial contact improvisa-
tion and then a verbal introduction. Having introduced
ourselves to each other, we were to then join a circle and
introduce our partner to the others in the group. I gave
Rhoda my history first. She replied with, "I have an over-
eating disorder. And I was molested as a child." It
seemed clear that she was introducing herself as alter-
abled. So not everyone had physical dis-ablements.
Now I looked around the room with a new attitude, and
a sense that even those of us with virtually no cause to
consider ourselves disabled might in fact be there for other
reasons that a good workout and altruism.
I asked Rhoda if I should introduce her as frankly as she
had introduced herself to me. She said I should. At the
end of that session a couple of women thanked her for her
forthrightness. Indeed, why shouldn't people simply de-
clare what happened and clear their air? Rhoda was seek-
ing an experience of safe touch. I believe she found it on
this weekend, as we all did.
We also got a lot of exercise and physical challenge. It
was at first difficult to manipulate a body in or out of a
Volume 17 1992
rolling wheelchair. People not in chairs quickly discovered
where the brakes were, and learned the advantageous ways
to extend the dance beyond the chair. Not that the chair
dido 't have a lot of possibilities. Certainly square-dancing
arm figures could be used between partners of different
abilities. And the chairs are designed for strength so it is
possible to clamber up on top of a seated partner, to lay
across the armrests, to catch a ride on a backstop or foot-
rest.
Several children spent little time in their chairs; being
small they spent a lot of time being carried, flown and
rocked.
Others were heavy to lift and manipulate, and all avail-
able contact improvisation skills were used to turn the
transitional moments into fluid ones. It certainly seemed
possible that someone would be dropped. But it became
clear that those who could leave their chairs enjoyed the
adventure, and those who couldn't such as Bjo Ashwill
with arthritis and a recently operated hand still in traction,
knew their limits, which were respected by their partners.
As the information that we were in a safe touch, safe
adventure environment dawned on us, the warmth grew.
First came safety, then came warmth. Then came ... well,
what one goes dancing for. Meeting people, doing and
seeing unusual things, finding physical challenge while
laughing. Being moved.
Our Western society has marginalized physicality. Not
just to mention the voluntary couch potatoes, or car pota-
toes, or office potatoes. We marginalize physicality by
smoothing the environment, contriving convenience. By
removing its challenges we reduce the environment's abili-
ty to stimulate our potentials. There is a trap laid for us by
city planners and architects, decorators and designers-the
couching of the country. We adore youth, but what with
one thing and another-parenthood, jobs, etc.-we lose it
too early, then cling to its superficial qualities with makeup
and varieties of cosmetic surgery. News services distribut-
ed a 'filler' this last summer: the same percent of the U.S.
population is overweight today as was in the 1960s. The
joggers are more evident, the potatoes are not in the public
eye.
So if it is normal for us to marginalize physicality, the
folk who are disabled are relegated to an even more sedate
existence. Emery, however, has found a way out. He is a
notorious bicycle rider. He tours Eugene for hours most
days, cycling 30-40 miles on a bike specially designed for
him. With this and dancing with Alito he has a body
packed densely with muscle; and when he is lifted, the
hoister quickly realizes that, although he appears slim and
cannot control many actions, his is an athletic body.
Gerry Overington, who is blind, finds most available
forms of exercise boring. Tandem cycling or running,
without the visuals, is just work for the legs and a little
wind on the face. Touch dancing with a sighted partner
allows Gerry to move his body freely, without worry about
the space. Gerry has become proficient at contact improvi-
sation because it provides the fun of moving, and because
he and his partner meet as equals in the sense of touch.
For the quads, paraplegics, CPs, etc., the exploration of
CQICI Sourcebook 233
spherical movement retains its thrills, and the kinetic plea-
sure of leaving the chair and having a dance which in-
cludes rolling, taking and giving weight, guiding and being
guided, accelerating, pausing, and balancing alone provides
enormous stimulation. Emery and Pete seemed to become
rapturous when dances went well.
The spirit of the event was nothing like the usual physi-
cal therapy. In fact there is no 'therapeutic contract' with
the participants. The contract is to experience contact
improvisation.
With the video and the performances of Friday evening
everyone had a notion of what it is. But teaching it physi-
cally is another, more complex, matter. A group including
various disabilities is like a United Nations of the senses.
Instructions must be translated into specifics appropriate for
those on legs, wheels, crutches and must be signed for the
deaf. Demonstrations must be verbalized for those who
can't see, which is in itself a translating skill, because
English is not a very flexible language in terms of the
body.
Questions and discussions may be slowed by the inabili-
ty of some of the participants to speak clearly, or at all.
Pete, for instance, communicates by glancing at letters and
numbers on a plexiglass board. Sighting through the plas-
tic and meeting Pete's gaze, the reader can see the letter
Pete is looking at When it is verbally identified, he
changes to the next letter. This is ingenious, if laborious,
but Pete's patience never seems strained. Several children
were both physically disabled and deaf, and Penny, a fluent
signer, was on hand to ensure they weren't left out
Bruce called the large group to order every so often and
talked about skills of weight-giving or rolling. Perhaps he
was not so concerned with imparting skills as changing the
atmosphere from the good-natured chaos which tended to
prevail to one where anyone who was confused., or in an
unhappy partnership could find ground and conscious
change.
The rest of us who were there to facilitate, Karen Nel-
son, K.J. Holmes, Riccardo Morrison, Alito and I, tended
to roam the gymnasium looking for difficulties. We would
expect at least some minor difficulties in such a huge
mixed group of relative non-dancers, but few if any were
found over the weekend. I think we tended to err on the
side of caution. Folk who weren't dancing were choosing
to watch or rest But their presence at the event spoke for
them. They had come to dance.
SUNDAY ...
And dance they did. All day Saturday, all day Sunday.
By Sunday afternoon the gym was spread with picnic
lunches and people were casually sharing their food. I sat
around a generous spread with a number of others, and
realized that most of it had been provided by Rhoda, with
her overeating disorder, who had brought enough for others
who might have overeating disorders. It was consumed.
People talked and laughter echoed often. Children of
participants played in and out of groups, appropriating
empty wheelchairs to play in. Being children of disabled
parents meant they knew how to use them.
One toddler had bells on her shoes. Her main amuse-
ment was to toddle, and the jingle on her feet let her moth-
er, a young blind woman, know where she was.
What her mother didn't see was that her daughter tod-
dled in and out of dancing spaces where adults were con-
tending with difficult balancing and fairly high-level physi-
cal forces. Occasionally gasps were raised among watch-
ers. But the child spent ten hours over the weekend doing
this and never came to harm. She just tinkled through
whizzing wheelchairs and under lifted adults, the bells
giving us some warning that she was there. Perhaps hav-
ing a blind mother gave her a sense of responsibility for
her own seeing and moving far beyond her years, because
rarely did anyone have to alter movement to accommodate
her, and finally everyone accepted that she could partici-
pate as she pleased.
Another woman brought two kids, a boy of about 8, and
a small, sweetfaced girl about 4. This child was not as
independent as tinkle-toes, and sometimes wanted to crash
her mother's dances-to join in the fun or reclaim her
mother's physical attention. Her brother however, with
many a strange noise and apparently casual intervention,
would divert his little sister. He would step in the way or
invent a pulling game, or distract her with a fantasy image.
Sometimes this didn't work. When she persisted, she was
welcomed into the dance by both dancers. Or mom would
see that her daughter did need some individual attention
and excuse herself to dance with her. The boy's job was
effective though. Part sheepdog, part Charlie Chaplin, he
managed to give his mother a lot of dancing time.
Emery
Pete's Story
Pete Theophanes, 19, had in Emery a strong ally in com-
munication. Emery had taken him out for events and knew
Pete's situation at home. When Pete needed to express an
urgent problem, Emery provided the background informa-
tion which clued the rest of us into the picture. We were
pulled into the thick of it
It happened during a meeting of the whole group, when
one of the facilitators had called for a round robin, in
which the group forms a circle and a few couples at a time
dance in the center. Outside the main group, a few of us
were circled around Pete, taking turns reading through his
letter board, trying to figure out what he wanted. Cerebral
palsy happens at birth. If born with an inability to con-
sciously control one's body, including speech, it can be a
struggle to convince the world to take one's mind serious-
ly. Pete's body has to be propped up in the wheelchair,
234 CQ/CI Sourcebook
Volume 17 1992
with both elbows tucked inside the armrests, or he slumps
alarmingly to one side. His wrists are flexed 90 degrees.
Pete could control only the direction of his eyes. Before
he learned to read there must have been little communica-
tion.
On Saturday he had a good time, I gather, because he
came again on Sunday, though against the wishes of his
grandparents, his guardians. They are devout Christians,
and Sunday is the day for church. Nonetheless, they al-
lowed Pete to return for the middle part of Sunday's work-
shop.
Pete didn't want to go home. He wanted to explain that
we should call his aide and tell him to come at 6 rather
than at 4 p.m. But as interpreters we were too tired to
keep this long sequence of letters in mind. Emery sat
pondering as several of us tried and failed. Suddenly he
got it and said, "What about your grandparents? We'll
have to phone."
Pete's excitement was contagious at this point. He was
grinning and sighting out his message: "Phone her, tell her
I'll be home at 6. And hang up before she can answer."
It came to me to make that call. I could not follow
Pete's script, though. I had been warned about the strength
of personality of Pete's grandmother, and in the event my
proposal of a two-hour extension for Pete was swept aside.
Pete had to get ready for church. His driver was coming.
These aides are sometimes in delicate relationships.
When Pete's aide learned what Pete wanted, he was tom
between Pete's needs and rights, and the agreed arrange-
ments. Pete was proposing to change that arrangement on
his own, yet if the aide granted Pete that power, he would
be dismissed, or in hot water, at least. Pete, at 19, is
legally able to determine his own schedule. And grandpar-
ents might be old-fashioned, stuck in their ways, and over-
protective. But an agreement had been made.
Those engrossed with Pete and this issue, an island
where time went by in the single letters of the complex
story, were Emery, Alito, the aide, myself and a young
woman named Gani. Though sympathetic to Pete's de-
sires, we were counselling a pragmatic approach: don't
jeopardize your aide and stress your grandparent's generos-
ity for just two more hours of fun. It could curtail future
chances.
Gani, to her credit, said, "Having heard their advice, if
you still want to stay I'll support your decision."
Pete needed that support. Circumstances had deprived
him of the power to execute his wishes. Friends coun-
selled against his direction. But at least one person put
Pete's wishes at top priority. Gani recognized his right to
do a thing, albeit a rash one, and kept her focus right there,
rather than looking sagely at the larger picture. When I
returned from the phone call and told Pete what his grand-
mother said, Pete emitted a peculiar sound. It was a shriek
through clenched teeth. The intensity of frustration was
ominous, but quickly passed-on the outside.
Pete was invited to a last dance, and was lifted into the
air by four dancers, and soon was smiling down at us as
they paraded by. His aide allowed this little extra time,
then Pete left; back to a world where there would be no
parades, nor much movement at all. He would, we were
told, be taken from bed in the morning to a couch, where
Volume 17 1992
he would watch T.V. and his spasming legs would be tied
to protect him from falling off. Or on fair days, he would
be wheeled into the yard for airing and sunning, his arms
tucked inside the armrests of his chair. No wonder he
enjoyed the movement this weekend.
I hope his tardiness didn't harm his relationship to his
aide, or to Emery who had invited him. Emery believes
Pete can live, as Emery does, in a place of his own with an
aide. Knowing someone who believes this encourages Pete
to try to achieve it. How does a person get from here to
there but by belief? What encourages belief like the agree-
ment of one's 'big brother'? Emery is important to Pete.
Until the last moment before DanceAbility began it was
not clear that video coverage could be afforded. Tom
Giebink, from Austin, agreed to exchange taping for partic-
ipation and made a visual record of the weekend.
The tapes usually focus on a pair of dancers and some
exciting moments were preserved. But beyond the immedi-
ate subjects the background extends like a savannah and
the event is rather like a wildlife film-herds on the Seren-
geti of Africa, with different animals grazing and interact-
ing peacefully, young ones tripping through the foreground,
encounters, withdrawals, peripheral activities.
This effect is heightened by the live music which was
provided on Sunday by Suse Millimon. It was amplified
voice and keyboard with long chords. The atmosphere it
made was meditative and earthy.
To shoot the fmal meeting Sunday afternoon, Tom sat in
the center of the circle around which we all sat or squatted,
hunkered down in. It was a moment for every voice to be
heard, or read. People spoke up when there was silent
space and they felt moved. Most participants thanked the
other participants, and the organizers. It was difficult not
to be a little teary inside. We bad spent three days togeth-
er and a warm bond had grown. Everyone bad bad fun,
been treated with dignity; so we respected each other and
felt an intense vicarious pleasure in each other's smiles and
laughter.
A large family group was beaded by the Klopen-Owens,
a couple from Corvallis, Caucasian, who have 4 kids-at
least 3 of whom were from India, two of whom were deaf,
one of these with CP as well. He was Dana, a small 9
year-old.
Dana is a bit athatoid, like Emery, with bent wrists, and
is unable to stand alone. At some point Sunday morning
Alito had placed him in my arms. He was a light bundle
and felt fragile. During this dance I became hopelessly
confused about Dana.
For one thing, he had frequent involuntary movement of
his head which was, in ordinary body language, 'no'.
When he became excited be squealed. His smile was
bright and handsome, but when he was asked a question,
the effort of answering caused it to fade. The sort of
CQICI Sourcebook 235
question I asked was, "Is this OK?" The smile would fade,
the head shake 'no'.
When carried on my shoulder, he shouted, "Dad, Dad!"
and shrilled a squeal. A bit insecure by this point, I as-
sumed he was frightened. Later I discovered he just want-
ed his dad to see him, like most kids.
But most perplexing was an event which occurred mid-
dance. The enormous gymnasium was constructed with a
raised center ceiling panel, and between this and one of the
side panels a forlorn basketball was lodged, having soared
and stuck. Lifting Dana high, I said, "Do you see that
basketball?" and he immediately said, "How did it get
there?"
So ordinary. Later I was told that Dana was deaf, and I
never heard another sentence from him. It does sometimes
happen; a state of grace slips in during an unconsidered
moment, and clarity emerges. But when self-conscious or
under social pressure, grace goes and communication is
strained with the distortions and complications attending
the affliction.
Penny used sign language with him, and we have on
tape what I take as an interesting misunderstanding be-
tween them. Alito was dancing with Dana, and Penny
chanced by. Alito asked her to ask Dana if what he was
doing was OK.
She sign-mimed the question. Dana thought it over. His
smile faded, his head shook from side to side, and with
effort he made a sound To me it sounded affmnative.
Penny thought it was negative and asked if he wanted to
go slower, if anything had hurt his body. Dana struggled
to reply. He had trouble coordinating 'yes' and 'no'. Now
he had to convey that nothing hurt, and that he loved to
fly.
Alito intuited what was going on and said, "Naw, he
wants to go fast, don't you?" Dana grinned. So Alito,
holding Dana in the cup of his belly, rocked over back-
wards, and continued to rock forward and back, higher and
higher. Dana's smile got bigger and wider.
Later I saw Dana's father flying him. With his hands
under Dana's arms, he swooped him up from the floor,
circling him high into the air and back to the floor. This
probably was what Dana was accustomed to.
As we were saying goodbye to each other in the final
group circle, Dana's mother and father spoke for their kids.
They thanked all of us who had touched their children's
lives, and Mrs. Klopen-Owen thanked us all for giving her
a day off. "We did hope that Dana would meet others like
him. It isn't easy for a little guy with CP to find a role
model. But luckily for him, Dana has found Emery, and
has something for him."
And with that, Mr. Klopen-Owen held up Dana's arm.
Clutched in the small hand was a yellow scrap of paper.
"His address!"
Dana's head was, for once, not wavering. His gaze was
fixed on Emery, and he grinned hugely as his arm was
raised.
Alito Alessi, Pete Theophanes and Emery Blackwell
at DanceAbility, 1991.

But then no one knew what to do. A protocol had been
tacitly accepted not to give aid unnecessarily. So his
parents did not convey the address, or Dana, across the
circle to Emery. Dana fidgeted off of his father's lap, and
a few feet beyond Then his coordination failed him and
he lay, limbs moving uselessly, still trying to get to Emery.
Emery was hip to it and reacted quickly. He heaved
himself off the several friends he was draped across and
heaved and rolled, just as in his solo, over the expanse of
the gym floor.
No one moved to help him, either.
Spasmodically, grotesquely, Emery humped over to
Dana. He attempted to take the paper, but a further prob-
lem emerged; neither he nor Dana could make their hands
work to make the transfer. Emery's fingers were all coil-
ing with effort, but not coordinating. Dana's hand could
not unclench. Emery began to emit groans.
Failing to refine his motor effort, Emery tried to envelop
Dana, address and all. Both were, at this point, on the
floor at angles to each other and no available coordination
created the cuddle which was intended In the attempt,
Emery was flailing Dana with his wrists, and emitting
involuntary roars of effort.
Dana was mostly without expression, looking dazed
Fortunately a smile appeared to reassure us viewers that he
was not frightened by the energy directed at him. He
might well have supposed that his role model was trying to
devour him. Dana managed to unloose the note. It fell
onto Emery's chest, then to the floor between them.
Emery knew he couldn't manage the finesse of picking a
paper up from the floor, and rose unsteadily to his knees
above Dana. His arms stretched out from his shoulders
and his wrists bent down with fmgers writhing like sea
anemonies. He looked like a mad magician triumphantly
incanting over his victim; or possibly about to topple on
his victim.
But then, all these images are beside the point. These
are masks, the body language thrown up by CP, which
cause a confusion in interpretation.
All Emery was trying to do was hug Dana, and this he
managed, .sort of. He rolled Dana up against his knees,
then sank down beside him, pinned him with his forearm,
and nuzzled Dana with his goatee. Dana grinned
Finally someone from the circle spoke. "Will anyone
describe what's happening for those who can't see?" It
was the stuff of old Russian novels, or operas, or long
silent films, but as I described it it flattened in my mouth
into a street comer encounter. What a high failure of
communication. Fifty-eight people saw a moment of dra-
ma, and the blind heard only the plot line. The sighted
heard my pitiful effort, but only a couple of people spoke.
Under the pressure of the moment we gave up and in
silence felt the gaps between the senses.
Emery meanwhile was declaiming •.• something. "Songay
ewiri whyyeahh ooeyer." "Someday," Emery was telling
Dana, "we will ride bicycles together." +

Alito Alessi and Emery Blackwell in Wheels of Fortune.
by Bjo Ashwill
The kleig lights click into total
black. A single round spotlight creates
a circle on the black, bare stage floor.
The audience hears a metallic rattle and
a thumping sound. Into the light,
lurching and then pausing, lurching and
then pausing, staggers a manual wheel-
chair. A man seated in the chair,
wearing a second manual wheelchair
like an Iron Maiden over his head and
torso, is pushed into the light by the
power of a man with cerebral palsy
walking on his knees. Two crutches,
hanging off the back handles of the first
chair, drag alongside this clunky, noisy
entourage.
The performance dance piece Wheels of
Fortune, created by Alito Alessi of Joint
Forces Dance Company and Emery
Blackwell, has begun. The two men
then explore the "in's" and "out's" of
wheelchairs, crutches and a single
straight backed kitchen chair.
The space, the props, the emotional
entanglements of physical shape, speed
and movement are used to their full
advantage. It is an emotionally power-
ful piece. Why? Is it the subject mat-
ter? Or is it the dancing? Is it the fact
that one man has a physical disability?
It is impossible to sort out all the factors
and isolate what exactly motivates the
positive response and feedback it
receives. Maybe it doesn't matter. The
end result is perhaps what is important.
But Emery Blackwell, a dancer and a
man with a disability, sometimes
wonders. It is hard to measure how
much of the response is to his talents as
a dancer and how much is ·the audi-
ence's preconceived notions of how
little a person with a disability can
actually accomplish. If the audience be-
lieves people with cerebral palsy can't
do much more than hang out in a chair
and spasm, then anything he does on
stage will create a "standing ovation."
Emery, the dancer, has a hard time
reading the responses accurately.
Honest feedback is what he wants.
An example of how people view him
throu~ their own disability "biases" or
"filters" came at a feedback session at
the Breitenbush Contact Jam in Oregon.
Emery had danced his solo piece There
and Baclc Again. Several of the dancers
made comments like how powerful his
smile was when he rose from the floor
and up onto his knees. And how sad
they felt when he went from the kneel-
ing position back to rolling on the floor.
To them, the dance meant that for a few
brief moments a person with a disability
rose up, nearly standing (like them), but
fell back into the agonies of disability.
When asked what was in his mind
when he choreographed the piece, Em-
ery said, "It's a piece about cycles. I am
a flower blooming. It grows to full
bloom and then dies, to bloom again.
It's about joy and spring."
238 CQ;t:I Sourcebook
Volume17 1992
Emery has been a man with a physical
disability all his life. Being Emery, the
dancer, is a new wrinkle. And he loves
it
Asked if he considered himself a danc-
er or someone who plays with dancing,
he responded, "I think in time I will
study to be a dancer. At first, it was
fun. I spent a few weekends and the
time moved on. Alito pushed me more
into dance and I got more into it after
that first performance of Wheels of For-
tune. After I worked with Alito all win-
ter, I got hooked. Then I started ques-
tioning if people took me as a dancer or
just as a gimp. I would label myself a
beginning intermediate dancer. I know
some basic Contact, but I feel I am new
with it. You know, if it weren't for
Alito, I wouldn't be dancing. He was
open-minded and positive. He even
reminds me that my own movements
are normal and natural. I keep forget-
ting, trying to dance like the able-bod-
ied. Me, thumping my knees on the
floor, is dancing. Not disability dance
technique-but just plain dancing."
All this "dance stuff' happened one
day, several years ago, in 1988. Emery's
first love was and still is bike riding.
Emery was wheeling down the side-
walk on his three-wheeled cycle, a hel-
met on his head, strapped in like an
astronaut straining against gravity and
pining for the open roads where he
could feel the wind racing past his face,
pushing against him in a rough and
friendly embrace, like wrestling with a
good buddy. Suddenly, a smiling man
flagged him down and handed him a
flyer, yapping on about some kind of
DanceAbility workshop where people
with disabilities and able-bodied people
get together and dance.
"It's called Contact Improvisation.
You'll love it," Alito continued.
"Sure," smiled Emery, taking his flyer.
Anything to appease this very friendly,
obvious nut "Sure, I'll come," he said
and wheeled off, not giving it another
thought.
The universe, in her gentle way, never
leaves things to just a single chance.
She places more than one hint in front
of a person. Emery's friend Keith came
by and asked Emery to go to this Con-
tact Improvisation workshop with him.
Emery shrugged, "OK." It would prob-
ably be an all right way to spend a
weekend. Why not. So he went.
Interesting stuff started to happen.

Emery in front of WOW Hall, Eugene, 1991.
Volume 17 1992
People with all kinds of disabilities
were sliding out of their wheelchairs
and rolling around on the floor, moving
with able-bodied people. That, in and of
itself, wasn't so unusual. Physical Ther-
apy and Therapeutic Recreation created
that kind of experience. What was
different was the feeling. The attitude.
These people weren't trying to make
Emery move faster or differently than
his body could or wanted to. These
people didn't sit there, waiting, with
disguised impatience as Emery tried to
catch up to them. No. These people
acknowledged his body, its particular
movement style and speed as perfectly
normal and acceptable. Just as Emery
had done since birth.
More than that, they actively appreci-
ated his movements as something
they'd like to learn to experience, to use
in their own repertoire in dances. Alito
has often said that he creates the Dance-
Ability workshops for selfish reasons.
Most people credit him with a do-good-
er attitude of bringing dance and move-
ment to the disabled. "Ain't he just
such a good-hearted guy?" Alito laughs.
"I do this because it brings me more
information I can use in my own explo-
ration of physical movement and dance.
Everyone has body movement habits.
In order to break my habits, I try on
other people's movements, like a new
hat or a shirt. I gain a great deal from
people with disabilities. They show me
how to move and experience my body
in new ways."
Emery laughs, "You learn by other
people's movements. It's the opposite
for me. I'm learning to relax and ex-
plore my own movements, without the
pressure from others to do it 'more
normally' or faster. It's freedom to feel
my own natural rhythms and be accept-
ed and enjoyed for being exactly who I
am."
Emery went to several DanceAbility
workshops, which are presented once a
year. After the second workshop, Em-
ery attended another Joint Forces
event-the Breitenbush Contact Jam, an
annual weeklong international gathering
of contact improvisors. At that first
jam, Emery realized there was more to
this dance than just having fun. Here,
he created and performed in his first
choreography, a group piece called
Bumped. Asked how he created Bumped,
Emery described it in this way: "I was
trying to get across that I am not dis-
abled and that anyone could become
disabled any time. Sometimes when
you are seen as disabled, you become
invisible. I was coming back to the jam
after spending a day at the Salem Legis-
lature lobbying for the rights of the
disabled. I was thinking about that
experience. They thought I was dis-
abled, but when I returned to the jam,
CQICI Sourcebook 239
photo: Cliff Coles
Alito Alessi and Emery Blackwell
in Wheels of Fortune.
these people did not think of me as dis-
abled. Hey, neat, I thought That
would be a neat dance. I was thinking
of my political work, how I always try
to tell them you have more chances of
becoming disabled than you do winning
the lottery!!
"So, at the jam, four of us were play-
ing around with the idea. I told them
the main idea and we worked from
that. Everything else just fell into
place." When someone was bumped
into the wheelchair, they became invisi-
ble and the others would continue danc-
ing, bumping into the wheelchair with-
out noticing it or the person in it Then
the person would get bumped out of
the chair and become visible and the
others would begin relating to them
again and someone else would get
knocked into the chair and become
invisible, etc. "The chair made the piece
either fast or slow. If you dance with a
chair, it governs the speed.
"I'll tell you something else," Emery
continued. "Dance has helped me reach
people more powerfully than my years
of lobbying the legislature have. As
President of OIL (Oregonians for Inde-
pendent Living), I have spent hours
(which sometimes feels more like years)
240 CQ;O Sourcebook
intellectually talking about equality,
equal access, that we are people too,
just like everyone else. I'd talk and talk
and make small progress. But one
dance and people just 'get' it. It opens
up communication in more equal ways."
Alito and Emery did a performance
for the Governor's Arts Awards at the
Hult Center in Eugene, Oregon. After
the show, Emery sat back, trying to be
out of the way, but he failed! These
people, white, rich, older, the same
people Emery lobbied at the State
House, came up to him. They lined up
to express their admiration about the
dance.
They responded to Emery in just the
same way the Eugene schoolchildren
did. Emery and Alito did their dance
for 8 different schools in a three week
period. Five thousand kids saw Emery
and Alito dance. A barrier had been
broken. It was OK to reach out and
talk to this human being.
The dance made people question their
own enculturated thinking. The Wheels
of Fortune dance is all about who sup-
ports whom, what helps what, who is
disabled and who is able-bodied.
Sometimes the wheelchair supports
Emery, sometimes Alito. Sometimes
Emery supports the chair, or Alito
wears the chair, or a "normal" chair (one
without wheels, a mere Chevrolet com-
pared to a Cadillac) is the support. It
all twists and turns, changing like a
kaleidoscope. That is the truth. And
that truth is different than the set-in-
concrete notions of the general "tempo-
rarily able-bodied" person. They think
the chair holds up the person, period.
End of discussion. The piece dances
around the question of 'What is
normal?"
That isn't a question that Emery
spends much time on. He knows what
is normal. His body and the way it
moves is normal That's the way it has
been from birth. Unlike a person who
meets with an accident later on, Emery
has no memories of it being different.
The trouble is that other people haven't
always agreed. They spent time and
energy trying to get Emery's body to
move differently and faster.
When he was growing up, he'd get
around by crawling on his knees every-
where. The floor, the lawn, the gravel,
it didn't matter. Then he got a manual
wheelchair. He'd propel himself
around by pushing himself backwards,
looking over his right shoulder to see
where he was going. As a result, the
skin on his knees went soft, no longer
calloused, making him unable to crawl
over the ground without pain. Now,
that was a disability.
At the age of 18, Emery got a power
wheelchair and suddenly had to change
his body habits and look forward in
order to avoid crashing into walls and
running over people. For the first time
in his life~ he felt disabled. It was a
change from old habits. He had to
adjust to it. Feeling disabled was not
an always kind of thing; it would occa-
sionally pop into Emery's head, but
mostly, he was thinking about bike
riding.
After he was introduced to the bike,
he spent some time struggling, barely
able to get around the block. With
practice and time, he now tours around
the state, on weeklong treks, zooming
downhill at 35 miles an hour. What
Emery loves about bike riding is the
same thing he's learning to love about
dance. Freedom, acceptance and being
in control independently.
Contact Improvisation has made some
changes in Emery's life. Physically, he
has learned to move more smoothly
with better balance. On bike rides,
Emery used to push hard and with
jerky movements to propel his bike on
the 40 or more mile treks. Now, his
pacing is softer, smoother and more
powerful A clear demonstration of his
capability at better balancing happened
in the bathtub. He fell and his body
used the new dance techniques of roll-
ing he had learned. A safe landing.
He learned to have more fun. Actual-
ly, that's not true. Emery always has
fun. He learned a new way to have fun
when he began dancing. One of the
major commitments Alito and Emery
have in their dance work is to have fun
as they are dancing. If they are having
fun, this will telegraph to the audience
who will be able to relax more and have
fun with them.
Emery discovered the power of the
unspoken word-the speech of dance
and movement that tells stories and
feelings more completely than the spo-
ken word. Although the main reason
Emery dances is for the fun and for the
personal experience and satisfaction, he
can't avoid the educational aspects of
Volume 17 1992
his dancing. Simply because he is dis-
abled, everything he does teaches.
Dance, Emery has learned, is a marvel-
ous, positive teaching tool. He will be
volunteering his time giving perfor-
mances in the schools and Alito is
working on grant proposals to create a
weeklong DanceAbility workshop next
year.
At the DanceAbility workshops, peo-
ple have more time to interact with him,
Emery notes-to experience him long
enough to break through their own
empathy for able-bodied people who
have to slow down their movements to
dance with him. Emery forgets he's
disabled.
At the same time, dancing with able-
bodied people makes him realize he is
disabled. Makes him aware of the dif-
ferences. Is that good or bad? Maybe
both. It depends on how he's feeling at
that moment about himself. If he's in a
good mood and feeling good about
himself, it's good. If he's tired or feel-
ing less sure of himself, it feels uncom-
fortable. It is all a part of the learning
process.
Emery in There and Back Again, Breitenbush, OR, 1991.
mental barriers. Often people start out
hesitant or outright reluctant "It is the
difficulty understanding my speech, I
think, that distresses them the most
After they realize I am an intelligent
human being, they feel relieved," Emery
says.
An interesting thing happens for Em-
ery at these workshops. There are peo-
ple at the workshop that are "more
disabled" than he is. And Emery finds
that he tends to relate more with the
able-bodied participants than with the
more disabled. Having to slow his
movements down to dance with people
with more severe disabilities than he
has (and less dance experience), Emery
began to understand and have more
At one jam, Emery danced with Gerry
Overington of England. Gerry is blind.
Emery had never danced with someone
who couldn't take visual cues as to
what the next move might be. Now
Emery was experiencing what able-
bodied people experience when they
dance with him: assuming they need to
take more responsibility for leading,
initiating the movement. This was a
somewhat new experience for Emery.
His dances have evolved from a passive
responding to a more active pursuit and
exploration of movement in contact
with others in space. But, isn't that
what Contact Improvisation is all about?
Emery also has gained the personal
freedom of acceptance. Among dancers,
Volume 17 1992
he is accepted as a dancer. His own
normal body movements and speeds
are fine just the way they are.
You know, it's funny that society has
decided to label someone who walks
differently or not at all as disabled.
Often, it's the so-called "able-bodied"
person with fine motor skills, that has
disabilities-like not being able to get
along well with others, or express their
emotions, or feeling unhappy and de-
pressed. When people who are strug-
gling with their own pain see someone
physically disabled, they often project
their initer feelings of inadequacy and
pain onto the disabled person. They
latch onto the disabled person, wanting
to help them, soothe their supposed
pain and make everything all better.
The trouble is, it doesn't work. It back-
fires. Because it's not the disabled per-
son's pain. It's theirs. People often say
how it hurts them to hear Emery's
knees thump on the floor during his
dance, thinking it hurts him. Emery
says, "Sure, sometimes it hurts. Mostly,
it doesn't. But everything in life costs a
little pain. If you want to really help,
let me experience my own pain my own
way."
People at dance workshops are often
worried they will hurt Emery, but he's
as tough as hickory wood. By being
afraid to roll on him or put weight on
him, they lessen the dance experience
for both of them. Emery tries to tell
them it's OK; after they get over their
original resistance, Emery gets bounced
off the walls and loves it
In the workshops, it also happens that
people often talk to somebody else
when they are talking to Emery, as
though they think he is deaf as well as
has speech problems.
But these are minor annoyances, ones
Emery meets with every day of his life.
What Emery gains from dance is well
worth the difficulties. Dance has wid-
ened his circle of friends. Like bike
riding, it has given him an opportunity
to meet people on an equal basis.
Dance speaks for him, breaking down
barriers and walls like so many tinker
toys.*
This article is 'based on conversations between
Emery Blackwell, Alita Alessi and the author
in March, 1991.

THE MOVING BODY
an integrated movement course
Exposed to Gravity workshop, Berkeley.
by Alan Ptashek
In the Fall of 1990, with Bruce
Curtis, co-founder of The Exposed To
Gravity Project, I began teaching a
course at The University of California
at Berkeley called The Moving Body.
Having facilitated movement work-
shops for disabled and non-disabled
adults, adolescents and children for
five years, Bruce and I were interested
in bringing our work into the universi-
ty setting where movement exploration
could be sustained throughout a se-
mester-length period of time.
The city of Berkeley bas one of the
most visible and active disabled com-
munities in this country. At the uni-
versity, several organizations are cen-
tered around the creation of programs
242 CQ/CI Smircebook
Volume 17 1992
Bruce Curtis (bottom) and Alan Ptashek of The Exposed to Gravity Project, in The Moving Body class, UC Berkeley.
and services for both the public and
student disabled populations. Despite
the efforts of independent recreational
sports programs on campus, disabled
students lacked support and inclusion
from the Physical Education or Dance
Departments. While issues such as
liability and accessibility were often
heard, there clearly were no university
instructors who had the orientation and
skills with which to develop a viable
course where people of different abili-
ties could move and interact with one
another.
The Exposed To Gravity Project
was given county and campus organi-
zational support and we designed and
implemented a course through the
Department of Dance and Dramatic
Arts. Undergraduate and graduate-
level students would receive academic
credit and provisions were made for
allowing public participation as well.
Few administrators nor faculty fully
understood the history or scope of our
work and they had no precedent for a
course of this nature from any other
college or university in the country.
With a class of twenty five students,
we had an even ratio of those with and
without physical disabilities. Students
were majoring in and doing graduate
studies in dance, theatre, psychology,
anthropology, social welfare and pub-
lic health. Disabilities included multi-
ple sclerosis, cerebral palsy, spina
bifida, polio, arthritis and spinal-cord
related injuries resulting in paraplegia
and quadriplegia. Discussing disabili-
ties as limitations and differences that
are not always apparent, several people
in the class considered themselves as
having "hidden disabilities," such as
chronic fatigue syndrome. As in all of
our past workshops, there were mixed
feelings of excitement, caution, confu-
sion and an appreciation for new pos-
sibilities.
As a movement practice, Contact
Improvisation has been at the center of
our work, providing a coherent physi-
cal language for all participants. The
focus on physicality and sensation
established a ground for the stimula-
tion and discussion of personal, social
and political issues that became imme-
diately relevant and integral to The
Moving Body. This course is one that
feeds into areas that are societal, clini-
cal, bio-medical, theoretical, educative,
psychological, artistic, performance-
oriented and, largely, improvisational.
Steven E. Brown, in his article,
"Creating a Disability Mythology,"
adds what may be another component
area of the course, or an entire con-
tainer holding all of the before-men-
tioned areas. He says:
"Language, humor, music, stereo-
types, history, heroes. Each of
these shared experiences comprise
elements of a culture. People
with disabilities are beginning to
recognize that we, as a group,
have been developing each of the
aforementioned topics into our
own culture-a disability culture.
I am convinced that every commu-
nity harbors many heroes with
disabilities. I believe it is time to
work on the development of a
disability mythology. Myth is
Volume 17 1992
CQICI Sourcebook 243
more accessible than a document-
ed history of events; myth permits
us to be creative and expand our
tale into a story to be repeated
and retold for generations, a form
understood by every other cul-
ture."
We live in a culture that holds strict
definitions and value judgements about
what it sees as moving and not mov-
ing. With so much of daily motion
and perception dictated by task, func-
tion and particular examples of pro-
ductivity, movement at deeper levels
than external appearances are less
valued, ignored, and, at worst, unper-
ceived. How we are being seen by
others influences our self-image, pres-
ence and movements in the world. In
our classes, I feel that we were creat-
ing new layers of a collective story, a
body politic or body mythology.
Myths take shape in a ritualized space.
Several guest teachers (Dance and
Drama Therapist, Bronwyn Warren;
Feldenkrais teacher, Lawrence Gold-
farb; and Dance Therapist, Neala
Haze) were invited to present their
work during the semester. Neala
facilitated a session in Authentic
Movement. Having each student move
with eyes closed, "witnessed" by the
group, was a beautiful and evocative
ritual space. We were able to appreci-
ate the most subtly articulated move-
ments of each person, sensing and
referring to our own bodies for the
depth of response we gave one
another.
The experiential practice of the
course was augmented with readings
on dance, performance history, body
systems, developmental movement,
perception, sensory awareness, somatic
therapies, communication theories and
the use of touch in human experience.
Students kept journals and papers were
part of the requirements for academic
credit.
Many of the early classes brought
forth the awkwardness and anxiety
around physical touch between dis-
abled and non-disabled partnering. It
is very common for non-disabled
people to feel hesitant and fearful of
having physical contact with a person
in a wheelchair or one whose physical
form is shaped and moves differently
than their own. Often it is assumed
that the disabled person is brittle, in
pain or umesponsive to being touched.
In the class, students were strongly
encouraged to express their needs,
physical functioning and capabilities to
one another. This exchange of person-
al information helped to relax them
and elicit trust of an improvised and
collaborative spirit.
When encouraged to leave their
wheelchair or crutches through their
own movement or with assistance, the
initial experiences of many disabled
students (and of the non-disabled
assistant) ranged from extreme disori-
entation and vulnerability, to pleasure
and intense joy. A contained and safe
Student in The Moving Body class, University of California, Berkeley.
t::

2

244 CQ/CI Sourcebook
Volume 17 1992
Bruce Curtis and Charlene Curtiss, Berkeley, 1990.
environment in which to express feel-
ings is very important in this context.
The process of dialoguing after danc-
ing allowed for us to speak from
changes in self-image and self-organi-
zation with increased awareness, sensi-
tivity and a mutual regard for one
another as moving, embodied beings in
the world
Our learning environment carried as
much influence as any of the specific
skills for falling and supporting
weight. Deane Juhan, in his book
Job's Body (Station Hill Press, 1987),
conveys a layer of this learning envi-
ronment from a physiological under-
standing: "The process is anything but
random or trial and error. It is highly
specific, and it is the periphery which
helps to organize the connections in
the central nervous system, not an
organized central nervous system
which reaches out to innervate the
periphery. It suggests that the use of
touch and sensation to modify our
experience of peripheral conditions
exerts an active influence upon the
organization of reflexes and body
image deep within the central nervous
system." Clearly, not all of the class
participants came with the same inten-
tions and interests. Most students,
both disabled and non-disabled, loved
the freedom to play and seek new
physical forms, to feel themselves
buoyant and resilient, fearless and
beautiful in their dance.
In the past year, I've been facilitat-
ing groups and workshops for profes-
sionals who work with or wish to
extend their practice to physically
disabled men and women. While
working with movement and somatic
practitioners, the focus is on how we
make contact with ourselves while
touching, supporting and moving with
someone who has a physical disability.
We look at ways to establish more of
a peer relationship. This process is a
risky one. Conventional and tradition-
al techniques are challenged, while
mutual dependency and the unknown
are brought into the relationship. This
is a process that medical science has
little familiarity with.
The impact of this course as ex-
pressed through responses from the
students and faculty of The Moving
Body strongly indicate a need and
desire for the continuance of this work
in university, clinical, community and
artistic settings. *
The Moving Body was videotaped and is
available to the public (see Dance Map in
this issue).
