PATHS OF CHANGE notes on contact improvisation
in an institutional setting
a lecture by Aat Hougee
delivered along with an improvised performance
by Karen Nelson and KJ Holmes
at the 7th European Contact Teachers Conference
Geneva, Switzerland
October 28, 1991
First introduction
There must be a deeper reason for me standing here and
for Karen and KJ being here, with me on stage, at the same
time. An important reason is that it will certainly be less
boring this way. Probably the best reason.
Many of the decisions I have taken in my professional life
during the last 17 years seem to have come out of nowhere.
I don't dance, I am not an artist. but in my work as an
organizer of art. I do try to follow my intuition. This is
about the sixth lecture I am giving in collaboration with
performers. So it might be time to find out why I have been
making that choice.
[Karen reading Aat's notes]: ''To learn
about dance, I had to fall in love with
a dancer, and another one and anoth·
er one. I know, it just comes out in
words and figures, but I've gone as far
as I could. Much too far, but which
dancer doesn't ••• "
Aat Hougee was co-director of the Modem Dance
Department of the State Theaterschool in Amsterdam,
Holland (now called the School/or New Dance Develop-
ment, SNDD),from 1975 to 1989. He currently directs the
Center for New Dance Development (CNDO), a department
of the College of Arts in Arnhem, The Netherlands, which
he co-founded in 1989.
These institutions are conservatories, in effect, specializ-
ing in new forms of training dancers that have been devel-
oping in the U.S. and Europe over the last twenty years.
Putting myself in the position of a lecturer means that I
will have to use words to communicate my ideas. By using
words and using a language that most of us will understand.
we tend to create a situation that is quite characteristic for
most educational systems: it is based on the presence of those
who know, or pretend to know, and those who don't know,
or pretend not to know. It is a clear division of roles in
which most of our ideas about learning find their roots, but
somehow I am strongly under the impression that it is exactly
this division of roles that keeps both the person in the role of
the knowing as well as those who opt for the not-knowing
role, from real learning.
246 CQ/CI Sourcebook
Volume 17 1992
[Kl reading]: "As a kid in school I
used to know everything better than
everybody else. One day the teacher
made clear to me that I didn't know
anything. That load has stayed with
me through the years and it's only
whlle watching tv or reading my mys·
teries, that I feel secure. Standing
here, somebody will come up with a
pin, and the balloon will be gone."
When I started preparing my appearance here, some
thoughts came to mind that seem to be quite closely related
to the work that all of the people here are involved in. It has
to do with the sharing mode of Contact Improvisation and
about the challenging aspect of improvisation in general. It
seems to be a philosophical problem as well: by using
language to enter and clarify aspects of a field that goes
beyond language, one immediately seems to reduce the level
of thinking and perception.
What we want to do here is to create a simple situation
which might give us some more insight than just words. I
prepared a text including several subjects that deal with the
relation between and the development of improvisation,
education and an institution. At the same time I would like
to offer you a second line of thought-personal, emotional,
non-rational. Actually it is that line of thought, some flashes
coming from my personal life, my motivation and most of all
my fears, that might have been crucial for many of the
decisions I have made in my career and for most of the
thinking I have developed
At this point in my life I think it's possible and certainly
more objective, if that's what we are going for, to include
these aspects; maybe what I'm trying to say is: once we were
convinced that we were on our way, finding the truth, but as
we struggled along we started realizing that there is no other
truth than the one we are creating ourselves.
This personal material I handed out to Karen and KJ and
they will work with it, confronting themselves, each other and
me with material that is probably closer to not knowing and
sharing than what I have to say.
We agreed on one thing: we won't talk at the same time.
Language as a means of communication tends to be quite
vulnerable. And then: your role as an audience, you can take
it passively if you like, but there will certainly be one slot
where you will be openly invited to come in, but if you feel
the need to enter before that, or after, go ahead
Volume 17 1992
----..
A second introduction
About watching improvisation. When we go to see a set
performance we can expect that the choreographer has
developed or has tried to develop work in which he/she
knows what will happen from beginning to end We, as an
audience, know that the choreographer and the performers
know, and with that knowledge we will watch the perfor-
mance.
With improvised work, it's different. The improvising
performer enters into a process of discovery and although
certain parameters might be known, the performer will
confront himself with unknown material and will force
himself to take decisions through which new, unknown
territories will be entered. Sometimes these decisions will
lead to clear solutions, but just as often there will be empti-
ness, a search for solutions. An audience watching this kind
of work needs to be in a different state of mind than an
audience witnessing set work. When we buy a ticket at the
box-office for a set performance, it's almost like buying a
product in a store: whether we like the product or not, one
thing will be clear-it's supposed to be a complete product,
meant to be consumed like it is and we don't have to add
something unless the instructions say we have to, but in
general, the product is ready for use.
An improvised performance will confront us with a very
different situation: the ticket we buy at the box-office does
not stand for a finished product. Those tickets I would like
to call "shares." By buying one, the spectator becomes a
share-holder: sharing the risk that the performer is taking,
being present at the performance in a state of mind which
will equally value brilliant solutions as well as struggle and
despair, exciting moments as well as emptiness.
CQICI Sourcebook 247
(cont'd)
Some history
[Kl & Karen reading together]: "When
I got this job, now 17 years ago, I was
nobody, but nobody knew. It was
1975 and having some fantasy would.
open doors. I had never seen any
dance and after some weeks this Dutch
choreographer told me that if I want-
ed to do something for the dance in
Holland, I should quit my job. I had
to cry, since what else could I do with-
out a real profession.''
It was 1975 when I entered the field of dance education.
I was 29 years old, had no history in dance, but Amsterdam
was still a playful city where fantasy was valued as a valid
motivation for getting a job.
It would be quite an interesting story to lead you thr_ough
the development of the Modem Dance Department's program
and staff, especially since in that development, in the organi-
zation as well as in the curriculum, the anarchistic force and
strength of improvisation has been quite striking. But what
I think is more interesting here is to look at the development
of the students during those years and how that development
relates to the material the school has been offering and is
offering now: what were the abilities and needs of the
students at that time and what has changed since.
In 1975 the main educational objective seemed to be: to
teach students how to move, how to use their bodies in a
more complete way, to change their thinking about movement
and dance--that dance is about movement, that it is a
creative act, and that copying historical form, suffering and
sweating, are not the right ingredients to train a dancer. At
that time we would get two types of students. There were
those who saw being a dancer as meeting with accepted
standards, to be reached through intensive daily training,
strongly dominated by imitation of existing form. Most of
those students, however, were lacking the discipline to work
in such a way, partly due to the times we were living in.
Also, those students often had interesting ideas, where the
traditional training wouldn't give them the right skill to
realize those ideas.
There were other students who came without these kinds
of traditional expectations about their training. In many cases
disappointed with previous theoretical university studies, they
were ready to dive into this new world of explorative
physical and mental work. Quite a few of those students,
however, tended to take the work as a vehicle for self-
discovery. They were often not very motivated to be
performers, nor were they very interested in stretching their
248 CQICI Sourcebook
physical abilities too far. One could have blamed them for
that, but looking back, I am still grateful that these people
had the courage to support new approaches and by doing that
enabled the work, its content as well as its methods, to
become more clear, more defined. One has to realize that at
that point in time, although this was an official school in The
Netherlands, outside this school the work was not given much
value.
Results came slowly in those days: it would take the
average student about four years to become a mover, a dancer
who would show an integrated physical knowledge.
All this is about 15 years ago now: students who enter the
school in 1991 seem to be able to get to this level of physical
knowledge in not more than one or two years. It is clear to
me and to those who have helped develop the program, that
Contact Improvisation has made a strong contribution to this
process. As a very active approach to body-mind integration,
Contact seems to honor the need for pure physical work as
well as for the development of physical and mental sensitivi-
ty. Or simply said: with the arrival of Contact Improvisation,
a lot of energy entered into the work that we had been doing
until then. It would give us a new approach to training, an
explorative and creative way to learn physical skills.
Although we shouldn't underestimate the role of Contact
Improvisation and other new approaches to dance in relation
to the development of the learning-process, there is at least
one more phenomenon that is often neglected but that has
actually been quite crucial for the development of the
program. Through the years it seems that not only teaching
methods become more clear, and that certainly happened at
the school in Amsterdam, but ideas and even physical
knowledge, through time, seem to gain acceptance within our
thinking even without our ever actually having been confront-
ed with them. Certain aspects of physical knowledge that
Volume 17 1992
were once new to all of us, seem now to contain sufficient
known elements to enable both students with a more tradi-
tional background, as well as those without any experience,
to go through physical changes much quicker than when we
started with this work. It is because of this process, that's
mainly guided by time, that the way we teach and what we
teach and the way we build a program will have to change
continuously.
An open space for audience participation
[KJ reading from scraps of the text she
and Karen had torn up]: "After six
years she said she never I she asked
me for my hand to read I while she
touched the inside of my I crying and
then I my position doesn't too often"
C.I. as part of an institutional curriculum
It was 1985. The first Contact Teachers Conference took
place at the school in Amsterdam. Steve Paxton was there,
not attending the conference, but teaching at the school,
working with improvisational scores. One afternoon we were
standing in the ball and through the window in the door we
could see the meeting of the Contact teachers. At that
moment, Steve said: "Somehow I would like to open the door
and say 'no'." This is an anecdote, maybe I made it up
myself, maybe it was really said, but even if I made it up
myself, I experienced this as one of these moments that
became crucial for the development of my thinking since.
When Contact Improvisation entered the school, which
happened through an introduction by Pauline de Groot
[around 1980] and a little later through workshops taught by
David Woodberry, Steve Paxton, Lisa Nelson, Nancy Stark
Smith, Christie Svane, Danny Lepkoff and Mark Tompkins,
it did add many new elements to our thinking about dance,
improvisation and especially about dance-training.
Although strongly related to other forms we were using at
that time-release-work, alignment and several improvisation-
al approaches-Contact seemed to work as an integrative
force as well as offering a very physical alternative approach
to training. For us it felt new in terms of vocabulary, and
also in the teacher/student relation it offered new exciting
forms. It seemed to be open, explorative, daring. I remem-
ber that around that time the Trisha Brown Company came
Volume 17 1992
I
)
to the school for a lecture/demonstration, and several of the
more traditional Dutch choreographers were there as well.
One of them asked about the training of the dancers, suggest-
ing that they certainly would have a strong traditional
background. It was Randy Warshaw who answered the
question saying: "No, my only training was Contact Improvi-
sation." It felt like a true moment of Victory!
After this first intensive introduction to Contact Improvisa-
tion, we considered giving it a more permanent place in the
program, not just in the form of intensive workhsops, but on
a regular basis. At that moment, looking at the content and
format of the work, we were certainly convinced that C.L
could offer a creative and realistic alternative to replace at
least part of the more traditional technique classes. But the
problem was that unfortunately there were no teachers
available who would be willing to teach on a regular basis.
We did try to organize a regular jam, but that initiative,
which came from the students, only lasted for a few weeks.
In the years after, we kept bringing guest-teachers to teach
C.L workshops. It always would stimulate the enthusiasm in
the school, and time after time we tried regular scheduling,
using local teachers as well as long-term guests, but it never
really succeeded. Contact never took the place that we
thought it deserved.
An important reason for why this happened can probably
be found within C.L itself.

[Karen & KJ reading together]: "She
asked me to find 'something that is
really yours'. But there was nothing
left that wasn't connected to dance
and to other people."
C.L has mainly been developed (outside of institutions) in
short workshops with students who made a free choice to do
this work and who were not overloaded with all the other
approaches a school program offers. In those workshops,
methods of teaching were developed in relation to the specific
demands of short and ()pen worksllop ~ituatic:ms. Cl:>ntact
Improvisation was and is not about· a historically defmed
form, like most of the other approaches to dance used in
education, but about exploration in a democratic and social
environment.
Students who study within an institutional situation seem
to develop a tendency to pass responsibilities on to their
teachers: "You tell me what I have to do, I might protest, but
if you don't tell me I will protest as well." Work that is
taught through this principle will normally fit in easily.
Contact Improvisation worked in the school program as long
as it was set up as a very special event. The lack of authori-
ty within the work made it impossible to use on a regular
basis as a replacement for more defined dance forms.
All this was quite a while ago. A lot has changed since,
especially when we look at the expectations and mentality of
the students who join the school. I think by now C.L could
have become part of the regular curriculum. But again it
didn't. Why?
During the first fifteen years of development of the school
we collected a whole range of alternative methods to train
dancers. Finding out about these methods, bow they can be
used in a school situation, how they are relating to each
other, etc. took a lot of time and energy. Through regular
workshops, C.I. played an important role in this process.
Although we managed to learn more and more about these
methods, and especially about how to use them in a more
250 CQ/CI Sourcebook
----
efficient way, we realized at a certain point that studying
these methods should never become a goal in itself. And so
we made the choice to use elements of them in the program
in an integrated way, but not to use any of the methods in a
pure form as a specialist option.
Dutin_g these years in Amsterdam,. people unconnected to
the school would often make remarks about the work being
too introverted, not outgoing enough. At that time we would
become quite defensive when remarks like that were made.
Understandable. New approaches are hardly ever clearly
recognized and we were also in the middle of a process of
discovering the applications of new methods that certainly did
require a reflective attitude. Combining this process-oriented
work with more form-oriented traditional methods was
something many of the students were asking for: but in our
opinion at that point in time, we saw these as being conflict-
ing approaches. I don't think we ever lost our belief in the
importance of form, but how to get to that non-historical
form, how to develop a way of working that would allow an
ongoing process of change in form, that was a new path we
had to find first, through exploring all those different methods
that connect physical development with the use of imagina-
tion.
[Karen reading]: "It took a long time
and I found this small green spot that
was mine and It became bigger and
bigger and I took the plane and ended
up in the mountains and got married
again soon after."
Volume17 1992
During my last years as a director of the school in Amster-
dam, a model was developed where the different alternative
"techniques" were having a clear position in the program,
through regular classes or through carefully planned intensive
workshops. Although it felt as if we had finally reached the
point where we were able to give full value to the new work,
there were doubts coming up as well.
At a certain point, one of the teachers, who had been
teaching t'ai chi for many years, retired. Students and staff
were asking: "Who will be teaching t'ai chi next year?". My
first reaction was-we'll have to find a replacement. My
answer was, however: "Nobody. We won't have t'ai chi next
year." At that moment I realized that making a program
based on subjects would, through time, inevitably lead to a
static situation, comparable with the situation most traditional
modem dance schools are in, working with methoc:ls and
ideas of people who (artistically) died long ago.
In our opinion, a school for dance should have one main
objective: to train students to be artists who will be able to
develop new forms through the use of physical means. An
objective like this does not allow any limitations. Methods
to develop creative and physical skills are just methods to
reach a goal that lies beyond these methods. Contact
Improvisation is one of them and any artist using improvisa-
tion as a way to discover new form, will not accept the
limitations that are part of this method, or of any method, but
will in fact include anything that will support this process.
Volume 17 1992
Does that mean, since many elements of Contact Improvi-
sation have been defined, that that's the end of C.L?
Certainly not. C.L has added several innovative aspects to
the training of dancers that cannot be found in other ap-
proaches. Part of teaching C.I. is teaching students to find
improvisational solutions within a certain frame of thought.
At the same time, artistically, we want students to break
through existing frames of thought and we want teachers in
their teaching to be clear about that. Because that is exactly
the lesson we learned from our battle with previous approach-
es to dance education: the importance of teaching the creative
process instead of already-defined form, teaching physical
skill through the use of physical and creative imagery, instead
of forcing students to go through forms that are no longer
connected with this time. Teaching C.I. in these terms means
teaching the knowledge that has been developed tl:lrougfi tfils ·
work, but it means at the same time teaching a mentality that
will stimulate a next generation to go beyond the results of
that: to fmd new solutions, even if that would mean the
disappearance of C.L as it is known to us at this moment.
I realize that I am able to say much of what I say because
I have been able to work from a relatively comfortable
position. Comfortable in the sense that we did get the space
and the funding to go through this development.
When I look around me in Europe, I see many studios
offering workshops. It is great that that happens, but most of
the workshops are quite short and they are often not able to
offer sufficient continuity for students, nor for teachers.
Students are not able to receive government support for their
studies and because of fmancial reasons most of the studios
are not able to select their students.
In an institution there is continuity, for students as well as
for staff, and we are able to develop material over a longer
period of time. We do have problems as well. Motivation
can be one of them, sometimes things can be too easy.
It is inevitable that, slowly, alternative approaches to dance
will enter most of the existing dance institutions. The
question is, however: in what form will that happen and what
status will they get. Most institutions work through a simple
model in which the teaching is based on a clear image of
what a student who has gone through the program should
CQ/CI Sourcebook 251.
look like. This is not just a matter of style, it is a very
specific idea about education. But even institutions like that
will, as time goes by, start including new approaches. Not
the process-aspect of the new work, but probably the form of
it. And since those forms are certainly more interesting and
connected with this time than forms they have been using
until now, it will be experienced as quite a revolution.
However, when it's about improvisational work, it could be
like a trap. Improvisation, I think, including C.1, basically
is an anarchistic phenomenon. It is about finding new
solutions. Changing existing structures and allowing it into
a static educational system would mean, if we are not just
teaching a form, the beginning of the destruction of that
system, unless one would adjust the work itself to make it fit
into the system.
Within 20 years from now the Dutch National Ballet will
include Contact duet forms in their grand gala performances
and every ballet school will have their weekly Contact hour.
In the year 2010, C.I. will have taken over the place that
Graham Technique occupies now. But also in the year 2010
there will be some people struggling somewhere in a small
studio, maybe in a few institutions, trying to develop work
that goes beyond Contact. And ·if there happens to be a
conference that year, I am sure Steve Paxton will appear,
look through that window, and whisper "no" again. *

around at all. I wish there was no

'
One of the reasons why we have been able to develop two
schools in The Netherlands has been the fact that we were
able, and made the conscious choice, to enter the institutions
on a formal level. We gained power, not through the work
itself. The work that was done in the studios, the perfor-
mances we made, hardly ever received any positive recogni-
tion. It was on the level of management, educational
organization and strategy that we gained respect and estab-
lished our role. By doing this we managed to create a
comparatively safe environment in which the seeds that were
planted by many artists in this field could develop. Within
this framework there was no need for compromise and
especially no need to let the work establish itself in a too
recognizable and static form.

JUMPING PARADIGMS
a response to Aat Hougee
"Once we were convinced that we
were on our way finding the truth,
but as we struggled along we started
realizing that there is no other
truth than the one we are creating
ourselves."
-Aat Hougee
Dear Aat,
There may be wisdom in this remark.
If it were shouted in a crowd I would
feel it deeply. But it comes from you
in your brown brick ivory tower. I
read it through again: .. . no other truth
than the one we are creating our-
selves," and see that it has a self-
serving logic.
Also, who are "we," here: The staff
and teachers at your school? Them,
plus the students? The whole human
race?
Whomever it may be, suppose some-
one is off creating a truth of their
own; do we then have two equal but
different truths? What about those
who found truth for themselves in
times gone by? What will happen to
your truth as future generations create
their own?
None of these seem durable, singular
truths. They seem to be temporary,
subjective truths. They seem to be all
in our minds.
This is not good enough. We know
it's all in our minds. The problem is,
as it always has been, what to do
about it
If we are going to be fair about it,
one thing we must do about it is not
trash the truths of others. Your chilly
remarks about traditional forms created
by (artistically) dead people through
which students must be forced, toss
aside the truths of many people, alive
and (really) dead. Why? They are not
perfect truths, of course, but you are
speaking of the dance tradition in the
West which every teacher at your
institute knows, and which I also stud-
ied and which gave me a lot of the
basis for Contact Improvisation.
Did you imagine that Contact Impro-
visation dropped out of the sky? You
use it in your institute as if it were
unconnected from past techniques.
But so intertwined is it with modem
dance (and modern to ballet) that to
dismiss any of the past dance work is
to orphan it It eliminates a lot of the
training which I assumed would be
part of the studies of people who use
it Have you noticed that there are no
exercises for the feet or legs in the
typical contact class? I assumed that
the feet, legs, circulatory systems and
joints from the past were quite sub-
stantial truths and there was no imme-
diate need to replace or erase them.
Furthermore, I assumed that these
people would also be studying Tai Chi
Chuan and Aikido, so I made CI a sort
of bridge for Western (traditional)
dance students into the unfamiliar
Eastern forms. It is an inclusive ap-
proach, based perhaps on advice I
heard from Pauline Koner, a famous
modem dancer: to study as many
techniques as I could with integrity. I
found this excellent guidance because
the real study is the human body, and
in the various technical materials from
different times and cultures a larger
range of information was available to
help with perennial problems: aware-
ness, efficiency, longevity.
'To study' meant to get it into the
body, not only the mind, which I think
requires at least 2 years of regular
class in the specific technique to em-
body the essentials.
Is there some way to use 'truth'
which is not so subjective? To follow
Pauline Koner's advice creates the
hope that one's subjective understand-
ing will continue to grow, and more
parts of one's body will come under
conscious training. It takes one from
the narrow confines of preliminary
understanding to a broader attitude
which can see other truths than one's
own (yet continue to see one's own).
In the aims you mention, " ... to get
to that non-historical form, to develop
a way of working that allows an ongo-
ing process of change in form," you
have a very clear description of the
aims of modern dance, founded at the
end of the last century. (Happy Cente-
nary, Modem Dance.) Ballet training
of foot and leg have been retained,
however.
You are a pure modem dancer and a
bit of a redneck, but these appalling
qualities will not be punished. The
past doesn't care, and you are sur-
rounded and supported by its gifts,
even though you don't seem to realize
it
Contact Improvisation is not anar-
chistic, because it is a formal coopera-
tion. It is difficult in academic pro-
grams because it has no standard
which can be graded, which may be as
difficult for the students to understand
as for the staff to cope with. It is to
be experienced rather than judged, and
our modem dance research above
suggests that the experience begins to
deepen after two years of regular clas-
ses and goes on deepening, widening,
furthering, whatever. You don't 'get
there'.
Steve Paxton
Vermont

THE FOUNTAIN AT I.D.E.A.
The Fountain is a form for collective watching and reporting
one's experience of watching. It is a direct way of sharing
with a dancer some evidence of what happened in each of
us as we watched her dance. It can be used for listening
to music, poetry, looking at a painting, etc., just as well. !rs
most important feature is that discussion is not allowed: It
has four parts:
1. Watching
2. Writing-immediately, for 3 minutes, in silence
3. Reading-out loud, in a circle, only what you wrote
4. Collecting and printing what was written
by Christina Svane
In August 1991, feeling a collective need for
feedback and support in the new dance and perfor-
mance community, I conceived of I.D.E.A. (Internation-
al Dance Exchange, Amsterdam). In October, I collab-
orated with Dutch choreographer Pauline de Groot to
create a platform for such an exchange-monthly
I.D.E.A. meetings at Pauline's studio "DE KOE." On
the Saturday closest to the middle of the month,
I.D.E.A. sponsors an open showing of work and perfor-
mance/audience interaction structures, followed by
informal exchange.
At these meetings, I have been developing a form
called The Fountain, in which spontaneous reflections
are written during a three-minute pause between
dances/performances. These spontaneous writings are
read, without discussion, at the end of the entire show-
ing. The writings are then gathered and published in
the now-bimonthly I.D.E.A LOG, nee I.D.E.A. News-
letter. The Fountain form and its resultant writings
constitute the beginning of an on-going experiment
central to the purpose of I.D.E.A.: To further the explo-
ration of dance as a collective experience in present
time and space, and to create forms through which
glimpses of that experience can be passed.
Over the course of the first I.D.E.A. meetings, I
found that by restricting formal performance feedback
to only reading what was written during the three
minutes between seeing pieces kept the exchange vital,
surprising and full of imagination. To be in communi-
cation with, rather than about, what was seen opened
up a more direct and creative transference between
audience and performer, an exchange in the dimension
dance comes from: the imagination.
The model is conversation: taking each dance as a
statement and responding likewise. The realness of
the moment and our mutual share in creating that
moment is not conditioned or tamed in any way by
calling a dance a "work-in-progress." The moment is
still absolute; the future we envision may never arrive;
the improvements we seek are secondary. In conversa-
tion, someone says to you, "I love you!" You don't
respond by telling them they should focus more on
their pronunciation. You say, "I love you, too." Or you
say something else. So this experiment is a challenge
to us to be in the moment with the performer.
Although my dream is to have a gathering of all
kinds of artists, all kinds of people, the truth remains
that we as an audience for each other are primarily
dancers. And this is, we are, a gold mine of attention
-'-highly developed attention-for each other. As danc-
ers we are skilled as listeners-listening to our sensa-
tions, to the moment we are in, to the transformation
of energy through the moment, to the level of state-
ment that moves you when you see it. It is this won-
derful degree of listening that we bring with us to each
Open Showing. The dances are the tips of the icebergs
of our experience. They are the part we can show.
For complete information on I.D.E.A meetings and I.D.E.A.
LOG, contact Christina Svane, Ruysdaelkade 25b, 1072 AH
Amsterdam. Tel. (020) 662-6827.

TECHNIQUES
by Steve Paxton
How awkwardly we are damned if we don't or if we
do, I thought to myself after the European Contact Confer-
ence in Berlin in 1988. During a lecture event I had been
asked, "Just what is Contact Improvisation?" and I had
muffed the answer. I was being asked by a woman with an
attitude, in the presence of a number of veteran contactors,
general public, and students, and I hesitated in order to think
how the question should be answered for the whole group.
The discussion up to that point had been full of historical
revisions, elisions, pointed remarks about Americentric
heavy-footedness. In short, the European contactors consid-
ered themselves second generation users of this form and
wanted to know if they had to go through every step the
Americans had gone through; and a few people who had
come to the conference without prior experience ju~t wanted
Volume 18 1993
CQICI Sourcebook 255
I
~
an explanation of what this whole thing was and how it was
run and how did one improve and get recognition-things
like that, which would be reasonable questions in a normal
organization.
The journal you are now reading had, among other things,
been working for 15 years to elaborate on the basic answer
to "what is CI?" And as time has gone on, the answer has
shifted: away from an experimental dance phenomenon and
toward a physical practice allied with a number of complex
new body and mind studies. I have come to think of contact
improvisation as a physical event best described negatively-
not art, not sport, not most of the things which characterize
dancing in this century.
But I hate to describe things in the negative. It is
accurate, but the mystics used it first and I don't like using
their devices, which were used to try to inform students in
conversations about the ineffable.
We, on the other hand, are trying to describe the corpore-
al. But it is no longer easy to do so. It is, in fact, getting
more difficult We now know too much, or think we do; the
corporeal seems to be a complexity of social, physical,
geometric, glandular, political, intimate, and personal infor-
mation which is not easily renderable.
Once things were relatively simple. I thought I knew how
this then-nameless work should not be described. It was to
be an improvisation without any ambiguous appeals to the
imagination, because I did not know precisely what "the
imagination" was (in fact I thought doing this work might
educate me to the meanings of imagination, improvisation
etc.). For the same reason there should be no mention of
sexuality, psychology, spirituality. I would leave these in the
hands of the experts and proceed with what seemed more
immediate-the senses and the physical body.
It did seem that before I could begin to train the senses of
students, something had to happen in their brains. In
recognizing that we do not begin to move from zero, that first
we have a desire or image to launch the system into action,
I decided that I had to work in the area of images, though
cautiously. The images were to be, well, "real." That is,
they were not to be obviously umeal.
The effect of obviously unreal images upon the body is
fascinating. For example, if you are told to imagine (that
word!) your head is filled with a gas that's lighter than air,
it is difficult not to respond with neck extension and postural
256 CQ/CI Sourcebook
straightening. Why should this be so?
It may be related to the sort of mobilization which
happens when we are searching for a lost object-first, the
thought of the object, then the eye movements, the head
turning, standing up, facing in different directions, a tentative
walk to a new viewpoint or a possible site of the object All
this prompted by the mental image of the desired object
The responsiveness of the body to the image is innate,
apparently, and with this innate connection the body may be
responsive to any image the mind holds. But this is specula-
tion about things which should be the subject of investiga-
tions. Without a theory of imagination to support our
investigations, it seemed to me, we would have to improvise
without fictitious gasses in the head-if that were possible.
At any rate, I had only about a week to come to convey
the central idea before we, a group of apt students and
myself, would be doing it in public at the John Weber
Gallery in NYC each afternoon. This time pressure meant
that this effect of images on the body would be an essential
tool for quickly transmitting the initial states which would let
this physical duet/improvisation manifest as directly as
possible.
The first job was to point out this image-action connec-
tion. Then, exercises which demonstrate it in various parts
of the body had to be produced. For instance, a mental
exercise I gave while people were standing, in which they
were to "imagine, but don'tdo it, imagine that you are about
to take a step forward with your left foot What is the
difference? As you were. Imagine ... (repeat). Imagine that
you are about to take a step with your right foot Your le~
Right Left. Standing."
At this point, small smiles sometimes appeared on
people's faces and I suspected they felt the effect They had
gone on an imaginary walk and had felt their weight respond-
ing subtly (but really) to the image; so when "Standing" was
said, the smiles revealed that they got the small joke. They
realized that I knew about the effect We had arrived at an
invisible (but real) place together.
The stand was useful. The basic event was standing and
observing the body. This was an exercise in itself, though
a very reductive one. What gets exercised in there, inside the
standing body, is the habit of observation; a noticeable
movement of consciousness through the body. Within this
exercise there are encounters with parts of the body which
tick along or breathe along as we watch. It clearly seems to
be one sub-system, consciousness, examining others.
The other sub-systems are not obviously connected to the
wandering consciousness, except that the encounter happens
in what one calls "my body." The consciousness-as-observer
regards the other sub-systems as separate from itself.
The consciousness can travel inside the body. It is
analogous to focusing the eyes in the external world. There
is also an analogue for peripheral vision, which is the
awareness of the whole body with senses open. Knowing
these things and practicing them are different things, of
course. We know far more than we can practice. We had to
decide what to practice.
Volume 18 1993
One choice was the tiny movements the body makes while
standing. I felt they were examples of reflex actions. They
were not directed by the observing consciousness. Observ-
ing them might train the consciousness to understand reflex
speed without going through an emergency experience which
is when we're most often aware of our reflexes.
We would be provided with many examples of reflex
action when we began improvising in contact, but they might
be of little use to us, because the consciousness can easily
turn off to the experience of these reflexive moments. In
other words, we can do something without "knowing" it.
This successfully preserves the integrity of the body but does
not train the consciousness; it leaves a hole in the knowing
of the experience. Could the consciousness learn to see these
gaps of awareness? Or, if not, could it at least learn to
observe reflexive action calmly during the highly adrenalized
moments?
Why is fall consciousness so important to me? Because
consciousness can be felt to change according to what it
experiences. If a gap of consciousness occurs at a critical
moment, we lose an opportunity to learn from the moment.
A black-out lasting fractions of a second during a roll is not
acceptable as full consciousness of the roll, and the gap will
remain embedded in the movement as part of the over-all
feeling of the movement. If consciousness stays open during
these critical moments, it will have an experience of them,
and will enlarge its concept to match the new experience.
This expanded picture becomes the new ground for moving.
_My s_pecuh!tion is that _ the gaps are mo01~n!S_ Vlh~n
consciousness goes away. I don't know where. But I think
I know why. Something is happening which is too fast for
thought. For instance, the navigation of space is normally
done with bead erect and visual impressions comfortably
constant, with the horizon's horizontality remaining an
important reference for our orientation. When this visual
reference changes too rapidly for our (rather slow) conscious-
ness to comprehend, as in spinning, rolling, and other
"disorienting" movement, something reflexive and much
faster than consciousness takes over. We play with this dual
aspect of ourselves on carnival rides, or when learning to turn
in dance.
Dizziness and nausea are, I think, signals that we have
reached the borderland between these two aspects of physical
control-conscious and reflexive. When we linger in the
borderland on purpose, we become our own experiment. We
are subjecting the reflexes to stimuli so our consciousness can
watch them jump. Normally, consciousness easily slips out,
Volume 18 1993
reflexes step in, and then step aside again, as in the blink of
an eye where most of the time we are unaware of any gap in
our visual continuity.
Visual continuity is one of many ways we know "where
we are," and not knowing where we are is experienced as an
emergency situation. How many times a day, or an hour, do
we re-orient, I wonder. At any rate, contact improvisation
constantly challenges one's orientation: visual, directional,
balance, and where in the body the consciousness is posi-
tioned. The challenge to orientation is not just in the more
acrobatic aspects of the movement. Students were sometimes
made nauseous by noticing their internal space while standing
still. This, oddly enough, is probably a form of motion-
sickness, which bas been described as having a moving deck
under one's feet but a steady horizon. In the stand, one
experiences still feet and moving consciousness inside the
body. In rolling or turning, the room seems (from the point
of view of the eyes) to move, while the floor remains stable
to the touch. What I bad to do was resolve the problem of
orientation so that the consciousness could stay aware of the
movement. A chicken and egg problem.
Everyone in this group was athletic and many of them
were dancers, so presumably their consciousness bad already
been informed by body movement. But they weren't being
asked to do what they knew bow do to. They were being
asked to improvise; to do (or allow themselves to do) what
they didn't expect to do. Under these circumstances previ-
ously learned movement techniqq.es often hamper rather than
help the desired sort of manifestation. Movement techniques
are useful for other reasons, of course, but not for examining
gaps of consciousness during unexpected movement.
Consciousness, supported by a collection of images and
internal ob'servations which reinforced each other, had a job.
It should not press the body, nor engage in time travel out
of the body into memories or schedules because these bring
images which will also affect the body, distracting from the
improvisation at band. In this improvisation the conscious-
ness was to hang in with the body, during real time, and stay
alert. It should be a witness.
This sort of consciousness-awareness of the present
physical reality-is familiar to all of us. In fact the reason
it is useful is that it is commonly understood, though employ-
ing it requires choices. Without a process of sorting we are
confronted with such a variety of current physical images that
we are inundated rather than guided into a practice.
CQ/CI Sourcebook '1S7
As the right kind of images were found, a working model
began to emerge in my mind. The working model was based
on aspects of an experience that I'd had while working with
another person, in which an interesting event occurred and
was confirmed by both of us. Moments of the duet, typically
manifesting as "accidental" and flowing streams of move-
ment, were for both of us pleasant, highly stimulating, and
elemental. With other partners, more confirmations. It was
worth pursuing.
The working model was predicated on this experience and
a desire to articulate the experience so others could find it.
If others did find it, we were perhaps examining a basic
mode of communication between the reflexes of people in
physical contact as they moved. It was an idea which took
two people to have. It wasn't wrestling, embracing, sex,
social dancing, though it was an element of all of those. It
needed a name, so we could refer to it without unwanted
connotations. Contact Improvisation ... ?
To envision the exercises, I took the working model to be
a simple imaginary person with no physical, sensorial, or
social inhibitions. It was a generic person with positive
elements I had observed in many students, dancers, martial
artists, and children. I had met such a being in my partners
when we were doing "it," the duet phenomenon. I had been
such a being to them, apparently,. when they too confirmed
the moment. It had to be a fairly simple model, because the
users (the students and I) were actually functioning human
beings with more possible neuronal connections than there are
particles in the universe. I could not cope with that of
course, nor other evident human complexities. In terms of the
safety of the body in active interaction with another body,
though, I saw I could ask the student to concentrate on
movement and how it feels, and then suggest concentrating
on the sensations of their weight, momentum, friction, the
touch of their partner, the sensation of the floor under their
body, and to learn to maintain their peripheral vision of the
space. The working model had these characteristics and
others equally easy to understand These were aimed at
security, not at improvising. I recall saying that improvising
could not be taught, though it could be learned.
But I did think that safety could be taught "Keep your
knees over your toes," for instance, is taught to students of
dance. With the characteristics of the working model
developed and understood by the students, I hoped they
would be safe even if they were ass-over-teakettle.
I pressed on. I made assumptions. I assumed I could
explain things directly to the body ("notice gravity"). I
assumed the body knew what I was talking about (gravity,
usually ignored, moves into focus as a sensation of weight).
I assumed that the body, having evolved for millions of
years on this planet, was tuned first by planetary things which
create our potentials, and second by cultural things which
develop select parts of the potential. This is in line with
some current theories about how we acquire language; we are
born with potential to make the variety of human sounds and
language connections, but are guided into the specific
language of our culture, and the unused potential withers with
disuse.
For the working model, the basic question was-potential
for what? What had the culture physically suppressed or
selected out which we might reclaim? We may, with a bit of
experience of sub-cultures of our own, and other cultures,
understand something of what our culture requires of us for
inclusion: certain gestures, modes of posture and behav-
ior-body language, as they say-which constitute proper
social activities and communications, as well as the accompa-
nying mental attitudes we acquire or aspire to for proper
presentation of our 'selves'. Indeed, the very idea of a 'self
is probably a cultural construction.
In sports and dance the rules of physical behavior are
altered from the basic social behaviors to encompass activities
understood to be outside the social norm but allowable in a
controlled way. These activities require different sensing
modes than does the usual body language we must learn for
eating in company or attending school. The working model
and its characteristics were like sports and dance in that they
were altered. However, they depart from one another in how
they are learned. Contact Improvisation behavior evolves
from sensing movement; dance and sports, from attempting
movement and then letting the senses fall into line, or not.
"517t+£<~\t;.i'ri..,
'S ? A<..€.
As to how we are educated... Not to become too em-
broiled in this, we may simply note that most of us learn to
sit still and focus our attention for hours each day. The
missing potential here is obvious - movement of the body and
varieties of peripheral sensing.
Our alterations involved the investigation of space, time,
and mass with senses in peripheral sensing mode: space
becomes spherical, time is the present, mass is a changeable
orientation to gravity.
In devising an approach to this working model of the
body, I skipped blithely by constraints and taboos of touch-
ing, because people are accustomed to ignoring them on the
subway, during sports, or in the doctor's office. And I did not
emphasize the unpredictable nature of the improvisation, akin
to dancing on constantly shifting ground, but suggested
instead a steady state of watching the reflexes. Let the
reflexes figure out how to deal with the unpredictable.
This method aimed to reclaim physical possibilities which
may have become dormant, senses we have been trained to
disregard. What would this lead to, really? It sounds very
nice, this reclaiming and opening, but where would these
changes take us? Who would we be?
When we began, in 1972, I went boldly into all this. The
people in the group were young, healthy, and alert I
assumed that if we didn't consciously focus on problems we
wouldn't create them, and at least such difficulties as
258 CQICI Sourcebook
Volume 18 1993
surfaced would not have been projected. As I said earlier,
things seemed simpler then.
It was to be assumed that some of these students would
eventually teach something of what they were doing to
others, and I felt the need to develop this material in such a
way that the students were aware of the teaching mecha-
nisms. It boiled down to making the principles-physical,
mental, and cultural-known to them as they learned them.
We had the services of Steve Christiansen and his video
camera and spent hours, when we were not working on the
mat, examining the movement and ourselves, the movers.
· This was very useful. Video was, in those days, a new
tool for witnessing the body/mind, and let us see points in
action when we were operative but not conscious. Such
moments, noticed with the help of the videotape or close
observation by a partner, could later be examined in action.
Clues about the nature of the gap might be noticed. We
began to notice where the gaps appeared, at any rate, and
something of their frequency. We also noticed high unfet-
tered moments (and recalled the accompanying feelings).
And we observed fettered duets where reflexive movement
was not to be seen. Sometimes people seemed wary, unable
to let go of conscious control. Disorientation, fear, or fixed
habitual responses would be noticed.
In the working model, events of the emotions and disori-
entations which were felt in conjunction with a gap in
awareness were considered to be symptomatic that the senses
wer~ not ~uite ready to--report what was happening to the
consciousness, that instead they were reporting to the
reflexive part of the mind and body.
I couldn't understand the wary duets. Obviously trust was
missing. With some other partner or on some other day, this
tended to change. All I could do to cope with this mode was
to have partners change regularly. Eventually everyone found
a duet which led them to their reflexes.
When I proposed movement techniques they sometimes
contravened ingrained movement habits: for instance, the
aikido roll, which is a diagonally forward roll that arrived on
our shores from the Orient. At the very attempt at this roll,
students translated it into a somersault, the symmetrical
forward roll we learn in Western movement. This misunder-
standing could persist through any number of repetitions of
examples and attempts and was seriously frustrating to both
the students and me. If they couldn't put their finger on what
was going wrong, I decided, it was perhaps because nothing
in previous movement experience had prepared them for the
movement principles which were developed in the Eastern
martial arts.
These repeated attempts at the aikido roll provided the
first gap I noticed. The act of propelling the body into the
roll is slightly scary, and many people have open eyes before
and after the roll, but in the moment when they are taking
weight on their neck and shoulders, they have their eyes
squeezed shut, and they are not aware that this is so.
I did not solve this two-part problem in time for the first
performance, nor for many years after, though the students
did perfect their somersaults.
I learned that before the body could find the new rolling
Volume 18 1993
pattern it was useful for it to learn the sensations which an
aikido roll creates.
The new sensations, and the parts of the arm, shoulder,
and back where the sensations arise in the roll, could be
explored without committing the whole body to the roll. This
changed the position of the consciousness relative to the
exercise. Instead of an unknown action vs. an habitual
known action which is so similar that it subverts the attempt,
the student has two knowable actions. One is new, tentative,
but understood through the sensorial model. The other is
habitual. Having remedied the first difficulty by establishing
the difference between the two concepts of rolling, the gaps
of consciousness in either could be ferreted out.
These explorations evolved into new technical approaches.
It was noticeable that the approaches require that the student
workwith helpfolmental attitudes as-wellas-directly with the
physical body. In the aikido roll example above, it was less
helpful to know the pattern mentally and try willfully to do
it than it was to become an observer of the sensations and to
work calmly on each emotional, orientational, or habitual
block as it arose.
The quality of consciousness was coming into focus. It.
wasn't all of consciousness and all possible interactions, of
course. A working model for consciousness in this case is
Swiss cheese. We buy it as a lump, and don't attend to the
holes because we don't use them. I was trying to point to the
holes, assuming that if a Swiss cheese could be aware of
itself, then the cheesy part was one sort of consciousness and
the holes were another sort, integral to the shape, the nature
of the whole cheese. We can't take this model much further,
I realize; which may be to the advantage of getting on with
the movement and its interaction with the consciousness.
As we began work on opening up the senses to their
larger potential, it became clear that each of the senses has
images of differing natures. The Swiss cheese above, mostly
a visual image in that I am concentrating on how the cheese
looks, occurred in my brain and was transferred to another
person by my voice, so it was a verbal transmission of a
visual image. What was most needed in our work was
kinetic imagery which was both appropriate to the movement
mode and true (unlike the speculation about the consciousness
of the cheese).
The small movements of standing "still," which formed
the basis of this sort of investigation, are true; they are really
there. But are they images or sensations? If we observed
them, would they filter through the observing mind and affect
CQ/CI Sourcebook 259
the body as images do? Does the nervous system and its
mediation of posture relative to gravity have the possibility
of teaching the consciousness, and does the consciousness
have the property of amplifying or strengthening that media-
tion?
I assumed this reciprocity did exist I decided that
sensations are what we feel to be happening at the moment,
and they can become images when we take notice that we are
observing them. This suggests that the consciousness can be
aware of itself-a defining characteristic-and that there is a
positive use for this ability to split into self-regard. However,
consciousness doesn't work very well with the unknown, as
was revealed in the aikido roll. It seems to need to know or
to be noticing in order to direct itself and the body. The
attitude of "witnessing" may change this internal control
issue. The search for internal sensations reveals that "I can
feel the small movements." Then our attitude begins to shift
toward, "The body is kept upright by constant reflexive
muscular actions around the skeleton." First we feel the
movements, then we can objectify the feelings into images.
The moment between these perceptions can be as short as a
hyphen. All the above suggests that there is an important
difference between knowing-noticing and noticing-knowing.
These are the sorts of thoughts which came to mind
during the working period from January to June, 1972. Or to
say it in terms of the work: from Magnesium at Oberlin
College through the spring term at Bennington College where
we further identified the premise, the phenomenon, to the
presentation of the work in NYC I called "contact improvisa-
tions." Students from those two institutions (plus members
of the downtown dance community in NYC who "sat in")
were joined by students from the classes of Mary Fulkerson
at the U. or' Rochester. The Rochester group was least
familiar with the proposed movement, and as Fulkerson was
teaching them Release Technique via intensive image work,
it was in order to reach these students quickly that the
approach outlined above was developed beyond what had
been necessary at Bennington College.
At Bennington, I had begun by introducing these students
of Western dance to Aikido techniques. However, I soon
abandoned teaching Aikido and started work on specific tools
for the interior techniques. Although physical techniques
could be found for specific movement training, what those
students required was a vision of Eastern martial arts philoso-
phy underlying the techniques (or a viable replacement for
that). This laid the ground for introducing an improvisation
which requires a state of personal involvement and physical
responsibility with/for another person.
260 CQ/CI Saurcebook
It must be pointed out that many of the ideas about
moving which I used existed before this study. The teach-
ings of Release Technique, Aikido, and several other physical
and mental disciplines were influential, and were adjusted
according to my understanding at the time in order to convey
the movement principles of the area of improvisation de-
scribed by the words contact improvisation.
Beyond that, to enable the movement to be accomplished
safely, came physical exercises designed to introduce sensa-
tions that arise during "flying," lifting, and being lifted. and
to physically strengthen the body for these energetic extremes
of the form. Physical strength was seen as the result of
finding the right, i.e., easiest, most efficient inner "pathways."
Stress was discouraged.
We were starting to work on the technical method, a work
which continues today, in an effort to describe what Contact
Improvisation is on its own terms as a phenomenon of duet
movement as well as in the personal terms of a student, who
may for instance have physical strength but spatial weakness,
or improvisational skills but does not trust their weight to
another person.
To summarize: images were used to focus the mind and
then give the mind foci within the sensations of the body.
The words had to be unambiguous, unthreatening, infor-
mative and generally understood.
The statements had to be true, obvious, relevant.
To speak was a.lso to ~ettheton(!. I trieci to simplify the
issues of the body for transmission to another body. I moved
the mind of my mouth into my body, located issues, and
reported the issues speaking (I assumed) directly to another
body. The student's consciousness was enlisted to manage
attitudes and observe the effect of image on the body, and to
derive from these effects, and others observed within their
bodies, images which could be named and otherwise objecti-
fied and discussed, and fitted into the improvisational
structure.
I made a number of assumptions. To study something
like improvisation, which is defined as you go, everything
you assume is going to affect the result I tried to be aware
of what the assumptions were and to let the phenomenon we
explored, the narrower field of improvisation when commu-
nicating via touch with another person, guide the definitive
imagery into appropriate areas.
This guidance caused us to consider all sorts of things,
such as communication, emotion, psychology, sex, education,
childhood development, culture, taboos, space, time, and the
self. It is all very well to say that one takes responsibility for
one's self in improvisation, but it is indeed a staggering job
in its details. An improvisor's job is never done. All this to
explore the ability of the consciousness to cleave to the
body's moment and remain there as the moment changes.*
for John Cage
